All plants and animals worldwide can be classified using seven categories: kingdom, phylum, class, order, family, genus, and species. These categories distinguish species within the biological world. Goats are no exception, belonging to the Animal Kingdom, Phylum Chordata, Class Mammalia, Order Artiodactyla, Family Bovidae, Genus Capra, and Species Capra. Therefore, goats can chew the cud and are one of the many ruminants in the world. When ruminants are young, their stomachs resemble those of monogastric animals. However, as they age, their stomachs expand into four or more compartments with distinct but mutually supportive functions. From the mouth to the esophagus, these compartments are named the rumen, reticulate stomach, omasum, and abomasum. This is the most significant difference between the digestive systems of ruminants and monogastric animals like pigs and chickens.

Because goats' teeth and jaw joints are structured differently from those of cattle, their lips can flexibly grip grass. This explains their grazing behavior, which differs from cattle's reliance on tongue-rolling. When grazing, goats first break off the grass stalks with their incisors and then grind them down with their molars. During this process, their mouths secrete saliva, helping the goat grind the fodder or feed and swallowing it more easily. Once the swallowed food reaches the rumen, it is home to nearly a thousand species of microorganisms. These microorganisms break down plant fibers and food debris, forming a bolus that is regurgitated and then chewed and swallowed. The partially digested chyme enters the honeycomb stomach, omasum, and finally the abomasum. Once in the abomasum, the chyme and microorganisms are digested and broken down into nutrients that can be absorbed and utilized by the goat's small intestine.

The abomasum of a newborn lamb accounts for approximately 85% of its total stomach volume. As the lamb feeds its mother's milk, a "sulcus" forms between the end of its esophagus and the reticulum, delivering milk directly into the lamb's abomasum. At this point, the lamb's abomasum, like the abomasum of a monogastric animal, secretes gastric acid to break down the milk into absorbable nutrients, which are then excreted into the lamb's duodenum and other organs for easy absorption. As goats age, their rumen gradually enlarges. Ultimately, the rumen and reticulum of mature goats account for nearly 85% of the total stomach volume, with the omasum accounting for approximately 5% and the abomasum around 10%.

The rumen and recombustion of adult sheep are large, capable of accommodating large amounts of feed and supporting a large population of microorganisms. These microorganisms break down plant fiber into sugars and volatile short-chain fatty acids. The microorganisms also use the sugars they break down as energy, converting protein, amino acids, and even non-protein nitrogen such as urea in the feed into their own protein. The proteins contained in these microorganisms contain most of the important amino acids required by sheep. Once in the abomasum, the microorganisms are digested and broken down into nutrients for the sheep to utilize. Simply put, the rumen and recombustion of sheep act as microbial fermenters, where microorganisms continuously thrive and perform the following functions in the sheep's digestive process: 1. They help convert high-fiber feed, which is difficult for monogastric animals to digest, into usable nutrients. 2. They use the free nitrogen and non-protein nitrogen in the feed to synthesize their own protein , which can meet the majority of the sheep's protein needs. Therefore, even if urea is added to the diet at approximately 1%, microorganisms can convert the nitrogen contained in the urea into high-quality microbial protein. 3. Rumen microorganisms can also synthesize B vitamins and vitamin K. Therefore, supplementing these vitamins in goat feed is unnecessary. However, to fully utilize the functions of the rumen and recombinant stomach, high-quality silage or hay should be provided to young goats to stimulate their development and enable them to fully function as ruminants and digestive systems as they mature.

Energy: Goats require energy to meet their basic maintenance, growth, activity, pregnancy, and lactation needs. Generally speaking, a goat's energy requirements vary depending on its body weight and living environment. Carbohydrates from forage fiber, starch from grains, and feed additives such as fat and molasses are the primary sources of energy in goat diets. When formulating goat feeds, energy can be calculated using total digestible nutrients (TDN), net energy (NE), or metabolizable energy (ME). The simplest way to calculate energy requirements for meat and dairy goats is using total digestible nutrients (TDN). However, since lactation is the most important production trait for lactating goats, net energy (NE) can also be used to calculate energy requirements when formulating lactating goat feeds.

The carbohydrates and fats in forages account for approximately 60-70% of the total energy in a goat's diet. Pennisetum, silage, sedge, semi-dried sedge, Bermuda grass, alfalfa, alfalfa grains, green corn, and corn silage are all excellent sources of carbohydrates in goat fodder. Corn, soybean meal, barley, cassava, bran, rice bran, molasses, oil, and rumen-skimmed fat are also excellent sources of carbohydrates in goat concentrates. Rumen microorganisms in the goat's rumen break down these feed ingredients into short-chain fatty acids such as acetate, propionate, and butyrate. These short-chain fatty acids are then synthesized into essential nutrients for goats. For example, acetate and butyrate are synthesized into milk fat, while propionate is synthesized into amino acids. Therefore, goats rely on adequate energy to obtain the other nutrients they need. The energy concentration of forage is approximately 40-65% of the dry matter content of forage. Goats that consume more forage have higher concentrations of acetate and butyrate in their rumens, resulting in a higher percentage of milk fat. Most cereals contain approximately 70-90% energy on a dry matter basis. Goats that consume a higher amount of cereals produce a higher proportion of propionic acid in their rumen, leading to faster weight gain. Therefore, formulating a diet for dairy goats should prioritize the proportion of forage, while formulating a finishing diet for mutton goats should prioritize the proportion of concentrates.

Generally speaking, goat diets typically contain 65-80% energy as a percentage of dry matter, adjusted according to the farmer 's specific goals. In Taiwan's hot and humid climate , energy deficiencies often occur in feed formulas, particularly in high-yielding dairy goats. Most goat farmers try to address this energy deficit by purchasing green corn for silage or adding fat and rumen-pass fat to the feed. Long-term consumption of a low-energy diet can lead to slowed growth, weight loss, insignificant estrus, decreased farrowing rates, increased farrowing intervals, a shortened lactation period, and reduced milk production. Goats also experience a significant decrease in resistance to parasites and disease.

Protein: Protein is a complex structure composed of amino acids and is a key component of goat tissue . Goats require a continuous intake of protein to renew their somatic cells and synthesize milk proteins. The amount of protein required by goats varies during growth, fattening, reproduction, and lactation. Therefore , goats with different feeding objectives , weight stages, and lactation levels have varying protein requirements. Rumen microbes in goats typically break down dietary protein and non-protein nitrogen into ammoniacal nitrogen . Rumen microbes use the energy from this ammoniacal nitrogen to synthesize their own proteins . The remaining protein, which cannot be broken down by the microbes , is referred to as undissociated protein or rumen-passing protein. These microbial proteins and undissociated proteins flow to the abomasum , where they are broken down by gastric acid into peptides and amino acids . These peptides and amino acids are then absorbed and utilized by the goat in the small intestine. Since many essential amino acids such as lysine and methionine are the first or second limiting amino acids for the synthesis of milk protein, and the quality and quantity of microbial amino acids obtained by goats every day are very fixed, high-yielding dairy goats often have insufficient quality and quantity of essential amino acids entering the small intestine to meet their milk protein synthesis, which affects their milk quality and milk production. Therefore, when formulating feed for high-lactating goats, feed raw materials with a high percentage of undissociated protein or rumen bypass protein can be used to ensure that their daily diet contains more than 30% undissociated protein or rumen bypass protein.

When formulating goat feed, expressing the protein content of a diet as a percentage of crude protein (CP) is the simplest and most easily understood method. Leguminous forages such as alfalfa and grasses such as pennisetum, sedge, and bermudagrass contain large amounts of digestible protein in their leaves when cut. While the protein content increases with growth, its availability decreases. Therefore, for dairy goats, where forage constitutes a significant portion of their diet, it is crucial to ensure that the forage provided is high in leaves and cut at the appropriate time.

Many plant seeds, animal waste, and agricultural byproducts are also rich in protein. Soybean meal, fish meal, blood meal, and distiller's grains are excellent sources of protein in feed. The recommended protein percentage in goat diets is generally between 14-20%. Early lactation and high lactation dairy goats should aim for 18-20%, mid- lactation dairy goats, growing females, and finishing goats should aim for 16-18%, and pregnant goats and dry goats should aim for 14-16%. While goats can compensate for protein deficiency in their diets by utilizing muscle protein or amino acids in their blood, chronic protein deficiency can lead to slower growth, decreased appetite, weight loss, coarse hair, decreased milk production, irregular estrus, and reduced conception rates. Therefore, goat farmers must pay close attention to protein intake when balancing their diets.

Minerals: Generally speaking , grazing goats are not lacking in minerals. However, as goat farming has shifted from free-range to pen-feeding , insufficient minerals in goat concentrate can lead to metabolic disorders , which can affect production and growth .

Calcium: Calcium is one of the important nutrients for bone formation. If goats lack calcium, their lambs will suffer from rickets or osteomalacia, and adult goats will develop osteoporosis and be prone to fractures. Grass raw materials contain a high percentage of calcium, but because grass has a high cellulose content, it reduces the free calcium, so the utilization rate of grass calcium is not high, while the calcium content in cereals is low. Goats often need a lot of calcium for growth or lactation, so when preparing goat concentrate, it is often necessary to add calcium hydrogen phosphate, shell powder or limestone powder in moderation to meet the needs of goats. Calcium accounts for about 0.3-0.8% per kilogram of diet in goat diets. (2) Phosphorus: Phosphorus is one of the important nutrients for the formation of goat bodies. Phosphorus deficiency can cause goats to lose their appetite and develop pica behaviors such as gnawing on hair, skin and other debris. In fact, when goats lack phosphorus, they will become seriously emaciated, which will affect their reproductive traits. Forage grasses typically contain a low percentage of phosphorus. While cereals contain a higher percentage, this is organic phosphorus and is not easily utilized. Therefore, when formulating feed, it's important to add inorganic phosphorus, such as dicalcium phosphate (also known as dicalcium phosphate), to prevent phosphorus deficiency in goats. Therefore, grazing goats require special attention to phosphorus deficiency, while pen-housed goats must also be mindful of phosphorus excess. Furthermore, when formulating goat diets, the calcium-to-phosphorus ratio must be considered. The optimal ratio is 2:1. An inappropriate calcium-to-phosphorus ratio can lead to urolithiasis in fattening castrated rams, and in severe cases, even death. Phosphorus constitutes approximately 0.3-0.5% per kilogram of diet in meat goat diets and 0.45-0.8% per kilogram of diet in dairy goat diets.

Magnesium: Magnesium is an element that promotes enzyme activation and plays a crucial role in nerve-muscle transmission. It is also involved in calcium and phosphorus metabolism. Magnesium deficiency in goats can lead to muscle cramps and, in severe cases, death.

Sulfur : Sulfur is a key component of sulfur-containing amino acids . If sulfur is insufficient, rumen microorganisms will also produce insufficient amounts of essential amino acids such as methionine and cystine. Sulfur is also a building block for many coenzymes , so sulfur deficiency can lead to metabolic disorders in goats.

Potassium, sodium, and chloride: Potassium, sodium, and chloride play important physiological roles in goats. Functions such as osmotic pressure are all related to potassium, sodium, and chloride. Potassium deficiency can lead to endocrine disorders, resulting in weight loss and muscle weakness. Generally speaking, forage grass contains a large amount of potassium, so goats are less likely to suffer from potassium deficiency. Sodium and chloride, on the other hand, are supplied by table salt. A deficiency in these two factors can affect protein metabolism, leading to decreased appetite, slowed growth, and weight loss. Therefore, when preparing goat concentrate, add 0.5% coarse salt to meet the goat's needs.

Trace minerals: Cobalt, copper, iodine, iron, manganese, selenium, and zinc are all trace minerals. Although goats require very small amounts daily, they are essential for their metabolism. Furthermore, a deficiency in a particular trace mineral often affects the absorption and utilization of other trace minerals, minerals, and vitamins. When formulating feed, care should be taken to ensure that excessive trace mineral supplementation can cause toxicity in goats. Therefore, appropriate amounts should be added to the feed.

Vitamins: Vitamins are essential for goats' metabolic processes. Generally speaking, fresh grass contains a high amount of vitamins, so grazing goats are not likely to be deficient. However, for pen-raised goats, when their diet primarily consists of concentrates or sun-dried hays such as sedge hay, alfalfa hay, and alfalfa pellets, the vitamins in these feeds are destroyed during feed processing and hay making. Therefore, vitamins must be added to the concentrate mix to meet the goats' physiological needs.

Vitamin A: Vitamin A deficiency can lead to decreased appetite in goats, reduced semen quality in rams, and a lack of libido. It can also cause estrus in ewes and reduced reproductive efficiency. Severe deficiency can also cause night blindness in goats. Therefore, the 1981 edition of the NRC recommended a minimum of 3,500 international units (IU) of vitamin A per kilogram of goat diet. The 2007 edition of the NRC changed the estimated vitamin A requirement for goats to be based on retinol equivalents (RE).

Vitamin D: Vitamin D plays a crucial role in calcium and phosphorus absorption in goats. A deficiency can lead to an imbalance in calcium and phosphorus absorption, impairing bone development. Grazing sheep synthesize vitamin D from sunlight on their skin and therefore are not susceptible to vitamin D deficiency. However, confined sheep, deprived of prolonged sunlight and limited access to fresh pasture, often suffer from vitamin D deficiency. Therefore, the 1981 edition of the NRC recommended a minimum of 250 international units (IU) of vitamin D per kilogram of diet for goats, and the 2007 edition revised the vitamin D requirement for goats.

Vitamin E: Also known as reproductive alcohol, vitamin E deficiency can lead to white muscle disease in lambs and reproductive failure in ewes due to retained placenta. Therefore, the 1981 edition of the NRC recommended a minimum of 5 mg of vitamin E per kilogram of goat diet. The 2007 edition of the NRC revised the vitamin E requirement for goats to be estimated in International Units (IU), with a minimum recommended intake of 5.3 IU/kg body weight and a maximum tolerable intake of 75 IU/kg body weight.

Vitamin B complex and vitamin C: They can be synthesized by rumen microorganisms, so they are not easily deficient.

Water: Water is crucial for goats. It's essential for growth, metabolism, reproduction, and lactation. Generally, water accounts for 75% of a goat's body mass, but this amount varies with age and environment. The majority of water consumed by goats comes from drinking water, feed water, and metabolic water. An adult goat can consume between 4 and 12 liters of water per day. Lactating goats require an additional 2.5 liters of water for every additional liter of milk produced. Factors that influence goat water consumption include feed type, milk production, environment, temperature, and the protein and salt content of the diet. When providing drinking water to goats, it's important to consider water quality; it must be clean and uncontaminated. Farmers relying on groundwater as their primary water source should first aerate the groundwater in aeration tanks before providing it to goats. It's also recommended that the groundwater be tested for contamination by heavy metals, pesticides, and nitrates. Lack of water will cause goats to have lower feed intake, slower growth, and lower milk production. Therefore, goat farmers should not neglect the supply and quality of water because it is easier to obtain water.

Dietary Feeding Standards: Even though the microorganisms in the goat's rumen can synthesize microbial protein, they still need to consume sufficient nutrients daily for effective utilization. The daily supply of nutrients, including energy and protein, must be appropriate. Insufficient nutrition can slow growth and reduce reproductive efficiency, while excessive amounts can be wasteful. In fact, advanced livestock farming countries around the world have conducted countless feeding and nutrition trials over the years, gaining a deep understanding of the energy and protein requirements for goats of varying weights and physiological conditions to achieve established feeding goals. Detailed data on goat nutritional requirements is now available for reference, and this data serves as the "feeding standard."

This article adopts the goat feeding standards set by the U.S. NRC in 1981 and 2007, which are briefly translated as follows: 1. When kept in a pen and with little exercise, the basic nutrients required to maintain body weight are as follows: (1) For a body weight of 10 kg, the required amount is total digestible nutrients (TDN): 159 g, crude protein: 22 g, calcium: 1 g, phosphorus: 0.7 g, vitamin A: 0.4 × 1000 IU, vitamin D: 84 × IU; (2) For a body weight of 20 kg, the required amount is total digestible nutrients (TDN): 267 g, crude protein: 38 g, calcium: 1 g, phosphorus: 0.7 g, vitamin A: 0.7 × 1000 IU, vitamin D: 144 × IU. ×IU; (3) For a body weight of 30 kg, the required amount is: total digestible nutrients (TDN): 362 g, crude protein: 51 g, calcium: 2 g, phosphorus: 1.4 g, vitamin A: 0.9 × 1000 IU, vitamin D: 195 × IU; (4) For a body weight of 40 kg, the required amount is: total digestible nutrients (TDN): 448 g, crude protein: 63 g, calcium: 2 g, phosphorus: 1.4 g, vitamin A: 1.2 × 1000 IU, vitamin D: 243 × IU; (5) For a body weight of 50 kg, the required amount is: total digestible nutrients (TDN): 530 g, crude protein: 75 g, calcium: 3 g, phosphorus: 2.1 g, vitamin A: 1.4 × 1000 IU, vitamin D: 285 ×IU; (6) For a body weight of 60 kg, the required nutrients are total digestible nutrients (TDN): 608 g, crude protein: 86 g, calcium: 3 g, phosphorus: 2.1 g, vitamin A: 1.6 ×1000 IU, and vitamin D: 327 ×IU. 2. In addition to maintaining body weight, the additional nutrients required for different daily gains are as follows: (1) For a weight gain of 0.05 kg, the additional nutrients are: total digestible nutrients (TDN): 100 g, crude protein: 14 g, calcium: 1 g, phosphorus: 0.7 g, vitamin A: 0.3 × 1000 IU, vitamin D: 54 × IU; (2) For a weight gain of 0.10 kg, the additional nutrients are: total digestible nutrients (TDN): 200 g, crude protein: 28 g, calcium: 1 g, phosphorus: 0.7 g, vitamin A: 0.5 × 1000 IU, vitamin D: 108 × IU; (3) For a weight gain of 0.15 kg, the additional nutrients are: total digestible nutrients (TDN): 300 g, crude protein: 42 g, calcium: 2 g, phosphorus: 1.4 g, vitamin A: 0.8 × 1000 IU, vitamin D: 162 × IU. 3. Additional nutrients required by ewes during late pregnancy include: Total digestible nutrients (TDN): 397 g, crude protein: 82 g, calcium: 2 g, phosphorus: 1.4 g, vitamin A: 1.1 x 1000 IU, vitamin D: 213 x IU. 4. Additional nutrients required daily for different growth stages include: Total digestible nutrients (TDN): 347 g, crude protein: 72 g, calcium: 3 g, phosphorus: 2.1 g, vitamin A: 0.8 x 1000 IU, vitamin D: 760 x IU.

The nutritional composition of soybean meal is dry matter: 89%, total digestible nutrients: 81%, crude protein: 44%, calcium: 0.36%, phosphorus: 1.75%, rumen bypass protein 30%.
The nutritional composition of corn is dry matter: 87%, total digestible nutrients: 91%, crude protein: 8%, calcium: 0.02%, phosphorus: 0.35%, rumen bypass protein 30%.
The nutritional composition of pangu hay is dry matter: 85%, total digestible nutrients: 51%, crude protein: 4%, calcium: 0.9%, phosphorus: 0.29%, acid washed fiber: 46%, rumen bypass protein: 30%
The nutritional composition of alfalfa hay is dry matter: 91%, total digestible nutrients: 52%, crude protein: 15%, calcium: 1.13%, phosphorus: 0.18%, acid washed fiber: 44%, rumen bypass protein: 30%.
The nutritional composition of molasses is dry matter: 75%, total digestible nutrients: 72%, crude protein: 58%, calcium: 1.00%, phosphorus: 0.11%
The nutritional composition of silage corn is dry matter: 33%, total digestible nutrients: 70%, crude protein: 8.1%, calcium: 0.23%, phosphorus: 0.22%, acid washed fiber: 28%

Balanced feed formula steps: (1) Review the feeding method of the flock. (2) Estimate the daily dry matter intake of the sheep. (3) Determine the average daily weight gain of meat sheep or the daily milk production and milk fat percentage of lactating sheep. (4) Check the flock weight table to find out the daily nutrient requirements of the flock. (5) Find out the nutrient content of the feed available from the feed nutrient composition table. (6) Calculate the feed composition required for the flock. (7) Calculate the calcium and phosphorus requirements and the calcium-phosphorus ratio. (8) Calculate the acid-washed fiber content of the diet. The acid-washed fiber content of lactating sheep should be greater than 19%. (9) Add 0.5 kg of dicalcium phosphate per 100 kg of feed. (10) Add 0.4 kg of limestone powder per 100 kg of feed. (11) Add 0.5 kg of coarse salt per 100 kg of feed. (12) Add 0.1 kg of vitamin and trace element premix per 100 kg of feed.

Urolithiasis: Urolithiasis in goats often occurs in castrated male goats. Castration narrows the urethra, causing minerals in the urine to form tiny stones, preventing them from being excreted smoothly. These tiny stones can accumulate in injured or narrowed areas of the urethra, eventually forming larger stones. This can cause the goat to urinate only in dribbling bladders. Goats may also kick their abdomens and cry out in pain if touched. If left untreated within 24 hours, the goat may die, and its meat will have a urine-like odor. Urolithiasis in goats is preventable. Management strategies include avoiding castration of male goats before five months of age, as early castration can result in a smaller urethra. However, adjusting feed formula can effectively prevent the symptoms of urolithiasis in castrated goats.

Prevention of urolithiasis includes the following: 1. Ensure the calcium-to-phosphorus ratio in feed formulas does not exceed 2:1, and the phosphorus content does not exceed 0.45% to prevent the formation of phosphate stones. 2. Increase the salt content in the feed formula to 0.5% to encourage urinary intake in animals with this condition. 3. Increase the ammonium chloride content in the feed formula to 0.5% to alter the urinary pH of animals with this condition. However, due to the poor palatability of ammonium chloride , other additives may be needed to improve the flavor of the feed. 4. Provide clean water to encourage urinary intake in animals with this condition. 5. Ensure drinking water facilities are convenient for animals to drink freely and are easy to clean.

Pregnancy toxemia or ketosis: Pregnancy toxemia in goats often occurs in female goats in the late stages of pregnancy, while ketosis also often occurs in female goats that have just given birth. This condition often occurs in ewes with a litter size exceeding two or more lambs or in high milk production. The lambs in the uterus squeeze the rumen, causing the ewe's daily energy intake to be insufficient to meet the needs of her body and the fetus. This causes the ewe to metabolize her own fat, resulting in a high concentration of ketone bodies in her blood and acidemia, which often leads to the death of both the ewe and her lambs before giving birth. In highly productive ewes that have just given birth, their milk production needs far exceed their daily food intake, causing them to metabolize their own fat, similarly leading to acidemia with high ketone bodies, which often leads to postpartum death.

Preventing pregnancy toxemia or ketosis can be done by: 1. Avoid overweighting ewes before breeding. Record their pre-breeding body fatness scores for follow-up management and tracking. 2. Increase the proportion of concentrates and reduce the proportion of forage in the feed formula during late pregnancy and early postpartum to improve the energy concentration of the feed. 3. Increase the percentage of high-quality grains in the concentrate to increase the energy concentration of the feed. 4. Use a small amount of rumen-free fat in the feed formula to increase the energy concentration of the feed. 5. Supplement molasses water two weeks before or after calving. 6. Ewes of concern should be isolated and managed individually, and the feed provided should be fresh, small, and frequent. 7. Feed propylene glycol according to veterinary instructions.

Rickets in lambs: Rickets in lambs is caused by vitamin D deficiency or lack of sunlight. A small part of the cause is an imbalance in the calcium-phosphorus ratio in the diet, which causes the lambs' limbs to bend and they are unable to stand.

Rickets in lambs can be prevented by: 1. Keep ewes and lambs in direct sunlight. 2. Ensure vitamin D supplementation during pregnancy and a balanced calcium-phosphorus ratio in the lamb's diet. 3. Ensure vitamin D supplementation at birth and a balanced calcium-phosphorus ratio in the lamb's diet. 4. Healthy ewes produce high-quality colostrum.

White muscle disease in lambs: White muscle disease in lambs is caused by a deficiency of vitamin E or the trace element selenium. It often occurs when lambs graze on land lacking the trace element selenium or eat hay of very poor quality, causing the newborn lambs to be unable to stand.

Prevention of white muscle disease in lambs can be as follows: 1. Vitamin E supplementation should be provided to ewes during pregnancy. 2. Vitamin E supplementation should be provided to lambs at birth. 3. Only healthy ewes can produce high-quality colostrum. 4. Ewes with retained placenta should be treated. 5. Lambs can receive vitamin E injections. 6. Vitamin E can be added to the concentrate feed of adult lambs.

Pica in goats: Pica in goats is caused by a lack of the mineral phosphorus. Phosphorus deficiency can cause goats to lose their appetite and even develop pica behaviors such as gnawing on hair, skin, and other debris.

Ways to prevent pica in goats include: 1. Balancing the calcium-phosphorus ratio in the diet. 2. Increasing the proportion of cereals in the diet. 3. Increasing the proportion of calcium hydrogen phosphate in the diet.

Night blindness in goats: Night blindness in goats is caused by vitamin A deficiency. Lack of vitamin A in the diet can cause goats to lose their vision.

Night blindness in goats can be prevented by: 1. Be aware of vitamin A deficiency in goats raised on pasture. 2. For goats that cannot be raised on pasture, provide them with grass. 3. Add vitamin A to their feed. 4. Provide vitamin A supplements during pregnancy. 5. Provide vitamin A supplements for lambs at birth. 6. Healthy ewes produce high-quality colostrum.

Bloat in goats: Bloat in goats is typically caused by consuming large amounts of rich, moist, fresh forage or grains, such as clover, alfalfa, or legumes. These moist, fresh forages form tiny bubbles in the rumen, causing the goat to belch and unable to pass gas. The rumen expands with bubbles, compressing the diaphragm, and the goat may quickly die from respiratory or circulatory failure.

Prevention of bloat in goats includes the following: 1. Those raising goats on pasture should avoid feeding them rich, moist, fresh forage. 2. For non-urgent cases, remove the current feed and avoid feeding the same feed again. 3. Use rumen puncture to relieve rumen pressure. 4. Feed the goats dry.

Enterotoxemia in goats: Enterotoxemia in goats is caused by goats consuming a large amount of concentrate in a very short period of time, which causes the pH value of the goats' rumen to become acidic, allowing clostridia in the intestines to multiply rapidly, resulting in the goats' death from poisoning.

There are several ways to prevent goat hyperacidity: 1. Maintain an appropriate ratio of roughage to concentrate in the goat's diet. 2. Adjusting the amount of roughage to concentrate in the goat's diet takes about 14 days and should be done gradually.

Enterotoxemia: When lambs over-suck goat's milk or eat feed containing a large amount of grain, or when the sheep's daily diet contains rich grains and low hay content, the clostridium in their intestines will multiply and produce toxins, causing the sheep to die rapidly.

Enterotoxemia can be prevented by: 1. Maintaining an appropriate ratio of roughage to concentrate in the goat's diet. 2. Adjusting the amount of roughage to concentrate in the goat's diet takes about 14 days and must be done gradually.

Hypocalcemia: In the late stages of pregnancy or early lactation, due to inappropriate calcium ratio in the diet, the calcium concentration in the blood of the lactating sheep is low, resulting in the ewe's loss of appetite and inability to stand.

Hypocalcemia can be prevented by: 1. Treatment is the same as for ketosis. 2. Calcium supplementation, either orally or through an IV drip. 3. Adjusting the feed formula during late pregnancy or early lactation to increase the ratio of calcium concentrations between these two stages.

Nitrate toxicity: Under normal conditions, nitrate ingested by goats is converted to ammonia in the rumen and then to bacterial protein. However, nitrate converts to nitrite more rapidly than nitrite converts to ammonia. Therefore, when goats consume large amounts of nitrate, the excess nitrite in the rumen diffuses through the rumen wall and enters the goat's bloodstream, converting hemoglobin into methemoglobin. Methemoglobin is unable to transport oxygen, resulting in death from hypoxia.
Ways to prevent nitrate toxicity include: 1. Avoid using young plants. 2. Avoid using plants that have just been fertilized. 3. Avoid using water sources contaminated with nitrates.

Polioencephalomalacia: Polioencephalomalacia (PEM) is a common neuromuscular metabolic disorder caused by a vitamin B1 deficiency. Polioencephalomalacia in goats typically occurs in small ruminants during their growing season who consume a diet high in concentrates. Possible causes include sudden changes in feed, feeding goats a high ratio of molasses, providing goats with moldy hay, providing goats with a high concentrate concentration, weaning stress, and heat stress. This condition occurs when an unbalanced diet or stressful management causes a decrease in rumen pH, preventing the bacteria responsible for vitamin B1 from producing it properly. Excessive sulfur ions in the diet or water can also contribute to the condition.

Prevention of cerebral palsy can be achieved through the following methods: 1. Maintain a balanced diet for goats. 2. Ensure an appropriate amount of sulfur in the diet. 3. Provide high-quality grass or hay as part of the diet. 4. If using grazing water or groundwater for goats, check the sulfur content of the water.

Urea toxicity: Goats can experience excess ammoniacal nitrogen production in their rumen, which then diffuses into their bloodstream, where it is synthesized in their liver and returned to the rumen via saliva. Urea is commonly added to ruminant diets, but excessive amounts can cause death in goats.

Prevention of urea poisoning includes the following: 1. When adding urea to goat feed, use it with ingredients that quickly provide energy. 2. Mix the urea thoroughly into the goat feed. 3. Goats need at least three weeks to acclimate to the urea before they can effectively utilize it. 4. The recommended dosage of urea should not exceed one-third of the total crude protein in the feed.

Ulcerative posthitis: Ulcerative posthitis is a bacterial inflammatory disease that often occurs in breeding rams, rams and wethers. It is caused by the interaction between goats eating high-protein diets or eating large amounts of leguminous forages, which leads to excessive production of urea.

Prevention of ulcerative balanitis includes the following: 1. Provide clean, freely accessible water. 2. Provide a balanced goat diet. 3. Avoid high protein intake. 4. Avoid large amounts of fresh, juicy legumes.

Parasites: Common parasites in goats can be categorized as external and internal. The best preventative measures are to provide a clean environment from birth and minimize exposure to parasites. Balanced nutrition is crucial for maintaining goat health and helping them develop adequate resistance to external pathogens, especially for grazing ewes before and after giving birth.

The following are some ways to prevent parasites: 1. Avoid grazing in wet areas and in the early morning and evening when there is dew on the pasture. 2. Regular deworming.


Forage: Forage is a key component of herbivorous animal diets. To enhance sheep health and performance, forage quality and a stable supply are becoming increasingly important. Whether producing or purchasing, sheep farmers must balance labor costs, economic benefits, and meeting animal needs. Domestically cultivated forages include sedge, pennisetum, Nile grass, and green corn; imported forages include hay products such as alfalfa, Bermuda grass, oats, and Timothy. The following briefly summarizes the types and characteristics of major domestic and imported forages, including their cultivation, management, preparation, and utilization, for reference in forage selection and utilization.

Pangu Grass: Pangu Grass is native to South Africa and is a perennial tropical grass of the Poaceae family . It has runners with roots growing from each node . Its stems are thin and leafy , making it highly competitive against weeds. Currently, the strain cultivated in China is almost exclusively A254, a triploid plant that flowers in summer but does not produce viable seeds . It is propagated vegetatively by stem seedlings. Pangu Grass prefers high temperatures and high rainfall and is adaptable to a wide range of soils . The optimal planting period is March to September . It is propagated by cuttings of 2-3 stem nodes or by broadcasting whole stem seedlings. Cuttings require approximately 300-400 kg / hectare , making them suitable for soils with poor water retention or insufficient moisture. Broadcasting requires approximately 1000-1200 kg / hectare . Whole stems or cuttings should be broadcast when the soil is moist and then pressed down with a disc harrow to ensure the stems are firmly integrated into the soil and allow rooting to develop. Apply 300-400 kg/ha of Compound Fertilizer No. 2 or No. 39 during new planting or at the beginning of each growing season. Apply 300-400 kg/ha of Compound Fertilizer No. 1 after each harvest. Harvest the grass 30-45 days after planting to increase coverage and suppress weeds. Harvest when the grass is 60-80 cm tall. Pangu grass is in its peak production period in spring and summer, with growth stagnating in winter. The annual dry matter yield is approximately 20-30 metric tons per hectare, making it suitable for fodder, hay, and semi-dry silage. The main nutritional components are crude protein (6.1-12.5%), fiber (63.9-75.6%), acid-washed fiber (32.4-43.8%), and medium-washed fiber.

Nile grass: Native to South Africa , Nile grass is a perennial, slender-stemmed grass of the Poaceae family. Bred and selected by the Animal Husbandry Research Institute , three varieties are available : Taichucao 1-3 . Nile grass is not only adaptable to hot and rainy climates , but also thrives well in autumn and winter with irrigation . It flowers year-round but does not produce seeds . Its stems are upright and hollow , with roots forming at the nodes and new shoots growing from the underground rhizomes . Its strong regeneration capacity allows it to be propagated vegetatively by stem seedlings. While Nile grass is best planted during the rainy season , it can be broadcast year-round if irrigation is available . Approximately 2,500 kg of seedlings are used per hectare , using the same method as for pangu grass. For paddy fields, water the soil after preparation and scatter the stem seedlings on the surface . Keep the soil submerged for about five days , then drain after the nodes germinate. Apply 300-400 kg/ha of Compound Fertilizer No. 2 or No. 3-9 during new planting or at the beginning of each growing season . Apply 300-400 kg/ha of Compound Fertilizer No. 1 after each harvest. Harvest the grass 50-60 days after planting (or when the grass is 90-120 cm tall), and harvest every 6-8 weeks thereafter for optimal yield and quality. Nile grass requires more water than pangu grass, so irrigation is necessary if rain does not fall for an extended period during the growing season. Annual dry matter yield per hectare is approximately 25-30 metric tons, making it suitable for fodder, hay, and semi-dry silage. Nile grass varieties are divided into Taixucao No. 1, Taixucao No. 2, and Taixucao No. 3. The main nutritional composition is: (1) The main nutrients of Taixucao No. 1 are crude protein: 8-10%, acid-washed fiber: 38-42%, and medium-washed fiber: 61-67%; (2) The main nutrients of Taixucao No. 2 are crude protein: 9-11%, acid-washed fiber: 33-38%, and medium-washed fiber: 63-68%; (3) The main nutrients of Taixucao No. 3 are crude protein: 8-10%, acid-washed fiber: 33-38%, and medium-washed fiber: 63-68%.

Pennisetum: Pennisetum, also known as elephant grass , is native to South Africa and is a perennial tropical grass of the Poaceae family. Bred and selected by the Animal Husbandry Research Institute , seven varieties are available: Taixucao 1-7 . With the exception of the extremely dwarf variety 6 , suitable for pets , the remaining tall varieties (2 , 4 , and 5 ) and semi-dwarf varieties (1 , 3 , and 7 ) are all upright, high-yielding forage grasses with strong weed competitiveness. Varieties vary in plant shape, leaf-to-stem ratio, and growth characteristics. Tall varieties offer the highest yield , reaching up to 300 tons of fresh grass per hectare annually , while dwarf varieties offer superior quality. Although Pennisetum blooms in autumn , it is cultivated asexually using stem seedlings. Pennisetum prefers high temperatures and high rainfall , and is suited to moist, deep soil . The optimal planting period is from March to September . Cuttings with 2-3 nodes or whole-stem seedlings can be planted flat in a shallow trench and covered with soil. For cuttings, plant rows 50-100 cm apart; for full-stem seedlings, plant rows 80-100 cm apart, and for dwarf varieties, plant rows 50 cm apart. Plant spacing is 50 cm for all varieties. In areas with poor drainage, it is best to create drainage ditches and establish borders for planting. Seedlings should be planted at a rate of approximately 2,000-3,000 kg/hectare. Apply 400-600 kg/hectare of No. 2 compound fertilizer during new planting or at the beginning of each growing season. Apply 400-600 kg/hectare of No. 1 compound fertilizer after each harvest, and 300-400 kg/hectare of No. 2 compound fertilizer after the last harvest of the year. Mow 40-50 days after planting. Irrigate and fertilize after harvest to promote growth and suppress weeds. From the second harvest onward, harvest approximately every 5-8 weeks in summer and 8-12 weeks in winter. Leave stumps approximately 5-15 cm above the ground for optimal yield and quality. In poorly drained areas, avoid mowing during periods of heavy rain and waterlogging. The stumps should be left high to prevent rot at the stem base and roots. Pennisetum yields approximately 20-60 metric tons of dry matter per hectare annually, making it suitable for both fodder and silage. For silage, try mowing during full daylight hours, near noon, for better silage quality. The varieties of Pennisetum are divided into Taixucao No. 1, Taixucao No. 2, Taixucao No. 3, Taixucao No. 4, Taixucao No. 5, Taixucao No. 6 and Taixucao No. 7. The main nutritional composition is as follows: (1) The main nutrients of Taixucao No. 1 are crude protein: 12.8%, acid-washed fiber: 35.0%, and medium-washed fiber: 61.8%; (2) The main nutrients of Taixucao No. 2 are crude protein: 9.2%, acid-washed fiber: 37.9%, and medium-washed fiber: 63.8%; (3) The main nutrients of Taixucao No. 3 are crude protein: 13.1%, acid-washed fiber: 30.9%, and medium-washed fiber: 5 7.3%; (4) The main nutrients of Taixucao No. 4 are crude protein: 11.6%, acid-washed fiber: 38.5%, and medium-washed fiber: 66.0%; (5) The main nutrients of Taixucao No. 5 are crude protein: 9.2%, acid-washed fiber: 46.4%, and medium-washed fiber: 72.9%; (6) The main nutrients of Taixucao No. 6 are crude protein: 12.0%, acid-washed fiber: 31.8%, and medium-washed fiber: 60.7%; (7) The main nutrients of Xucao No. 7 are crude protein: 10.2%, acid-washed fiber: 37.1%, and medium-washed fiber: 67.0%.

Green-cut corn: Green-cut corn is an annual grass family forage plant, harvested whole for animal consumption. Varieties include hard corn for feed, edible corn, or specialized green-cut corn varieties. Common varieties include the imported Mingfeng No. 3, Tainan No. 24 and No. 29, suitable for both hard and green-cut, and Kenting No. 1, developed at the Animal Husbandry Research Institute specifically for green-cut corn. Because green-cut corn has cobs rich in starch and sugar, its nutritional value doesn't drop dramatically with delayed harvesting, making it easy to prepare into silage, earning it the nickname "king of forage." The optimal planting seasons are primarily spring and autumn. Depending on regional climate, spring sowing occurs from early February to early March, while autumn sowing occurs from late August to early October. To minimize supply gaps and effectively utilize land, it is currently cultivated year-round, allowing for rotation with appropriate crops. Plant rows 70-80 cm apart, with plant spacing 20-25 cm. Seed usage varies depending on seed size, approximately 18-20 kg per hectare. Base fertilizer can be 400 kg/ha of No. 39 compound fertilizer. When plants reach 30-40 cm tall, inter-row cultivation should be applied, followed by topdressing with 400 kg/ha of No. 1 compound fertilizer. When plants reach 30-40 cm tall, inter-row cultivation and soil building should be performed along with topdressing to suppress weeds and prevent lodging. Choosing resistant varieties is the most basic pest and disease control method. If pests and diseases occur, refer to control methods for hard corn or edible corn. To avoid pesticide residues, early application of pesticides and biological control should be prioritized. If control measures are not available, early harvesting can be used. Avoiding continuous cropping can also reduce pest and disease occurrence. Green corn yields approximately 10-15 metric tons of dry matter per hectare per crop, making it suitable for green fodder and silage. Yields peak at the yellow ripening stage, and nutritional value is enhanced by increased kernel fill and starch accumulation. However, to accommodate land use, the optimal harvesting period for green fodder can be slightly relaxed , but it should still be avoided too early. For silage, it should still be harvested after the kernels have sunken, when the plant's moisture content is approximately 65-70%. Harvesting too early or too late is not conducive to silage preparation. The main nutritional components are crude protein (6.7-8.2%), acid-washed fiber (28.2-32.6%), and medium-washed fiber (51.2-57.5%).

Alfalfa: Native to southwestern Asia, alfalfa is considered a highly nutritious forage grass in livestock farming, often referred to as "fodder." It is a temperate perennial legume, but can adapt to tropical climates. Flower colors vary, depending on the variety, including purple, yellow, blue, and white. After flowering, it produces pods containing several seeds, which are used for propagation and cultivation. Numerous cultivars of alfalfa exist, and low-lignin, highly digestible varieties have been developed. Alfalfa has a wide temperature adaptability and thrives in well-drained soils. It tolerates drought but is not tolerant of wet conditions. Compared to other forage grasses, alfalfa is high in protein and digestible fiber, rich in minerals and vitamins, and possesses a high feeding value. However, the nutritional value of alfalfa varies significantly depending on the region of production, variety characteristics, and harvest maturity. Several grades of alfalfa are distinguished based on their crude protein and fiber content, with higher crude protein and lower fiber content being considered superior. The main nutritional components are divided into Supreme: crude protein >22%, acid-washed fiber <27%, medium-washed fiber <34%; Premium: crude protein 20-22%, acid-washed fiber 27-29%, medium-washed fiber 34-36%; Good: crude protein 18-20%, acid-washed fiber 29-32%, medium-washed fiber 36-40%; Fair: crude protein 16-18%, acid-washed fiber 32-35%, medium-washed fiber 40-44%; Low: crude protein <16%, acid-washed fiber >35%, medium-washed fiber >44%.

Bermuda grass: Also known as wiregrass or bermudagrass, Bermuda grass is native to Africa and introduced to America for use as lawns or pasture. It is a tropical perennial grass of the grass family. Bermuda grass is highly adaptable, tolerant of both barrenness and drought, and well-adapted to diverse soils. There are numerous varieties of Bermuda grass, including Common, Giant, Coastal, Tifton 44, and Tifton 85. Common and Giant are primarily propagated by seed in the United States. They have small stems and leaves and can be harvested as hay or used as lawns and golf course turf. Coastal and Tifton are interspecific hybrids that cannot produce seeds and instead reproduce asexually by runners or stolons. They have taller plants with long, large leaves, high yields and nutritional value, making them suitable for pasture use. They are better suited to tropical environments than other varieties, but are less cold-tolerant. Bermuda grass is the most common type of imported grass hay. While its acid-washed fiber content is lower than that of pan-grass, its dry matter digestibility is not as good. However, its advantages lie in its finely chopped, dried, and compact packaging. Most imported bermuda grass is a local variety with thin stems and small leaves. Some imported bermuda grass is harvested seed leftover for lawn use. This type of hay has very low nutritional value.
The main nutritional components are hay : crude protein 4.9-7.1%, acid-washed fiber 36.3-43.3%, medium-washed fiber 63.3-69.7%; straw: crude protein 5.7-6.3%, acid-washed fiber 40.2-44.4%, medium-washed fiber 63.1-73.9%.

Oats: Oats are temperate, single-year crops harvested for food and feed, or as a whole plant for forage. The main cultivated varieties for grain are common oats and red oats, both of which can also be used as forage. Black oats are primarily grown as forage or green manure, primarily in Brazil and other South American countries. Common oats thrive in cool, humid environments and are sensitive to heat and drought, while red and black oats are more tolerant of heat and drought. Currently, most oat hay in China is imported from Australia. Australia produces a wide variety of forage oats, categorized by growing season as early-maturing and late-maturing varieties. Early-maturing varieties are suitable for areas with low rainfall, while late-maturing varieties are suited to areas with high rainfall. In suitable seasons, late-maturing varieties produce higher hay yields and quality than early-maturing varieties. Furthermore, Australian forage oats are bred for a high content of water-soluble carbohydrates throughout the plant, resulting in a sweeter hay that is more palatable to animals. Oat hay, particularly the seed-containing ear, is favored by animals for its palatability and nutrient content. The main nutritional components of oat hay are: 3.9-14.6% crude protein, 29.3-40.2% acid-washed fiber, and 49.2-64.5% medium-washed fiber; and 4.6-5.6% crude protein, 33.0-45.7% acid-washed fiber, and 52.9-75.2% medium-washed fiber.

Timothy Grass: Native to temperate regions of Europe and Asia , it is now found throughout temperate regions worldwide . It is a perennial temperate grass of the grass family. It thrives in cool, humid climates , but is not suitable for areas with insufficient rainfall and average temperatures above 25°C . It is primarily grown in the northern United States and Canada . It can be grown alone or mixed with other legumes ( alfalfa or clover ) . Timothy Grass is an upright, clumping grass , reaching 60-120 cm in height . Its spikelets are densely clustered into long, cylindrical shapes . It has a shallow, fibrous root system and lacks runners or underground rhizomes . It cannot propagate vegetatively , requiring only seed sowing to establish new pastures. Timothy Grass is primarily used for hay and can be harvested twice a year . It has excellent palatability and nutritional value , making it suitable for both ruminants and pets. In pastures where Timothy Grass is mixed with legumes, most plants use the first harvest for hay and subsequent harvests for grazing. The main nutritional components are crude protein (7.1-14.5%), acid-washed fiber (27.9-47.8%), and medium-washed fiber (53.3-78.6%).

Green mowing: Green mowing is a common method used by domestic sheep farmers , meaning the grass is cut directly and fed to the animals. While this method allows for more control over forage supply , it requires significant labor and time investment , and quality can be affected by the timing of mowing. With the exception of green-cut corn , other domestic forages experience a significant increase in fiber content and a decrease in crude protein and other digestible nutrients as the plant matures with later mowing dates , leading to lower forage quality. Besides mowing date , other factors influencing forage quality also affect green mowing , with temperature having the greatest impact. High temperatures significantly increase the accumulation of fiber content and the degree of lignification in forage . Lower temperatures generally result in better quality . Therefore, given the same mowing cycle , green-cut forage quality is lower in the summer , while quality is better in other seasons. Summer green-cut quality can be improved by shortening the mowing cycle, reducing fertilizer application, and mowing only the upper ends of the forage. In recent years, due to dramatic climate change, prolonged droughts and continuous heavy rains have become increasingly frequent. Lack of irrigation facilities or poor drainage can easily stunt pasture growth or cause the grass to fall over due to rain, leading to the accumulation of nitrates in the grass, especially at the base of the stems. When this occurs, the cutting height should be raised to prevent excessive nitrate poisoning of animals.

Silage: Silage involves selecting appropriate silage materials and storing them in appropriately sized, compactable, and sealable containers. This utilizes the water-soluble carbohydrates in forage as an energy source for lactic acid bacteria, producing large amounts of lactic acid through anaerobic fermentation. This increases the acidity of the forage microenvironment, inhibiting the growth of other bacteria and preserving the nutrients in the forage. Choosing the right silage material requires consideration of the forage's maturity, moisture content, water-soluble carbohydrate content, and acid-base buffering capacity.

In addition to the material properties of forage affecting silage quality, silage container, degree of fineness of cut, silage speed, preparation methods such as compaction and sealing, and post-opening management all influence silage success. Silage Container: To facilitate silage preparation and minimize damage after opening, depending on the number of sheep and their feed intake, barrel-type silage (approximately 80-120 kg), large bales (approximately 500-600 kg), or the recently introduced small round bales (approximately 400 kg) are more suitable for sheep farmers to prepare and use, and are also convenient for handling. Fine Cutting: Fine cutting increases packing density, facilitates air removal, and improves silage success rate. This is especially true for overly dry green corn, which should be finely cut. However, for Pennisetum or other plants with high moisture content, excessive silage cutting should be avoided, as it releases too much water. Silage Speed: After harvest, the grass plant and surface microorganisms continue to respire, resulting in nutrient consumption and heat generation. Pennisetum is particularly prone to heat production and a decrease in water-soluble carbohydrate content after being piled, so silage must be completed as quickly as possible. Compaction: Regardless of the type of silage being prepared, it is essential to utilize various methods to compact the material, such as using hydraulic equipment or heavy objects for barrel and bag silage. Compacting expel air from the silage container as quickly as possible to achieve a low-oxygen state, promoting lactic acid fermentation. Tight compaction also reduces air infiltration after opening, minimizing spoilage. Sealing: Keeping the container sealed preserves the nutritional value of the silage. When preparing barrel silage, use film to seal the opening or invert it to prevent air from entering. Bag silage requires a secure seal to ensure optimal silage quality. If barrel silage is properly sealed and used immediately after opening, the compaction density can be reduced during filling. This maintains good silage quality while also minimizing the inconvenience caused by compaction. Management after opening: When the silage is opened, if the compaction is insufficient , the silage will become corrupt due to air infiltration. Therefore, the appropriate size of silage container should be selected according to the scale of breeding. If it cannot be used up in one go, it should be sealed immediately after use and used up as soon as possible.

To ensure a stable supply of high-quality forage for ruminants, hay and silage are the primary methods for preserving forage nutrients. Domestically grown pangu grass and Nile grass are primarily used for hay production, while green corn is primarily used for silage. However, the summer months, when pangu grass and Nile grass are most suitable for growth, are often subject to weather conditions. After harvest, they are exposed to rain while drying in the fields, making it difficult to produce high-quality hay and even resulting in the loss of the entire batch. Depending on weather conditions, semi-dry silage can be produced. While silage is typically produced with a moisture content of 60-70%, semi-dry silage is produced with a moisture content of 40-60%. Moisture content must be carefully considered during production; excessive moisture content results in significant dry matter loss. However, excessive withering before ensilage, which reduces the moisture content, inhibits fermentation and compromises silage quality. Because pangu grass is difficult to finely chop and fill, film bales are the ideal method for production. Currently, there are machines capable of producing film-wrapped round bales weighing approximately 150 kg each, suitable for sheep farms. Semi-dry silage not only reduces hay curing losses caused by weather conditions, but also overcomes the problem of Pangu hay's insufficient water-soluble carbohydrate content for good silage. Semi-dry silage can be fully fermented after just one month of storage. Experimental testing has shown that well-cured Pangu hay semi-dry silage significantly outperforms Bermuda hay in terms of palatability and feed intake.

Hay: Hay is convenient to use and can be stored for a long time, making it a common forage feed for herbivorous animals. However, hay production requires specialized machinery and large-scale operations, making it difficult for sheep farmers to prepare on their own. Therefore, they choose to feed their animals imported or domestically produced hay. To meet the nutritional needs of animals, hay quality is a key consideration when purchasing. This can be assessed by the type of hay, its appearance, and analytical reports provided by the vendor or submitted by the farmer. While many grasses are used as hay, grasses and legumes are the primary forages. These grasses can be categorized as tropical or temperate based on their adaptability to their growing environments. Generally speaking, legumes have higher protein content than grasses, while temperate grasses have lower fiber content than tropical grasses. Therefore, alfalfa, a temperate legume, generally makes for a better-quality hay.

Hay Type: Visual evaluation can also provide a rough estimate of hay quality, such as leaf ratio, color, presence of spikes, and inclusions. A higher leaf ratio and greener color generally indicate better quality, but color varies between different hay types, and excessive exposure to the sun can also affect hay color, making color inappropriate as the sole evaluation criterion. Generally, the fiber content of grasses increases after flowering, so hay with a high spike content is of lower quality. Inclusions such as weeds and dead branches can affect animal palatability, and a higher percentage of inclusions indicates lower quality. Odor is also an important evaluation criterion; a musty or rotten odor indicates poor hay quality.

To accurately formulate a diet, hay type identification and appearance evaluation are insufficient to accurately represent hay quality. Compositional analysis is required for quantitative description and quality grading. Key analytical items include dry matter (DM): the percentage of the total weight of the forage after deducting water, which serves as the basis for diet formulation and animal feed intake calculation; crude protein (CP): This consists of both true protein and non-protein nitrogen. Forage with a high protein content is more likely to meet animal needs and reduce the purchase of protein supplements; acid detergent fiber (ADF): The part of the forage that is difficult for animals to digest. A higher acid detergent fiber content means lower digestibility and lower forage quality; neutral detergent fiber (NDF): The part of the cell wall structure of the forage, mostly slow-digesting substances. The NDF content is generally inversely proportional to the animal's feed intake; a higher NDF content means lower feed intake; and relative feed value (Relative Feed Value). RFV) is an index composed of digestible dry matter rate and dry matter intake, which can be used as a basis for evaluating forage quality and feeding effect. The higher the value, the better the forage quality.

Pangu Grass: Used for green fodder, hay, and semi-dry silage, it has an annual dry matter yield of 20-30 metric tons per hectare. Pangu Grass is characterized by its creeping stems, each node of which can root and grow, and has many thin stems and leaves. The planting area required for every 100 goats is 1.1-1.7 hectares.

Nile Grass No. 1: For green fodder, hay, and semi-dry silage, it produces 25-30 metric tons/hectare of dry matter year-round. Its stems are upright and hollow, and its nodes can root, with strong rooting ability. The planting area required for every 100 goats is 1.1-1.3 hectares.
Nile Grass No. 2: For green fodder, hay, and semi-dry silage, it produces 25-30 metric tons/hectare of dry matter year-round. It easily sprouts roots at the base of the stem, has many tillers, and offers high ground coverage. The required planting area is 1.1-1.3 hectares per 100 goats.
Nile Grass No. 3: Used for green fodder, hay, and semi-dry silage, it has an annual dry matter yield of 25-30 metric tons/hectare. It has fast stem germination, early maturity, drought tolerance, and lodging resistance. It regenerates quickly after mowing. The planting area required for every 100 goats is 1.1-1.3 hectares.

Pennisetum Taixucao No. 1: Used for green feed and silage, it produces 24-33 metric tons per hectare year-round. It has a short plant with short internodes and numerous, hairless leaves. Planting area required for every 100 goats is 1-1.4 hectares.
Pennisetum Taixucao No. 2 is used for green feed and silage, with a yield of 44-58 metric tons per hectare throughout the year. The plants are tall and have high yields. The planting area required for every 100 goats is 0.6-0.8 hectares.
Pennisetum Taixucao No. 3 is used for green feed and silage, with an annual output of 34-46 metric tons per hectare. It has a short plant with very short internodes, many leaves and tender stems. The planting area required for every 100 goats is 0.7-1 hectare.
Pennisetum Taixucao No. 4: For green feed and silage, it produces 46-61 metric tons/hectare year-round. It has tall, thick stems and is high in fiber. The planting area required is 0.5-0.7 hectares per 100 goats.
Pennisetum Taixucao No. 5: Used for green feed and silage, it produces 26-35 metric tons/hectare year-round. It has a tall stem, is rich in anthocyanins, and has excellent antioxidant capacity. The planting area required for every 100 goats is 0.9-1.3 hectares.
Pennisetum Taixucao No. 6: Used for green feed and silage, it produces 20-26 metric tons/hectare year-round. It grows in a very short, clump-like pattern with thick, hard stems and large leaves. The planting area required for every 100 goats is 1.3-1.7 hectares.
Pennisetum Taixucao No. 7: used for green feed and silage, 36-48 metric tons/hectare throughout the year, semi-dwarf upright clump, with many leaves and few stems, the planting area required for every 100 goats is 0.7-0.9 hectares.

Green corn: used for green feed and silage, with a crop rate of 10-15 metric tons per hectare per crop. It has high nutritional value and is easy to make into high-quality silage. The required planting area for every 100 goats is 1.1-1.7 hectares (two crops per year).

Alfalfa: This product is hay, high in protein and digestible fiber, and also rich in minerals and vitamins.
Bermuda grass: There are two types of items. One is hay, which is a tropical forage grass. Although its nutritional value is slightly lower, it is finely chopped, dry and tightly packed. The other is grass straw, which has a high fiber content, low nutritional value and digestibility, and is more suitable as a feed for non-lactating sheep.
Oats: There are two types of items. One is hay, the whole plant has a high content of water-soluble carbohydrates, good palatability and rich in nutrients. The other is grass stalks, which have a high fiber content and lower nutritional value than oat hay, and are more suitable as feed for non-lactating sheep.
Timothy Grass: This product is hay with good palatability and nutritional value. In addition to being used for ruminants, it can also be used as forage for pets.

Soybean hulls: Soybean hulls are the outermost seed membrane layer of soybeans. After drying, the soybean hulls are fluffy, very light , transparent and light yellow, and have a fragrant taste. They contain more than 8% protein, 46% acid-washed fiber, 64% medium-washed fiber, and more than 30% crude fiber. They are high-quality fiber and protein supplement feed for ruminants.

Sake meal: Sake meal is a water-rich byproduct of the alcohol production process. In Taiwan, brewer's meal and sorghum sake are commonly used for ruminants. Because the starch is largely broken down during the alcohol production process, the remaining nutrients in these byproducts, such as protein, minerals, and trace elements, increase three to fourfold on a dry matter basis. Therefore, they are commonly used as a protein supplement for ruminants in Taiwan. However, their high water content makes them difficult to transport and store.

Bran: Bran is the outer husk of wheat. It is fluffy and has a pleasant texture, stimulating ruminants to increase feed intake. Rich in protein and phosphorus, it is often used by feed formulators to supplement ruminant diets with protein and phosphorus. However, due to its high phosphorus content, its use in fattening castrated wethers should be minimized.

Other domestically produced agricultural and food by-products: Taiwan's subtropical climate, characterized by high temperatures and high humidity, is ideal for growing a wide variety of agricultural products. Consequently, a significant number of agricultural and food by-products are produced. However, when using agricultural and food by-products, care must be taken regarding their high water content, pesticide content, transportation and storage, and nutritional balance.

Corn distillers' grains: Corn distillers' grains are a byproduct of producing corn ethanol from corn starch, replacing petroleum. They are high in protein, crude fiber, minerals, and trace elements, and are affordable and offer a stable yield. They are popular not only with large ruminant breeders but also with feed mills to adjust protein levels in feed formulations and reduce feed costs.

Soybean meal: Soybean meal is the residual byproduct left after soybeans are degreased and oiled . It is very high in protein , though its protein content varies significantly depending on the degreasing method . It is the primary source of protein in ruminant concentrates.

Corn: Corn is the world's most produced crop. Most of the corn used in Taiwan's livestock industry comes from North and South America. Generally speaking , corn kernels graded 2 and below are primarily used as feed. They contain approximately 8% protein and 91% total digestible nutrients, making them an excellent source of energy for ruminants.

Cereal concentrate formulation: To maximize ruminant production efficiency, livestock producers supplement ruminants with cereal concentrates to meet their nutritional needs at various stages of development, production, and reproduction. Cereal concentrate supplementation aims to provide ruminants with four key nutrients: crude protein, energy, minerals, and vitamins.

Preservation of wet by-products: Wet by-products contain a lot of water, which makes them easy to go rancid and difficult to preserve. It is necessary to add an appropriate amount of drier by-products to reduce their moisture content by at least 50%. They should then be sealed and preserved in silage pits, sausage-type silage bags or plastic barrels. This can prevent the wet by-products from going rancid and achieve the goal of long-term and stable supply of ruminant diets.

Traditional Hand-Feeding: Meat-producing ruminants raised using the traditional method require daily forage to be divided into four parts, with a ratio of approximately 1:4:1:4. Concentrate can be divided into two parts, with a ratio of approximately 1:1. In the morning and evening, 1/10 grass is fed first, followed by 1 part concentrate and 4/10 grass. Dairy ruminants raised using the traditional method require daily forage to be divided into four parts, with a ratio of approximately 1:3:3:3. Concentrate can be divided into three parts, with a ratio of approximately 1:1:1. At dawn, 1/10 grass is fed first, followed by 1 part concentrate and 3/10 grass. In the evening, 1 part concentrate and 3/10 grass are fed. At night, 1 part concentrate and 3/10 grass are fed again.

TMR mixer: TMR mixer can completely mix the daily feed ingredients for goats to prevent goats from being picky eaters. TMR mixers are available in fixed and mobile types, with different prices depending on the space planning and breeding scale of the goat breeder's sheep house.

Feeding Order and Precautions: To maximize the uniformity of the TMR mixer's mixing , the order in which feed ingredients are added must be carefully considered. The principle of feeding order is as follows: 1. Longer or larger-particle forages, such as hay, should be placed in the TMR mixer first. 2. Cereals, concentrates, or dry premixes should be added in the following order. Mineral or vitamin premixes should be dispersed with cornmeal before addition. 3. Wet by-products, corn silage, semi-dried silage, molasses, and other liquids should be added. 4. Mixing time should not exceed 15 minutes , and after the last feed ingredient is added, mixing should not exceed 5 minutes. 5. Do not overfill the TMR mixer, leaving room for mixing. 6. The moisture content of feed mixed in a TMR mixer will vary depending on the moisture content of the ingredients , with the final moisture content being approximately 30-40 %.

Uniformity Analysis: When using TMR feed blends , due to the varying physical properties, moisture content, and particle size of feed ingredients, some of the mixed feed may separate from the coarse and fine materials after mixing. This can lead to picky eating in animals , negatively impacting the original purpose of TMR use and reducing the economic benefits of using TMR . The Penn State Particle Separator (PSPS) is widely used for particle size analysis and can be used to measure the effective fiber quality of TMR on-site .

Preventing Picky Eating: To prevent ruminants from being picky eaters, please note the following: 1. TMR feed should contain an appropriate amount of moisture. Hold the feed gently so that no water drips, and the feed clumps do not fall apart when you open your palm. 2. Screen with a University of Pennsylvania particle size separator to ensure uniformity. 3. Pre-cut long grasses or other agricultural byproducts. Hay, green corn, silage, etc. should not exceed 2 cm in length when placed in the TMR mixer. 4. The mixed TMR feed should be fragrant and light yellow in color. 5. Prepare the prepared TMR feed just before each feeding, and ensure that the entire feed is fully prepared to avoid fermentation and heat generation from leftover feed, which can affect the ruminant's appetite. 6. When feeding ruminants with TMR, ensure that the animal can finish the feed each time. 7. Clean any remaining feed from the feed trough before the next feeding. 8. Feed small, frequent meals whenever possible.

Feeding time, amount, and frequency: Ruminants are typically fed TMR twice daily, typically in the morning and afternoon. However, for high-yielding ruminants, TMR may be provided up to three or four times daily, in the morning, afternoon, and evening. The amount provided should be sufficient for the ruminant to consume within 10-15 minutes.


Newborn lamb rearing: The period from birth to weaning is a critical one. The growth rate during this period directly impacts the ewe's future breeding age, the number of lambs born per litter, the number of litters during her lifetime, and milk production. Other factors include the wether's fattening progress and the ram's breeding size. Numerous factors influence lamb weight gain, broadly categorized as ewe, feed, environment, and disease. Indeed, careful management is crucial from the late stages of pregnancy. If a ewe experiences a difficult birth before lambing, her physiological response to stress triggers the release of adrenaline. This hormone, however, is transferred across the placenta to the fetus, leading to a weakened immune system and subsequent growth retardation. After leaving the mother's womb, the cleanliness and warmth of the newborn's birth environment, the intake of high-quality, sterilized colostrum, the amount of milk consumed by the lamb, the quality of milk replacer and starter feed , and diseases such as diarrhea and respiratory problems are all often overlooked factors that can impact the lamb's weight gain rate and stability before and after weaning. The care of lambs from birth to weaning involves many small details that have a big impact . Mastering the feeding management to improve the growth and development of lambs will surely achieve the basic business goals of replacing culled ewes, maintaining the economic scale of the flock, and selling lambs or goat milk , thus laying the foundation for the sheep farm's profits.

Post-natal examination of lambs: Newborn lambs should be assisted in clearing the mucous membrane fluid from their mouths and noses immediately to enable them to breathe smoothly on their own and reduce respiratory problems caused by inhalation of foreign objects. In addition, help to tidy up the lambs' hair and keep their bodies dry and clean to prevent the lambs from hypothermia and cold. The broken umbilical cord will be the main wound infected with pathogens and inflamed. Prepare cotton thread and scissors soaked in iodine solution for disinfection. Then use cotton thread to tie the umbilical cord about 1-2 cm away from the abdomen and tie a knot. Cut off the excess cotton thread and umbilical cord below the knot. Then spray iodine solution to disinfect the umbilical cord area at the abdomen after each meal until the umbilical cord is dry.

Lamb Barn Environment: After birth , lambs face a dramatic shift in their environment , from the constant, sterile temperature of their mother's body to one characterized by fluctuating temperatures and the omnipresence of bacteria. Lambs are fragile , and their organs are easily affected by cold, weakening their immune system and leading to respiratory and digestive tract diseases. Domestic sheep pens are built with raised beds , making it crucial to provide lambs with draft-proof space ( Figure 2) and heating facilities ( Figure 3). This allows lambs to choose warmth based on their body's needs , minimizing the risk of cold. Hygiene in lamb housing requires adequate ventilation and a dry, clean pens.

Colostrum Intake and Absorption: Lambs should receive their first colostrum feeding within 15-30 minutes of birth, preferably within an hour. The first colostrum expressed after farrowing should be used. Adequate colostrum feeding (10% of body weight, divided equally into 3-4 feedings) should be provided within the first 6 hours of life. This colostrum-only feeding regimen should continue for 2-3 days. Bottle feeding ensures that each lamb receives an adequate amount. If the lamb is nursing from the ewe, it may not receive enough, especially in multiple births. Lambs that are weak and unable to suckle on their own should be fed through a nasogastric tube and syringe. Colostrum contains essential antibodies and immunoglobulins, and colostrum intake protects the lamb against disease. The amount of colostrum consumed and the concentration of antibodies in the colostrum determine the lamb's total antibody intake. Insufficient total colostrum intake will prevent the protective effect from lasting until one month of age. Following farrowing, the antibody content in colostrum decreases rapidly, and the lamb's intestinal ability to absorb antibodies diminishes 10-12 hours after birth. Therefore, delaying the collection of ewe colostrum or feeding colostrum to lambs after birth will prevent the intestine from absorbing sufficient antibodies. Hot barn conditions, dams experiencing difficult births, overcrowding, fighting between lambs, and rough handling and feeding of lambs can cause stress in lambs and reduce antibody absorption. Bacterial contamination of colostrum and its adsorption to intestinal mucosal surfaces can also reduce antibody absorption. If lamb probiotics are to be supplemented , they should be started six hours after the last colostrum feeding.

There are two methods for nursing lambs: natural nursing and artificial nursing. Natural nursing lambs nurse from birth until weaned, requiring less effort than artificial nursing. However, it can cause udder deformation and nipple damage, making weaning more difficult. Furthermore, it can delay the return of estrus and conception after birth. This method is primarily used in meat goats. Controlling colostrum quality primarily relies on observing the ewe's udder for abnormalities and ensuring cleanliness. However, if caprine arthritis encephalitis (CAE) is not eliminated from the flock, vertical infection from goat suckling can occur.

Artificial lactation is commonly used for dairy goats. After birth, the lambs are separated from their dams and fed colostrum by hand for 3-5 days, then switched to a milk substitute until weaning. This method requires considerable care and effort, with strict adherence to feeding schedules, feeding amounts, milk quality, and clean utensils. Colostrum handling is also strictly controlled. The use of artificial lactation on domestic mutton goat farms is on the rise.

Ewe milk can contain pathogens, which can cause lambs to become infected and suffer health consequences. Prevention of caprine arthritis encephalitis (CAE) through milk transmission is particularly important in China. Immediately separating lambs from their dams after birth and sterilizing their milk can offer some protection. Another alternative to preventing CAE from colostrum in lambs is the use of artificial colostrum powder, which typically contains high concentrations of immunoglobulins and added nutrients. It is pathogen-free and therefore does not require heat sterilization. Furthermore, it can be prepared quickly and easily, facilitating hygiene control. While its use is limited to one day, it can increase lamb feeding costs.

Natural feeding: Most mutton farms house lambs and ewes for natural feeding , eliminating the need for manual milk measurement, temperature control, and feeding timing , saving both time and effort. After birth , some lambs may need to be coaxed into nuzzling and suckling. Naturally fed lambs nurse at will, feeding multiple times and mimicking the ewes' learning to eat dry feed more quickly , allowing them to reach their maximum weight .
