However, there is considerable variability in growth progress between lambs of the same litter. Naturally fed lambs are dependent on their dams , making it difficult to switch to bottle feeding if their milk supply becomes compromised . They also tend to be more feral and less amenable to human control. Most natural feeding operations utilize group pens , where ewes and lambs are housed in groups , leading to frequent pushing and fighting , and difficulty detecting problematic lambs. Providing lambs with free access to shelters or warm, protective wooden boxes can provide protection from aggression by larger lambs and help protect them from drafts in raised beds.

There is an increasing trend in the use of single-ewe penning for lactating sheep on mutton farms. Ewes and their lambs are kept in separate pens, eliminating the tendency for group-housed ewes to reject non-family lambs. Abnormal lambs can also be quickly identified, improving sheep care efficiency. Pens can be equipped with gates, allowing lambs to enter and exit the pen freely while keeping ewes away from creep feeding areas.

For naturally nursing lambs to be weaned as early as eight weeks of age, a plan can be implemented at two weeks of age to separate them from their ewes in the mornings and keep them together at other times, but in adjacent pens, maintaining visual contact. After a week, separate them during the day, and at six weeks of age, keep them together only in the mornings, until they are fully separated at eight weeks of age, at which point the ewes are removed from their pens while the lambs remain in their original pens. Regardless of the planned weaning time, this concept of gradually increasing the time spent away from their ewes and reducing the number of times they are together can be applied to shorten the nursing period, achieve early weaning, and promote the return of reproduction after parturition. This allows ewes to give birth three times within two years, while simultaneously ensuring the lambs' transition from weaning to weaning is successful.

three days of colostrum feeding , lambs can switch to regular goat milk or a milk substitute. During the first week after birth , breastfeeding should be frequent , but the amount of milk each time should be small . Starting in the second week, the frequency of feedings decreases , but the amount of milk each time increases. At six to seven weeks , the amount of milk and the frequency of feedings begin to decrease until weaning to facilitate the transition. If the lamb develops loose stools or diarrhea , the amount of milk fed each time should be immediately reduced , and the frequency of feedings increased. Artificial feeding requires careful attention to the temperature of the milk, the frequency of feedings, the amount of milk fed, and the intervals between feedings . The milk should generally be warmed to 37-38 °C . Overheating can easily cause burns to the lamb 's mouth and throat , while overcooling can often cause diarrhea . In these cases , the amount of milk fed each time can be split over several feedings.

Starter Feeding Plan: To quickly establish a complete rumen ecosystem similar to that of adult ruminants, lambs must be taught to consume solid feeds early to promote rapid rumen growth and development. Providing concentrate feed promotes the development of the rumen wall and papillae, while providing fodder promotes rumen volume expansion and increases absorptive surface area. However, under moderate to high-sugar feeding regimes, lambs may become satiated by excessive milk intake, leading to delayed starter feed intake and weaning discomfort. This is primarily due to the fact that starter feed contains only approximately 40-60% of the metabolizable energy of breast milk. To maintain the weight gain benefits of moderate to high-sugar feeding during the early stages of life, lambs must be fed a sufficient starter feed early to bridge the gap in nutrient intake from weaning to concentrate feed, at least to avoid weight loss during the weaning period.

During the nursing period, an ideal creep feed feeding strategy is essential. Dry matter intake and water intake are proportional, so water intake can promote creep feed intake. Lambs can be encouraged to drink water starting three days after birth. Since lambs cannot yet use a drinker, clean water should be provided in a bucket. The bucket should be cleaned and changed at least once a day. At one week of age, lambs can be encouraged to consume creep feed. The amount of feed should be increased as the lambs age. The goal is to have lambs consume at least 180 grams of creep feed daily by the time they start weaning and at least 200 grams daily by the time they are weaned.

Forage and hay are only supplied to lambs when they are 7 weeks old. Since the digestible nutrients contained in forage are much lower than those in concentrate, and lambs have poor fiber digestion ability at this age, and the bulk of forage easily takes up a lot of rumen space, thus reducing concentrate intake, it is generally recommended that forage be supplied in the middle and late stages of the lactation period compared to concentrate, and high-quality grass hay or alfalfa hay is preferred, and the feeding amount should be adjusted and increased weekly.

Weaning and Weaning Plan: Weaning is a new phase for lambs, and some lambs may have difficulty adapting, resulting in weight loss. The timing of weaning depends on the lamb's developmental status and solid feed intake. Generally, it is recommended that goats be housed at least two to three months of age.

Post-weaning lamb planning: After weaning, lambs transition from an incomplete solid diet to a complete solid diet consisting of only concentrates and fodder. Poor adaptation to the transition can delay the entire growth process. To minimize the stress of the transition and the resulting instability in feed intake and weight gain, lambs should remain in their original pen for at least two weeks after weaning, with minimal drastic changes to their diet. Once their feed intake and weight gain stabilize, adjust feed amounts and subsequent feeding plans based on individual lamb feeding goals.

Ewe Weight Gain and Breeding: The ideal breeding goal for ewes is to be able to give birth and produce milk by 12 months of age. Based on a five-month gestation period, breeding is essential at 7 months of age. For breeding, the recommended weight for ewes is 60% of their adult body weight (approximately 30 to 35 kg). Therefore, if a ewe weighs 3 kg at birth and is bred at 7 months of age, this ewe must grow at least 1 kg per week, an average daily gain of approximately 143 grams. This is a moderate growth rate and requires adequate nutritional support to maintain this steady growth. Once the ewe reaches breeding weight, a gradual increase in concentrate feed can be initiated 6-8 weeks prior to breeding. A daily intake of 0.5 kg of concentrate per female should be provided one week prior to breeding to stimulate estrus and ovulation. During this period , constant attention must be paid to the ewe's growth and development to avoid infertility caused by obesity.

Post-weaning lambs are in a period of rapid physiological growth and should be fed a high-nutrient diet to promote rapid weight gain. In addition to providing ample, high-quality forage, ewes should also be given a small amount of concentrate feed, but the daily amount should not exceed 0.2 kg to prevent obesity and reproductive problems. Providing high-quality fodder can reduce reliance on concentrate feed.

Weight Gain and Fattening of Rams: The ideal breeding goal for meat goats is to be marketable by 12 months of age, meaning a weight of over 50 kg. For a ram weighing 3 kg at birth, for example, 1 kg of growth is required per week to achieve this goal. To reach marketable weight earlier, weaned castrated rams can be fed a lower feed to concentrate their grain ratio, opting for a high-energy, high-protein diet. However, careful nutrient balance is crucial. Maintaining a consistent ratio of calcium and phosphorus can lead to urinary stones and urethral obstruction. Castrated rams are more susceptible to this condition, so a calcium-to-phosphorus ratio of 1.5-2:1 is generally recommended. Additionally, an adequate supply of clean drinking water helps prevent urine concentration, which can lead to stones. Fattening goats must reduce their exercise intake to avoid wasting energy and affecting fattening results.

Late Pregnancy Management: During the last 50 days of pregnancy, 70% of fetal development is complete, and most mammary gland development also occurs during this time. Colostrum synthesis then begins, leading to a dramatic increase in the ewe's nutrient needs. Late pregnancy feeding and management impacts lamb survival, birth weight, postnatal survival, and postnatal weight gain. The quality of the ewe's colostrum and reproductive efficiency after parturition are also impacted. The rapid fetal growth during late pregnancy occupies a significant amount of abdominal space, particularly in multiple pregnancies. This significantly reduces the space available for gastrointestinal storage of feed. Insufficient energy intake and a negative energy balance can lead to metabolic disorders such as preeclampsia. Pregnant ewes should be fed a high-digestible energy diet containing at least 12% crude protein and high-quality forage to maximize forage intake. Reduce the use of juicy, unstable, and unpalatable cut grasses and silage. Instead, use alfalfa mixed with Bermuda hay. However, be aware that legumes are high in calcium, so they should be reduced or avoided before farrowing and replaced with other forages to prevent hypocalcemia (milk fever). Grass hays with high potassium content should also be fed in reduced amounts to avoid the same problem. Pregnant ewes fed low-quality or hay-based forages should supplement their feed with vitamin A to prevent fetal growth impairment. This can also improve colostrum quality and postnatal immunity. Gradually increase concentrate feed intake in late pregnancy to promote rumen microbial adaptation and stimulate the redevelopment of rumen papillae. However, ewes should still be grouped according to their leanness and adjust concentrate feed intake to increase or maintain leanness . The recommended leanness before farrowing is 2.75-3.5. Ewes should have an appropriate amount of body fat stored at farrowing to achieve high milk production in early lactation. Concentrate feeding should be gradually increased, reaching at least 1% of body weight before farrowing. Forage intake should not be neglected; high forage intake in late pregnancy will encourage high forage intake during lactation. Ensure that there is an ample supply of fresh water; pregnant ewes may drink up to 15 liters daily. During the final weeks of pregnancy, the udder develops rapidly and, in high-producing ewes, may appear very swollen and asymmetrical. It's often tempting to express some milk at this time to reduce udder pressure, but unless the ewe is clearly uncomfortable, this is best avoided as it can affect colostrum production during farrowing.

Drying Period Management: The dry period is a critical transition period for lactating sheep. However, because milking is stopped, observation opportunities are reduced, making it easy to overlook problematic sheep. Drying can be performed naturally or artificially. Natural drying occurs primarily due to reduced nutrient intake and low milk production, resulting in a rapid decline in milk production after pregnancy. Artificial drying involves gradually reducing milk production through changes in the sheep's lifestyle, such as reducing milking frequency, reducing concentrate feed and water intake, changing the diet, and increasing exercise. During the dry period and the immediate post-dry period, the udder should be inspected frequently to check for abnormalities in milk color and taste, and close attention should be paid to the presence of mastitis. A dry period of at least six to eight weeks before farrowing provides ample time for the lactating sheep to rest and recover, while also allowing for mastitis treatment. Conversely, the latter part of the dry period, two to three weeks before farrowing, is designed to facilitate the physiological transition from dryness to milk production. The stocking density of dry sheep should be reduced, or individual farrowing pens should be provided (Figure 13). Ewes should be grouped or kept together to avoid crowding. First-time lambs and multiparous lambs should be kept in separate pens.

Goat Pregnancy Period and Precautions: The gestation period for goats ranges from 143 to 157 days, with an average of 150 days. Therefore, the expected date of birth can be estimated from the time of breeding. Pregnancy can be confirmed by observing estrus behavior at least one month after breeding, or by performing an abdominal ultrasound scan. Pregnant ewes should have their hooves trimmed and receive deworming treatment at least two months before birth. The delivery area should be clean, dry, and draft-proof to prevent newborn lambs from catching cold, which reduces their survival rate. The ewe's abdomen and vulva should be kept clean and tidy, and all necessary delivery equipment should be available. During the week before birth, monitor the ewe's physical condition, behavior, and appearance for changes. Avoid excessive concentrates during this period. Consider providing a mild laxative, such as bran, to help with bowel movement and facilitate a smooth birth. Additionally, feeding straw to dilate the stomach and intestines can help stimulate appetite after birth.

Postpartum Care for Goats: After giving birth, pay attention to the temperature in the delivery room to avoid heat stress and prevent drafts from entering to prevent cold. After delivery, use warm water to clean the ewe's buttocks, vulva, tail, and hind legs to remove fetal fluid, mucus, and dirt to prevent infection . The delivery room should be kept clean and dry. After delivery, pay attention to the passage of the afterbirth. If it persists for more than 12 hours , it may be related to weak uterine contractions , which can easily lead to uterine inflammation . Oxytocin can be injected to facilitate expulsion , but it is still recommended to consult a veterinarian as soon as possible. About one hour after delivery , the ewe can be fed molasses water . Take 2 liters of warm water , add 200 grams of molasses, and mix . This will replenish lost water and iron , and provide quick energy to promote milk production. Ewes should be provided with high-quality hay after delivery. Supplement with juicy green grass to stimulate the appetite. Gradually increase the amount of concentrate feed until one week after delivery, at which time they can resume their normal diet.

Early Lactation Management: High feed conversion efficiency during the period from farrowing to peak milk production is crucial for dairy farms. Although ewes' feed intake gradually increases after farrowing, it lags behind the increase in milk production, resulting in a negative energy balance. Ewes must compensate for their insufficient feed intake by breaking down body tissues (fat and protein) to produce nutrients. The diet must contain a high level of digestible nutrients to prevent excessive breakdown of body tissues (fat), which can lead to metabolic disturbances such as ketosis. To increase productivity and ensure high feed efficiency, a challenge feeding strategy can be employed. This involves continuously increasing the concentrate feed intake based on fatness, milk production, or litter size (for mutton sheep), while ensuring ample access to fresh, clean water. In addition to adding 0.5 kg of concentrate for every kg of milk production increase, an additional 250 to 500 grams of concentrate can be fed to challenge or induce milk production. If milk production does not increase further, it indicates the ewe has reached its genetic limit. After farrowing, increase the amount of concentrate feed to avoid digestive discomfort such as clumping and loose stools. The concentrate to roughage ratio in the diet should not exceed the upper limit of 60:40 to prevent acidosis, bloating, and other problems. Feeding ewes high-quality forage helps stimulate milk production. Each dairy goat should have at least 1.75 square meters of space; if the flock is composed of unfamiliar, unrelated goats, double the space is required. Overcrowding during this period can cause extreme stress, resulting in reduced production. Furthermore, the troughs and waterers used in the late-gestation pen should be the same as those used in the early-lactation pen to prevent poor adaptation after the pen move, which could impact the ewe's productivity and health.

Goat horns: Goats with horns are associated with fertility; however, in intensively confined farms, horns can easily cause their heads to become trapped in fences, potentially leading to serious injuries during fights. Furthermore, horns often make it difficult for goats to feed through the feed trough.

Horn Buds: After birth, lambs should be dehorned by four days of age. Female lambs are slightly more susceptible to dehorning than male lambs, so male lambs should be dehorned by two days of age. Delaying dehorning is painful for lambs and reduces the success rate. In mature rams, regenerated horns often become scars. Poorly regenerated horns often grow in curls. If dehorning is performed at this time, care should be taken to ensure that the horn retains vascularized areas and avoid excessive bleeding.

Horn growth: To identify the growth point of the lamb's horns, you can observe that the hair on the horn-growing part of the top of its head will appear in a swirl shape, and when you touch it with your hand, you can feel a hard protrusion. When the hair is cut off, you can see small white protrusions.

Horn bud removal methods: First, the lamb must be restrained. This typically requires two people: one person holds the lamb in place while the other removes the horn. Other methods, such as cauterization or chemical dehorning, can be used. 1. Cauterization: First, use scissors to remove the hair at the horn bud site. Then, place a red-hot iron around the base of the horn, working until the periosteum is removed and the keratin is broken. Each cauterization lasts 10 to 15 seconds, and complete removal takes 3 to 5 minutes. After surgery, it is best to spray the horn site with iodine disinfectant daily to accelerate wound healing. 2. Corrosion: First, use scissors to remove the hair at the horn bud site. Apply petroleum jelly around the horn bud to prevent injury to the affected area and to prevent the drug from getting into the eyes. Then, use a blade to make a well-shaped cut on both sides to induce slight bleeding. Then, apply cauterizing potash or sodium hydroxide to corrode the skin. After restraining the horn for 30 minutes, rinse the wound with acetic acid and finally rinse with clean water. After surgery, it is best to spray the area where the horn buds are removed with disinfectant iodine solution every day to accelerate wound healing.

Hoof Trimming: Goats' hooves are constantly growing. Removing and trimming the horn and flesh on the hoof bottom allows the goats to stand steadily on flat surfaces and maintain foot health, extending their lifespan. Hoof trimming can easily lead to hoof deformation, which can seriously cause lameness, an unsteady gait, and difficulty moving, which in turn reduces the goat's appetite. In late pregnancy, the rapidly growing fetus compresses the ewe's gastrointestinal space for feed, naturally reducing her appetite. Therefore, her hoof must be in good shape to prevent further impact on her feeding. Another common hoof deformation is the ingrown horn on the outer edge, which can trap foreign matter, feces, and dirt. If this causes injury, it can lead to bacterial infection. These bacteria thrive in long, damp hooves and can spread to the entire flock. Deformed hooves often resist restoration after prolonged neglect, so regular hoof trimming is essential. It is generally recommended to begin hoof trimming at around six weeks of age or earlier, with inspections every four weeks. Do not trim the hooves of pregnant ewes within two months of giving birth to avoid causing stress or pressure on the abdomen, which can lead to miscarriage. If goats are raised on pasture, the hooves wear less frequently due to the soft grass, so the frequency of hoof trimming must be increased appropriately.

Reasons for hoof trimming: The goal of hoof trimming is to make the bottom of the hoof parallel to the angle of the coronal band, so that the side of the hoof has a trapezoidal appearance and the bottom of the hoof is approximately elliptical, so that the meat of the hoof is flat and in contact with the ground.

Hoof trimming steps: 1. Secure the sheep in a standing position, leaving space on one side of the body for trimming, and place the other side against a wall or fence. 2. To trim the front foot: Face the sheep from behind, grasp the joint of the hoof with your left hand, and using your knee as a fulcrum, bend the front foot upward and backward, with the sole facing up. 3. To trim the hind foot: Position yourself close to the hip. Because the hock cannot be used as a fulcrum for bending upward and backward, the operator must lift the entire hind leg with the sole facing up. Be careful not to lift the hind leg too high, and never pull it outward from the sheep's body, as this may cause discomfort and resistance. 4. Clean the hoof of any foreign matter to help distinguish the horny (harder) from the fleshy (softer) area. 5. Trim the hoof in accordance with the normal hoof shape, trimming any horny material that protrudes beyond the fleshy area, allowing the fleshy area of the hoof to rest flat on the ground. 6. Excessive fleshy growth, usually at the heel, should also be trimmed. The goal is to even the fleshy area with the horny surface of the hoof. However, trim only small amounts at a time to avoid cutting into the vascular area of the fleshy area. 7. In cases of extreme hoof deformation, it is important to carefully distinguish between the horny and fleshy areas, and only trim a small amount of horny at a time to avoid removing too much fleshy area at once, which could cause accidental injury and bleeding.

Castration of goats: Young rams reach sexual maturity very early, with some being bred and pregnant at three months of age. If a ram is to be kept beyond three months of age, it is best to castrate it if it is not intended for breeding. Castration not only makes the ram more docile, but also reduces body odor, improves meat quality, and increases meat mass. The earlier castration is performed, the faster the recovery, and it is usually easiest to perform it within one to two weeks of birth.

There are two types of castration methods: 1. Surgical method: This method is best performed 1-2 weeks after the lamb is born. In surgical castration, the scrotum is cut open with a surgical blade and the testicles are removed. The wound is coated with disinfectant iodine solution to prevent inflammation. It is best to be performed by a veterinarian. 2. Bloodless castration methods are divided into two types: (1) Forceps and file method: Using castration forceps, the scrotum is cut at a right angle to the spermatic cord on the outside, crushing the blood vessels and vas deferens at the base of the scrotum. During the operation, be careful not to move the spermatic cord. Press the spermatic cord for 30-60 seconds before removing it. The scrotum needs to be disinfected and coated with disinfectant iodine solution. After a few weeks of the operation, the testicles can be felt to be smaller and harder. (2) Rubber band method: It is best to perform the operation within 1 week after birth. Use a special tool, expansion forceps (Figure 23), to open a thick, strong rubber band and place it above the scrotum, so that it remains at the base. Before the rubber band is released from the tool, make sure that both testicles are in the scrotum. This method stops blood circulation, causing the tissue inside the testicles or scrotum to shrink and necrotize, and fall off after a few weeks. This is the simplest and safest method of castration.


Reproduction: Reproduction is the same as reproduction. A prerequisite for a successful goat breeder is to be able to produce a large number of lambs each year and successfully raise them for sale. Although it is important for goats to have high-quality genetic composition, growth traits, and lactation traits, production is reproduction. Animals with low reproductive capacity or reproductive disorders will increase costs significantly. Those that cannot reproduce successfully even with relevant artificial assisted reproductive technology must be eliminated.

The ewe's reproductive system is divided into the vulva: The vulva is the external part of the reproductive tract, also known as the vulva or vulva. It is located below the anus and connected to the perineum. It consists of three parts: the labia vulva, the clitoris, and the vestibule. The vagina: The vagina, located from the vulva to the inside of the body, receives and deposits semen during mating. It is also part of the birth canal and serves as a barrier to the reproductive tract, preventing bacteria and foreign matter from entering the uterus. The vagina is typically glandless, but it does contain mucus, which is secreted by the cervix and flows into the vagina. The uterus: The uterus is divided into three structures: the cervix, the uterine body, and the uterine horns. The cervix (cervix): Located between the vagina and uterus, it secretes mucus to trap bacteria and flush them out of the reproductive tract, forming a physical barrier and serving as the second barrier of the reproductive system. The uterine corpus (Uterus) and uterine horns: The goat's uterus is bicornuate (Figure 2), with a small corpus and uterine horns primarily responsible for the development of the fetus. The uterine corpus and horns are composed of three layers of tissue: the endometrium, which nourishes the fetus and is composed of a single layer of columnar epithelium, glands, and connective tissue. The myometrium, composed of circular muscles and extremely thick, helps push the fetus out of the uterus during birth. The perimetrium, formed by extensions of the peritoneum, provides support and attachment to the mother. Ruminants such as cattle and sheep have a specialized tissue within the uterus called the caruncle. This gland-less tissue is rich in blood vessels and serves as the attachment point for the cotyledonous lobes, supplying oxygen and nutrients to the fetus. The oviduct projects outward from the uterus and is where fertilization occurs. It provides a pathway for sperm to reach the egg. After the egg is released from the ovary, it is transported to the uterus via the oviduct. The oviduct's front end extends outward to form the infundibulum, a funnel-like structure sometimes called a trumpet tube. Its function is to catch the egg, released by the rupture of a mature ovarian follicle, and transport it to the oviduct and uterus. The ovary is the most important structure of the reproductive system. Goats have two ovaries, each containing numerous follicles at varying stages of maturity. When the follicle matures, it releases the egg. In addition to producing eggs, the ovaries are also important endocrine organs in females, primarily secreting estrogen and progesterone. Estrogen primarily influences the development and growth of female reproductive organs, promoting oogenesis, follicular development, stimulating corpus luteum growth, and promoting estrus. Progesterone, secreted primarily by the corpus luteum cells of the ovaries, promotes endometrial thickening and secretion, and supports pregnancy. The most important structure in the ovaries is the follicle, which can be categorized by size and shape into primary, secondary, tertiary, and Graafian (or vesicular) follicles.

The reproductive structure of a ram is primarily composed of the testes (testis) . Paired, the testes are located within the scrotum . A mature ram's testis weighs approximately 100-150 grams. Attached to the upper edge of the testis is the epididymis (epididymis), which contains the head of the testis and a vascular plexus at the anterior end and the tail of the testis at the posterior end . The epididymis is covered with a thick connective tissue rich in blood vessels , called the tunica albuginea . The testis is divided into numerous lobules by the tunica albuginea . The seminiferous tubules (tubuli seminiferi) are located within the testicular parenchyma and function to produce sperm and secrete androgen. The epididymis (epididymis) stores and matures sperm , confers fertilizing ability , and transports sperm from the testis to the vas deferens. Accessory sex glands : These include the seminal vesicles, prostate gland, and bulbourethral glands. Their primary function is to secrete seminal plasma, which contains sugars that nourish sperm and cleanse and protect the male urethra. Penis: The end of a ram's penis has a structure called the urethral process, which is an extension of the urethra beyond the end of the penis. The penis is the primary organ of copulation, protected by the foreskin. Its primary function is to transfer semen to the ewe's vagina during mating. A ram's penis is normally retracted within the body and only extends when the ram is aroused. The scrotum is a muscular sac that houses the testicles and epididymis . From the outside in, it consists of the cortical muscular membrane (which contains sweat and sebaceous glands), the scrotal fascia, and the common capsule . Its function is to protect the testicles and epididymis , and through scrotal contraction, it regulates the distance between the testicles and the body to maintain an appropriate temperature , which is beneficial for spermatogenesis . However, it should be noted that if the ambient temperature exceeds 39 ° C , goats cannot regulate their own cooling.

Puberty: Puberty is the age at which a male or female goat first comes into estrus, signifying that the gonads have begun producing gametes (sperm and eggs), but sexual maturity still takes several weeks. Puberty timing varies depending on breed, nutrition, and season of birth. Generally, goats puberty between four and six months of age, but breeding is not recommended until they reach at least 60-70% of their mature weight to avoid hampering subsequent growth and increasing the risk of dystocia.

Seasonal estrus: Goats are seasonal, short-day breeding animals. Autumn and winter are their breeding seasons. When the daylight hours begin to increase in spring and in the summer, reproductive activity slows down or stops.

When a ewe is in estrus, she will rapidly wag her tail, bleat, become restless, move around, eat less, and experience swelling and redness of the vulva. Increased vaginal discharge can make the vulva appear damp, dirty, and cloudy, with discharge sometimes hanging from the vulva. To confirm a ewe's estrus, a ram will sniff and lick her vulva, displaying Flehmen behavior, where the ram's upper lip lifts and sniffs. Rams also often test the ewe's receptivity by kicking her with their front legs and licking or biting her shoulders and back. If the ewe does not resist, he mounts her and completes the copulation, ejaculating. The entire process typically lasts no more than two minutes.

Estrus Cycle: After estrus, ewes begin to undergo cyclical changes in their reproductive physiology. These physiological changes from the onset of estrus to the next estrus are called the estrus cycle. The estrus cycle is divided into four phases: proestrus: the stage before estrus, lasting approximately 2-4 days. During this period, progesterone levels decrease, while follicle-stimulating hormone (FSH) is secreted, stimulating follicle growth. Simultaneously, estrogen levels begin to gradually rise, triggering estrus. Estrous: the period of sexual receptivity. Under the influence of estrogen, ewes allow males to ride and mate without avoiding them. The estrus period lasts approximately 30 hours, but can vary by several hours between individuals. During estrus, ewes may be seen swinging their tails rapidly, approaching rams, howling, and possibly riding other ewes. Ovulation occurs a few hours after the estrus period ends. Metoestrus (metoestrus) can last for 3-5 days. Ovulation usually occurs during this period. After ovulation, the follicle transforms into a corpus luteum and begins to secrete progesterone. Ewes may be seen exhibiting silent behavior and avoiding being ridden by rams.
And dioestrus: Diestrus, also known as anestrus, lasts about 10-14 days. It is the stage of entering the next estrus. At this time, the vaginal epithelial cells are not keratinized, and the corpus luteum secretes a large amount of progesterone to maintain the integrity of the endometrium for embryo implantation and development.

To achieve fertilization, sperm must undergo capacitation and acrosome reactions before they can penetrate the oocyte's zona pellucida and complete fertilization. If sperm undergo capacitation and acrosome reactions too early before fertilization, their fertilizing ability will be reduced.

Fertilization : When a mature egg reaches the fertilization site , a cluster of granular vesicles , known as the corona radiata and cumulus cells , still surround the oocyte 's zona pellucida . In most mammals, the cumulus cells disintegrate within a few hours after ovulation. When the sperm collides with the egg , the sperm head releases hyaluronidase or acrosin , enhancing its ability to penetrate the cumulus cells. As the sperm penetrates the zona pellucida , the sperm cap also contains proteolytic enzymes such as trypsin-like enzymes , aiding passage through the zona. As the sperm passes through the zona pellucida , it loses its cell membrane , and the outer membrane of the cap gradually peels away . By the time the sperm reaches the vitelline membrane , the cap has completely fallen off. Oocytes in higher mammals typically only allow fertilization by a single sperm to prevent polyspermy. After the first sperm passes through the zona pellucida, the yolk cortex changes, preventing further sperm from passing through the zona pellucida . This is called the zona reaction. Due to poor zona reaction in aging mammalian oocytes, multiple sperm penetration often occurs, hindering the normal development of the fertilized egg and representing a pathological condition.

Capacitation : After mating, sperm is ejaculated from the male body and exposed to the female reproductive tract for a period of time , passing through the cervix and into the uterus and fallopian tubes . During this time, the glycoproteins on the surface of the sperm cap are degraded by alpha- and beta- amylases in reproductive tract secretions. Simultaneously , the ratio of cholesterol to phosphatidylcholine in the cap membrane and its structure, as well as its membrane potential , change, reducing its stability. This allows the sperm to possess the ability to fertilize. In addition to capacitation , sperm undergo agglutination in the fallopian tube , which allows them to concentrate near the oocyte and increase their chances of attachment.

Acrosome reaction : The sperm cell membrane fuses with the outer membrane of the head cap. The contents of the head cap then flow out through the resulting pore. Typically , the head cap reaction occurs when the sperm encounters the zona pellucida. Once the sperm cell encounters the oocyte and reaches the zona pellucida , it undergoes the head cap reaction on its own , releasing increased amounts of hyaluronidase and other enzymes. These enzymes help the sperm head further penetrate the oocyte's zona pellucida.


Anterior pituitary: The anterior pituitary secretes follicle-stimulating hormone (FSH) and luteinizing hormone (LH). FSH stimulates follicle growth in females and testicular sperm production in males. In females, LH stimulates ovulation and the formation of the corpus luteum in the ovary. In males, LH stimulates testosterone secretion by the Leydig cells of the testes, leading to the male LH being called interstitial cell hormone. FSH and LH are collectively known as gonadotropins, also known as gonadotropins.

The hypothalamus regulates hormones and a negative feedback system: The hypothalamus secretes gonadotropin-releasing hormone (GnRH), which is delivered to the anterior pituitary gland via the hypothalamic-pituitary portal system. Upon receiving GnRH, the anterior pituitary gland secretes FSH and LH, which are transported via the bloodstream to the gonads to stimulate the secretion of sex steroids (estradiol and progesterone from the ovaries, and testosterone from the testes). However, when blood levels of these sex steroids become too high, they are transported to the anterior pituitary gland, inhibiting FSH and LH secretion. Furthermore, they are transported to the hypothalamus, inhibiting GnRH secretion, creating a negative feedback mechanism.

Pregnancy: Pregnancy begins with fertilization and ends with birth. After fertilization, the embryo begins cell division, forming a morula. The embryo then travels down the fallopian tube through the tubouterine junction to the uterus, where it implants and develops. This occurs approximately 15-20 days after fertilization, and is when most pregnancies terminate. If the pregnancy is terminated at this time, the ewe will return to estrus. The average gestation period for ewes is 150 days (approximately five months). The second half of pregnancy is generally uneventful, but in the last third of pregnancy, the rapid growth of the fetus increases the ewe's nutritional needs. During this period, attention must be paid to feed quality. The growing fetus takes up space in the abdomen and compresses the rumen, making it difficult for the ewe to consume large amounts of feed. Therefore, a high-quality feed is especially important during this period to ensure sufficient energy supply even with reduced feed intake. Insufficient energy intake during this period can lead to pregnancy toxemia, which can lead to death and significant losses. Therefore, before breeding, it is necessary to pay attention to the body shape of the ewes to be bred, and adjust the body shape of ewes that are too thin or too fat during pregnancy to cope with subsequent production and management.

Delivery: Goats deliver approximately 150 days after mating. Observe the ewe's abdomen and udder swelling. Some ewes may begin to secrete fluid from their nipples, gradually changing from amber to milky white. The hip ligaments begin to soften and stretch, signaling the onset of labor. As labor approaches, the ewe's vulva may swell and become sticky with discharge. The ewe may also become restless, kicking and stopping eating. During delivery, the cervix dilates and the uterus contracts, causing the amniotic membrane to rupture and the release of amniotic fluid. This phase can last for several hours. As the uterine contractions push the fetus into the birth canal, the lamb is quickly expelled from the mother's body. In a normal fetal position, the lamb's head and front feet emerge first. If the front feet are visible but not delivered within an hour, it indicates a difficult labor and requires assistance. After the fetus is delivered, the mother will expel the afterbirth and allantois (Figure 9). The entire delivery process, from start to finish, takes several to 12 hours. In most cases, the ewe can complete the delivery naturally.


Puberty: Puberty refers to the age at which goats are able to release gametes (sperm and eggs) and mate. Puberty typically occurs between 4 and 20 months of age, with the timing affected by factors such as breed, size, crossbreeding, inbreeding, health, nutritional status, and season of birth. Puberty does not necessarily indicate full maturity; most goats must reach two-thirds of their mature weight before their first breeding. Rams typically puberty between 4 and 6 months of age, but are more consistent between 5 and 7 months. Larger breeds tend to puberty more slowly than smaller ones, and within the same breed, faster-growing goats tend to puberty earlier than slower-growing ones.

Sexual maturity: Goats generally reach sexual maturity between 8 and 10 months of age, at which point their bodies and reproductive organs reach full development, and their reproductive function becomes fully developed, enabling them to reproduce normally. The age and state of sexual maturity are influenced by factors such as breed (hybrid > purebred, dairy > meat, native > exotic), individual differences (female > male), and the natural environment (excellent > adverse).

Mature and Age-Adapted: Physical maturity refers to the age at which a goat reaches adult weight and all organs are fully developed and functioning normally. Sexual maturity generally precedes physical maturity, with females typically reaching physical maturity after giving birth to one or two litters. Suitable breeding age is determined artificially based on the ram's developmental status and intended use. Generally, first breeding should occur in the late stages of sexual maturity or even later.

Seasonality: Goats are seasonal estrus animals, generally reaching peak reproduction between September and January. The reproductive performance of both rams and ewes is affected by light exposure. Goats are not infertile in spring and summer, but their behavior and symptoms are less pronounced than in the autumn and winter breeding seasons, and the reproductive system and behavioral signs are also less pronounced.

Libido : A ram's libido, vitality, and breeding ability play a crucial role in reproductive efficiency . A ram with a high libido can breed with a larger number of ewes during the breeding season. Factors such as the sheep's body condition, genetics, ambient temperature, disease, and parasites can all influence libido. Generally , it's appropriate for one ram to breed with 50 or fewer ewes during a breeding season . Breeding with too many ewes will cause the ram to consume excessive body mass and energy during the breeding season , affecting both breeding success rates and the ram's subsequent useful life.

Heredity: As with other species, certain goat breeds or goats with certain genes have a higher rate of multiple births. For example, the Nubian breed has a higher rate of triplets than other breeds. Furthermore, through selection and screening, the multiple birth rate of a flock can be improved.

Age: Age will affect the reproductive capacity of ewes. Generally, the reproductive capacity of ewes reaches its best when they are 3-5 years old , and the twin rate of ewes at the age of 4 years old will be higher than that of ewes at the age of 2 years old.

Temperature : When temperatures exceed 32 °C for a sustained period , embryo survival and subsequent fetal development decrease . High temperatures in late pregnancy may result in smaller and weaker lambs. Heat stress in rams can cause decreased sperm concentration and motility, and increased rates of malformations . Prolonged exposure to temperatures exceeding 35 ° C can lead to reduced fertility or infertility.

Nutrition: Optimal nutritional management can improve the reproductive capacity of ewes. Besides genetics and breed, ewe size is also determined by nutrition. Larger ewes within the same breed tend to have a higher rate of multiple births compared to smaller ewes. A good nutritional balance helps improve conception rates, maintain pregnancy, and reduce the incidence of acute metabolic disorders in late pregnancy. However, it's important not to blindly supplement nutrition. Overweight or underweight ewes can reduce conception success rates, and overweight ewes may suffer losses such as dystocia.

The reproductive physiology of ewes indicates that they begin puberty at an average age of 5 to 7 months, with an average range of 5 to 20 months. The average estrus cycle is 21 days, with a range of 15 to 24 days. Each estrus period lasts approximately 30 hours, but can actually be over between 24 and 48 hours. After giving birth, estrus returns approximately 33 hours later, typically between 30 and 40 hours. The average gestation period is 149 days, with a range of 144 to 155 days. The average litter size is 1.5 lambs, but the actual litter size can range from 1 to 4. The recommended time for breeding is when the ewe reaches 60-75% of its adult body weight.

Rams have reproductive characteristics. Their average puberty age is 4 to 6 months, with a range of 5 to 20 months. They are ready for breeding at an average age of 8 to 10 months, with a practical range of 6 to 12 months. A mature ram can breed an average of 40 ewes, with a range of 35 to 60. A young ram under one year old can breed an average of 20 ewes, with a practical range of 10 to 25. Rams produce approximately 6 million sperm per day, with a range of 4.8 to 7.2 million. The average ejaculate volume is 1 ml, with a range of 0.5 to 1.5 ml. The average sperm concentration in semen is 3.01 million sperm per ml, with a range of 0.5 to 6 million.

Breeding methods can be categorized into natural breeding: During the estrus season, both rams and ewes experience peak sexual hormone production and reproductive activity. Lambs born during this period are healthier and more efficient than those produced during the off-season. Generally, a mature ram can mate with 35-60 ewes during the breeding season. Artificial insemination: Artificial insemination is not restricted by season, but estrus synchronization is often required to ensure a consistent estrus period within a short period of time for easier artificial insemination. Frozen semen in Taiwan is mostly imported from France or Australia. This semen is sourced from rams with proven progeny. Sheep farmers can select semen based on their flock's future breeding direction to improve their performance. Artificial light regulation: Light regulation mimics the natural daylight variations of summer and autumn to induce estrus. Lighting adjustment takes four months. If the ewes are scheduled for breeding in May, a lighting adjustment plan should be implemented starting in January. The method involves installing fluorescent lights in an open-plan sheep pen at an appropriate height above the bedding surface. Adjust the light intensity to a specific level at the sheep's eye level. A timer is then set to turn on the lights one hour before sunset and off for four hours at midnight. During the day, the sheep receive natural light for a total of 20 hours of light per day. This process is repeated for two months, followed by a two-month wait. This method allows the sheep to enter estrus during the non-breeding season, thereby adjusting the time for winter milk or winter mutton to be marketed.

Artificial insemination: Artificial insemination involves the use of both human and mechanical assistance to inject ram semen into the female reproductive tract without direct contact between the goats. The main reasons for its limited adoption in the goat industry are the small scale of the industry, the low economic benefits of artificial insemination, and the need to maintain rams for semen collection. Furthermore, the success rate of pregnancy in ewes is low when the semen used for artificial insemination is frozen-thawed semen. However, with advancements in artificial insemination technology, its effectiveness has significantly improved. Ultimately, however, it remains crucial to adapt to producer needs and be flexible to increase industry acceptance.

The advantages of artificial insemination technology are as follows: (1) It can increase the genetic performance of the population: Producers are usually most concerned about how to improve the continuation of the excellent genetic performance of sheep, or how to spread excellent genetic traits in the flock. Current selection is usually carried out on males, because males can produce far more offspring than females in their life history. The production traits of excellent males will directly affect the performance of their offspring. If the selection program continues, it will have a long-term impact on the performance improvement of future generations. Under natural breeding conditions, each male can breed 50-100 females a year, but if fresh semen is diluted and artificially inseminated through the cervix, more than 1,000 females can be bred in 2-3 weeks. Even if the success rate of artificial insemination is not as good as natural breeding, the ability of excellent males to transmit genetic material is still several times better than natural breeding. (2) Quick and easy transmission of genetic material: Breeders usually hope to introduce new bloodlines into their own flocks. Compared with directly purchasing breeding rams, semen is the cheapest, fastest and most effective way to avoid the spread of diseases. Frozen semen allows excellent genetic material to be exchanged and used around the world without being restricted by distance . (3) Semen can be stored for a long time: Semen can be stored in liquid nitrogen for permanent preservation, and its excellent genetic material can be effectively preserved even after the death of the ram. (4) Improve breeding efficiency: Artificial insemination can ensure that every female animal is fertilized and avoid missed breeding. New female sheep often have silent estrus and their estrus symptoms are not obvious. Using artificial insemination can improve the success rate of breeding for female sheep during their first estrus. (5) Reduce or avoid ram maintenance costs: Small farms do not need to maintain rams, reducing the time, space and cost required to manage rams. (6) Reduce and control the spread of diseases: Artificial insemination does not require direct contact between males and females, which can avoid the spread of diseases, but it can only prevent the spread of diseases and has no function of treating diseases. (7) Improve the efficiency of using goats that have lost their ability to breed: Some rams with excellent performance may be affected by genital injuries or old age, which may affect their breeding behavior. However, artificial insemination technology can still be used to enable them to continue to reproduce. (8) Accurately record breeding information: Artificial insemination can accurately record the breeding time, so the time of delivery can be accurately calculated, or adverse genetic traits can be prevented from being introduced into the flock. (9) Use estrus synchronization to breed in the non-breeding season: Goats are seasonal estrus animals, but in order to meet the market demand for milk or meat, they must be bred in the non-breeding season to meet production timelines. Because the quality of ram semen in the non-breeding season is poor, frozen semen must be used for artificial insemination to achieve this goal.

The disadvantages of artificial insemination technology are as follows: (1) Inbreeding: High selection intensity is often accompanied by increased inbreeding, which reduces genetic variation and leads to the adverse effects of inbreeding. (2) Potential breeding errors: Incorrect labeling of straws during the production of frozen semen may lead to the subsequent use of incorrectly labeled ram semen. (3) Disease transmission: If diseases that can be transmitted through semen are not screened before being produced into frozen semen, then diseases can be spread through artificial insemination, and this hazard spreads faster and wider than natural breeding. (4) Reduced reproductive efficiency: Compared with natural breeding, the reproductive efficiency of artificial insemination is lower, especially when estrus synchronization is carried out or when semen is improperly stored during artificial insemination, which will aggravate this phenomenon. (5) Cost: As with any technology applied to the sheep industry, the initial cost will increase, but as the technology becomes more proficient and the additional benefits generated by the implementation of the technology increase, the cost will gradually decrease.

Semen quality assessment: Semen consists of sperm (15%) and seminal plasma (85%). Semen quality assessment can be divided into basic semen characteristics and sperm performance assessment. Basic semen characteristics include semen volume, color, sperm concentration, pH, and osmotic pressure; sperm performance assessment includes motility and activity parameters, survival rate, head cap integrity, mitochondrial potential, and DNA integrity.

Estrus observation and detection: Observing goat estrus plays a crucial role in artificial insemination. If the timing of estrus is well understood , the success rate of artificial insemination will be greatly improved.

To determine if a ewe is in estrus, here are nine characteristics to consider. 1. Frequent vocalization: Ewes don't normally make much noise, but those in estrus will make more vocalizations than usual. Nubian ewes are more vocal than other breeds, and if they haven't been bred by a ram, they may even mimic the ram's vocalizations. 2. Tail wag: When in estrus, ewes will wag their tails rapidly. They will also allow their tails to be grasped without resistance. 3. Personality changes in estrus: Hormonal changes can cause a change in the personality of ewes, and normally docile ewes may exhibit aggressive behavior toward other sheep. 4. Sticky and dirty tail: In estrus, the ventral side of the tail will be red and swollen, accompanied by a sticky vaginal discharge. If the hair on both sides of the tail is sticky or clumped together, and there is no evidence of illness, this indicates estrus. 5. Changes in milk production: Normally milked ewes in estrus may occasionally refuse to enter the milking parlor. If a ram is forced to penetrate, the ewe's milk supply will decrease, accompanied by a loss of appetite. 6. Mounting Other Ewes: If a ewe cannot wait for a ram to mount her, she will mount other ewes or allow them to mount her. When another ewe sniffs the genitals of an estrus ewe, she will respond by raising her tail. 7. Frequent Urinary: When a ewe is in estrus, she will urinate more frequently than usual. This urine contains chemicals (pheromones) that tell the ram she is waiting to be bred. If a ram is introduced, he will lick the ewe's urine with his muzzle, then raise his head and raise his upper lip (Flemen's response) to pick up the ewe's estrus scent. 8. Stable Standing of the Ewe: If a ewe is not in stable estrus, she will not accept being mounted by a ram and will flee if he attempts to mount her. If a ewe accepts being mounted by a ram, she is in stable estrus. 9. Vaginal Mucus Changes: The average duration of estrus in ewes is 30 hours. However, during artificial insemination, even if the ewe is receptive to the ram, it is still necessary to observe the condition of the vaginal mucus. Generally, clear vaginal mucus indicates the beginning of estrus, thick and slightly turbid mucus indicates the middle stage, and cloudy mucus indicates the optimal time for artificial insemination.

Estrus detection: The sheep used for estrus detection can be divided into three categories: (1) Using normal rams that have not been vasectomized. When using this type of ram, wrap a cloth around their abdomen (the penis must be covered) to prevent the penis from being inserted during detection. (2) Using vasectomized rams. The advantage of this type of ram is that you do not need to worry about whether they actually ejaculate, because the ejaculated semen does not contain sperm, only seminal plasma. (3) Using ewes that are injected with hormones to make them behave like rams. This method is very stressful for the sheep because it uses a large amount of hormones artificially injected. Once the injection is stopped, the ewe's male-like behavior will disappear, so this method is not recommended.

Artificial insemination techniques can be divided into four main categories based on the location of semen insertion: intravaginal artificial insemination, intracervical artificial insemination, intrauterine artificial insemination, and laparoscopic artificial insemination. The success rate of artificial insemination increases with deeper insertion into the uterus. The key to successful artificial insemination is timing. The estrus period of a typical ewe lasts 12-36 hours, and ovulation occurs approximately four hours after the ewe has stabilized and can be ridden by a ram and then regains a position where it cannot be ridden by a ram. Therefore, the optimal time for artificial insemination is between 12 and 24 hours after the ewe is stable and can be ridden. Before artificial insemination, estrus synchronization is typically performed to ensure that the ewes are in estrus at a similar time, reducing both time and cost. The following describes the methods of estrus synchronization and artificial insemination.

Estrus synchronization: The most commonly used method for estrus synchronization currently involves inserting a controlled internal drug release (CIDR) intravaginally, marking day 0. On day 9, intramuscular injection of 400-500 IU of pregnant mare serum gonadotropin (PMSG) and 0.5 mL of prostaglandin F2alpha (PGF2a) is administered. The CIDR is removed on day 11, and estrus is detected using a test ram or ewe on day 12. Artificial insemination is typically performed 37-43 hours after CIDR removal.

The instruments required for artificial insemination are: constant temperature water tank, artificial insemination gun, lubricant, vaginal opener, light source, straw cutter and insemination gun sleeve. The operation steps of artificial insemination are divided into three parts: (1) Ewe restraint: pull the ewe to the artificial insemination restraint frame, fix the neck of the sheep with a neck clamp, lift the sheep's hind legs and fix them on the restraint frame to complete the sheep restraint. (2) Thawing of frozen semen and loading of artificial insemination gun: If the semen used is frozen thawed semen, first take the semen out of the liquid nitrogen storage barrel and put it into a 37℃ constant temperature water bath for 30 seconds to thaw. After the thawed straw is thoroughly dried with a paper towel, cut the powdered end of the straw with a straw cutter. Insert the open end into the artificial insemination gun sleeve, then put on the artificial insemination gun, push the straw to the bottom, and pull the buckle at the end of the insemination gun back to make the sleeve and the insemination gun stable. The artificial insemination gun with the semen device should be placed in a pocket or in a dark place to avoid direct sunlight before insemination. (3) Semen injection: After properly lubricating the front end of the vaginal opener, lift the sheep's tail and slowly insert the vaginal opener into the vagina, stretching the vagina. After finding the cervical opening, insert the artificial insemination gun from the cervical opening at a downward angle of about 15 degrees. If there is an obstacle, you can swing the insemination gun to try to pass through. If it still cannot pass, do not force it, and place the semen at the obstacle. The best place to place the semen is in the uterine cavity, but only a few sheep can successfully place it. Do not use brute force when performing artificial insemination, otherwise the immune response caused by vaginal or cervical bleeding will be extremely unfavorable for insemination.

Pregnancy Diagnosis: If a ewe returns to estrus after artificial insemination, it can be observed 18-24 days after artificial insemination (a new estrus cycle ). Ultrasound scanning is usually performed 45 days after artificial insemination. If the placenta has formed at this time, the uterine funiculus will be clearly visible. Blood tests for pregnancy-associated glycoproteins (PAGs) can also be performed 28-35 days after artificial insemination, with an accuracy rate of approximately 99.3%.


Sperm cryoprotectants: The main function of cryoprotectants is to prevent or reduce the physical and chemical damage to sperm during freezing and thawing. Cryoprotectants can be divided into two categories: permeable and non-permeable: (1) Permeable cryoprotectants. Common permeable cryoprotectants include glycerol, ethylene glycol, dimethylsulfonate, and propylene glycol, among which glycerol is the most commonly used. This type of cryoprotectant acts as a solute during cell freezing, achieving the purpose of dehydrating sperm. The water content inside the cell decreases, the freezing point drops, and the amount of ice crystal formation is reduced, thereby reducing damage to sperm. In addition, permeable cryoprotectants can also increase the permeability of the cell membrane, which is more conducive to sperm dehydration and thus improves the survival rate of sperm after freezing and thawing. However, the final concentration (v/v) of these cryoprotectants and the equilibrium time and temperature need to be determined based on their toxicity and the effect on the quality of semen after sperm freezing and thawing. The toxicity of the four cryoprotectants mentioned above is as follows: propylene glycol is the most toxic, followed by dimethyl sulfoxide, then ethylene glycol, and the least toxic is glycerol. (2) Permeable cryoprotectants. The most commonly used non-permeable cryoprotectants are egg yolk, skim milk powder, and sugars. These cryoprotectants cannot penetrate the sperm cell membrane and therefore only function outside the cell. Their functions are to modify the sperm cell membrane, dehydrate, and lower the freezing temperature of semen during freezing.

Semen Collection: Semen can generally be collected using an artificial vagina. This artificial vagina consists of a hard rubber cylinder lined with a rubber sleeve . Warm water at approximately 45 °C can be pumped in between the rubber and hard walls, and air blown into the lining to expand , thereby simulating the temperature and pressure conditions found in a ewe's vagina. Alternatively, electrostimulation can be used. This method requires restraining the ram and using an electrostimulator. After restraint, the foreskin is cleansed with saline to prevent contaminants from entering the semen. The collector stands at the ram's side . Another assistant lubricates the tip of an electrode rod and inserts it directly into the rectum through the anus, adjusting the current intensity. Initially , a low-voltage current is applied through the rectum to stimulate the reproductive tract muscles . The ram experiences a strong stiffness when the current is applied , but returns to normal when the stimulation ceases . Stimulation is typically repeated, with brief intervals of low current followed by a higher current , to induce ejaculation.

Sperm Freezing and Thawing: After removing the seminal plasma from the ram semen , add the appropriate amount of first-stage goat cryoprotectant diluent based on the sperm concentration at room temperature. Transfer to a 4 °C cold room to equilibrate for 2 hours. Then, add an equal amount of second-stage goat cryoprotectant diluent ( glycerol is added to the first-stage goat cryoprotectant diluent , which is divided into three equal parts , with 10- minute intervals between additions ) . After adding the second-stage diluent, let the mixture stand for 50 minutes. The semen is then transferred into 0.5mL French straws , sealed with PVC sealing powder, and frozen in a computer-programmed automatic freezer/cooler pre-cooled to 4 °C . The straws are then placed in liquid nitrogen for storage. Thawing procedures: After removing the frozen semen from the liquid nitrogen tank , immediately place it in a 37 °C water bath for 30 seconds to thaw.

Oocyte and Embryo Freezing and Thawing: The application value of oocyte and embryo cryopreservation technology not only accelerates livestock herd improvement and increases the commercial value of livestock, as well as facilitates disease control in seed import and export, but also provides a technical platform for in vitro embryo production, embryo transfer, nuclear transposition, and transgenic animal research in artificial reproductive technology. It can also be used to preserve the gene pool of elite breeds, rare mammals, or any individual, enhancing livestock stock diversity. It also facilitates the transfer of livestock embryos produced in vitro and in vivo without time or space constraints, achieving the goal of rapidly expanding high-quality livestock herds. The freezing efficiency of embryos or eggs is still affected by various factors, including cryoprotectant toxicity, intracellular ice crystals, fragmentation damage, and changes in osmotic pressure. Commercial frozen embryo production has been relatively successful in cattle and sheep.

Freezing methods for oocytes and embryos: (1) Slow freezing: This freezing method mainly involves placing oocytes and embryos in 0.25 mL straws , placing them in a programmed cooling device , and allowing the temperature to drop to -6 °C and then stop at this temperature . At this time, the liquid in the straw will not freeze . Then, an ice-planting action is performed ( knocking the straw or using an object with a lower temperature to touch the straw wall ), and the liquid in the straw freezes instantly. This action is intended to accelerate the freezing rate of the liquid and speed up the time it takes to pass through the ice crystal formation zone (-1 to -5 °C ) , and then continue to cool to -80 °C. The advantages of this method are low concentration of cryoprotectant, low toxicity to cells, and low damage to the osmotic pressure difference between the inside and outside of the cell membrane. However, the disadvantages are that the freezing rate is still not fast enough , resulting in greater damage to the embryonic cell membrane and organelles caused by ice crystal formation, expensive equipment, and time-consuming and complicated processing. (2) Vitrification: This method uses a cooling rate of up to -25,000 °C per minute to freeze . This method is usually carried out with a higher concentration of cryoprotectant because a high concentration of cryoprotectant can provide a better cooling rate for vitrification. Successful vitrification can produce a transparent solid structure with almost no ice crystal formation. The advantages of this method are that the freezing rate is extremely fast and there is almost no ice crystal formation , so the damage to cells is minimal, the required equipment and processing procedures are extremely simple and inexpensive , and the processing time is only more than ten minutes. However, the disadvantage is that a high concentration of cryoprotectant is required , which has a greater damage to cell toxicity and the osmotic pressure difference between the inside and outside of the cell membrane. Vitrification can take several forms according to the required carrier, such as : freezing ring, freezing plate, microscope net, hot-pull open straw and nylon net, etc. There is also a microdrop method that does not require a carrier. According to research, the embryonic development ability of those frozen using vitrification is significantly better after thawing than that of those frozen using general slow freezing, so vitrification has gradually replaced slow freezing.

Embryo transfer technology: Embryo transfer technology is an artificial reproductive technology that can rapidly expand purebred sheep flocks and significantly improve sheep production performance in the shortest possible time. However, this technology requires high operational skills and more careful personnel training, but it still has great commercial application value.

Estrus synchronization treatment: The estrus synchronization method for embryo transfer is the same as the estrus synchronization method for ewes during artificial insemination. The only difference is that the time of embryo transfer will be delayed by several days compared to the time of artificial insemination.

Ovarian Superovulation: The goal of superovulation is to increase the number of eggs released per ovary, facilitating oocyte or embryo collection. The procedure for superovulation is similar to estrus synchronization during artificial insemination, with the difference being that six intramuscular injections of FSH are administered daily, at 12:00 PM and 4:00 PM intervals, for three consecutive days from day 7 to day 9. The doses are 4, 4, 2, 2, 2, and 2 mg (a total of 16 mg). The final two doses of FSH are accompanied by an injection of 1.7 IU of LH.

Embryo Transfer Method: Thawed, viable goat embryos are surgically transferred into the uterine horn on the same side as the ovarian ovary. Pregnancy is confirmed 45 days after embryo transfer using an ultrasound scanner. Pregnancy is confirmed by imaging the uterine cavity and fetus.

Other assisted reproductive technologies include laparoscopic artificial insemination, in vitro fertilization (IVF), and sperm sexing. 1. Laparoscopic artificial insemination: Laparoscopic artificial insemination of sheep has been practiced for many years. This technique was initially developed to address the low pregnancy rates associated with traditional cervical insemination using frozen semen. Laparoscopic artificial insemination involves withholding water and food for 24-36 hours prior to the procedure. During the procedure, a sedative is injected intramuscularly. Once the sheep becomes unsteady, unable to stand, or falls asleep, it is restrained and the laparoscopic procedure performed. Laparoscopic artificial insemination typically involves two incisions: one for the imaging and lighting system and the other for the laparoscopic semen injection tube. Ovarian ovulation is monitored before injection . Semen is injected into the uterine horn on the ovulatory side, and pregnancy is confirmed by ultrasound 45 days later. 2. In vitro embryo production: In vitro embryo production involves removing oocytes from goat ovaries, maturing them in vitro, fertilizing them, and culturing them in vitro. Once they reach a certain stage (blastocyst), they are transferred to a surrogate mother or cryopreserved. Currently, this method has a much lower success rate than embryos derived in vivo. 3. Sperm sex sorting: Using sexed semen for artificial insemination can increase the number of single-sex offspring born within a farm and even accelerate the rate of genetic improvement across all generations of goats. There are no significant differences in pregnancy length, fetal mortality, birth weight, or post-weaning survival rates between insemination with single-sex sperm and artificial insemination with standard semen. However, the disadvantages are that the sperm separation rate is still insufficient, and the sperm undergoes high dilution (5000 times), nuclear staining and culture, mechanical force (pressure), ultraviolet laser irradiation and electric field stimulation during the separation process, and subsequent spraying into the collection fluid (80-90 km/h) and centrifugation (700-1200g) after thawing, which reduces the sperm survival rate. In addition, the high production cost, the difficulty in obtaining semen from male animals with excellent traits, and the inability to conduct pre-analysis of relevant genes similar to that provided by early embryo sex identification are also important issues currently faced.

The process of in vitro embryo production can be divided into four steps: 1. Oocyte collection: Oocytes reside in ovarian follicles, so obtaining oocytes generally requires ovarian collection. Ovaries can be obtained from slaughterhouses, which can collect a larger number of ovaries and are more suitable for beginners. Alternatively, oocyte retrieval can be performed from live animals. Surgical oocyte collection can yield fewer oocytes, but the breed can be accurately determined. This is more suitable for applications requiring stricter control of results or for the preservation of high-quality stock. 2. Oocyte in vitro maturation: After oocyte collection, goat oocytes undergo 24 hours of in vitro maturation. After in vitro maturation, the cumulus cells transform from tightly packed to liquefied and diffused. Staining reveals the nucleus and the extruded first polar body. 3. Oocyte in vitro fertilization: Semen used for in vitro fertilization can be fresh semen, frozen-thawed semen, refrigerated semen, or sex-selected semen. After capacitation treatment, typically with heparin or caffeine, semen is co-cultured with oocytes for 18 hours. After staining, the second polar body, male pronucleus, and female pronucleus can be observed. 4. Embryo Culture in Vitro: Generally, embryonic cleavage can be observed 48 hours after fertilization. The embryo divides through multiple divisions, namely 2, 4, 8, 16, 32 (also known as the morula stage), and the 64-cell blastocyst stage. Development to the blastocyst stage typically takes seven days. Once the embryo has developed to the blastocyst stage, it can be transferred to a surrogate mother's uterus for pregnancy or frozen for future use.


Sheep breeding: The most important foundation of sheep breeding is health! It can usually be measured by several standards, including the performance of the sheep itself, the animal breeding environment, and the pathogens present around the animals . These three levels will affect each other and endanger the health of the sheep.

Key points of health management: There are several key points, namely, (1) health management of lambs, (2) reducing stress factors of the flock, (3) paying attention to pasture health management, (4) confirming the occurrence of diseases in sheep, (5) precautions for caring for sick sheep, (6) the necessary time for veterinarians to arrive for diagnosis and treatment, and (7) recording data in detail.

Lamb Health Management: Newborn lambs are particularly susceptible to disease because their immune systems haven't yet developed enough to fight pathogens. However, if lambs receive adequate colostrum after birth, they can obtain sufficient disease-fighting antibodies from their well-immunized dams. It's important to note that lambs' resistance to disease differs significantly from that of adult lambs, so try to avoid mixing lambs of varying ages. Furthermore, lambs are much more vulnerable to respiratory pathogens and environmental parasites than their dams, making them susceptible to contracting respiratory and parasitic diseases from apparently healthy ewes.

Reduce stress factors in sheep: Factors that cause stress in sheep flocks include bullying, overcrowding, long-distance transport, and the increasingly severe extreme climates, especially heat stress. Heat stress affects the sheep's brain nerves, stimulating the adrenal cortex to secrete cortisol into the bloodstream, which in turn affects the physiological performance of various internal organs. It also particularly weakens the sheep's immune system, leading to increased susceptibility to disease.

Emphasize farm health management: Veterinarians must collaborate with the farm to develop a specific sheep health plan based on the farm's objectives. These plans cover all stages of sheep husbandry, management, health care, and disease prevention. For example, regarding mastitis, the plan should include past mastitis cases and pathogen testing results, along with appropriate medical treatment and preventive measures for mastitis. Standard milking procedures should also be developed and implemented at least annually. A mastitis treatment process should also be established, specifically addressing strategies for handling various mastitis issues encountered during milking.

Identify the disease in sheep: If a sheep becomes ill, it is important to identify the disease immediately, as timely treatment can reduce the impact of the disease and minimize animal losses. Symptoms of sick sheep may include:
1. Loss of alertness and curiosity; 2. Decreased appetite, or shorter or no rumination ; 3. Dull eyes, dry nose, or abnormal eye and nasal discharge; 4. Dull, rough coat; 5. Changes in stool characteristics, such as lumps, mud, or water; 6. Rapid breathing rate or abnormal sounds when breathing; 7. Slow gait, staggering, or inability of the animal to walk in a straight line; 8. Decreased milk production, changes in milk characteristics, and redness and swelling of the udder.

Notes on caring for sick sheep: Veterinarians can only treat the initial onset of illness, or more seriously ill sheep, after which field personnel must continue the important work of caring for the sick sheep. There are a few points that require special attention:
1. Sick sheep, like sick people, particularly need a quiet environment, so it is best to isolate sick sheep from other sheep in the flock; 2. The isolated animal ward must be dry, bright and well-ventilated, and the floor must be clean or covered with clean bedding; 3. Provide clean drinking water, hay and concentrate; 4. Sheep are sometimes reluctant to stand up, so they must be assisted to stand to increase their food and water intake and recover quickly from the illness.

Every sheep manager or owner should be aware of the following: delaying medical attention can seriously endanger the sheep's life or even lead to more serious harm to the flock. Immediate veterinary attention is required in the following situations: fractures, severe bleeding, neurological symptoms, recumbency, suspected poisoning, uterine prolapse exposing the vulva, severe bloating, loss of appetite for more than 8-12 hours, lethargy, or a temperature below 36°C.

Detailed records: All health management or disease treatment must be recorded in detail in the health management record form for future tracking and treatment reference.


Sheep farm self-defense and epidemic prevention: Prevention is the most important thing for sheep diseases. Therefore, establishing a complete and implemented epidemic prevention system (self-defense and epidemic prevention plan) can effectively avoid the occurrence of diseases.

Key points for self-prevention and epidemic prevention: These self-prevention and epidemic prevention plans and work should include the following: (1) Animal breeding areas should be surrounded by fences, and entrances and exits should be equipped with cleaning and disinfection facilities, such as disinfection pools and automatic spray disinfection equipment, for personnel and vehicles. Animal transport vehicles, feed trucks, chemical processing plant collection trucks, and visitor vehicles are strictly prohibited from entering the area . When necessary, they must undergo a rigorous cleaning and disinfection process before entering. Animal sheds should be disinfected regularly, at least weekly. Do not mix more than two disinfectants when preparing disinfectants before disinfection. When vacating animal sheds, they must be thoroughly cleaned and allowed to dry. After disinfection, they should be flame sterilized and then sprayed with disinfectant. After cleaning and disinfection, the shed should be vacant for at least one week before introducing animals. It is best to disinfect the shed again before moving animals in. (2) In principle, visitors are prohibited from entering the breeding area. If necessary, they must at least change clothes or rubber shoes and disinfect their footwear before entering.
