(3) A quarantine area should be established outside the breeding area to house and quarantine newly introduced animals. Newly introduced animals should be kept in the quarantine area for at least 3-4 weeks to ensure they are healthy and free of potential infection before they can be moved into the herd. (4) Sheep must be vaccinated to obtain sufficient antibodies to fight disease. After the foot-and-mouth disease vaccine was withdrawn on July 1, 2018, the only annual vaccine required for domestic sheep is the sheep pox vaccine. Sheep over 4 months of age should receive a full annual sheep pox vaccination. (5) The farm should also implement a regular deworming program for internal and external parasites, and perform regular fecal microscopic examinations to monitor the health of the sheep. (6) If an animal owner or manager discovers or suspects an animal-borne disease or has died of an unexplained disease, they should report it to the animal quarantine agency to strengthen quarantine measures and prevent the spread of the disease.

Foot-and-mouth disease (FMD) is caused by the RNA virus Picornaviridae and is a serious infectious disease that primarily infects even-toed ungulates, including pigs, cattle, sheep, and goats. The FMD virus is divided into seven serotypes: O, A, C, Asia1, SAT-1, SAT-2, and SAT-3. Each serotype is further divided into numerous subtypes. For example, in Taiwan, the FMD virus isolated from pigs in 1997 was O/Taiwan/97, while the virus isolated from cattle infected in Kinmen and Taiwan in 1999 was O/Taiwan/99. The antigenicity of these seven serotypes differs significantly, resulting in poor cross-protection between FMD vaccines against different serotypes. The foot-and-mouth disease virus can spread rapidly, including through wind (vector) spread, through contact with animals and their products, through contact with clothing on people and through contact with animals' equipment. In addition, the disease can also be transmitted through vehicles and equipment carrying people or animals.

Symptoms of foot-and-mouth disease: The incubation period for sheep and goats infected with foot-and-mouth disease is approximately 2-7 days. Initial symptoms include a high fever (40-41 °C ) , poor spirits, loss of appetite, and excessive salivation. The tongue, lips, and gums of infected sheep become congested and develop small grayish-white spots . These spots then form blisters , which mature and rupture. Along with the oral lesions , blisters also form on the skin around the coronary arteries and between the toes. When these blisters rupture , secondary bacterial infection can cause the hoof to fall off, leaving the sheep lame or even unable to stand. The mortality rate for adult sheep infected with foot-and-mouth disease is not high (2%), but it can reach 50% for lambs. Surviving sheep typically regain their spirits and appetite within a week , and blisters, if not infected by bacteria, resolve in 1-3 weeks.

Foot-and-mouth disease prevention: Preventing foot-and-mouth disease primarily relies on strengthening epidemic reporting practices: Immediately notify veterinarians and local livestock quarantine agencies if any animals display symptoms suggestive of foot-and-mouth disease, enabling rapid control or eradication of the disease. Ranches should also strengthen their self-defense and epidemic prevention mechanisms, including enhanced disinfection of areas around livestock sheds and control of personnel and vehicle access. Furthermore, Taiwan, surrounded by the sea, previously enjoyed significant advantages in disease prevention. However, rampant smuggling has created significant loopholes in animal disease prevention. Therefore, it is imperative to foster a national awareness of epidemic prevention to prevent the influx of diseases from abroad. All citizens should heed the lessons of the foot-and-mouth disease outbreak and refrain from engaging in illegal smuggling, transportation, and trafficking. Citizens must also cooperate by not importing meat or other quarantined items by mail or courier to prevent the recurrence of foot-and-mouth disease.

Peste des Petits Ruminants (PPR) is a viral disease that primarily infects goats and sheep, also known as goat plague. The pathogen belongs to the Paramyxoviridae family, genus Morbillivirus. Goats are generally more susceptible than sheep, and clinical symptoms are more severe. Susceptibility varies between goat breeds and individuals within the same breed, with European goats being more susceptible. The disease occasionally occurs in other wild animals, such as deer. This disease is a Category A infectious disease. If a person is found to be infected, suspected to be infected, or potentially infected, they should be treated according to the regulations of the competent animal health authorities regarding Category A infectious diseases. This disease primarily affects goats and is not a zoonosis, posing no public health concern. China first reported cases of PPR in 2007, and in recent years (2014 and 2015) outbreaks have spread throughout mainland China, even to coastal areas such as Jiangsu, Zhejiang, and Anhui.

Symptoms of Peste des Petits Ruminants: During the acute phase, infected animals excrete the virus through secretions, excretions, and exhaled breath, becoming a source of infection. Infection is primarily transmitted through direct contact or short-range droplet transmission between animals in the same area through coughing. The virus is found in semen and embryos, making it potentially transmitted through artificial insemination or embryo transfer. It can also be transmitted through breast milk. The virus primarily attacks lymphoid tissue and epithelial tissues of the digestive tract. Symptoms include high fever, increased eye and nasal discharge, salivation, necrotizing stomatitis, gastroenteritis, and bronchopneumonia. The incubation period is generally 3-6 days. Clinical signs include sudden onset of high fever, reaching 40-42°C on the second or third day, with death occurring in the later stages of the fever. With the onset of fever, animals develop a decreased appetite and a depressed demeanor. Nasal discharge changes from watery to catarrhal, and nasal mucosal necrosis occurs. Animals may also develop conjunctivitis and increased eye discharge. Secondary bacterial pneumonia, cough, rales, and abdominal breathing are often associated. Within four days of the onset of fever, the oral mucosa initially develops mild hyperemia and surface erosions, accompanied by drooling. Small areas of necrosis typically begin in the mucosa beneath the gums and then rapidly spread to the gums, hard palate, cheeks, oral papillae, and tongue. Necrotic tissue sloughs, leaving irregular, shallow erosions. Some sheep experience mild oral lesions that resolve within two days. Following the onset of oral lesions, most animals develop severe diarrhea with bloody, watery stools, rapid dehydration, and weight loss. Pregnant sheep may miscarry. The infection rate and mortality rate in young sheep can reach 100%. Hyperacute cases may be free of lesions, presenting only with fever and death. Pathological changes in acute cases include necrotic lesions in the mouth and nose; hyperemia in the ileal and cecal valves; linear hyperemia and black lines (zebra striping) in the folds of the cecum, proximal colon, and rectum; splenomegaly and necrosis; and lymphadenopathy, particularly in the mesenteric lymph nodes.

Prevention of Peste des Petits Ruminants: This disease is a Category A animal infectious disease. Once introduced, prevention measures include culling and compensation. Quarantine efforts should focus on preventing the spread of the disease, with strict quarantine measures implemented for animals and animal products from infected areas (particularly mainland China). For the safety and development of Taiwan's livestock industry, ranches must implement self-prevention measures such as access control, entry and exit controls, and disinfection of livestock farms. Suspected cases should be reported immediately to the local animal quarantine authorities. Avoid visiting or interacting with animals at livestock farms in mainland China or other affected countries. Upon return, change clothing, shower, and thoroughly disinfect, and wait one week before re-entering animal farms to ensure the health and safety of the animals.

Goatpox: The Capripox virus, belonging to the Poxviridae family and the Capripoxvirus genus, is acid-labile and resistant to drying. Literature reports indicate that the virus can survive in dandruff and scabs for at least three months, and may persist in dried, crusted dandruff for several years. Goatpox virus can be transmitted through the respiratory system but can also enter the body through abrasions of the skin. Most animals become infected through close contact with infected goats. The virus can be present in all endocrine and exocrine secretions, skin lesions, and scabs. Dust and scabs containing the virus can also transmit the disease. The virus can also be transmitted mechanically through contaminated materials or insect bites, but no chronic carriers have been found to date.

Symptoms of Sheep Pox: The incubation period for artificial infection is 2-3 days , while for natural infection it is 1-3 weeks. The mortality rate in lambs is high , peaking around 2 weeks after the onset of systemic lesions . Even on sheep farms with no history of the disease , an outbreak can lead to high mortality rates. The first clinical sign of infection is fever , often accompanied by depression and loss of appetite . Conjunctivitis, rhinitis, and lymphadenitis may then develop. About 2 days later, a red rash may appear on sparsely furred areas, such as the armpits, udders, eyelids, lips, nostrils, labia, scrotum, and groin . Lesions may also appear on the skin or penetrate into muscle tissue. The rash gradually develops into raised papules and blisters , which eventually rupture or shrink and heal with scabs . Sometimes, ruptured blisters can lead to secondary bacterial infection . Recovery from the onset of symptoms takes approximately 20-40 days. Malignant sheep pox often develops on the hairless skin, respiratory mucosa (causing respiratory distress), and oral cavity (reducing feeding desire) of both lambs and adult sheep. Severe mucosal lesions often lead to necrosis and purulent oral and nasal discharge. In severe cases, animals can die before skin lesions develop. The rash-like skin lesions can sometimes lead to secondary infection at any stage of infection, causing fever and sepsis, with a mortality rate of 50-80%. Benign sheep pox, more common in adult sheep, involves milder skin lesions, sometimes confined to the ears or tail, and a mortality rate of approximately 5-10%.

Goat pox prevention: Currently, all goats in Taiwan are required to be vaccinated to control goat pox. Goat pox is currently a Class A zoonotic infectious disease. Suspected cases must be reported to local epidemic prevention authorities, who will conduct an inspection and collect samples for testing. Animals must be prevented from moving. Any animals found to be infected with goat pox must be culled to prevent the spread of the disease. After clearing the infected area, thoroughly disinfect it with an effective disinfectant such as sodium hypochlorite. Animals in surrounding pastures must undergo further sampling, testing, and monitoring.

Caprine Arthritis Encephalitis: Caprine arthritis encephalitis (CAE) is a viral disease that occurs commonly in advanced goat-raising countries worldwide. Surveys show an average positive rate of over 75% in Taiwan, indicating widespread transmission of the disease. CAE is primarily transmitted through lambs consuming colostrum or milk from infected ewes. The risk of transmission is particularly high when colostrum or milk from multiple ewes is mixed and fed to lambs. Horizontal transmission of CAE between flocks can also occur. Mixing CAE antibody-negative and -positive sheep can easily become infected through direct contact, feed troughs, drinking water, or through contaminated needles used in continuous injections. The virus has also been confirmed to be present in the semen and reproductive tissues of naturally infected CAE rams, making it possible for CAE to be transmitted to ewes through mating. Furthermore, studies have shown that the CAE virus is present in the ovaries, uterus, and fallopian tubes of goats, suggesting the disease can be vertically transmitted to embryos or lambs, or through embryo transfer.

Symptoms of Caprine Arthritis and Encephalitis: Approximately 20% of sheep infected with CAE virus will display clinical symptoms during their lifetime. The most common is polysynovitis-arthritis, which occurs primarily in adults but can also occur in sheep as young as 6 months of age. Once clinical synovitis-arthritis develops, joint swelling and lameness occur, with the wrist joint being the most common site of infection. The lesions may also be latent and asymptomatic. Encephalomyelitis can occur in lambs aged 2-4 months, but can also occur in older lambs or adults. Initial symptoms in infected lambs include weakness, ataxia, and hind limb weakness. Hyperreflexia or dyspnea are also common in CAE-infected lambs. Over time, symptoms may progress to paraparesis, tetraparesis, and paralysis. Other neurological symptoms include depression, head tilt, cyclotorsion, opisthotonos, torticollis, and water-skiing movements. Interstitial pneumonia associated with CAE infection is more common in adult sheep and less common in lambs. Ewes may experience a "hard udder" syndrome, characterized by a firm, swollen udder and an inability to produce milk after parturition. Sometimes, the udder tissue is softer and the milk is normal, but milk production is low in ewes with persistent CAE mastitis.

Prevention of Caprine Arthritis and Encephalitis: There is currently no effective treatment for CAE virus infection , but supportive care may be helpful for individual animals. Several measures can improve symptoms of polybursitis - arthritis in sheep , including regular hoof trimming, thicker bedding , and the use of nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs. Sheep with encephalomyelitis require several weeks of intensive care , while bacterial infections secondary to interstitial pneumonia or sclerosing mastitis can be controlled with antibiotics. Providing a high-quality, easily digestible diet can delay the onset of the wasting symptoms of the disease. Establishing a CAE- free farm is currently the only way to prevent the disease. Since the disease is primarily transmitted from ewes to lambs through lactation, strict isolation of postpartum lambs to prevent them from consuming colostrum and milk from infected ewes, thus cutting off the transmission route, combined with culling of infected lambs, can effectively reduce the prevalence of CAE on a farm, and even eliminate the disease.

Precautions for goat arthritis encephalitis prevention: The following four points should be noted when planning the prevention: (1) Serological monitoring: Serological examinations should be carried out on all sheep in the flock, and the serum-positive and serum-negative sheep should be immediately separated; if the proportion of serum antibody-positive sheep in the flock is not high, all positive sheep can be considered for elimination. Serological examinations are usually repeated every 6 months to screen out antibody-positive sheep. (2) Reduce internal infection: Because CAE is still horizontally contagious, some studies have shown that if negative sheep are in close contact with positive sheep, they will have a higher rate of turning positive after several months to years. Therefore, sheep with clinical symptoms should be eliminated immediately regardless of the situation. (3) Block the transmission route of CAE: If the proportion of antibody-positive sheep in the flock is too high after serological examination, it is necessary to squeeze out as much colostrum as possible from ewes that have two negative serological examinations during the birthing season, freeze them in small packages and store them for future use. When an antibody-positive ewe gives birth , the lamb should be removed immediately to prevent it from consuming colostrum . Instead , the lamb should be fed thawed colostrum from a negative ewe. Thawing colostrum involves placing frozen colostrum in plastic bags , unsealed , in warm water at 37-40 °C. If colostrum from an antibody-negative ewe is insufficient , colostrum from an antibody - positive ewe can be expressed and sterilized at 56 °C for one hour before feeding to the lamb. (4) Improve the feeding environment and management measures: Lambs should be fed in a well-ventilated isolation house until they are weaned at 3 months of age . It is not easy for ewes to give birth , and there is also the trouble of giving birth at night. Therefore, estrus synchronization can be used for simultaneous breeding, and prostaglandins can be injected intramuscularly on the 145th day of pregnancy to induce delivery . The ewe will give birth within about 48-72 hours after the injection. After the ewe gives birth, the lambs should be taken away immediately for feeding separately. This should help to implement the isolation of ewes and lambs and effectively control the spread of CAE in the farm.

Contagious Pustular Dermatitis (Orf): This disease, caused by a parapoxvirus, typically occurs between April and September, causing pustules on the skin of sheep and wild ruminants. In severe cases, it can lead to death, causing significant economic losses to sheep farmers. Orf is a zoonotic disease. In humans, it is typically characterized by a solitary lesion on the hands, arms, or face. The lesion initially appears as a maculopapular rash or pustule, then progresses to a rapidly proliferating nodule with an umbilical central depression. The disease primarily affects goats and sheep, affecting all breeds and sexes, but is most susceptible in lambs. It often occurs in clusters, with an incidence rate exceeding 50%. Adult sheep can also develop Orf , but the incidence is lower , with sporadic cases and milder symptoms. Sick sheep and infected sheep are the main sources of infection of this disease . The virus can exist in the pustules and scabs of sick sheep , and is mainly transmitted through trauma to the skin and mucous membranes ; contaminated feed, drinking water or scabs of sick sheep remaining on the ground can spread the disease.

Symptoms of contagious purulent stomatitis in sheep: The incubation period is 4-7 days . Initially, scattered small red spots appear on the skin and mucous membranes of the lips . These quickly develop into sesame-sized nodules , which then become blisters or pustules . These rupture and form yellow or brown, warty scabs. The scabs gradually enlarge, thicken, and dry , shedding and healing after 1-2 weeks. In severe cases, the lesions may spread to the cheeks , developing papules, blisters, pustules, and scabs. Granulation tissue proliferates beneath the scabs , causing the lips to swell and protrude outward , forming mulberry-like protrusions . This severely limits the sheep's ability to feed , leading to gradual emaciation, weakness , and even death. The illness can last up to 2-4 weeks. The sheep's body temperature rises to 4142 °C , and they lose their appetite . In severe cases , their appetite is completely lost . The illness is prolonged , with a mortality rate of approximately 10% . This disease may cause pustular lesions in other areas of the lamb where there is less hair, such as the eyelids, inside the ears, on the ventral side of the tail base, in the udder, in the esophagus, and in the rumen. Therefore, it is important to distinguish it from other diseases such as sheep pox, bluetongue, foot-and-mouth disease, and peste des petits ruminants.

Prevention of Sheep Contact Suppurative Stomatitis: Mild cases require no treatment and resolve in approximately 1-2 weeks. Severe cases require systemic medications in addition to topical oral medications, primarily to reduce inflammation and prevent secondary infection. Because there is no vaccine for this disease in Taiwan, special attention is paid to preventing secondary bacterial infection of the wound. This can be achieved by applying or spraying 1% betadine solution or antibiotics. The wound will scab and heal after 4-5 days, and healing is generally good. The mortality rate in lambs is high, so special attention should be paid to preventing starvation and malnutrition caused by insufficient milk intake. Bottle feeding and intravenous glucose and other nutrients may be necessary if necessary. Vaccination is commonly used for prevention abroad. Antibodies develop approximately 2 weeks after vaccination, and the effective antibodies last for at least 6 months. However, no vaccine is currently available in Taiwan. Other preventative measures include reducing transportation stress, frequently isolating and observing animals before adding them to the flock, isolating and treating affected sheep during outbreaks, and feeding healthy sheep first before treating or feeding affected ones. On-site staff should also be especially vigilant and wear gloves when handling affected animals. Since the disease is zoonotic, gloves and other materials that come into contact with affected areas must be cremated. Also, be careful not to consume milk from sheep with udder or nipple lesions to avoid infection.

Scrapie (Spongiform Encephalopathy): Scrapie was first discovered in sheep in England in 1730 and was prevalent in Central Europe at the time. Affected sheep experience itching and may rub against walls, tree trunks, or fences. Affected sheep can exhibit unsteady gait, tremors, blindness, falls, and ultimately death. The causative agent of scrapie is a protein-only particle containing no nucleic acid, called a prion. Prions that infect cattle were later found to cause variant Creutzfeldt-Jakob disease (vCJD) in humans and bovine spongiform encephalopathy (BSE) in cattle. It is currently known that this denatured prion protein causes the normal prion protein in animal neural tissue to change its structure, becoming abnormal and pathogenic. It gradually accumulates within the nervous tissue, causing nerve cell degeneration and necrosis. Because nerve cells cannot regenerate after death, microscopic examination of brain tissue sections reveals cavitation and a sponge-like pathological change. Clinically, animals present with a chronic, progressive, and fatal disease. Radiation, ionization, ultraviolet light, and microwaves are ineffective in killing prions. Even after dry-heat treatment at 360°C for one hour, a small portion of prions remains infectious.

Symptoms of scrapie in sheep: This disease has a long incubation period, with natural infection lasting approximately 1-4 years. Clinical symptoms typically appear in sheep between 2 and 5 years of age. In the early stages of infection, sheep will repeatedly scratch against rocks, trees, or fences, leading to hair loss and restlessness, especially when exposed to sudden noise or movement. Some infected sheep will raise their heads or make gnawing motions if their backs are scratched. Clinical symptoms include tremors in the head and neck muscles, ataxia, staring, and dry, brittle wool. In the later stages of infection, affected sheep experience paralysis, particularly in their hind legs, eventually becoming unable to stand and becoming emaciated. Death typically occurs within 6 months of the onset of symptoms.

Prevention of scrapie in sheep: Currently, there is no vaccine or effective medication for scrapie in sheep. Therefore, countries around the world have implemented a number of preventative measures to prevent and control the disease, including enhanced disease surveillance, culling of infected animals, and prohibiting the use of high-risk animals as feed ingredients. Prions are quite resistant to traditional disinfection methods . High-pressure steam sterilization (steam autoclave) at temperatures above 132°C for at least one hour can effectively eliminate the pathogenicity of prions. Alternatively, disinfectants such as 5% sodium hypochlorite solution or 2.5N NaOH solution can partially kill prions. On the preventative side, the European Union passed legislation banning the use of animal feed products for herbivores starting January 1, 2001, to prevent animals from contracting spongiform encephalopathy and subsequently entering the human food chain, causing harm. In 1997, both the United States and Taiwan enacted legislation prohibiting the import of animals from epidemic-affected areas (including cattle and sheep) and their products (meat and bone meal, meat meal, bone meal, poultry meal, blood meal, animal feed fats, animal feed oil residue, cattle and sheep embryos, serum, etc.). The import of beef, beef products, beef offal, and other bovine raw materials for human consumption from epidemic-affected areas that fail risk assessments is also prohibited, and the re-export of products from epidemic-affected areas through non-epidemic areas is prevented.

Caseous lymphadenitis is a common disease in goats. It involves one to several abscesses of varying sizes in or near the surface lymph nodes of the goat. This disease can cause cosmetic and health problems for the goat and can lead to significant economic losses for the ranch. This disease, also known as pseudotuberculosis, is mostly caused by the Gram-positive bacillus Corynebacterium rubrum tussotuberculosis. This facultative anaerobic bacterium can survive in the environment for months. It can invade through epidermal wounds and be carried to the lymph nodes by phagocytes or spread through the bloodstream to the systemic lymphatic system, causing abscesses to form in or on the surface of the goat's lymph nodes. Most livestock owners, once the lesions mature, use a scalpel to incise the swelling to drain the abscesses. While this may reduce the swelling, it often leads to the development of new abscesses elsewhere. The disease can be transmitted through ingestion of food contaminated with Corynebacterium, traumatic infection, bites of blood-sucking insects, or transmission through external parasites.

Symptoms of caseous lymphadenitis: This disease is common in the lymph nodes or their vicinity near the ears, neck, front of the shoulder, flanks, and hind legs. In addition to the abscess lesions visible on the body surface, this disease often invades the lymph nodes of the lungs and mesentery and other internal organs. Although the abscess can be seen to be 3-5 cm in size, the sheep do not show obvious systemic symptoms such as fever or decreased appetite. Clinically, deep abscesses in the neck can sometimes be seen compressing and hindering the sick sheep's feeding and rumination. If the abscess lumps appear on the face, causing facial nerve paralysis, or if the salivary glands are compressed, the sheep will experience frequent drooling. Severe pulmonary and mediastinal lymph node abscesses may cause sheep to suffer from chronic coughing and abdominal breathing. According to survey reports, the mortality rate of adult sheep can reach 20%. Sheep with visceral disease will experience growth stagnation and gradually lose weight. The visceral type is also called pseudotuberculosis.

Prevention of Caseous Lymphadenitis: Lymph node abscesses caused by this disease are treated surgically by incision, removing as much pus as possible , and irrigating with betadine solution (0.5% available iodine) 3-5 times until the iodine solution returns to its original color. This treatment may require continuous treatment for 2-3 days. Deep neck abscesses are sometimes located in close proximity to major blood vessels, the trachea, and the esophagus. Therefore, for safety reasons, it is best to have a veterinarian sedate or anesthetize the sheep before surgical removal. For more effective treatment of lymph node abscesses, in addition to surgical treatment, intramuscular injections of penicillin or cephalosporin are also necessary for 3-5 days to prevent recurrence. Because sheep are active and like to rub against railings, their skin becomes rough and cracked, leading to trauma. The disease is primarily transmitted through wounds. Therefore, sharp objects must be removed from the sheep pen, old, rusted, and cracked metal railings must be replaced, and the sheep pen must be kept clean and hygienic. A regular disinfection program must be implemented to reduce the spread of the disease. General disinfectants or hot water with a temperature above 60°C can kill this bacteria.

Tuberculosis: This disease is a major zoonosis caused by infection with Mycobacterium spp. bacteria. The primary pathogen is Mycobacterium bovis, but it can also be caused by M. tuberculosis, M. avium, and other so-called atypical mycobacteria. In Taiwan, tuberculosis in dairy sheep is a notifiable livestock disease, and a "test and cull" policy is implemented. The test method is the intradermal tuberculin test (ITT). Following intradermal injection, the test is performed by inspection and palpation 72 hours after injection (within the first and last 6 hours). Any swelling at the injection site is considered positive. The primary route of infection is the respiratory tract (droplet infection), followed by the digestive tract through contaminated milk, feces, urine, feed, and drinking water.

Symptoms of Tuberculosis: Although severely infected sheep may only exhibit symptoms such as coughing, swollen lymph nodes, loss of appetite, diarrhea, emaciation, and decreased milk production, due to current regular inspection and culling policies, infected animals are generally culled when they are mildly ill and have no clinical symptoms. Symptoms of tuberculosis in sheep vary depending on the location and number of tuberculous nodules. Lung infections may present with pneumonia-like symptoms such as a persistent dry cough and difficulty breathing, especially with temperature fluctuations or tracheal compression. Digestive tract infections may cause intermittent diarrhea or constipation. Uterine infections may produce purulent, cheesy, and bloody discharge. Central nervous system infections may cause partial paralysis, hyperesthesia, and motor disorders such as cyclotorsion. Uterine infections may result in nodular, localized, or diffuse swelling, as well as enlarged upper mammary lymph nodes. Nursing lambs can become infected within hours or days of birth through suckling. The basic lesion of this disease is a granuloma (tuberculosis nodule), ranging in size from a small spot to several tens of centimeters in diameter. The center is yellowish-white or white, with some having a cheesy or cottage cheese-like texture. Occasionally, it may feel calcified when cut. It is often surrounded by a yellowish-white connective tissue capsule. As the nodule grows, it often ruptures, releasing a cheesy fluid.

Tuberculosis Prevention: Tuberculosis is a zoonotic disease. According to Article 13 of the Regulations on the Prevention and Control of Infectious Diseases in Animals, sheep over three months of age on farms must undergo annual testing. Sheep that test positive for tuberculosis are incurable and must be immediately isolated, milking prohibited, and culled as soon as possible in accordance with regulations. Compensation of up to three-fifths of the assessed value of the animals will be paid (this culling compensation will be handled by the Bureau of Animal and Plant Health Inspection and Quarantine and the respective counties and cities in accordance with existing regulations). All contaminated pastures with tuberculosis-positive cases should undergo enhanced testing, with retests every three months. Regular testing can resume after three consecutive retests and when all sheep are negative. Because tuberculosis bacteria are more resilient than other microorganisms, they can survive for over three months in grazing pastures, 160 days in sheep pens, and 160-293 days in untreated manure. It is recommended that sheep farms where tuberculosis has occurred, in addition to routine tuberculin testing and culling of positive infected sheep, should wash the sheep houses and utensils with clean water several times, rinse them with hot soapy water once, and then disinfect them with an effective disinfectant.

Pregnancy toxemia: Pregnancy toxemia is a metabolic disease that occurs in ewes during late pregnancy. Each fetus requires approximately 30 to 40 grams of glucose per day for metabolism. Rapid growth requires a high energy supply. If the ewe's supply is insufficient, the body will rely on fat metabolism to generate energy for the lambs in utero, resulting in insufficient energy. Research data shows that elderly, obese, and multi-fetal ewes are more susceptible to pregnancy toxemia. This energy conversion and metabolism occurs in the ewe's liver, resulting in hepatic lipidosis or fatty liver, ultimately leading to liver dysfunction. Excessive fat metabolism also produces highly toxic products, such as ketone bodies, which are released into the bloodstream and throughout the body.

Symptoms of pregnancy toxemia: Pregnancy toxemia typically occurs one to three weeks before delivery and can result in the death of the ewe. Symptoms include mild to severe loss of appetite, depression, lethargy, slowed movements, and ataxia, often leading to difficulty standing and consequently lying down. Affected sheep may also exhibit neurological symptoms, such as teeth grinding or vision loss. Affected sheep may become comatose within hours to two days of the onset of these symptoms, ultimately dying from impaired liver and kidney function. Of course, the fetus will also die if the ewe dies. Furthermore, if the fetus dies first, it can lead to sepsis in the ewe if not removed urgently.

Prevention of Pregnancy Toxemia: Effective treatment of pregnancy toxemia requires early diagnosis and treatment at the earliest stages. Medications include propylene glycol (60-90 ml) administered orally 2-3 times daily. Oral electrolytes or intravenous Ringer's lactate can also be helpful. If the ewe has already reached the lethargic stage , treatment is often expensive, uneconomical, and has a poor prognosis.

Precautions for Pregnancy Toxemia Prevention: For prevention, special attention should be paid to the husbandry and management of ewes in late pregnancy. Important tasks include: 1. Drying off. Pregnant ewes should have a 40-60 day dry period to allow the body weight lost during lactation to gradually increase and the udders to rest adequately in preparation for the next lactation. During the dry period, an antibiotic ointment used for dry cows can be applied at the same dosage as for cows, instilled into both stalls. This dry period treatment is crucial, even if the udders are healthy in late lactation, as many new udder infections occur in the first few weeks of the dry period. Furthermore, if a multiparous flock has a high incidence of mastitis after parturition, it is recommended that the ewes be given appropriate teat dips during the first one or two weeks of the dry period, even if they are not milking during this period, until milk production ceases and the udders begin to shrink. 2. During the last six weeks of pregnancy, the growth and development of lambs in utero increases significantly compared to the early stages. During this period, the ewe's dry matter intake decreases slightly, exacerbating energy depletion. Fat tissue begins to metabolize in response to the energy deficit, leading to pregnancy toxemia. To prevent metabolic diseases, especially pregnancy toxemia, during this period, it is crucial to provide the ewe with high-quality forage that meets her nutritional needs during the early dry period to ensure proper rumen function. Two to three weeks before farrowing, the ewe's metabolic needs change dramatically. Therefore, in addition to high-quality forage, it is essential to provide her with more energy in the form of concentrates, such as corn. It is also important to note that female lambs still require a higher-nutrient and energy-rich diet to maintain growth during the final stages of pregnancy. Female lambs should not be housed in the same pen as dry ewes to prevent aggression, which could harm the female lambs' health and lead to miscarriage.

Mastitis: Mastitis is defined as an inflammatory reaction of the mammary gland, typically resulting in characteristic changes in the properties, composition, and bacteriological characteristics of the milk secreted. Once mastitis occurs, prompt diagnosis and treatment are essential to prevent severe and irreversible tissue damage and loss of milk production. Pathogenic bacteria are the most common cause of mastitis in goats. These pathogens initially colonize the streak canal before entering the mammary gland, causing infection. Improper milking technique and poor hygiene increase the risk of infection.

Symptoms of Mastitis: The most important pathogen on pastures is Staphylococcus aureus, which causes symptoms ranging from acute to subclinical. In severe cases, the udder may become gangrenous, with a bluish-purple appearance and no warmth to the touch. The milk may become watery and dark red due to the presence of blood. Other less commonly isolated bacteria include Streptococcus spp., Escherichia coli, and Mycoplasma spp. The systemic symptoms of acute mastitis caused by E. coli vary greatly in severity , ranging from mild cases with only localized inflammation of the mammary tissue to severe cases with significant systemic symptoms, including rumen hypomotility, dehydration, shock, and even death. The infected udder will have a watery, serous discharge, and the body temperature may reach 40°C or higher. Within a few hours to a day of infection, the infected udder will become edematous, swollen, and sensitive to the touch, and a serum-like fluid will be secreted.

Mastitis Prevention: The most effective way to control Staphylococcus aureus mastitis is to strictly follow correct milking procedures and maintain good milking hygiene. In particular, teat dipping ewes with an effective antiseptic immediately after milking reduces or eliminates pathogen transmission during milking, minimizes pathogen contamination of the ewe's teat ducts, and prevents new infections. Additionally, certain nutrients, such as dietary supplementation with appropriate amounts of vitamin E, selenium, vitamin A, and beta-carotene, can enhance an animal's resistance to mastitis. Treatment of clinical Staphylococcus aureus mastitis is often ineffective, with frequent recurrences and a high risk of infection in healthy sheep. Milk sampling and microbial culture can be performed on individual pens throughout the herd to identify subclinical mastitis, which is infection but not yet clinically evident. These cases can be treated with an effective antibiotic ointment instilled into the udder. Most E. coli infections require specialized veterinary treatment, including antibiotics, intravenous hypertonic saline, antiprostaglandins, steroids, antihistamines, and calcium therapy. Important steps in treating E. coli infections include frequent milking of infected sheep to remove white blood cells, bacteria, and toxins from the mammary glands. Regular milking every few hours is particularly effective. Injecting oxytocin at the end of milking can also help eliminate residual milk containing toxins. Good environmental and milking hygiene can effectively control E. coli mastitis. Teats must be cleaned and dried before milking. Pre-milking dips can prevent environmental mastitis caused by Streptococci and E. coli. Pre-milking dips require sufficient time for the disinfectant to kill bacteria, typically about 20-30 seconds. Afterward, wipe the solution off with a clean paper towel to prevent it from entering the milking equipment and remaining in the milk. Sometimes, teats are dipped before milking without being cleaned. This often prevents the disinfectant from reaching the bacteria due to feces and dirt on the teats, thus failing to reduce mastitis and somatic cell counts, resulting in reduced milk quality. In addition, the vacuum low pressure of the milking machine should not fluctuate too much, and the milk cup rubber must be replaced regularly to prevent E. coli from invading the breast.

Q Fever: Q fever is a zoonotic disease caused by the bacterium Coxiella burnetii. It has become a major cause of abortions, particularly in sheep farms, resulting in significant economic losses. Coxiella burnetii can be transmitted to a wide range of domestic and wild animals, including horses, dogs, rabbits, deer, and even reptiles, birds, and arthropods, through the air, contact with infected animal feces, and the bites of various tick species. Sheep, cattle, and goats are the primary sources of Q fever in humans.

Q fever: In animals, Q fever infection typically presents with no clinical symptoms or nonspecific symptoms such as fever, anorexia, and shortness of breath . However, pneumonia, conjunctivitis, hepatitis, and reproductive failure may also occur. During late pregnancy , Q fever can reactivate, resulting in a latent infection , resulting in high concentrations of the pathogen in the amniotic fluid and placenta. The main symptoms of Q fever in sheep are stillbirth, premature birth, weak fetuses, and placental infection . While aborted fetuses may appear normal , aborted placentas may show intercotyledonary fibrosis and turbid exudate . Severe inflammation may occur in the myometrium and adjacent stroma . In cattle, metritis and recurrent infertility are the main symptoms. Q fever infection in sheep may occasionally present with bronchopneumonia and pink eye , but these symptoms are generally mild or absent. The pathogen has a high affinity for the udder and placenta, and after reactivation in the placenta, it rapidly multiplies, easily causing miscarriage or stillbirth in infected ewes. The placenta or amniotic fluid of these pregnant animals becomes a significant source of infection. Infected animals can transmit the pathogen through urine, feces, milk, or birth materials (particularly the placenta). Animals infected with Q fever can excrete the bacteria through multiple pathways. Reports indicate that sheep primarily shed the bacteria through the reproductive tract and feces, while cattle primarily shed the bacteria through milk and the reproductive tract. Due to the high tolerance of Q fever bacteria in the environment, continuous excretion, latent reinfection during pregnancy, and the lack of clinical symptoms following infection, the pathogen persists and is difficult to eliminate.

Q fever prevention: This disease is a zoonosis. To minimize contamination of animals and the environment, special precautions must be taken during on-site prevention and control: 1. Exercise extreme caution when importing new animals to farms not infected with Q fever. Farms should regularly monitor animals for antibody testing. Farms with positive serological results should only export animals to Q fever-positive farms. 2. During the farrowing and late pregnancy periods, when the disease is most prevalent, it is best to separate pregnant ewes from the farrowing area. 3. After farrowing or abortion, fetal and placental tissue must be removed by farm personnel wearing protective clothing such as gloves, masks, and respirators. These products must be promptly buried or incinerated to prevent contact and ingestion by domestic animals or wild animals, which could cause contamination. 4. In addition to the reproductive tract, this pathogen can be excreted through feces. Therefore, proper composting and bedding treatment is crucial. Lime or 0.4% calcium cyanamide should be mixed in and covered. 5. For environmental disinfection, 1:100 diluted bleach (containing 5.25% effective hypochlorite), 5% H2O2, or 1:100 diluted Lysol (a mixture of saponified alkyl aromatic carbolic acid) can be used for disinfection. However, alcohol, 1% carbolic acid, 1% formalin, and quaternary amine compounds are not effective against Q fever pathogens.

Chlamydiosis: Chlamydia is an infection caused by an intracellular, Gram-negative bacterium and a significant zoonotic disease in pastures. Currently, 11 species of Chlamydophila have been classified, with Chlamydophila pecorum and C. abortus ( Chlamydophila abortus ) being common in cattle and sheep. Serological surveys in various countries have shown antibody prevalence rates ranging from 45% to 100%, indicating that Chlamydophila is now widespread in pastures and has become an enzootic pathogen. Chlamydophila can cause abortions and carry high levels of the pathogen in fetuses and placentas. Chlamydophila can also be spread through other secretions (such as vaginal, ocular, and nasal secretions, semen, urine, etc.) and feces, contaminating the environment. Other animals may ingest or inhale the pathogen, potentially causing infection. Furthermore, infected bulls can transmit the pathogen to cows during natural mating.

Symptoms of Chlamydia: Infected sheep often show no clinical symptoms, but may experience reproductive problems such as miscarriage, endometritis, vaginitis, and repeated infertility. A few may develop other organ problems, including pneumonia, arthritis, enteritis, hepatitis, encephalomyelitis, keratoconjunctivitis, and mastitis. Recent studies have also shown that in addition to causing these reproductive problems, chlamydia may also affect milk production, shorten the lifespan, cause premature delivery, reduce the survival rate of lambs, and affect the growth and development of lambs. Explosive abortion in ewes, also known as endemic abortion in sheep, specifically refers to a large-scale abortion outbreak in late pregnancy caused by Chlamydia abortus (C. abortus).

Chlamydia prevention: Some literature recommends administering long-acting oxytetracycline subcutaneously twice weekly to pregnant ewes until the last month of pregnancy to prevent miscarriage. However, it is important to note that while medication can slow the onset of miscarriage, it may not completely eliminate the bacteria or reduce the likelihood of bacterial excretion. Additionally, farmhands can reduce the spread of the pathogen by isolating aborted animals and removing aborted fetuses and placentas from the barn to prevent ingestion and infection by other animals. Chlamydia is resistant to dry cold but unstable to heat, becoming inactive after 10 minutes at 60°C. Maintaining clean barns, sheep beds, walkways, and animal surfaces is crucial for farms free of chlamydia infection. Reducing the number of sheep purchased from outside and providing separate farrowing pens are also important factors in reducing chlamydia infection. After ewes give birth, the farrowing pens should be disinfected with a disinfectant such as a quaternary amine disinfectant or 1% bleach to prevent the spread of the pathogen.

Lamb pneumonia: The greatest economic loss in sheep farming is low lambing rates. This problem is caused not only by common ewe abortion but also by respiratory diseases (pneumonia). Suckling lambs often suffer from primary or secondary respiratory infections, either viral or bacterial. This is because the respiratory mucosa of young animals has greater vascular permeability, the peripharyngeal lymph nodes are underdeveloped, the bronchial glands produce less mucus, and the ciliated epithelium is less motile, resulting in weaker foreign body clearance and barrier defenses. Inadequate first milk intake, incomplete immunization, overcrowding, improper husbandry, sudden changes in weather, and cold weather can all weaken the young animal's physiological defenses, leading to the proliferation of invading respiratory pathogens and the secretion of toxins that damage respiratory tissue, causing symptoms. Pneumonia in lambs can be caused by pathogens including viruses, mycoplasmas, and bacteria . Common bacteria include Escherichia coli ( E. coli) and Pasteurella spp. E. coli is a common environmental bacterium that can be isolated from the feces of most warm-blooded animals . However, its pathogenicity varies depending on its virulence factors . In adult lambs , E. coli infection primarily causes toxemia , while in lambs, it often causes enteritis, septicemia, or toxemia . Factors such as overcrowding, insufficient colostrum intake, and a dirty environment increase the risk of oral infection with E. coli , leading to diarrhea. Affected lambs suffer from diarrhea, dehydration, and ultimately death from shock. Septicemic infections are relatively common in lambs . The pathogen may enter the bloodstream through the intestinal tract, respiratory tract, or umbilical cord. Due to the effects of endotoxins , the disease progresses rapidly and has a high mortality rate. Lambs that survive may later develop arthritis. If newborn lambs are infected with E. coli, sometimes the sick lambs do not develop diarrhea but suffer from severe pneumonia. It is speculated that the pathogen is a respiratory tract infection. This may be because the breeding environment contains a large number of pathogens, coupled with factors such as close feeding and poor ventilation. The lambs have been exposed to E. coli before eating colostrum, or the colostrum intake is insufficient. Intimate actions such as licking the lambs by ewes may also indirectly spread the pathogens, causing the lambs to inhale the pathogens, resulting in respiratory system infections, more serious pneumonia, and even fatal risks.

Symptoms of pneumonia in lambs: Respiratory symptoms in lambs and lambgirls include depression, loss of appetite, or even complete loss of appetite. Severe pneumonia can cause elevated fever, inability to stand, and coughing. Serous, mucous, or purulent discharge may be observed from the nostrils. Some lambs are unresponsive to treatment and gradually weaken and die. Those that survive tend to grow slowly and become emaciated.

Prevention of Lamb Pneumonia: Treatment is recommended with broad-spectrum antibiotics and nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs, plus a B-complex vitamin supplement, once daily for three to five days. However, the best preventive measure is improved husbandry and care. The first 24-48 hours after birth are crucial for lambs. The umbilical cord must be tied securely and soaked in a 7% iodine solution. Colostrum is the milk produced by the ewe between 18 hours after parturition. It contains essential immunoglobulins that protect the lamb against farm-specific diseases and provides nutrition and energy. Lambs require an average of 45cc of colostrum per kilogram of body weight, meaning each lamb should receive at least 120cc of colostrum as its first feeding. Lambs typically begin to suckle on their own after their first feeding of colostrum. However, if they are still unable to suckle, they should continue to receive milk via a nasogastric tube every two to three hours. Ideally, lambs should receive their mother's colostrum, but if the ewe is unable to produce milk, colostrum from a lamb that gave birth on the same day can also be fed. Therefore, freezing and storing the remaining colostrum for emergency use is a very important on-site feeding method.

Escherichia coli in lambs: Escherichia coli is a common environmental bacterium that can be isolated from the feces of most warm-blooded animals . Its pathogenicity varies depending on its virulence factors . In lambs, E. coli infection often causes enteritis, sepsis, or toxemia. Animals generally have good resistance to E. coli . After birth, lambs obtain effective antibodies from colostrum to combat invading pathogens. However, if the ewes themselves are not properly fed and managed, resulting in insufficient nutrition and immunity, coupled with other pressing on-site conditions, the ewes in late pregnancy may not be able to produce high-quality colostrum to provide adequate immune protection for the lambs . This can allow pathogens from the environment to enter , leading to serious outbreaks.

Symptoms of E. coli infection in lambs: Clinical signs of E. coli infection in lambs generally include depression, loss of appetite, or even complete insufficiency. Severe pneumonia can result in elevated fever, inability to stand, and coughing. Serous, mucous, or purulent discharge from the nostrils may be observed. Some lambs are unresponsive to treatment and gradually weaken and die. Those that survive experience slow growth and become emaciated. E. coli isolated from sheep can be divided into two groups: enteropathogenic and septicemic. Enteropathogenic E. coli is less invasive, resident in the small intestine (jejunum) and secreting enterotoxins that can cause diarrhea in lambs. E. coli may be responsible for approximately 25% of diarrheal illnesses in lambs, sometimes as high as 75%, with a mortality rate ranging from 10% to 50%. Septicemic E. coli is highly invasive, capable of penetrating the small intestinal wall and entering other organs and tissues. Lambs infected with septicemic E. coli typically die within three to seven days of birth. Clinically, they present with severe depression and tachycardia, but no other symptoms, such as diarrhea. The bacteria circulate through the bloodstream to the brain and joints, causing meningoencephalitis. Lambs may experience recumbency, opisthotonus, colic, paddling, convulsions, nystagmus, and hypopyoncia and panophthalmitis. Surviving lambs may also exhibit arthritis, swollen joints, and lameness.

Prevention of E. coli in lambs: Treatment is recommended with broad-spectrum antibiotics and nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs, along with a vitamin B complex. However, the best approach is to improve feeding and management, disinfect the lamb's umbilicus after birth, and provide high-quality colostrum. For prevention, special attention must be paid to the feeding and management of newborn lambs. Generally, after colostrum feeding, each lamb needs to drink 20% of its body weight in milk replacer daily for normal growth. This means that each lamb weighing approximately 4 kg needs 800cc of milk replacer daily. If fed four times daily, each meal should contain at least 200cc. If the lamb is too weak to drink colostrum, colostrum must be administered via gavage tube. Lambs that are weak or underweight at birth should only be given 10% of their body weight in colostrum daily, divided into four gavages. Other precautions include reducing chlamydia and Q fever infections in ewes, reducing the infection rate of mastitis in ewes, reducing the positive rate of caprine arthritis encephalitis (CAE) in the farm, and giving ewes vitamin E and selenium before delivery to improve immunity.

Coccidiosis: Coccidiosis is a common cause of diarrhea in lambs. Coccidia often cause an inapparent infection in animals, resulting in no clinical symptoms. When coccidiosis occurs and diarrhea develops in animals, it is often due to improper management, excessive sources of infection, and stressful conditions, which weaken the lamb's immune system and lead to clinical symptoms. Coccidiosis in goats is caused by Eimeria spp., which primarily infest the goat's intestinal epithelium, damaging the intestinal mucosa. This damages the intestinal epithelium, impairing digestion and absorption, and reducing the utilization of food, water, and electrolytes.

Symptoms of coccidiosis infection: Clinical symptoms include weight loss, depression, loss of appetite, soft stools, black stools, bloody diarrhea, watery diarrhea, dehydration or other secondary diseases. The age of onset is generally 1-3 months old.

Prevention of Coccidiosis: When sheep experience diarrhea, the cause should be identified immediately and laboratory tests performed to determine if the cause is coccidiosis. If diarrhea in lambs is due to coccidiosis, appropriate parasite-directed medication should be administered. In addition, intramuscular injections of nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs can be used to alleviate diarrheal symptoms. Severely ill lambs may also require oral electrolytes or intravenous fluids to replenish electrolytes and fluids. Sick lambs should be isolated to prevent transmission of coccidiosis to healthy sheep. For prevention, special attention should be paid to the immediate feeding of colostrum to lambs born to maintain their immune system. Lambs should not be purchased from farms with diarrheal illnesses. New lambs should be isolated for a period of time before being mixed with other lambs. These are all effective measures to prevent stress and reduce the risk of parasites.

Bloat: After digesting and metabolizing the feed consumed, the rumen produces large amounts of gas. Similar to other ruminants, this gas is released through the mouth as belching. If other factors prevent the belching and gas from being expelled from the rumen, the rumen will begin to distend, resulting in a noticeable swelling and bulging of the flanks. There are several reasons for sudden bloating in sheep, and on-site personnel should be aware of them. Bloat can be a serious life-threatening condition, and in severe cases, can lead to death if not treated urgently. The most common cause of bloat in sheep is overeating. Bloat can also occur in sheep that are prone to rushing for food, or when feeding time has passed and they are overly hungry because they have not eaten. Once feeding begins, they tend to rush for food, resulting in overeating and bloating. Sometimes, bloat is more common in sheep that consume large amounts of grain at once. Rapid changes in sheep's diets can also cause bloat. A stable diet maintains a balanced microbial environment in the rumen . If this balance is disrupted, ruminal gas can accumulate rapidly, leading to bloat. Lambs are also more susceptible to bloat after switching from ewe's milk to lower-quality milk substitutes (pre-mixed milk powder), especially when these substitutes are based on soy-based protein. Some milk substitutes contain high levels of processed or unprocessed crude fat, which can also contribute to bloat. In these cases, the excess fat coats the digestive tract, preventing efficient absorption of nutrients and water. Some farms have a high incidence of bloat in lambs, with mortality rates as high as 25-30%. Recent studies have shown that deficiencies in potassium and magnesium in lamb diets can contribute to bloat, especially when grazing pastures high in alfalfa. Therefore, supplementing the diet with these trace elements can help prevent bloat. Bloat in lambs aged 2 to 4 weeks may also be related to the proliferation of Clostridium perfringins type A or Sarcina spp. in the stomach. The proliferation of these bacteria metabolizes sugars in milk, producing large amounts of gas and acid. These acids kill other bacteria, while the gas accumulates in the fourth stomach.

Symptoms of bloat: The left abdomen of affected sheep rapidly swells, and the abdominal wall becomes enlarged, tense, and resilient when pushed. Rumen movements, belching, and rumination decrease significantly. Soft stools may be observed in the early stages of bloat, but may cease to be present later. Furthermore, the sheep may become restless, kicking their lower abdomen, rolling around, and unusually excited. Sometimes, they may become depressed, standing up and lying down repeatedly, and eventually become listless and unsteady. Mild cases may gradually expel gas through the mouth and anus, returning to normal after about half a day to a day. Severe cases of bloat can cause respiratory distress and circulatory problems, and without immediate and effective treatment, can lead to death within hours.

Bloat Prevention: If a sheep with bloat is still able to stand and walk , it is recommended to orally administer 120-150 ml of mineral oil ( liquid paraffin ) to massage the ventral rumen and stimulate movement to facilitate the expulsion of ruminal gas through the esophagus. Alternatively , 20-25 grams (g) of baking soda in 100 ml of warm water can be administered orally. If a sheep with bloat is unable to stand , it is recommended to call a veterinarian for immediate treatment . Excessive rumen gas accumulation can compress the lungs and heart , leading to shock. Emergency rumen puncture with a rumen trocar can effectively release rumen gas . However , trocar puncture should only be performed after all other treatments have been completed and the sheep has not yet fully recovered . The puncture can also cause gas to be released too quickly, leading to cardiopulmonary failure, and serious infection at the puncture site, resulting in peritonitis, which is life-threatening. Furthermore, when sheep experience foamy bloat, trocar puncture may not effectively release the gas. In this case, an emergency rumenectomy is necessary to completely remove the rumen contents. Then, approximately 300-500 ml of rumen fluid from healthy sheep or cattle should be infused to restore the rumen microbial environment. Sheep diets should be maintained as stable as possible. If changes are necessary, they should be made gradually, otherwise digestive problems may occur. Because deficiencies in potassium and magnesium in sheep diets can cause bloat, adding these trace elements to the diet can help prevent it . Baking soda can be placed freely in the sheep barn. It effectively balances the rumen pH, maintaining a normal rumen microbial environment, promoting digestive function, and reducing the incidence of rumen bloat. Furthermore, for sheep farms where lambs are prone to bloat, adding penicillin-like antibiotics to milk can reduce the incidence of fourth stomach bloat. In addition, it is best to feed lambs small meals frequently and feed them milk substitutes in proportion to their body weight. The feeding time should also be fixed, and try not to change the feeding amount and time to avoid bloating.


Taiwan's mutton industry experienced a rapid surge in production over the past decade , but after a sharp drop in mutton prices, it has returned to a trend of declining production each year. In 2004 , 122,533 domestic mutton were slaughtered , comparable to the 121,881 in 1994. Domestic mutton self-sufficiency rates were similar, at 9.95% in 1994 and 8.50% in 2004. Comparing various data points with current data, the production and sales figures for domestic mutton in 1994 and 2004 suggest that these figures represent the optimal marginal returns for sheep farmers. Regarding the structure of the mutton industry, on the optimistic side, production, processing, and marketing have established a satisfactory structure. However, objectively speaking, the outlook for the mutton industry is constrained by policy constraints and the large-scale import of cheap mutton from New Zealand and Australia. Consequently, the industry can only maintain a stable operation, rather than pursuing ambitious goals. If the current state of Taiwan's mutton industry in 2004 and 2005 is defined as a benchmark platform capable of stable profits, it means that over the next 10 years, the direction of Taiwan's mutton industry should be to set goals and strictly implement planned production. That is, to achieve a domestic mutton self-sufficiency rate of between 9 and 10%, a stable lamb rearing rate, and strive for zero growth in the number of sheep raised. The transaction price can be controlled by quantity to balance supply and demand, so as to avoid a recurrence of historical events in which prices collapsed due to increased production.

In November 1988, the Taiwan Provincial Government's Department of Agriculture and Forestry assisted the Yunlin County Meat Market in establishing a sheep auction business. That year, 27,931 domestic sheep were slaughtered, weighing only 371 metric tons. That same year, Taiwan imported 12,375 metric tons of frozen (or chilled) mutton from Australia and other countries, resulting in a domestic mutton self-sufficiency rate of only 2.91%. In 2004, 122,533 domestic sheep were slaughtered, weighing 2,781 metric tons. Compared to 371 metric tons in 1988, the total weight of domestic sheep carcasses increased 6.5 times over the past 16 years. Imported frozen (or chilled) mutton also increased 1.4 times over the same period. Despite the significant increase in domestic mutton supply, in 2004, domestic mutton accounted for only 8.5% of the total mutton supply. The long-term dominance of imported mutton in the domestic market has directly suppressed the development of the domestic mutton industry.

From the perspective of mutton consumption, Taiwan's mutton consumption is estimated to remain between 31,000 and 35,000 metric tons in the next two to three years. The domestic mutton industry will maintain its growth rate, and the market transaction price of mutton may remain stable . However, if the mutton self-sufficiency rate continues to decline, it may lead to issues that are related to the lifeblood of the sheep farming industry, which is worthy of reflection by agricultural policy agencies and the sheep farming community.

The cost of raising domestic mutton is significantly higher than that of imported mutton, making it clear that domestic mutton cannot compete with imported mutton in terms of price. Furthermore, the domestic livestock industry has historically lacked a robust response mechanism, leading to rapid and widespread abandonment of livestock during emergencies, repeatedly causing pain in the industry. It is undeniable that the domestic mutton industry has long been underdeveloped in both its physical and organizational structure.

Before 1997, most mutton farmers raised mutton as a sideline. A sharp drop in domestic mutton prices in 1996, coupled with an outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease in pigs in March 1997, led to a dramatic drop in the number of mutton farmers by over 2,600 in less than two years, devastating the industry. The scale of breeding and the number of mutton sheep have declined annually since then. Comparing 1995 and 2002, mutton farms had nearly 320,000 head of sheep at the end of 1995, but by the end of 2002, only 161,858 remained, a decrease of approximately 50%. Fortunately, the dairy sheep industry provided support during this same period. Comparing the number of dairy sheep raised between 1994 and the end of 2002, the decrease was only 2.8%. Objectively speaking, in the development of Taiwan's sheep industry over the past decade, the dairy sheep industry has supported the mutton industry. This reflects the fact that, under the normal supply and marketing system of provincial goat milk transportation and marketing cooperatives, the mutton industry has been able to rely on itself, and the subsequent organization of production and marketing teams has ultimately helped to maintain its stability. By the end of 2000, the number of sheep-raising, production and marketing teams in various counties and cities had reached 56. Subsequently, sheep-raising associations at the county and city levels in Nantou County and Taichung County were established, and the organizational structure gradually became sound.

Currently, the Nubian is the dominant meat sheep breed in Taiwan, commonly known in the market as "long-eared." "Gaizai" refers to a cross between Nubians and Taiwanese goats. Nubians command auction prices ranging from NT$15 to NT$30 per kilogram higher than so-called dairy rams (such as Sanen, Albain, Toggenburg, and Lai Manjiao). Nubian goats were introduced in 1985 by the Hengchun Branch of the Council of Agriculture's Animal Products Research Institute, and selected from crosses between Nubians and Taiwanese goats at the Hualien Stock Breeding Farm. Nubians and their hybrids were introduced in 1991 and 1992, and have been a dominant breed for over a decade. They are not only a favorite of meat farmers, but also a prized commodity for sheep traders and mutton hot pot operators. The market's preference for Nubians and their hybrids is primarily due to their superior meat quality, high dressing and lean meat percentages. One-year-old Nubian castrated rams are a top choice for both the domestic mutton hot pot industry and mutton lovers. According to data from two surveys of goat breeders in Taiwan conducted by the Taiwan Sheep Breeding Association in 2000 and 2004, the Nubian herd led the way among purebred goats. In 2000, there were 44,178 purebred Nubian goats, and in 2004, there were 27,320. As for hybrid goats, there were 135,618 in 2000 and 57,561 in 2004, both of which were the highest among all breeds. This suggests that Taiwanese goat breeders are less interested in purebred goat breeding and are instead pursuing hybridization to improve meat quality, with a focus on weight gain.

Since the Yunlin County Meat Market began operating mutton auctions in late 1988 , it has established a comprehensive listing and trading system. The average annual weight of sheep auctioned in 1989 was only 32.65 kg, rising to 35.43 kg in 1990 and reaching 41.51 kg in 1992. Live weight analysis reveals the remarkable success of the imported Nubian goat breed. Between 1991 and 1995 , the Chianan Goat Milk Marketing Cooperative in Taiwan imported over 11,000 dairy goats from the United States, Australia, and New Zealand to expand its milk supply. This multi-pronged approach resulted in improved growth traits for Taiwan's black native goats, driven by hybrid vigor. From 1994 to 1998 , the Nubian goat market dominated Taiwan's mutton market, with the average annual weight of marketed goats ranging between 43 and 45 kg. By 2000, the average annual weight of marketed mutton sheep had surpassed 50 kilograms. This is thought to be related to a public hearing on "How Taiwan's Sheep Industry Can Emerge from the Shadow of WTO Accession" held by former legislator Huang Xiumeng and the Taiwan Sheep Farmers Association on May 25, 1998. The hearing took place against the backdrop of a sharp drop in mutton prices in 1996, leading to an estimated 2,000 sheep farmers abandoning their flocks. Furthermore, an outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease in pigs in March 1997 further posed operational challenges for the sheep industry. Furthermore, with no imported breeding sheep for over two years starting in 1997, the demand for breeding rams was acute. One of the conclusions of the public hearing was: "It is recommended that the Council of Agriculture, Executive Yuan, approve a trial import of 200 breeding rams." This was a timely improvement, providing timely improvements to both domestic dairy and mutton sheep populations and driving up the live weight of marketed mutton sheep. The annual increase in the average weight of live sheep marketed for auction demonstrates that domestic mutton farmers are making some effort in their management. On October 15, 2002, the Council of Agriculture, Executive Yuan, held a meeting regarding the "Boer Goat Registration and Approval." Boer goats were imported from Australia in February 1995 under a special project and bred by the Hengchun Branch of the Animal Products Research Institute. After seven and a half years, the breed was approved for registration and officially launched in March 2003. Currently, many sheep farms in Taiwan have introduced Boer goats to improve the Nubian breed. While the benefits are still uncertain, it is expected that the average annual weight of live sheep marketed for auction will increase by another 3 to 5 kilograms within the next one to two years .

Driven by the promising domestic goat milk market starting in 1992, the domestic mutton industry experienced significant growth. By 1996, the number of sheep farmers reached 8,160, and the number of dairy and mutton sheep raised exceeded 400,000 by the end of 1995. The number of sheep slaughtered increased from 121,881 in 1994 to 179,591 in 1995, a 47.3% increase. Domestic mutton self-sufficiency also soared from 9.95% in 1994 to 18.18% in 1995. This exponential increase in supply ultimately led to a sharp drop in mutton prices in 1996. That same year, the domestic mutton self-sufficiency rate reached a record high of 20.75%, and 222,060 sheep were slaughtered. 1996 marked a watershed year for the sheep farming industry. The number of sheep farmers plummeted by over 2,000 that year, and the average annual price of sheep auctioned at the Yunlin County Meat Market reached an all-time low of NT$98.22 per kilogram, a 52.39% drop from the 1992 average of NT$206.30 and a 26.04% drop from the 1995 average of NT$132.80. Domestic sheep farmers learned a lesson from the 1996 price drop, recognizing for the first time the importance of balancing production and demand. Since then, with the exception of a 2001 decline in sheep trade due to the fake goat milk scandal and an outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease, the industry has generally adjusted to a planned production model of decreasing production annually. This adjustment finally bore fruit in the second half of 2004, with a peak market price of NT$281 per kilogram reached in January 2005.

A robust mutton trading mechanism: Public auctions of mutton were first launched in Taiwan at the Yunlin County Meat Market in November 1988. Although only 3,595 head were traded in 1990, the transparency of the transaction, without the risk of default or bad debt, earned the approval of both sheep owners and sheep traders. By 1992, the number of auctioned heads had increased to 8,022. In the following years, stable prices provided convenience for sheep farmers and boosted confidence in supply and marketing. By 2004, the Yunlin County Meat Market had traded a staggering 33,971 head of sheep. The Fengshan District Meat Market added a mutton auction in 1991, attracting sheep from Kaohsiung, Pingtung, and Hualien-Taitung to its nearby auction area, contributing significantly to the stable development of the mutton industry. On November 27, 2000, the Changhua County Meat Market, following the Yunlin and Fengshan markets, also launched mutton auctions. The listing and trading of domestic mutton has seen positive changes since the Changhua County Meat Market joined the market. Simultaneously, based on market volatility theory, mutton auctions have gradually shifted toward Changhua and Yunlin, reflecting the fact that the main consumer base for domestic mutton lies north of the Chiayi-Nan region. In terms of the number of sheep auctioned, the Fengshan District Meat Market has seen a significant decline in its mutton auction business over the past five years, replaced by the Changhua County Meat Market. In 2004, the Fengshan District Meat Market auctioned only 3,918 head of mutton, while the Changhua County Meat Market auctioned 13,507. By August 2005, the gap had widened. Based on 2005 auction data, it is estimated that within the next one to two years, the mutton auction business between the Yunlin and Changhua County meat markets will gradually close. For most sheep farmers, the transparent and open information, healthy market competition, and robust trading platform of the mutton auction market, coupled with the fact that mutton auction proceeds can be received within three days, naturally contribute to their high willingness to continue raising sheep.

The industry is proactively looking inward: Over the past decade, the domestic sheep farming industry has weathered numerous shocks, and from these lessons, they have developed a crucial survival principle: it's better to rely on oneself than on others. On July 1, 1996, facing a sharp drop in sheep prices, domestic sheep farmers sought solutions from the then-Taiwan Provincial Government's Department of Agriculture and Forestry. One month and 22 days later, 542 sheep farmers nationwide organized the Republic of China Sheep Farmers Association, hoping to revitalize the industry through this organization. In response to the sharp drop in sheep auction prices in 1996 and 1997, the Chiayi City Sheep Farmers Cooperative invested over NT$70 million in 1998 to build the first domestically produced lamb butchering plant in an effort to reassure consumers of the superior quality of domestic lamb. Although this facility was unable to compete with lower-priced imported lamb, it clearly demonstrates the sheep farmers' determination to survive. On February 15 and 23, 2000, two outbreaks of foot-and-mouth disease in sheep occurred. To make matters worse , on February 21 and April 12, newspapers reported that "over 30% of flavored goat milk is mixed with cow's milk" and "30% of goat milk tablets are mixed with cow's milk." This series of events led to a 40% loss in the domestic goat milk market, and the average auction price of mutton in 2001 fell another 15.36% compared to 2000.

Faced with dramatic changes in the industry, the Sheep Farmers Association of the Republic of China, under the guidance of the Council of Agriculture, Executive Yuan, and with the assistance of industry, government, and academia, held the "First Seminar on the Certification and Issuance of the Goat Milk Label" on February 15, 2000, the same day that the foot-and-mouth disease outbreak occurred in sheep. Subsequently, after 12 seminars and with the general consensus of domestic goat milk processing companies, the goat milk label certification was launched on October 12, 2001. As of August 2005, 170 million goat milk label stickers had been issued, not only regaining the existing market, but also setting a new record for domestic goat milk sales and boosting the operating efficiency of the mutton industry.

Regarding the legal slaughter of domestic mutton, mutton roasters in Xihu Town, Changhua County, raised funds to build Taiwan's first dedicated electric slaughtering line within the Changhua County Meat Market. This professional slaughtering system ensures the hygiene and safety of domestic mutton carcasses. Between 1999 and 2000 , sanitary slaughtering of domestic mutton was implemented at the Yunlin County and Chiayi City meat markets, but the number of slaughtered sheep was small. In 2003 , the Central Livestock Association, in collaboration with the Council of Agriculture's Animal and Plant Health Inspection and Quarantine Bureau, advocated that mutton be sent to legal slaughterhouses for slaughter. That same year, only 5,484 domestic mutton were sanitary inspected . By April 2004 , Xiangmeng Livestock Company's first dedicated sheep slaughtering line was operational, and domestic mutton slaughter numbers soared, reaching a total of 17,258 in 2004. From January to July 2005 , 16,003 domestic mutton were sanitary inspected. It is worth noting that the proportion of mutton listed for auction that is slaughtered directly in the meat market is increasing.

Steady and planned production: In recent years, the Council of Agriculture, Executive Yuan, has actively supported the sheep farming industry, particularly through surveys of sheep farm basic data and production dynamics, as well as analyses of domestic mutton production costs and efficiency. These efforts provide valuable insights into the overall supply and demand of domestic mutton production, thereby stabilizing the industry's trajectory. Regarding domestic mutton production costs, statistical data provided by the Council of Agriculture allows mutton farmers to adjust their mid-year expenditure increases and decreases. Combined with the transparency of mutton auction market prices, mutton farmers can predict operating profits and losses, eliminating the need for blind guessing and avoiding the same fate of losing everything.
