A comparison of the estimated annual production targets for domestic mutton and raw goat milk production over the past two years with actual production data shows a strong correlation, demonstrating the importance of conducting a census of breeding dynamics within the industry and the necessity of adhering to planned production. In the past five or six years, the domestic production cost of mutton has remained roughly between 117 and 130 yuan per kilogram. Therefore, if the number of sheep produced and the number of sheep put on the market for slaughter can be further adjusted, attention can be paid to the fluctuations in the amount of imported mutton, and transparent operations can be carried out, the risk of industry instability will naturally be greatly reduced.

A recent survey of mutton costs conducted by the Council of Agriculture, Executive Yuan, reveals that sheep farmers are struggling to reduce costs, including fowl costs, feed costs, and even labor costs. As for mutton farmers' efficiency, the average number of pigs born per litter, the average number of weaned pigs per litter, and the birth-to-weaning success rate have largely remained consistent, with no significant improvement. From another perspective, this is precisely why the sheep farming industry struggles to expand and is prone to experiencing zero growth.

A key factor in determining the future prospects of Taiwan's mutton industry lies in its ability to adapt independently. By setting annual production targets through agricultural agencies and industry groups, and through consensus among mutton farmers, mutton traders, and mutton hotpot operators, standardized output and an open trading platform can create a balanced production and marketing channel year-round. This, in turn, suggests there's still room for growth in the domestic mutton industry. In particular, domestic sheep farming is often confined, resulting in minimal pollution and no risk of soil and water conservation. Furthermore, facing the regular price increases of bulk imported raw materials, Taiwan's mutton industry can respond with domestically produced agricultural and sideline products. With comprehensive supporting measures in place, this industry should be a worthy and highly competitive one, both for increased production and forage. Ultimately, domestic lamb, like domestic pork and chicken, must meet national domestic product hygiene standards from breeding, slaughtering, cutting to packaging and refrigeration. This is especially true in special channels where lamb hot pot is the primary sales target. A verification mechanism should be in place, coupled with legal and hygienic slaughtering, immediate meat quality testing, and even the establishment of a domestic lamb mitochondrial gene barcoding system. This aims to give Chinese people greater confidence in consuming safe, hygienic, and healthy domestic lamb, while also preparing for entry into the international lamb market.

Taiwan's mutton industry need not limit itself to supplying the domestic market. After more than a decade of introduction and improvement, Taiwan's Nubian breed, a dual-purpose dairy and meat breed, has incorporated superior genetics from American and Australian Nubian sheep, creating a superior stock adapted to tropical and subtropical conditions. Once foot-and-mouth disease is eradicated, it should stand out among international goat breed competition. Regarding the continued influx of cheap Australian and New Zealand lamb into Taiwan, domestic mutton farmers should remain calm and diligently prepare for market differentiation. After all, under a free trade economy and a free consumer consciousness, as more and more consumers choose to eat lamb, domestic lamb will have the opportunity to showcase its superior characteristics and allow consumers to prove that it is indeed one of the finest livestock products.

Dairy sheep breeds: Currently, the main breeds registered by the American Dairy Sheep Association (ADGA) include: Saanen , Alpine, Toggenburg, Nubian, LaMancha, and Oberhasli: six. Other advanced livestock-raising countries, such as the United Kingdom, Australia, and New Zealand, primarily rely on the first four, while France primarily relies on Albanian and Saanen. Taiwan used to import these breeds from the United States, New Zealand, and Australia, but the dairy sheep breeds currently raised by sheep farmers have gradually simplified to focus on Albanian and Saanen, with Albanian accounting for 70% of the total.

Albaine: Albaines are divided into five strains based on their breeding region: English, French, Swiss, Rock, and Australian. The English Albaine has a solid black coat with white stripes on the sides of the face, and white on the ischial tips of the tail and below the knees. The Swiss Albaine is now officially registered as the Oberhasli breed. The Rock Albaine was bred by Mrs. Rock, and very few remain. The French Albaine is currently the most common breed in both the United States and Taiwan. In recent years, a new breed, originally registered as the Melan, has been developed and registered in Australia. It is now available to some sheep farmers in Taiwan. The French Albaine is a medium to large breed, with ewes at least 76 cm tall and weighing over 57 kg; rams at least 81 cm tall and weighing over 77 kg. Both male and female sheep have beards, straight noses, erect ears, slightly protruding eyes, and may or may not have horns. They are alert and agile. Although docile with their owners, they are more aggressive toward other sheep than other dairy sheep breeds. French Albaines in the United States have no fixed coat color; they can be a mix of black, white, reddish-brown, gray, and brown, but they avoid the typical coat colors of Tuggenheim and Sanen. The American Albaine can be generally categorized into eight different color types: Cou Blanc, Cou Clair, Cou Noir, Pied, Sundgau, Chamoisee, Two-tone Chamoisee, and Broken Chamoisee. Five of the more common color types are as follows: Cou Blanc: white on the neck and shoulders, with black hindquarters; Cou Clair: brown on the neck, black on the hindquarters; Cou Noir: black on the forequarters, white on the hindquarters; Chamoisee: chestnut or brown throughout, with black on the head, neck, backline, and legs; Sundgau: black throughout, with white stripes along the sides of the face, and white below the knees and on both sides of the tail, like the English Albaine. However, in France, only a single brown coat with black trim (Chamoisee) remains. Originally from the French Alps, the Albaine is hardy, tolerant of long journeys, and well adapted to various climates. It is very tolerant of rough husbandry and has a high lambing rate. Ewes have a long lactation period, and their average milk production is second only to the Saanen internationally. In the United States, milk production has reached 1,800 kilograms in just 305 days a year. Due to the aforementioned advantages of the Albain, and the fact that its coat coloration is predominantly black and brown, fattened dairy rams command a higher per-kilogram price than the Saanen at Taiwan's mutton auctions. Consequently, they are highly sought after by sheep farmers and are gradually replacing the Saanen as the mainstream dairy sheep breed. For many years, the largest number of Albain sheep have been imported to Taiwan from the United States, with a small number also coming from Australia. In recent years , the Hengchun Branch of the Animal Husbandry Research Institute and private businesses have begun importing frozen semen from French brown Albain sheep, hoping to genetically improve milk production and composition through corrective breeding. 

The Saanen is a large breed of sheep originating from the Saanen Valley in Switzerland and the world's most populous dairy sheep breed. Its coat is white or creamy white. Its skin is pink or tan. Some sheep may have black spots around the udder, eyes, ears, and nose, but this is still acceptable for breed registration. For sheep raised outdoors in tropical regions, pigmented skin protects against UV rays and sunburn, so tan skin has become a selection criterion in the Australian Saanen breed. Saanen sheep have erect, slightly forward-tilted ears, a straight or slightly concave nose, horns, and often two wattles around their necks. They are docile and quieter than other dairy sheep breeds, rarely engaging in serious fighting, bullying, or jumping fences. Both male and female sheep may have tan hair. In temperate climates, Saanen sheep often have long hair on their backs and thighs, and males also have bangs. Long-wool sheep, imported into Taiwan, typically undergo varying degrees of molting to adapt to the subtropical climate. Adult ewes are at least 76 cm tall and weigh over 61 kg; rams are at least 81 cm tall and weigh over 85 kg. Purebred Saanen sheep may occasionally produce lambs with black or other coat colors, known in the United States as Sable. Some American sheep farmers are developing a new breed from this. Congenitally hornless Saanen sheep are common. When both male and female sheep are congenitally hornless, the offspring are likely to be hermaphroditic or intersex. These sheep have a small vulva but a prominent clitoris. The distance between the vulva and the anus is larger than that of normal ewes. The reproductive tract is underdeveloped, and testicular tissue is often present in the ovaries. Most are infertile. Therefore, when selecting breeding sheep, ewes may be allowed to be hornless, while rams that are congenitally horned are preferred. Saanen ewes have a high milk yield and a long lactation period, averaging approximately 1,000 kg of milk over 305 days. Annual production of 2,000 kg is common in temperate regions, making them the highest milk producers among dairy sheep breeds. The current world record for milk production is held by an Australian Saanen ewe named Osory Snowgoose, who produced a whopping 3,582 kg of milk in her second calving over 365 days. Consequently, Saanen sheep are known as the "Dutch cattle" of dairy sheep. The milk fat percentage is generally 3.5%, and the protein content is 3.0-3.2%. Despite their high milk yield, Saanen sheep have poor tolerance to heat and rough conditions, making them suitable for more intensive husbandry. Taiwan's Saanen sheep were originally imported from Japan, and in the past decade, some have also been imported from New Zealand and Australia, though the largest number of imports still comes from the United States.

The Toggenburg is native to Switzerland and is most numerous in the Obertg-genburg region, hence the name. The Toggenburg's coat color is fixed, ranging from light coffee to dark chocolate. However, there is a white stripe on each side of the face from the base of the ear to the tip of the nose. The ears, below the visor, and where the neck wattles are attached are white. There is an inverted triangular white patch on each side of the rump at the base of the tail. Creamy white is acceptable, but white is preferred. The Toggenburg has erect ears and hair of varying lengths, with some sheep having long hair on the back, lower abdomen, and thighs. Slightly shorter than other breeds, it is a medium-sized breed. Ewes are at least 66 cm tall and weigh over 55 kg; rams are at least 71 cm tall and weigh over 70 kg. The udder of the Toggenburg sheep is firmly attached to the body wall, resulting in a plump, spherical milk. Lactation is long, and some ewes can continue milking for up to two years after calving, even without re-breeding. Because they can adapt to a wide range of climates, their milk production is relatively unaffected by latitude and season. They produce an average of 700-900 kg of milk in 305 days, resulting in high-quality milk suitable for producing dry cheese.

The LaMncha is the only dairy sheep breed bred in the United States. Originally developed by crossbreeding short-eared Spanish Murciana females with Swiss dairy rams, it was not registered with the American Dairy Sheep Association until 1958. LaMnchas have a straight nose, may or may not have horns, and have short, fine fur of varying color. They are a medium to large breed, with ewes standing over 71 cm tall and weighing 59 kg; rams stand over 76 cm tall and weigh over 73 kg. Short ears are characteristic of this breed, and there are two types of ears: the gopher type , with external ears less than 2.5 cm long and devoid of cartilage. Most sheep lack external ears, forming only a wrinkle. The American Dairy Sheep Association requires LaMncha rams to have gopher ears for registration. The other type is the Elf type, with external ears approximately 5 cm long, no longer than 7.6 cm, and drooping ear tips. Cartilage is permitted. Elf ears are not permitted in breeding rams. The main reason for this is that these rams may carry the recessive gene for the normal ear shape from the Swiss breed, resulting in offspring with ears that do not meet the breed standard. The Lai Man Jiao is robust and resistant to rough handling, with an average milk fat content of 4%. Average milk production is slightly lower than that of Swiss dairy sheep, but it has good meat characteristics and can be used as a dairy and beef breed.

The Nubian, officially known as the Anglo-Nubian, is a cross between British dairy goats and tropical sheep breeds such as the Zaraibi from Nubia in North Africa and the Jamnapari from India, which are characterized by prominent noses and long, drooping ears. The Nubian's defining characteristic is its hooked nose, also known as the Roman face, inherited from tropical breeds, and its long, drooping ears, which extend approximately 2.5 cm beyond the nose tip when held close to the cheek. Nubians have no fixed coat color, but brown is the most common. The American Dairy Sheep Association accepts all Nubian colors for registration, but the UK and Australia do not allow white facial stripes or wattles, characteristic of Swiss breeds. Nubian sheep are tall, with short, smooth wool, and excellent reproductive performance. They have a higher litter rate than other dairy sheep breeds and tend to produce multiple births. Due to their tropical ancestry, their breeding season is over two months longer than that of other European breeds, resulting in superior meat quality compared to other dairy sheep breeds. Ewes are over 76 cm tall and weigh over 62 kg, while rams are over 81 cm tall and weigh over 100 kg, making them a large dairy and meat breed. Their tropical ancestry makes them well-suited to Taiwan's climate and, over the past 20 years, have made the greatest contribution to the improvement of Taiwanese meat sheep breeds. Nubian milk has a high fat content of 5.0%, but yields are lower than other European breeds, and the lactation period is shorter. Once pregnant, milk production decreases rapidly. However, due to its rich milk, some dairy farms use Nubian milk to adjust the fat content and density of their summer milk to meet the minimum milk collection standards of dairy factories.

The Oberhasli, formerly known as the Swiss Alpine, is a dairy sheep breed bred in the Oberhasli-Brienzer Alps region of Switzerland. Its coat is a monochromatic color, primarily light to dark reddish-brown, but features a black stripe on each side of the face, running from above the eyes to the tip of the nose. Its forehead is nearly black, and its topline, underbelly, and below the visor are all black. The Oberhasli has a thinner frame than the French Albaine, a gentler temperament, and an alert and energetic appearance. It is a medium-sized breed, with ewes standing over 71 cm tall and weighing over 54 kg; rams standing over 76 cm tall and weighing over 68 kg. Its average yield in 305 days is approximately 725 kg, and this breed remains rare in the United States. Taiwanese sheep farmers imported Oberhasli from the United States in the 1980s, but the numbers are very small. Because its milk production is not as good as that of the French Albaine, it is now a rare sight on Taiwanese dairy farms.

Centuries of experience have honed the correlation between a sheep's appearance, body conformation, lactation performance, and economic lifespan. The sheep industry has long established an ideal type for dairy sheep, using this as a basis for competitions and breeding selection. Therefore, the ideal type is not merely aesthetically pleasing; more importantly, it represents the ewe's optimal production performance and longevity. While the ideal type is difficult to find in the real world, it is a crucial criterion for judges in sheep competitions and for sheep farmers when selecting or selecting breeding sheep. Knowledge and skills in body conformation assessment are invaluable in sheep flock management and breeding improvement.

Goat dentition formula and age identification: Goats have a permanent dentition formula of 0033/4033, lacking upper incisors and canines. The age at which deciduous and permanent teeth erupt is determined by the eruption of all eight incisors around the age of four. After four years of age, age can only be determined by the wear of the incisors. However, the degree of tooth wear varies depending on the type of diet and can only be used as a guide for age determination.

American Dairy Sheep Association's Body Conformation Score: (I) General Appearance (♀ 30%, ♂ 45%): Appealing individuality, displaying vitality and feminine qualities, well-proportioned and balanced body, outstanding physique, and graceful gait. 1. Breed Characteristics: 5%; coat color, body size, nose shape, and ear shape must conform to the breed characteristics. 2. Head: 5%; moderate length, well-defined, broad nose, large nostrils, strong jaw, full, bright eyes, and a broad forehead between the eyes. 3. Shoulders and Topline: 8%. (a) Shoulders: Continuing smoothly with the chest and scapula. (b) Back: Strong, straight, with clearly discernible vertebrae. (c) Loin: Broad, powerful, and nearly horizontal. (d) Croup: Long, broad, and level. (e) Loin: Broad and level with the back. (f) Hip bones: Wide apart between the hip protuberances. (g) Hip bones: Wide apart, slightly below the loin protuberances, and clearly discernible. (h) Tail Root: Poorly connected to the surrounding area. (i) Tail: Symmetrical with the body. 4. Limbs and Hoofs: 12%. (a) Limbs: Wide apart, square, strong, with the front feet vertical. (b) Hindquarters: Nearly vertical from hock to pastern. Viewed from the rear, the feet are wide and straight. Hind bones are flat and strong, with clearly discernible tendons. The pastern is of medium length, strong and flexible, and the hock structure and angulation are correct. (c) Hoof: Short and straight, with deep roots and flat soles.
( 2 ) Dairy Traits ( ♀ 20% , ♂ 30%) : Smart appearance, well-defined body, well-developed and free of excess tissue, and a long lactation period. 1. Neck: Slender, smoothly integrated with the body and thorax, with a well-defined throat. 2. Nasal fascia: Well-defined and wedge-shaped, with the vertebral process slightly higher than the shoulder blades. 3. Ribs: Widely spaced, broad, flat, and long. 4. Flanks: Deep, well-arched, and delicate. 5. Thighs: Well-curved when viewed from the side, and wide apart when viewed from the back, providing ample space for the udder and its posterior attachments. 6. Skin: Delicate, loose, soft, and elastic, with fine hair. ( 3 ) Body Capacity ( ♀ 20% , ♂ 25%) : The body is large, providing ample space for the digestive system. 1. Abdomen: 12% deep, well-supported, with wide and well-curved ribs, gradually widening and deepening from the forequarters to the rear. 2. Chest: 8% large, with a wide chest base between the forelegs and well-rounded elbows. (IV) Mammary System (♀30%, ♂0%): Large and well-attached udders, indicating high milk production and long life. 1. Breast Capacity and Appearance: 10% long, wide, with ample capacity, good forward extension, and strong attachment. 2. Posterior Attachment: 5% high and wide, with both udders of equal size and symmetry. 3. Anterior Attachment: 6% extended and tightly attached, without a prominent pocket-like appearance, and smoothly attached to the body wall. 4. Tissue: 5% soft and flexible, without excess connective tissue, and the udder deflates after expression. 5. Nipple: 4% moderate in size and uniform in length, ensuring easy and reliable milking, with well-separated and correctly positioned nipples for easy expression.

Australian Dairy Sheep Association's Body Conformation Score: (1) Overall Appearance (♀5%, ♂5%): Vibrant, displaying both feminine and masculine characteristics, with well-proportioned and harmonious body connections, and the correct form and color of the coat and skin. (2) Conformation and Quality (♀8%, ♂10%): Lively and energetic, displaying breed characteristics, with a fine, lustrous coat and soft, supple skin. (3) Temperament (♀3%, ♂5%): Alert yet docile. (4) Head (♀8 % , ♂10%): Moderately long, delicate, with both feminine and masculine characteristics, a broad forehead between the eyes. Swiss breeds have a straight or slightly concave, dish-shaped nose and erect ears. Nubian breeds have a prominent nose and long, wide, drooping ears. The nose and lips are wide, and the eyes are large, full, and bright amber. (5) Neck (♀3%, ♂3%): Ewes have long, slender necks, cleanly connected to the head, and free of dewlap (except in Nubians). Stud rams have strong, thick necks, demonstrating masculine characteristics. (6) Forequarters (♀10%, ♂15%): The spurs are sharp and free of excess fat, the shoulders connect evenly to the body, the back is straight and powerful, and the spine is well developed. (7) Body (♀13%, ♂15%): Large girth, straight, wedge-shaped topline, rounded, large, and deep abdomen, well-sprung, wide, flat, and widely spaced ribs, deep and full chest, no dip behind the shoulder blades, wide loin with prominent haunches, the distance between the hip bones wider than the distance between the haunches, prominent mammary veins, and well-developed teats in rams. (8) Hindquarters (♀10%, ♂15%): Muscular, with a wide distance between the ischial bones, thin, broad, and deep thighs, and a high, wide croup. Nubians may have hindquarters slightly higher than the spurs. ( 9 ) Legs ( ♀ 5% , ♂ 5%) : Clean, powerful, with erect forelimbs, moderately bent hocks, well-separated limbs when standing, balanced carriage, and strong, supple pasterns. ( 10 ) Udder ( ♀ 25% , ♂ 0%) : Well-developed, large, well-shaped, without sagging, with no excessive separation between the lobes, a strong and broad posterior attachment, and soft tissue. ( 11 ) Teat ( ♀ 5% , ♂ 0%) : Well-shaped, correctly positioned, with appropriate spacing between teats, moderate size for easy handling, and easy milking. Length: approximately 6-8 cm. ( 12 ) Reproductive Organs ( ♀ 0% , ♂ 12%) : The external reproductive organs of males are well-developed, with testicles of average size and no obvious bifurcation. ( 13 ) Body Size and Weight ( ♀ 5% , ♂ 5%) : Well-developed in height and weight.

Linear ap. praisal evaluation of dairy sheep: The primary purpose of this evaluation is to objectively assess the influence of sires on the inheritance of traits in their offspring by analyzing differences in physical appearance among individual sheep and their correlation with lactation and lifespan. This serves as a reference for individual sheep selection and breed improvement. Currently, the American Dairy Sheep Association and the French Dairy Sheep Improvement Association have established evaluation criteria and standards, and routinely accept applications from farmers for evaluation annually.

Taking the American Dairy Sheep Association's linear body evaluation as an example, 13 main traits of ewes are evaluated, with each trait scored from 5 to 50 points. However, this system does not set a certain score to represent the ideal. In other words, the higher the score of some traits, the more ideal they are. The scores of each trait marked in bold represent the most ideal body shape.

French dairy sheep are evaluated linearly based on 17 traits, including three body traits (chest girth, topline, and hip slope); three hind leg traits (hock distance, hoof distance, and pastern angle); and 11 udder traits (front attachment, udder firmness, udder depth, rear attachment, central suspensory ligament, teat spacing, teat angle, teat orientation, teat shape, teat diameter, and teat length). The three body traits exhibit less variation among ewes and are less important than the udder traits. Among the udder traits, four traits —udder firmness (US) , udder depth (UD) , rear attachment (RA) , and central suspensory ligament (CL) —are the most important. Albain and Sanen dairy sheep have phenotypic and genetic differences in udder firmness, so the evaluation index is different. Albain: 1.5US+1UD+IRA+1CL , and Sanen: IUS+UD+1RA+0.5CL .

Progeny Testing of Dairy Rams: In advanced livestock-raising countries such as the United States, New Zealand, Australia, and France, in addition to establishing relevant associations or organizations to register the pedigrees of purebred dairy sheep, they also implement dairy herd improvement (DHI) programs. Milk volume and composition are measured monthly for registered lactating sheep, beginning with the lactation period following calving. Because the genetics of stud rams significantly influence the milk production of their offspring, and stud rams themselves do not lactate, dairy rams must undergo progeny testing to assess their genetic ability to lactate based on the lactation performance of their offspring. This is especially true for stud rams intended to produce frozen semen for widespread artificial insemination, which are particularly important.

Under the French dairy sheep improvement program, the best parental combinations are selected and bred through artificial insemination. The resulting rams are then weaned at three months of age and then reared in a quarantined artificial insemination center for progeny testing. Between three and seven months of age, the rams undergo an initial screening for conformation, growth rate, and reproductive health. Selected rams are placed in individual pens between seven and nine months of age and begin 15 semen collections to assess their reproductive function. Semen collections are performed twice weekly for young rams and three to five times weekly for mature rams. The purpose of semen collection and testing is to eliminate rams that are unsuitable for prosthetic vaginas (approximately 10%), have an ejaculate volume of less than 0.2 mL per ejaculation or a sperm concentration of less than 1 billion per mL (approximately 15%), and produce semen that is not freeze-resistant (approximately 25%). Ultimately, only about half of the rams are selected for continued semen collection and production of frozen semen. This frozen semen is then used for artificial insemination at various dairy farms. Each dairy farm provides at least 30 ewes for artificial insemination, and each ram's frozen semen is used to inseminate at least 200 ewes. This results in the production of approximately 40 female goats for progeny testing. Because goats are seasonal breeders, progeny testing is completed when rams reach approximately four years of age.

Auction Competitions: Sheep competitions are very popular in Europe, America, New Zealand, and Australia. Each country has established a dairy sheep association to manage matters such as the registration and competition of breeding sheep, and regularly holds regional and national competitions every year. In addition to being a leisure activity, the main purpose of breeding sheep competitions is to select sheep with superior body shape through evaluation and explanation by professional judges, providing participants and spectators with opportunities for education, observation, and learning, and allowing sheep farmers to breed ideal sheep. In addition, competitions can bring together participants from all over the world to achieve the purpose of connecting with each other and exchanging sheep-raising experiences. They can also attract general audiences and beginners and cultivate an interest in sheep farming. At the same time, whether or not there is an auction of the participating sheep at the competition, it can increase the opportunity for sheep to be traded. Sheep competitions are generally divided into categories such as breed, gender, and age. The American Dairy Sheep Association categorizes ewes into females and lactating ewes. Females are further divided into three categories: 2-5 months old, 6-11 months old, and 12-24 months old. Lactating ewes are further divided into three categories: 1-2 years old, 2-3 years old, and 3 years old and older. Two weeks before competing, sheep must undergo a grooming session. This includes trimming hooves, removing stumps, shaving, leaving a tuft of hair at the end of the tail, and brushing to remove residual hair and dandruff. Before competing, sheep must practice using collars and leashes to accustom them to interacting with strangers and prevent them from panicking in the arena. Additionally, ear tattoos must be inspected for legibility, and registration certificates must be prepared for inspection. On the day of competition, sheep must be groomed and groomed if necessary.

The transformation of primitive local livestock breeds into modern, improved breeds began with the British farmer Robert Bakewell (1725-1790). R. Bakewell can be considered the father of modern livestock breeding. In the 1760s, he first kept a record of the descendants of his own sheep for breeding selection, initiating a personal stock registration system. To improve the performance of farm animals, he compared the bones of retired breeding stock, or used preservatives to create specimens of retired breeding stock, and then compared the performance of their offspring. This method of offspring testing, or pedigree selection, is still used today. Bakewell also possessed a keen eye, similar to the skill of a horse expert in the Warring States Period of China. He accurately selected livestock for meat or draft based on their appearance, and first improved the meat quality of Leicester sheep. At the time, genetics and its scientific foundation were not yet established. However, they did understand the concept that livestock traits are passed down from parent to offspring, and that good offspring must be selected from good parents. They also focused on body shape and appearance, favoring fine bones and high meat content for meat production, and prioritizing early maturity and early fattening. Blood is thicker than water, and to ensure that good traits are passed down to offspring, they resorted to inbreeding to select the best and eliminate the worst, a theory that remains valid today. The 1804 breeding of the Shorthorn bull Comet, with a high inbreeding coefficient (46.87%), led to the establishment of a national stock registration system in the United Kingdom, marking the beginning of modern stock breeding. Thanks to the development of livestock registration, Britain became a global exporter of a wide variety of livestock and a global superpower.

Sheep are classified in the phylum Vertebrata , class Mammalia, subclass Placentalia , order Unglata , suborder Paridigitata, order Ruminantias , family Cavicornia , subfamily Caprininae , genus Capra , or genus Ovis, respectively, as either species Capra hircus (goat) or species Ovis aries ( sheep ). Goat and sheep breeds belong to the genera Capra and Ovis , respectively , and interbreeding is not possible. Improved goat breeds are mostly bred for dairy, followed by meat, with very few breeds for wool. Conversely, improved sheep breeds are mostly bred for wool production, and are also mostly bred for meat, with very few breeds for milk production. Sheep outnumber goats in both breed and number of improved breeds. Sheep are more common in developed countries at higher latitudes in temperate and cold climates. Sheep and goats are distinguished from each other and are not simply referred to as sheep. Unimproved goats are more common in developing countries at lower latitudes. Since only goats are bred locally, they are simply referred to as sheep, without distinguishing between goats and sheep.

The history of meat sheep, or goats, in Taiwan has been a tumultuous one. Before the influx of Han Chinese into Taiwan, the native goats were of the Austronesian variety. Their coats varied in color, including shades of brown, gray, black, and white. Much larger than the native black goats of southern China, they lived in scattered herds in the woods, much like wild goats naturally colonizing in an environment devoid of predators. By the 16th century, when Han Chinese migrated to coastal areas of southern China, they brought with them Chinese black goats. However, these black goats were not able to crossbreed with the original Austronesian Taiwanese goats, which had already devoured the larger native goats in western Taiwan. By the late 19th century, when the Japanese took control of Taiwan, the original Austronesian goats were no longer found in western Taiwan, leaving only the black goats brought by the Han Chinese. The original Austronesian goats still survived in the undeveloped eastern regions of Hualien and Taitung, bordered by the Central Mountain Range.

Nearly a hundred years before the turn of the 20th century, to improve the native black goats, Chang Linglin (1907) published a Taiwanese goat-herding experiment in the Taiwan Farmers' Association Bulletin, which resulted in the whitening of black-soil goats. White is dominant over all other goat coat colors, followed by brown, gray, and black (cream, brown, and black), followed by red (red and tan). Cream is the most dominant of the black coats, while black is the most recessive. Furthermore, white markings on a black background are dominant over all solid colors. However, this dominance is not a simple opposing gene, but rather a more complex interaction of complementary genes. The darker line on the back, known as the eel line, is recessive.

Crossbreeding native black goats with improved goat breeds like Saoneng results in whiter coats, but also increased size, which improves milk and meat production. Ono (1910) published a survey of goat breeding in Beitou in the Taiwan Agricultural Newspaper (台农事), finding that farmers disliked the change in coat color, hindering goat improvement. Unfortunately, after World War I (June 1914-November 1918), sheep wool became an important military supply. Advanced countries competed to develop sheep wool production and the related wool textile industry. Yanagawa (1919) published a paper on the origins of sheep in Taiwan at the Taiwan Agricultural Bureau, Miyamoto (1919) wrote about how to select Merino sheep and the nature of sheep, and Ito (1920) published a paper on sheep breeding and management methods. These papers all focused on sheep. Taiwan also shifted its focus from developing goats to developing sheep. In the hot summer, Merino sheep and Japanese Corriedale sheep were introduced to conduct experimental research and development of Taiwan's sheep industry. The Department of Animal Science and Technology (formerly the Department of Animal Husbandry) at National Taiwan University now retains various measuring instruments used to study wool characteristics during the period of Taipei Imperial University. After this transformation, Taiwan's goat industry became neglected and undervalued for half a century. Following World War II, Taiwan was again subjected to China's lack of livestock technology, which imposed a harsh "cow food, goat food" motto. This severely suppressed the development of goats, further devastating the postwar goat industry, leaving farmers to secretly raise them to survive. In March 1997, at the end of the 20th century, Taiwan was struck by foot-and-mouth disease (FMD), a devastating international epidemic that had been absent for over 80 years. The pre-pandemic livestock industry reached its peak, and the goat industry, regardless of whether it was dairy or meat, was revived thanks to the intensified research and development efforts of the Animal Products Research Institute. In 1996, goat farmers established a goat breeding association to vigorously develop the industry.

The strength of a country's agriculture significantly impacts its national power, and developed nations like Europe, America, and Japan are often considered dairy-producing nations. Therefore, the GI ratio is used to assess the strength of a country's agriculture. G stands for grass and grazing, and I stands for intensive and industrial. This refers to the ratio of the number of herbivorous livestock, such as cattle and sheep, to the number of non-herbivorous pigs and chickens, within a country's livestock industry that does not compete with humans for food and contributes to land conservation. The G/I ratio for developed European and American countries ranges from 6 to 8, while Japan's is 1. This indicates that countries with excessively high G/I ratios, such as those in India or Brazil, which only graze cattle, are not considered advanced. However, China's G/I ratio has not yet reached a decimal point of 1, indicating a preponderance of intensively farmed pigs and chickens and a shortage of locally farmed cattle and sheep. This G/I ratio reveals the importance of developing dairy-related sheep farming in China's agriculture, making it a crucial industry that deserves focused resources and efforts.

With the exception of the Black Belly sheep (hair sheep, not wool) presented as a gift by the King of Barbados during President Yen Chia-kan's visit to Central America in 1975, all sheep in Taiwan are goats, and are therefore generally referred to as sheep. Taiwan's dairy goats are primarily the Saanen, which were introduced nearly a century ago. Four other breeds exist: the Albanian, Nubian, and Toggenburg. A study by Sung et al. (1999) found that the Albanian has the lowest somatic cell count in milk of these four breeds, with over 80% of its milk meeting US Grade A (GA) standards. Saanen also achieved 73%, Nubian 49%, and Toggenburg 41%. This difference is due to the smooth interaction between the milk production genes of the improved goats and the interaction between genetics and the environment. This indicates that Albain, Sanen, and Nubian sheep have excellent milk production, resulting in better growth and potential for meat production. This genetic relationship not only exists between breeds, but also within breeds and between individuals. Therefore, it is urgent to register the pedigrees of all purebred sheep to facilitate the selection of the best and the worst, and to improve and preserve the breed locally, ensuring that each generation of sheep is better than the previous one, thus ensuring successful and profitable sheep farming. Currently, the national purebred sheep registration program is managed by the Central Animal Husbandry Association, with the Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute fully cooperating in the development of pedigree registration software.

The most important part of the registration work in our country is that each sheep farmer fills in the pedigree registration information. The pedigree registration information includes (1) the ewe breeding daily report, that is, when the ewe is breeding, the name of the ram, ear marking number, pedigree registration number, and the method and date of breeding are filled in the ewe breeding daily report and sent to the livestock group breeding sheep registration work team of the Central Livestock Association for registration and filing. The registration is also filled in when the ewe gives birth; (2) the lamb birth registration card. The lamb birth registration card is filled in with the date of birth, parity, number of litters (i.e., the number of male and female lambs), and the name and registration number of the sire, etc. The computer is checked and verified on the ewe breeding daily report at that time. The registration is automatically completed after the registration is completed. Therefore, the sheep farmer filling in such pedigree registration information is the first step in the registration of purebred sheep, and it is also the beginning of the registration of breeding sheep in our country, rather than completing the registration of breeding sheep by applying for transfer registration of imported registered breeding sheep in our country. For those who raise purebred ewes, the testing project for measuring the milk production capacity of their ewes is also ready to be carried out, which will definitely significantly improve the milk and meat production capacity of goats.

Individual direct testing of goat meat production should also be implemented to understand the growth performance of various breeds in China and serve as a basis for selecting the best and eliminating the worst. The simplest and most feasible method for testing goat meat production performance is to establish a meat production testing station. Registered weaned rams weighing 30 kg or more with pedigrees from local goat farmers are collected. The initial weight is then measured one week after arrival. Thereafter, the weight is measured every 50 days, with a final weight at day 100, 150, 200, 250, 300, 350, 400, 450, and 500. The final weight is then calculated based on the growth rate (coefficient of growth) during each growth period, understanding the growth curve, and determining the optimal age and weight for market or sale that maximizes economic production efficiency. Growth rate is calculated by taking W1 as the initial weight and W2 as the final weight. The growth rate for each growth period is calculated as (W2/W1) × 100 , or [(W2-W1)W1] × 100. When the nutritional environment conditions for W1 and W2 differ, [(W2-W1)/1/2(W1+W2)] × 100 is more accurate. At the end of the test, the results are announced based on the ranking of growth rates. During the test, the growth rate for each period can also be calculated as the average daily gain (ADR ) of (W2-W1)/(T2-T1) = dW/dT . While growth rate increases or decreases with age in a geometric progression, this daily gain (DG) can be used as a reference in meat sheep testing.

Meat sheep breeds: All livestock, including sheep, regardless of whether they are sheep or goats, can be used for meat, especially the unimproved native sheep of each country, which are all used for meat. Therefore, improved sheep breeds are mostly used for wool production, or goats improved for milk production. The meat sheep breeds here refer to improved sheep breeds improved for meat production. If we look at the amount of mutton traded globally, mutton (Mutton) is the main type, but in our country, goat meat is the most popular , especially the local black goat meat.

In reality, the importance of goat breeds for milk or meat versus sheep breeds for wool or internal use, much like the importance of beef cattle for meat, milk, or both, varies by breed, within the same breed, and within different countries. Taking the total milk or wool use of goats or sheep as 10, the meat-to-milk ratio for meat sheep is 6:4 or higher. Conversely, for dairy sheep, the meat-to-milk ratio should be 4:6 or higher. For dairy-to-meat sheep, the ratio should be 4.5:5.5, 5:5, or 5.5:4.5. Similarly, for meat sheep, the meat-to-wool ratio is 6:4 or higher. For wool sheep, the meat-to-wool ratio should be 4:6 or higher. For both wool and meat sheep, the ratio should be 4.5:5.5, 5:5, or 5.5:4.5. Furthermore, the body shapes and appearances of dairy and meat sheep are relatively similar, unlike the distinct body shapes and appearances of dairy and meat cattle, as shown in Figure 2-2-1 in Section 2 above. When China introduces improved dairy sheep to breed meat sheep, it is crucial to guide their body shapes and appearance towards those of meat sheep in order to achieve success. Differences in appearance and appearance between meat and dairy sheep.

Nubian : Originating from the wild goat (Capraibex) of East Africa, the Nubian sheep is a cross between other dairy goats. They have dense, short hair, a short head with a prominent jaw, a long neck, large, flat, drooping ears (fapeared) , and a hooked nose ( Roman nose ) , distinguishing them from other dairy goats. Their coat can be chestnut, white, brown, or black. They are small, with long limbs and no body width. Their body shape is similar to that of a Saanen, but they lack the wale or beard and are smaller. They are generally hornless, though occasionally males have spiral horns. The original Nubian sheep are now rare. The Nubian sheep found in China are a modified breed introduced from the UK. The full name of the Nubian is the Anglo Nubian , a large, improved dairy sheep created by crossbreeding African Nubian sheep with native British dairy goats. It was registered as a breed in 1909. Nubian sheep have good growth performance and are highly productive, typically giving birth to two or three babies. While their milk production is not as high as that of Saanen or Albain sheep, their milk fat content is 4.0-6.5%. They are more tolerant of rough handling and easier to manage than other dairy sheep, making them well-suited to tropical environments. The average male height is 90.6±0.8 cm, body length is 101.6±4.5 cm, and chest circumference is 107.8±3.8 cm. The average female height is 77.1±4.8 cm, body length is 89.2±5.6 cm, and chest circumference is 94.4±6.6 cm. A year-old male ram reaches market weight of 70 kg, stands 81 cm tall, and is 92.3 cm long. The slaughter rate is 60%, and the carcass lean meat content is as high as 68%. Most of the existing Nubian sheep in Taiwan are descendants of the American Nubian, specifically the Anglo Nubian. The Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute reintroduced Nubian sheep in 1985 and has been breeding and promoting them for crossbreeding with Black Earth goats.

At the Hengchun branch ( Hong et al., 1987) , crossbreeding Nubian rams with Black Earth ewes resulted in a high farrowing rate of 210.7% and a triplet rate of 32.2% . The farrowing rates of Taiwanese Black goats and their crossbreedings during the same period were 152.2 and 150.0 , respectively. This demonstrates the excellent reproductive success of Nubian sheep in Taiwan. Similar results were observed at breeding farms in Hualien and Taitung, with the crossbreedings still outperforming subsequent progressive goats. Meanwhile, the average daily gain of finishing rams was 0.18 kg , with a feed efficiency of 6.26 . The average daily gain of crossbreedings with Black Earth goats, for both the first and second generations, after weaning was 0.12 kg and 0.10 kg , respectively. The conception rate of the crossbreedings reached 87.5% , with a high farrowing rate of 46.4% for twins and 32.2% for triplets . Nubian sheep are not only an excellent meat sheep in Taiwan, but also one of the four major dairy sheep breeds. In the United States, the certified lactation period for Nubian sheep is 10 months, with a total milk yield of 1,455 kg , resulting in a milk-to-meat ratio of 6 : 4 . In Taiwan, the milk-to-meat ratio is 5.5 : 4.5 or 5 : 5 , resulting in a higher meat yield than in developed countries, making it a dual-purpose breed.

The Boer Goat, a native meat goat, appeared in South Africa in the early 1900s. Its appearance and build are similar to the original Nubian goat before it was modified, making it the world's only relatively primitive meat goat breed. However, due to epidemics in Africa, importation into developed countries like Europe and the United States has been strictly prohibited for nearly three-quarters of a century. By the 1970s, it began to appear in German universities and in Australia in the 1980s. However, after six years of prolonged quarantine, the new meat goat was officially released between 1993 and 1995. Since then, the number of Boer goats raised has increased dramatically. Whether bred as purebreds or crossbred with other dairy goats, the selected and improved Boer goats have significantly improved goat meat production in Australia, with significant improvements in tolerance to rough conditions, growth, meat production, and slaughter rate. In addition to farmed Boer goats, an estimated 4 million Boer goats are now available for slaughter annually in the wild. This has contributed to Australia, which controls half of the world's mutton exports, becoming a major exporter of purebred Boer goats, and has established a global Boer goat industry. The United States established the National Boer Goat Association in 1997 to coordinate pedigree registration and development efforts among state Boer goat associations. In 1995, the Hengchun Branch of the National Animal Research Institute of Taiwan imported 13 Boer goats, two males and 11 females, from Australia to provide new genetic resources for Taiwan's meat sheep industry. Boer goats are large, heat-tolerant, and easy to raise, and crossbreeding with them has accelerated the improvement of meat sheep. Boer goats have a long, dense, and lustrous coat. Their eyelids, eyes, and other hairless areas are dark gray in color. The skin is thin, soft, and loose. Their head and the first 10 cm from the neck are brown or nearly red, while the rest of their carcass and limbs are typically white. Some may have brown-red markings on the carcass. Boer Sheep Associations consider the former purebred Boer goats, while those with brown-red markings on the carcass are advanced breeds. Boer goats have strong, broad heads, but are gentle and composed, with a prominent forehead. Their horns are round, yellowish-white, of medium length, curved upward and downward, with thicker gray tips. Their ears are long, wide, and drooping, resembling Nubian ears. Their eyes are large, brown, almond-shaped, and their noses resemble the hooked, or Roman, Nubian nose, with wide, flared nostrils that tip in line with the lower lip. The upper and lower jaws are even, not prominent, and the bite is correct, though the lower jaw may protrude slightly after 4-5 years of age. The neck is of medium length and proportionate to the carcass, blending smoothly into the thorax. The chest is broad, deep, and well-muscled, with a wide, but not prominent, buttocks. The midsection is long, broad, deep, with well-developed ribs. The back is broad, flat, and well-muscled, blending smoothly behind the shoulders with only a slight concavity. The hindquarters are broad, flat, and flat, with the loin extending from the ischial tuberosity to the rump. The glutes and thighs are well-muscled and extend downward, and the tail is set high and flat. The leg length is moderate in proportion to the carcass, with a strong, well-developed pastern, a square stance, and flat, rather than rounded, calf bones, indicating a more fleshy appearance.

Taiwan prefers black or darker colors for goat hair, so in 1999, the Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute imported 100 bottles of frozen semen each from black and red Boer goats from the United States. Using artificial insemination, they began selecting and improving Boer goats with fewer white spots, and crossbred them with various colored goats to create large black meat goats like Boer goats, thereby improving the competitiveness of Taiwan's domestic industry.

Crossbreeding trials at the Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute with Taiwan Black goats resulted in larger bodies and high reproductive performance in the first generation, with a conception rate of 70.6%, a birth rate of 171.0%, a twinning rate of up to 66.7%, a singleton rate of 25.0%, and a triplet rate of 8.3%. Crossbreeding of first-generation ewes from Nubian and Taiwan Black goats with Boer male goats resulted in a conception rate of 54.7%, a singleton rate of 22.3%, a twinning rate of 75.2%, and a birth rate of 179%.

The Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute conducted a cross-breeding experiment on Boer goats with various local goats at a diet of 15.8% crude protein and 65.3% total digestible nutrients. The results of the meat production were as follows: (1) Boer goats: daily weight gain 0.22kg, feed efficiency 5.9, slaughter rate 59.3%, lean meat rate 54.7%, fat 9.1%; (2) Taiwan Black goats × Boer goats: daily weight gain 0.17kg, feed efficiency 6.7, slaughter rate 54.7%, lean meat rate 55.9%, fat 8.3%; (3) Taiwan Black × Nubian × Boer goats: daily weight gain 0.21kg, feed efficiency 5.1, slaughter rate 56.9%, lean meat rate 55.8%, fat 9.5%.

The weight gain of any meat-producing livestock is influenced by factors such as environment and age. Purebred Boer goats in Australia typically gain significantly more weight than improved dairy goats currently available in Taiwan. Their average daily weight gain over a year is 0.20 kg when grazing, and can reach 0.25 kg when housed or in more intensively maintained conditions.

Boer goats also boast reproductive performance comparable to that of Nubian sheep. Their estrus cycle is 18-21 days, and their gestation period is 150 days, similar to that of other improved goats, but they estrus year-round, with no seasonal variations. Boer ewes mature at 24 months of age often give birth to twins or triplets, while first-time females are more likely to give birth to singletons or twins. Females can generally be bred as early as 7 months of age, but premature pregnancy and birthing can affect their growth and future fecundity. Therefore, breeding at 18 months of age, when they are first-time mothers, is most beneficial, as they can then expect to produce triplets. Boer goat milk has over twice the fat content of ordinary goats , resulting in exceptionally high-quality milk.

Taiwan Black Goats: Taiwan Black Goats refer to the black goats brought by our ancestors from China. They originally evolved with our ancestors in the western plains. Because of their small size, they were hybridized and improved with various imported improved goat breeds after 1900. In the first half of the century, they were mostly hybridized with the Saneng breed, and in the second half of the century, they were mostly hybridized and graded with the Nubian breed. The number of purebred Taiwan Black goats has greatly decreased, and most of the producers in the market are hybrids or graded breeds. In 1987, the few purebred Taiwan Black goats were listed as protected breed livestock, and are raised at the Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute and the Hualien Breeding Farm respectively.

Taiwan Black goats have been subjected to long-term artificial reverse selection, where meat traders often select large goats for sale under the eaves of goat farmers. This has resulted in a gradual decrease in size of Taiwan Black goats, exacerbating the size difference between native and improved breeds. At the Miaoli Breeding Farm of the Animal Research Institute (Li, 1981), ewes give birth mostly between February and March. Through artificial natural breeding, they can produce two litters annually. The reproductive capacity of 30 ewes is as follows: 30 ewes, 27 breedings, resulting in a breeding rate of 90%. 24 farrowed, with a reproductive rate of 88.9%, a singleton rate of 79.2%, a twin rate of 20.8%, and an average litter size of 1.12. Birth weight is 1.4 kg for males and 1.0 kg for females. At one month of age, weaning weight is 2.4 kg for males and 2.1 kg for females. At two months of age, weaning weight is 5.9 kg for males and 5.0 kg for females. At three months of age, weaning weight is 8.8 kg for males and 8.4 kg for females. At four months of age, the weaning weight was 11.0 kg for males and 10.8 kg for females. At five months of age, the weaning weight was 16.1 kg for males and 13.0 kg for females. At six months of age, the weaning weight was 18.0 kg for males and 15.0 kg for females. At seven months of age, the weaning weight was 21.5 kg for males and 18.6 kg for females.

The original flock of Taiwan's native black goats, preserved and banked, was later transferred to the Hualien and Taitung branches of the Animal Research Institute for conservation and utilization. Numerous studies are underway on economic traits related to growth, reproduction, carcass, blood biochemicals, and nucleic acid polymorphism. A 2001 barn-feeding survey revealed birth weights of 2.7 kg for males and 2.6 kg for females. Three-month weaning weights were 13.7 kg for males and 11.9 kg for females. The dressing rate was 41.1%, with a lean meat percentage of 33.6%. The littering rate was 161.3%, the twinning rate was 54%, the average daily milk yield was 0.8 kg, and the milk fat percentage was 5.5%. Sixty frozen embryos were successfully produced, and the conception rate after transfer to ewes was 50%. At the Hualien Field of the Animal Research Institute, the average daily milk production during the first month after farrowing was 474±75 mL, the average milk production during the second month was 503±62 mL, and the average milk production during the third month was 274±95 mL (Zhuang et al., Hualien Field of the Animal Research Institute, 2005).

The Ji'an Goat in Development: Taiwanese people often use the saying "one black, two yellow, three spotted, four white..." when it comes to animal nutrition. In the mutton market, black goats are often fetched higher prices than other mixed colors. In 1998, the Hualien Breeding Farm of the Animal Research Institute, as part of its mutton improvement program, selected and purified superior all-black goats from the first generation of hybrids between Nubian and Native Black goats. This resulted in a new breed of Taiwanese black goats with rapid weight gain, excellent feed efficiency, and superior carcass traits. The results have been very successful (Wang et al., Hualien Farm of the Animal Research Institute, 2005). Therefore, the tentative name "Ji'an Goat" was proposed, named after its place of origin, Ji'an Township.

Hualien Changzhuang et al. (2005) at the Animal Research Institute selected superior first-generation hybrids of black sheep, which had a pure black coat ratio of 44%. Self-crossing of this first-generation hybrid resulted in an F2 with a black coat ratio of 6%, indicating that self-crossing of pure black sheep could produce pure black "Ji'an sheep." Ji'an sheep have a large head, ears that are neither large nor droopy, but rather slant outward, a slightly hooked nose, a well-defined jaw, a well-aligned bite, a slightly long neck, a moderately long mid-body, a deep body, a deep chest, and limbs that are not too long, giving them an overall brick-like appearance.

Wang et al. (2005) from the Animal Research Institute in Hualien conducted carcass analysis on four one-year-old rams and found that the slaughter rate was 56.92%, the bone rate was 10%, the lean meat rate was 42%, the fat rate was 8%, the viscera rate was 11.9%, and the weight was 3.875±39kg. Other major carcass traits were heavy.

The large size and excellent reproductive capacity of the imported Nubian dairy sheep from Ji'an were crossbred with local sheep to achieve a high litter size. The goal was to develop a new breed of sheep with a high litter size, so the lactation capacity of the sheep was investigated to assess its high reproductive potential. Milk production was collected twice daily from birth to three months of lactation. The results showed that the average daily milk production in the first month was 1,059 ± 77 mL; the average milk production in the second month was 1,029 ± 149 mL; and the average daily milk production in the third month was 738 ± 82 mL. The average daily milk production during these three months before weaning was 902 ± 233 mL (Hualien Changzhuang et al., Animal Research Institute, 2005). The average birth weight was 2.37 kg, and the weaning weight at 90 days of age (three months) was 12.79 kg, with an average daily weight gain of 0.115 kg.

Imported Black Belly Sheep: In May 1975, during a visit to the Republic of Barbados in Central and South America, President Yen Chia-kan received a gift of five Barbadian Black Belly sheep, two males and three females, from the King of Barbados. The sheep were handed over to President Yen and returned to Taiwan on May 18, 1975. Central and South America is a frequent area for foot-and-mouth disease, while Taiwan was considered free of the disease at the time. Therefore, after undergoing standard import quarantine and an additional three months of quarantine, the sheep were released. However, after repeated discussions, a conclusion remained undecided as to which unit of the Animal Research Institute should take care of them. One suggestion was that the Penghu Stock Breeding Center on an outlying island be responsible for the care. However, placing a gift from the King of Barbados on an outlying island would be contrary to international etiquette. After another three months of deliberation, and indeed more than six months of quarantine , the sheep were finally taken over by the Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute.

The Barbadian Blackbelly sheep, derived from a cross between African shorthair sheep and European sheep, were introduced from West Africa by the Spanish and Portuguese in the 17th century and bred in the Caribbean and West Indies. They were established in the former British Barbados for 300 years. After Barbados gained independence in 1966 and became a member of the Commonwealth, these Blackbelly sheep were renamed Barbados sheep or Barbados Blackbelly sheep. In 1904, four females and one male Barbadian Blackbelly sheep were imported to the United States. The Barbadian Blackbelly sheep are well-suited to Taiwan's climate and have shown excellent growth and reproduction at the Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute, earning them a worthy gift from the King. To accelerate the expansion of the herd, eight males and 38 females were imported from the United States in 1989 and 1992 , but their performance was found to be far inferior to that of the Barbadian Blackbelly sheep. Barbadian Blackbelly sheep have short wool and a brown-red body. Their chest, abdomen, underside, and inner limbs are black, with no markings or spots, hence the name. They are hornless, unlike American Blackbelly sheep, with males often having horns while females are hornless. They have large, flat ears, large eyes, and strong jaws. The male's nose is slightly hooked, while the female's is less pronounced. The forehead and nose bridge are lighter and whiter. Their necks are of moderate length, with the female's neck being thinner than the body, while the male's neck is thicker and blends smoothly into the shoulders. They have a broad body with well-developed ribs, a broad back and loin, a wide hindquarters, and a croup that slopes downward from the loin. Their tails are low-set and long, with a lobe extending to the hock. Barbadian Blackbelly sheep have excellent reproductive performance, with over half of their ewes bearing twins or more. A comparison of the reproductive performance of 132 Barbadian, 66 American, and 73 Barbadian-American hybrid black-bellied ewes at the Hengchun branch revealed the following results: Barbadian black-bellied sheep performed exceptionally well in Hengchun, demonstrating excellent reproductive potential, surpassing even American black-bellied sheep. Birth weights of Barbadian males (2.82±0.53 kg) and American-bred females (2.60±0.70 kg) were significantly different, while birth weights of Barbadian females (2.64±0.54 kg) and American-bred females (2.52±0.44 kg) were not significantly different. Similarly, weaning weights of Barbadian males (17.74±3.22 kg) and American-bred females (15.81±3.21 kg) were significantly different, but the difference between Barbadian females (15.37±3.16 kg and 14.27±3.30 kg) was not significant. Birth weight, weight at 6 months of age, and weight at 18 months of age of rams of Barbadian Blackbelly sheep selected at the Hengchun Branch in 1992, 1993, 1994, and 1995.

In a research project report by Wu et al. (1996) at the Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute, seven six-month-old rams were subjected to forced fattening for four months on a 3:1 concentrate-to-roughage ratio, 13% crude protein (CP), and 68% total digestible nutrients (TDN). Their feed intake was recorded, but no significant differences were found between Barbary and American breeds. Four one-year-old fattened blackbelly sheep from each of these different strains were also selected for slaughter and carcass analysis. Carcass measurements of American blackbelly sheep revealed significantly better lean meat percentage, fat percentage, bone weight percentage, and skin weight ratio than Barbary blackbelly sheep, but no other significant differences were observed (Wu et al., Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute, 1996).

Taiwan's Blackbelly sheep have excellent reproductive stamina and robust growth and meat production, demonstrating the superiority of the Blackbelly sheep donated by Barbados. However, their tender, high-quality meat differs from the firmer goat meat favored in Taiwan. Despite their high productivity, their poor market acceptance and low price hindered their promotion. The Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute abandoned their breeding and promotion efforts, and most of them now remain on various leisure and tourism farms. Although Barbados is no longer a formal diplomatic ally of Taiwan, we should still be grateful and applauded for their gift of these valuable Barbadian national treasure meat livestock genetic resources.

The significance of identification: The means of livestock selection and improvement are nothing more than (1) body shape and appearance, (2) pedigree, and (3) performance testing. Advanced countries in developed countries have all enacted laws, such as the Livestock Improvement and Proliferation Act of the Animal Husbandry Act of our country (promulgated by the President in 1998). The central and local governments must actively promote the improvement and enhancement of livestock, master their genetic resources, and designate various breeding stock registration agencies to carry out pedigree registration, performance testing, and the improvement of livestock body shape and appearance. Obviously, the evaluation of body shape and appearance is one of the means of livestock breeding improvement. Although the purpose of raising meat sheep is not entirely to appreciate their appearance and body shape, but to appreciate their growth and meat production ability and rapid reproduction and reproduction ability, these growth and meat production and reproduction ability are comprehensive manifestations of the physiological functions of sheep. Physiological functions are inevitably unrelated to body structure. The content of this body structure is inevitably reflected in its shape and appearance, forming a form. This aligns with Hegel's (German philosopher Georg Wilhelm Friedrich HEGEL, 1770-1831) statement that "form is the form of its content, and content is the content of the form outside its behavior." In terms of livestock body type identification, this means that appearance symbolizes its physiological function. Therefore, in the selection and improvement of breeding sheep, the shape and appearance of a sheep can provide a glimpse into whether it will produce good meat. While carcass evaluation is certainly the most accurate method for identifying live animals, the appreciation of appearance during body type identification cannot be overlooked.

Meat sheep conformation is the classification, judging, and appearance of mutton. In developing countries, conformation is a complex process, serving both as an assessment of breed purity and an indicator of performance, making it one of the oldest and most primitive methods of selection. In developed countries, such as China's pig breeding registration system, where pedigree registration and performance testing are well-established, the conformation and appearance of breeding stock is a summary of their pedigree and performance testing results. This means that when selecting a breed, performance must be assessed based on performance testing results. To determine the heritability of these performance results, the pedigree must be examined. The performance of these tests is determined by examining the performance of the ancestral lineage over generations, all of which are recorded on paper. Finally, visual inspection and appearance assessment are necessary to summarize the pedigree and testing results. Before the establishment of a breeding sheep registration system in China, sheep farmers primarily relied on conformation and appearance assessment as the sole selection method. Appearance assessment is the most effective method for determining breed characteristics and assessing breed purity. Furthermore, identifying the body shape and appearance of meat sheep can improve their body type. For example, when improving the Nubian dairy breed to focus on domestic use in China, their body shape has shifted away from dairy production and towards a more boxy meat-producing body shape. Improving functional body shape not only improves the skeletal structure of meat sheep, as the length and width of the mid-quarters and hindquarters are related to meat yield, but also enhances milk production and reproductive performance. Improving the appearance of feet and hooves can affect longevity. Therefore, without a comprehensive pedigree registration and performance testing system, the only way to actively improve meat sheep is to strengthen the identification of the body shape and appearance of breeding sheep.

When identifying body type, the first thing to consider is whether the body type is specific to its intended use. Meat sheep must possess the characteristics of meat sheep, as centuries of livestock breeding have resulted in a body shape that reflects its intended use. This is especially true when combined with the successive generations of registered and improved pedigrees, resulting in a body type that is consistently tailored to the intended use. Like beef cattle or pigs, meat sheep have a sturdy, rectangular, cylindrical body, with a well-developed forequarters and a substantial, well-proportioned, rectangular or blocky hindquarters. Their limbs are appropriately short and muscular, with distinct, clean tendons and no excess. Secondly, attention should be paid to the varying characteristics and gender characteristics of breeds with the same intended use. The simplest way to identify body type is through gender, with differences between males and females. Males should resemble males, with a large, deep forechest that is heavier than the hindquarters. Females, on the other hand, have a deeper mid-quarters, heavier hindquarters than the forequarters, and are more docile and peaceful.

Although breeds share the same purpose, their distinct characteristics are derived from the physical appearance standards established by the respective registration associations at the time of their establishment. When registering pedigrees with each breed registration association, sheep farmers must follow the registration procedures of each registration association to select and maintain the purity of the breed, physical appearance, and performance improvements. Therefore, each sheep must be reviewed item by item according to the physical appearance standards established by the registration association. The review standards are based on the average of the body measurements of existing sheep, incorporating desired body parts to achieve the ideal body shape. The names of the various body parts of meat sheep must be known before effective body identification.

When registering breeding sheep in Taiwan, the body type and appearance criteria and scoring tables used to identify meat sheep require an understanding of the current physique and average size of the sheep. The ideal type (true type) is then determined based on the current body type and weight, and these are then compared and identified. For example, Nubian sheep, bred for dairy production in Europe and the United States, have been bred over centuries to develop a body type suited for dairy production. However, in Taiwan, the focus has shifted from dairy production to a meat-producing body type similar to the Boer. Therefore, the body type and appearance criteria are divided into two distinct standards for dairy and meat production. The results of selecting and improving body types based on specific uses are significant.

China's sheep breeding registration system divides the physical appearance of meat sheep into seven major scoring areas. A 30- point score for general appearance assesses overall symmetry and conformity to the functional body characteristics of meat sheep. This is further subdivided into forequarters, midquarters, and hindquarters, providing detailed identification of each meat-producing part. The forequarters is the area from the shoulder (the back edge of the foreleg) to the front chest and neck. The hindquarters is the area from the loin (the front edge of the hindleg) to the back, including the buttocks. The midquarters is the area from the front edge of the hindleg and the ribs to the back edge of the foreleg, excluding the forequarters and hindquarters. These important body parts should be broad, wide, and voluminous, with a straight, strong back and a flat, narrow, and nearly parallel backline. The head and neck are the most effective parts for identifying breed characteristics and are a valuable tool for determining the purity or hybridization of a breed, but they are rarely effective in assessing meat production. The strength of the feet and hooves plays a crucial role in a breed's longevity. The feet of meat-producing animals shouldn't be too thick or too long, as overly large bones will increase the bone-to-meat ratio after slaughter and reduce the lean meat content. However, the purpose of selecting a breed of sheep is to improve its offspring, ensuring each successive generation is superior. However, a good sheep must have a good body shape, so the limbs of a particular breed shouldn't be too short, as this would negate its purpose. Therefore, they should be neither too long nor too long, but rather of appropriate length. The last component is the skin and coat. Although this is the least important component to consider, the skin should be thin, not thick, and soft, and the coat should be fine, bright, and lustrous. This reflects the breed's physical and mental state, and also reflects the quality of its husbandry and management. Coat color is also a valuable indicator of breed identification.

When inspecting and identifying the body shape and appearance, first observe the general appearance of the whole sheep from a distance of 1-2 meters to get an overall impression, compare them with each other, and identify the upper, middle and lower ranking. Continue to compare the advantages and disadvantages of each part of the body, and the stance and pace of the feet and hooves. Finally, use the head and fur to assess the breed characteristics. If there are any that do not meet the standards set by the breeding sheep registration regulations, they should be identified as disqualified and not scored, or they should be punished as the lowest and not be registered as breeding sheep. In this way, the inspection is completed.

Auction and Competition: Carnivals that showcase and promote various agricultural products are often held across China. These primarily feature exhibitions and auctions, often lacking competitions, exchanges, or spectatorships. In fact, the essence of agricultural product or livestock carnivals (shows; fairs) lies in the fact that breeders and breeders from all over the country participate in these auctions and competitions, which have a significant impact on the nation's breeding stock. Therefore, Japan calls these competitions, exchanges, and auctions "Kyojinkai," which are clearly defined as carnivals for the joint improvement of breeding stock. The methods for improving livestock breeding are primarily based on (1) bloodline, (2) performance, and (3) appearance. In 1662, four centuries ago, the Royal Society was established in the United Kingdom to advocate the importance of studying natural sciences. A century before the establishment of the livestock pedigree registration system in 1775, improvements to the body shape and appearance of livestock were implemented, and livestock competitions were established. In 1838, the Royal Agricultural Society of England was established to host the annual Royal Livestock Show. Therefore, the improvement process of modern livestock breeding is actually the order of (1) appearance, (2) pedigree, and (3) performance testing. Knowing the improvement of appearance is an indispensable means. In 1908, Taiwan held its first pig breeding competition (Tongjinhui) in Tainan City. In 1911, the first national poultry competition was also held in Tainan City. Afterwards, an annual competition system for various livestock was established. This livestock breeding and improvement method was maintained during the provincial government era after World War II. Each year, different counties and cities would cooperate with farmers' associations or various industry associations to host national competitions. Unfortunately, after the foot-and-mouth disease (FMD) broke out in 1997, all livestock competition systems were abolished, severely damaging the integrity of the livestock breeding and improvement strategy.

After taking over the management of Taiwan's breeding sheep registration from the Council of Agriculture, Executive Yuan, the Central Livestock Association recognized the importance of competitions, exchanges, and auctions for improving breeder appearance beyond pedigree registration and performance testing. The association actively provides participating sheep farmers with opportunities to participate in these competitions, exchanges, and auctions, allowing them to showcase and discuss the strengths and weaknesses of their sheep's body shapes. In 2004 , the Sheep Breeding Association and the Central Livestock Association jointly hosted the first annual breeding sheep competition, exchanges, and auctions in Baihe Township, Tainan County, using the Baihe Pig Association's competition and auction venue. A similar competition, held at the same Baihe venue on November 30, 2005 , was held . This annual event provides opportunities for farmers to discuss and discuss breeding trends, fostering future aspirations and aspirations. The quality of meat sheep is easier to accurately assess based on their appearance than that of dairy sheep. Therefore, it is crucial to gather outstanding breeders from all over the country that meet the registered breeder standards. This allows for mutual observation, comparison, and exchange of opinions, fostering community recognition and unifying improvement goals to achieve educational objectives. The competition is divided into groups based on breed and size. Expert judges and judges appointed by the organizer determine the rankings. The results are then briefly reviewed and auctioned off, fostering the exchange and rapid dissemination of superior genetics.

Goat activity level: Goats are more active and lively than sheep, and their energy consumption increases. Therefore, the energy requirements of goats vary greatly with the breeding environment.
