Goat Feed Intake: Growing and lactating sheep consume approximately 3.5-5.0% of their body weight in dry matter per day, while dairy cows and sheep consume only 2.5-3.0% of their body weight. Given the relationship between feed intake and body weight, goats can consume large amounts of low-quality forage to compensate for their nutritional deficiencies. Similarly, if their feed intake is limited, goats require a higher nutrient concentration than other ruminants.

Goat Chyme Transmission Rate: Goats have a smaller rumen-to-bodyweight ratio, and because they eat more, their chyme remains in the rumen faster than sheep and cattle. However, goats still digest nutrients more efficiently than other ruminants. This is due to the presence of a larger population of rumen microorganisms. In particular, when fed low-quality forage, goats have higher concentrations of fiber-degrading bacteria in their rumen fluid than sheep. Therefore, studying the nutritional requirements of goats cannot be completely applied to other ruminants. However, research on some nutrients in goats is lacking, leading some scholars to suggest that recommended nutrient amounts for goats can be based on those for sheep.

Energy: Energy can be used for the maintenance, growth, reproduction, lactation, fur growth and activity of the goat's body. The energy source required by the goat's body comes from carbohydrates and fats, and only a small part may be derived from the catabolism of protein. Carbohydrates and fats account for 60-70% of the total energy of the feed, and they are converted into volatile fatty acids (mainly acetic acid, propionic acid and butyric acid) through fermentation, and then absorbed through the rumen wall to provide energy. The efficiency of nutrient utilization depends on whether the energy supply is sufficient, which is an important key to goat production. The range of energy requirements of goats is the largest among all livestock. This is related to the fact that there are many types of goats, production purposes (wool, meat and milk) and activity levels.

Take, for example, sheep are docile, while goats are more active and roam more freely, often digging under fences. This is the so-called "smart goats, tired sheep." Goats also have a "height-loving nature"—they enjoy climbing and jumping, which requires them to expend more energy. Goats kept in barns do not require increased energy maintenance; however, goats grazed in intensive conditions or in hot climates require a 25% increase in energy maintenance. Goats grazed in semi-arid areas and on small hills require a 50% increase in energy maintenance. Goats grazed on sparse grasslands, coarse rocks, and seasonal mountain grazing require a 75% increase in energy maintenance. Mountain grazing is particularly energy-intensive, leading to the saying among herders in mainland China that "once you've been to the mountains, you won't be able to recover for three days." Taiwan's goat farmers all raise lactating goats in barns. Although they do not consider the increase in maintenance energy due to activity, the energy needs of dairy goats are affected by age, body shape, growth, pregnancy and lactation. Therefore, the energy provided to dairy goats must be adjusted according to the pregnancy stage and milk production to stably meet the energy needs of dairy goats.

to provide adequate energy intake before breeding, during breeding, and two months before farrowing. Energy supply should be increased in dairy goats during the two months before farrowing and during lactation. Goat maintenance energy is often expressed in terms of digestible energy ( DE ) . In 1981 , the National Research Council (NRC) estimated the energy requirement for ewes during the final two months of pregnancy to be 1.74 Mcal/DE . However, this energy level is difficult for most goats, especially small goats. The energy requirement for small and medium-sized goats carrying a single litter is only 0.87 Mcal/DE . Some scholars believe that the NRC 's energy requirement for pregnant ewes is tailored to large goats carrying multiple litters.

Energy deficiency not only stunts goat growth, delays puberty, and reduces fertility, but also affects milk production. Sustained energy deficiency reduces goat resistance to infectious diseases and parasites. Adequate energy supply is particularly crucial for pregnant and lactating goats. Pregnancy toxemia, also known as ketosis, is a nutritional and metabolic disorder that often occurs in late pregnancy due to insufficient energy intake. The fetus develops rapidly during the last week of pregnancy. For example, in Alpine goats, weight gain is 1.5 kg/week. During this period, the fetus requires more energy and glucose. If the dry matter or energy intake of a ewe is insufficient, the ewe will increase lipid activity, i.e., the catabolism of adipose tissue. This involves breaking down stored fat and converting it into gluconeogenesis, which provides a source of energy. This results in an increase in plasma ketone bodies (e.g., acetone). If energy intake continues to be insufficient after parturition, the ewe's body will accumulate excessive ketones, leading to ketosis. Exercise can help reduce ketosis, which may seem unbelievable because it increases the goat's energy needs. However, exercise actually causes muscle contraction, which accelerates the utilization of ketone bodies and the release of lactic acid, a precursor to gluconeogenesis.

Energy intake is the primary factor influencing milk production. Energy deficiency can lead to a rapid decrease in milk production in dairy goats. If a goat receives only 85% of its total energy requirement, milk production will decrease within one to two days. However, energy intake in early lactation has less impact on milk production than in mid-lactation, as goats utilize stored energy to produce milk. High-yielding goats can produce 0.5-1.0 kg of 4% fat-corrected milk using their stored energy .

Energy insufficiency is more common in dairy goats during the late stages of pregnancy. This is due to a decrease in appetite and the continued rapid growth of the fetus, which compresses the rumen volume, leading to a decrease in feed intake. Therefore, feeding strategies during this period should include providing high-quality, palatable roughage to maintain rumen volume and prevent energy insufficiency. International studies have found that compared to goats fed high-quality forage, feed intake and milk production decreased by approximately 70% (1.1 vs. 1.9 kg DM/day) in dairy goats fed low-quality forage, but milk production decreased by approximately 90% (1.0 vs. 1.9 kg/day). Therefore, feeding dairy goats high-quality forage increases their feed intake and, consequently, milk production. Although it is necessary to try to meet the energy needs of dairy goats in the late pregnancy, it is not advisable to make them too obese, because lipids will accumulate in the abdominal cavity and relatively reduce the volume of the rumen, resulting in a decrease in their feed intake. At the same time, it will also put dairy goats at risk of developing pregnancy toxemia.

Because dairy goats' maximum dry matter intake occurs during peak lactation, the key to high-yield dairy goats lies in nutritionally providing them with the necessary nutrients from parturition to peak lactation. Goats' feed intake can increase by 30-40% one week after parturition, reaching peak lactation 6-10 weeks later. When supplementing energy during lactation, the timing of adding large amounts of concentrate is crucial. Providing large amounts of concentrate in late pregnancy and early lactation yields the best milk production and economic benefits, as a high concentration of concentrate in early lactation improves milk production more than during mid- and late lactation. Providing grain helps maintain peak lactation, but grain processing is not essential to improve goats' digestibility and milk production. Goats fed whole grain produce milk equivalent to those fed grain. Feeding goats powdered grain results in lower milk fat content than goats fed whole grain.

When it comes to providing forage for lactating goats, green grass is preferred over hay and silage. In a mild climate, dairy goats can maintain and produce 1-2.5 kg of milk with an ad libitum diet of hay and silage. A free diet of green grass can produce 2-3 kg of milk. Although Taiwan's hot and humid summers are ideal for grass growth, autumn enters a dry season with little rain, and the southern region lacks sufficient green grass for goats. Once goats become accustomed to their forage, they can become picky eaters. For example, if they are fed green grass or hay, they will refuse to eat, gradually becoming emaciated. Therefore, the Complete Book of Agricultural Administration states that pens should always be kept clean. Do not feed green grass throughout the year, as doing so will cause them to lose weight and become refusing to eat dry grass. Therefore, goat farmers should preferably feed lactating goats with hay or silage.

Water: Water is essential for goats' metabolism and is therefore crucial for their growth. The water content of a goat's body varies with age and body fat content. The average goat's body water content is over 60%. Sheep are domestic animals that dislike drinking water. Some tropical breeds can go for long periods without water, earning them the nickname "water horses and drought sheep." Goats drink even less water than sheep. Comparing water consumption with cattle, a normal goat consumes 1.4-1.7 kg of water for every kg of dry matter consumed, while cattle consume 2.1 kg of water for every kg of dry matter consumed. This indicates that goats have the highest water utilization efficiency of all livestock, comparable to camels.
There are three main reasons why goats require less water than other livestock: (1) Goats cannot withstand the rigors of high temperatures, unlike sheep or cattle, which require large amounts of water to evaporate to control their body temperature. (2) Goats can reduce the water content of their urine and feces to conserve water. (3) Goats' rumens can store large amounts of water ( for example, the black Bedouin goat stores 40% of its body weight in water ) , so goats can tolerate 2-4 days without drinking water and still produce a satisfactory amount of milk.
The daily water requirement of goats is related to their feed intake. The formula for water intake is: total water intake = (3.86 - dry matter intake ) - 0.99 or total water intake (g/kg BW0.75) = 2.98 dry matter intake (g/kg BW0.75 + 0.854% dry matter). The latter formula does not apply to fodder with a dry matter content of less than 20% . When the dry matter content of the fodder is less than 30% , goats can meet their water needs without drinking water. However, in fact, goats will still drink water if water is provided. From the above formula, we know that if a sheep consumes about 1kg of dry matter, its water intake is 2-3kg. Factors that influence goats' water intake include milk production, ambient temperature, moisture content of forage, exercise, and the salt and mineral content of the feed. Water cleanliness and temperature also affect the amount of water a goat drinks, so lactating goats need clean, fresh water. Lactating goats require 1-4 gallons of water per day, plus an additional 2.5 quarts of water for lactation. In temperate regions, the maintenance water requirement for lactating goats is 145.6g/kg 0.75 , and an additional 1.43kg of water is needed for every kg of milk produced .

If lactating sheep do not drink enough water, their milk production will decrease. Non-lactating sheep, if they have access to juicy grass or graze on juicy pastures, will consume very little water, generally only every other day, and do not need to drink much during the fattening period. Mainland Chinese scholars point out that sheepherders should "avoid hasty movement, thirst, and frequent drinking. They should be driven well, moving between areas with trees and grass near water, and drink a full drink every other day." Bu Shi, a Han Dynasty sheepherder, observed: "Drink only once every two days; frequent drinking can cause water loss and nasal discharge." Water drinking precautions for sheep include avoiding hasty movement, as drinking before catching your breath or drinking too quickly can easily lead to choking and pneumonia, commonly known as "exploded lungs."
Protein: Protein is a major component of the goat's body. It can be obtained from feed and, after digestion and absorption, is used for tissue and organ development, cell repair, secretion of digestive enzymes and endocrine hormones, fetal growth, and lactation. While protein supplementation in mid-lactation has little effect on milk production, in early lactation, high-yielding goats, in particular, are more sensitive to dietary protein. Increasing the digestible crude protein content of concentrate from 12.5% to 18.5% increases fat-corrected milk production by 5-10%. To produce 2.5 kg of fat-corrected milk in mid-lactation, a goat can maintain production on a diet containing 16% crude protein (CP). In late lactation, goats only need a diet containing 10% crude protein. Protein requirements for goats are often expressed in terms of digestible protein (DP). The daily protein requirement for all pregnant goats in the two months before farrowing is 57 g DP; during lactation, this requirement is 51 g DP/kg of 4% fat-corrected milk. Microorganisms convert organic matter and decomposed nitrogen in the rumen into microbial protein, which then passes through the abomasum and is digested into amino acids, ultimately forming the animal's protein. Feeding ruminants with urea as a nitrogen source for microorganisms can save on feed protein. However, different nitrogen sources affect the conversion of digestible nitrogen into milk nitrogen. Studies have shown that, given the same amount of digestible nitrogen, goats fed a diet of soybean meal and urea have a lower efficiency in converting nitrogen into milk (0.53 vs. 0.72) than goats fed a diet of soybean meal and fish meal .

Although dietary protein is an essential nutrient for lactating goats, increasing dietary protein content is not beneficial for ruminants. This is because high dietary protein levels increase rumen ammonia nitrogen (NH3-N) concentrations, preventing rumen microbial utilization and leading to excretion in the urine. Consequently, international studies have shown that reducing dietary CP from 18% to 16% can improve feed efficiency in goats aged 103 to 156 days. Dietary protein that can be broken down by microorganisms in the rumen is called rumen-degradable protein (RDP), with approximately 9 g of RDP requiring 0.24 Mcal of energy. Protein that cannot be broken down by microorganisms is called rumen-undegraded protein (UDP). To prevent the loss of ammonia nitrogen and energy from rumen microbial degradation of dietary protein, formaldehyde treatment can be used to preserve dietary protein. For example, feeding ground nuts treated with formaldehyde to goats can increase their growth rate and improve feed efficiency. For dairy goats with high milk production, additional UDP is needed.

Calcium and phosphorus: Calcium and phosphorus are key minerals that should be considered in goat feed formulations. A deficiency of either or both in young animals can lead to poor growth and poor bone shape (cartilage). In adult animals, calcium is particularly important for lactating goats, as goat milk is rich in calcium. The calcium and phosphorus requirements of lactating goats increase during production, particularly during growth and lactation. The recommended daily intakes for a 55kg lactating goat during maintenance are 3.7g and 2.5g, respectively. During pregnancy, these intakes are 3.7-7.9g and 2.5-3.6g, respectively. During lactation, the recommended daily intakes for 1kg/day of milk production are 7.9g and 4.0g, respectively. For every additional 1kg/day of milk production, the recommended intakes increase by 4.2g and 1.5g, respectively.

Excessive calcium intake does not pose an immediate danger to goats, but it can interfere with absorption and cause deficiencies in phosphorus and certain divalent cations (magnesium and manganese). Although fewer goats are affected by low phosphorus, the calcium-to-phosphorus ratio in the diet is crucial. Therefore, when supplementing goat diets with minerals, attention should be paid to the calcium-to-phosphorus ratio. The ideal ratio is between 1.2:1 and 2.5:1. However, ruminants can tolerate a calcium-to-phosphorus ratio of 7:1. During pregnancy and early lactation, severe calcium-to-phosphorus imbalances in goats can lead to lactation fever. High-yielding lactating goats are susceptible to lactation fever due to hypocalcemia. Normal blood calcium concentrations in ewes range from 9-10.5 mg/dL; in lactating goats, this condition can drop to as low as 5 mg/dL. This condition is related to dietary calcium content, but parathyroid hormone also plays a role, as it releases free calcium from bones. Impaired parathyroid hormone secretion can lead to hypocalcemia in goats. This disease can be treated with grape calcium, intravenous injection. In male goats, if calcium and phosphorus imbalance occurs (low ratio below 1:1), that is, the phosphorus content is too high, it will lead to urinary tract stones or kidney stones.

Most forages are a good source of dietary calcium but are very low in phosphorus. Therefore, goats grazing on phosphorus-deficient pastures can easily develop phosphorus deficiencies. Legumes are excellent sources of calcium. To prevent nutritional deficiencies in goats caused by calcium and phosphorus deficiency or imbalance, calcium and phosphorus supplementation is necessary. To compensate for calcium deficiencies, limestone powder is commonly used and allowed to feed ad libitum. To compensate for phosphorus deficiencies, monosodium phosphate , disodium phosphate, or feed-grade phosphoric acid can be provided. To supplement both calcium and phosphorus, commonly used supplements include dicalcium phosphate , defluorinated calcium phosphate , and steamed bone meal.

Why sheep prefer salt: A saying in mainland China goes, "Sheep naturally love salt, and they find it most beneficial to eat salt." This suggests that sheep enjoy salt, and providing them with sufficient salt is beneficial to their growth. The salt mentioned here includes both chloride and sodium. Sheep consume more salt per kilogram of body weight than cattle. Another saying goes, "If you don't eat salt in spring, you won't have a good summer; if you don't eat in the dog days of summer, you won't eat grass." This means that insufficient salt intake in spring hinders growth, and in the hot summer, the need for salt is even greater. If sheep are unable to absorb salt, their appetite is affected, and they will not eat grass. Sheep prefer salt because forage grass contains too little sodium, comprising only 0.05-0.15% of the daily diet, far from meeting their physiological needs.

Salt is important to sheep: Sodium maintains osmotic pressure within the body's cells, facilitating the transfer of nutrients and waste; while chloride is essential for maintaining gastric acid secretion in the abomasum (the fourth stomach of ruminants). Chronic salt deficiency in sheep can lead to a bland taste in the mouth, a loss of appetite, lethargy, decreased nutrient utilization, and poor growth. Sheep are also prone to pica, a habit characterized by chewing wood, wool, and soil. To satisfy their craving for salt, they often lick dirt and eat poisonous plants.

Salt Addition: Salt or saltiness can be used in animal husbandry as a means of regulating appetite and preventing disease. Adding salt powder to goats in spring can prevent disease and help them grow fatter. The recommended daily sodium intake for goats should be 0.5-1.0% of the total diet . For dairy goats weighing 55 kg , the recommended daily sodium intake is 1 gram for maintenance, 1 gram for the first three months of pregnancy , and 1.2 grams for the two months before farrowing . During lactation , the recommended sodium intake is 1.5 grams for maintenance and lactation, and 0.5 grams for every additional 1 kg of lactation per day . When salt is added to a mixture feed , it is usually added at 0.5% salt to a complete ration or 1 % salt to a concentrate . However, not all grazing sheep require salt supplementation. For example, water sources in alkaline soil areas of the western United States may already contain sufficient salt to meet the needs of sheep, so salt supplementation is not necessary.

Salt is used to control goat feed intake: When salt is used to limit the amount of supplements (concentrates) that goats can freely consume, provided sufficient fresh, cold water is provided, the proportion of salt in the mixture depends on the goat's feed consumption, typically ranging from 10-15% to as much as 50%. Mineral salts containing trace elements should not be used in this manner, as they may cause copper poisoning. For goats, it is best to allow them to feed freely. Enterotoxemia is a major disease in goats in Taiwan, with a high mortality rate (up to 86%). One of the causes is that confined sheep consume more concentrates and less roughage, which leads to impaired digestion and a higher susceptibility to disease. When feeding concentrates to goats, increasing the salt content to reduce overconsumption of concentrates should reduce the incidence of enterotoxemia.

Salt feeding methods: (1) Salt powder: The Complete Book of Agricultural Administration records the method of raising sheep with salt: "One liter of salt should be placed in a clay pot and hung in the sheep pen. The sheep like salt and will come back to eat it on their own, so there is no need for human shepherding." The method of feeding with salt powder in mainland China is to sprinkle salt powder thinly on bricks and stones, let the sheep eat it when they come in and out, or mix it with grass and feed it. (2) Salt brick feeding: The salt brick feeding method is to place salt bricks in the pen for the sheep to eat. In the early days, Taiwanese sheep farmers imported salt bricks from abroad, which were expensive. Now domestic salt factories also produce salt bricks, but the price is cheaper.

When feeding salt to sheep, there are some things to be aware of: (1) Damage to teeth: When providing edible salt, it is best to use loose salt rather than block salt. This is because sheep prefer to bite rather than lick salt from block salt. Salt blocks are very dry and hard in the wild, so excessive force can easily break their teeth. (2) Excessive salt intake in goats: When salt is provided to goats and they are allowed to explore freely, they will consume more than they need. If they are provided with sufficient drinking water, this will not cause any disease to the goats.

Magnesium: Magnesium is a component of bone, accounting for 60-70% of total magnesium, while soft tissues and extracellular fluids contribute 30-40%. Magnesium plays a crucial role in neuromuscular activity, requiring magnesium ions to activate many enzyme systems involved in carbohydrate, fat, and protein metabolism. Magnesium deficiency can lead to grass tetany, leg stiffness, and head retraction in animals. High potassium levels in the diet can negatively impact goats' magnesium utilization. Vigorously growing grass contains high levels of potassium, so goats grazing juicy, green pastures or winter grain pastures fertilized with nitrogen and potassium are more susceptible to magnesium deficiency. The recommended daily intake of magnesium for lactating goats is 1g, including 1.0g and 1.2g before and during late pregnancy, respectively. During lactation, the recommended daily intake is 1.70g for every kg of milk produced per day. For every additional kg of milk produced per day, the recommended intake should be increased by 0.7g. Because forage grasses contain high levels of nitrogen and potassium, the minimum amount of magnesium that should be added to goat diets is 0.22%. However, there is a lack of literature on whether excessive magnesium levels can cause poisoning in goats.

Potassium: The primary electrolyte within cells is potassium, while the primary electrolyte outside cells is sodium. Potassium influences intracellular osmotic pressure and acid-base balance. Potassium also activates enzyme systems involved in energy transfer and utilization, protein synthesis, and carbohydrate metabolism. Goats fed a basic roughage diet provide sufficient potassium for their needs. Potassium absorption occurs throughout the digestive tract. Potassium deficiency reduces feed intake, stunted growth, and reduced milk production. Severe deficiency symptoms include emaciation and poor muscle tone. The recommended potassium content in goat diets is 0.8%. The recommended daily intake for a lactating goat weighing 55 kg is 3.1 g, including 3.1-3.4 g during pregnancy. During lactation, the recommended daily intake is 2.3 g for every additional kg/day of milk production.

Sulfur: Sulfur is an important component of sulfur-containing amino acids ( methionine and cystine ) . Goat hair is particularly high in sulfur. If non-albuminate nitrogen is used as a protein source in goat diets, additional sulfur should be added. Rumen microorganisms can incorporate the added sulfur into protein. Elemental sulfur or sulfate can be used as sulfur supplements. If urea or other non-albuminate nitrogen sources are used in the diet, the sulfur to nitrogen ratio should be 1:10 . Goats also have an increased need for sulfur when they consume plants containing tannins. Symptoms of sulfur deficiency in goats include decreased appetite, weight loss, poor growth, and decreased milk production.

Cobalt: Cobalt is an essential element for the synthesis of vitamin B12 and is essential for the growth and development of goats. Symptoms of cobalt deficiency in goats include weight loss, poor appetite, weakness, and anemia. While sheep can meet their cobalt needs with a cobalt intake of 0.1 ppm , goats' cobalt requirements are believed to be similar to those of sheep. Goats should be fed cobalt regularly, ideally daily. Cobalt should be added to salt bricks at a rate of 5.45 g per 100 lbs . and allowed to freely explore the food.

Copper and Molybdenum: Copper is absorbed primarily in the small intestine, though the stomach and large intestine also absorb it. Excess copper is stored in the liver. Copper is an essential mineral for the biochemical synthesis of hemoglobin and red blood cell maturation. Copper is also a key component or activator of many enzymes . Symptoms of copper deficiency in lambs include muscle weakness, ataxia, and frequent recumbency. Symptoms of copper deficiency in adult goats include anemia, changes in coat color, and fertility impairment. Copper and molybdenum are interrelated in animal metabolism, so molybdenum should be considered when evaluating goats' copper needs. High levels of molybdenum in the diet can cause copper excretion from the animal, leading to copper deficiency. Therefore, additional copper supplementation is necessary. Sheep are sensitive to copper, and excessive copper consumption can lead to copper poisoning. Whether goats suffer from copper poisoning in the same way as sheep has not yet been reported. Molybdenum is found in the highest concentrations in the bones, liver, and kidneys of animals. Molybdenum is a component of many enzymes , such as xanthine oxidase , xanthine dehydrogenase , and nitrate reductase . Molybdenum is primarily involved in the formation of uric acid and the catabolism of nitrates. Molybdenum is absorbed primarily in the small intestine and excreted via feces, but less frequently through urine. While molybdenum deficiency rarely occurs in goats in the wild, as it does in cattle and sheep, studies have shown that molybdenum deficiency can lead to slowed growth, reduced conception rates, and increased miscarriage.

Fluoride: Small doses help strengthen bones and teeth, but excessive amounts can soften bones and cause tooth spots and wear. Fluoride deficiency rarely occurs in goats, but excess fluoride can be toxic. Fluoride poisoning in sheep occurs at dietary concentrations exceeding 200 ppm, and the doses for goats are similar.

Iodine: The thyroid gland contains 70-80% of the body's total iodine, the highest concentration of iodine among animal tissues and organs. Iodine is an essential mineral for the thyroid gland to produce thyroxine. Consuming an iodine-deficient diet can cause thyroid enlargement. Iodine can affect the basal metabolism and fertilization rate of adult goats. Iodine can also affect cell differentiation, growth, and development in fetuses and young goats. Consequently, iodine deficiency can lead to birth weakness or even death in lambs. Iodine deficiency is widespread worldwide. To prevent iodine deficiency in goats, feed them iodized salt. Ruminants absorb iodine primarily in the rumen, with less absorption occurring in the small intestine. However, overfeeding goats with iodized salt to achieve a restricted diet is unwise, as it may lead to excessive iodine intake. The maximum iodine tolerance of sheep is 45 ppm, and goats tolerate similar levels of iodine.

Iron: Iron is a component of hemoglobin. Iron deficiency rarely occurs in mature grazing goats, but it is more common in lambs. This is because newborn goats have low body iron stores, and the milk they consume also contains low iron. If the deficiency is caused by lamb milk, iron supplementation can be achieved by intramuscular injection of 150 mg of iron-dextran every two to three weeks, or by adding ferrous sulfate or ferric citrate to the diet .

Manganese: Manganese is an essential mineral for goat bone development and reproduction. Feeding goats a diet low in manganese, such as corn and sorghum, increases the likelihood of manganese deficiency. Symptoms of manganese deficiency in goats include reluctance to walk, forelimb defects, delayed estrus, difficulty conceiving, abortion, and a 20% reduction in birth weight. While manganese can be absorbed throughout the small intestine, only 1-4% of the dietary manganese is absorbed. Ruminants cannot effectively store manganese.

Zinc: Zinc is an essential mineral for goats, known for its role in the growth and regeneration of skin and hair. Symptoms of zinc deficiency in goats include decreased feed intake, weight loss, joint stiffness, excessive salivation, swollen feet, small testicles, and decreased libido. To alleviate zinc deficiency, zinc should be continuously added to the diet. Zinc absorption occurs primarily in the small intestine, leaving little available for use in the body. The zinc requirement for goats is 10 ppm , and the toxic dose is 1000 ppm .

Selenium: Although selenium is an essential mineral for goats, the requirement is very low. Glutathione peroxidase contains selenium. Selenium and vitamin E interact, reducing the need for each. Selenium levels as low as 0.04 mg per kilogram of dry matter in the diet can cause selenium deficiency in pregnant and lactating goats. Selenium deficiency in goats from birth to several months of age can lead to white muscle disease, which can cause sudden death or muscle paralysis, particularly stiffness of the hind limbs and an inability to lift them. Selenium is excreted in milk and stored in the fur. Like other livestock, goats are susceptible to selenium poisoning. The recommended dose for selenium poisoning in goats is generally 3 ppm , the same as for sheep . Symptoms of selenium poisoning in goats include loss of appetite, weight loss, depression, constipation followed by diarrhea, excessive thirst ( polydipsia ), polyuria, difficulty breathing, and a low rectal temperature. Excessive selenium can also cause hoof pain and loss.

Vitamin A: Goat forage does not contain vitamin A, but it does contain its precursor, beta-carotene. Approximately 1 gram of beta-carotene is equivalent to 400 IU of vitamin A. Animals primarily absorb vitamin A in the anterior small intestine, and the liver is the body's primary vitamin A storage organ, accounting for approximately 75-90% of total storage.

Vitamin A's functions include: 1. Maintaining normal vision: Vitamin A is essential for the formation and regeneration of rhodopsin pigment in the eyes. Therefore, vitamin A deficiency in goats can lead to night blindness, or even blindness in severe cases. 2. Maintaining normal tissue function: Vitamin A deficiency causes epithelial keratinization of cells in the respiratory, intestinal, reproductive, and urinary tracts, as well as the eyes. 3. Strengthening immunity: Vitamin A deficiency can make goats susceptible to disease and parasites, leading to poor reproductive performance.

There are two main causes of vitamin A deficiency in goats. The primary one is insufficient vitamin A in the diet , particularly during the dry season or winter due to limited grass availability, or poor quality hay fed to goats, such as prolonged or improper hay storage, which can lead to sun and rain exposure. During hay production, poor weather can cause the loss of nutrients from hay due to rain, or prolonged exposure to the sun, which destroys nutrients, can also contribute. Another contributing factor is that the lambs' bodies do not store sufficient vitamin A. Vitamin A supplementation can be achieved by adding vitamin A to the diet , providing ample grass and corn, and administering slow-release intramuscular vitamin A injections. However, it should be noted that vitamin A utilization efficiency does not increase with increasing vitamin A intake.

Vitamin D: Sun-dried forage is rich in vitamin D. Vitamin D is involved in the intestinal absorption of calcium and phosphorus, thus affecting their deposition and reabsorption in bones, and also affecting their excretion. An imbalance in dietary calcium and phosphorus increases the physiological requirement for vitamin D. Lambs raised in sheds without adequate sunlight should receive vitamin D supplementation. Oral vitamin D is absorbed in the small intestine. For goats, vitamin D has the same effects as vitamin D. Symptoms of vitamin D deficiency include skeletal abnormalities, including rickets and growth retardation.

Vitamin E : Vitamin E , chemically known as tocopherol , is categorized by structural formula into three types: α, β, and γ. α - Tocopherol is the most potent and widely distributed. Vitamin E can be transferred into milk, where its antioxidant properties extend the shelf life of goat milk. Normally, vitamin E is abundant in goat diets, particularly cereals , so supplementation is rarely necessary . Goats consuming pastures high in nitrates and soils low in nitrates may develop vitamin E deficiency , but there is currently no data on symptoms of vitamin E deficiency in goats. The recommended dosage for goats is unclear , but 5-100 mg of α - tocopherol should meet their needs. Vitamin E is primarily absorbed in the small intestine, but the stomach also absorbs it. Adipose tissue and the liver are the body's storage sites for vitamin E.

Vitamin K: Any fresh or dried green leaves are a good source of vitamin K. The rumen microorganisms of adult goats can synthesize a large amount of vitamin K2, so there is no need to add vitamin K to goat feed.

Vitamin B : Rumen microbes can synthesize vitamin B , so adult goats do not need to supplement their diets with it . Besides the rumen, goats absorb vitamin B primarily in the duodenum, using both active and passive absorption mechanisms. When vitamin B concentrations are high in the intestine, passive absorption occurs; when concentrations are low, active absorption occurs. Vitamin B is a coenzyme component of many enzymes and plays a crucial role in carbohydrate metabolism and nerve conduction. While adult goats can meet their vitamin B needs with rumen microbes , the rumen of newborn goats is not fully developed, and rumen microbes cannot synthesize sufficient amounts of vitamin B. Therefore, newborn goats must supplement their vitamin B intake with goat milk or milk replacer . Vitamin B deficiency in both young and adult goats can cause polioencephalomalacia (cerebrocortical necrosis).

vitamin B hypovitaminosis in adult goats are as follows: 1. Certain plants ( e.g., oak bark) contain thiamin antimetabolites ( which do not activate thiamin enzymes ) . 2. Certain bacteria or molds produce thiamin antimetabolites in feed. 3. Increased vitamin B requirements due to carbohydrate-rich feed intake .

Vitamin C: Except for primates such as humans and monkeys, which need to obtain vitamin C from food, the tissues of most livestock and poultry can synthesize vitamin C to meet their body needs.

Rumen Physiology in Meat Sheep: Meat sheep are ruminants, and their stomachs are divided into four parts: the rumen, the reticulate stomach, the omasum, and the abomasum. At birth, the rumen accounts for only 27% of the total stomach volume, but by adulthood, it has grown to 85% . The rumen of adult sheep can accommodate large amounts of feed and harbor a large population of microorganisms. These microorganisms help the sheep break down high-fiber feeds, which are unusable by the stomach's own lining, into usable monosaccharides. Microorganisms also utilize the energy gained from fiber breakdown to convert protein, amino acids, and even urea nitrogen in the feed into microbial protein. In short, the sheep's rumen is like a fermentation tank, where microorganisms continuously thrive and provide nutrients for the host's survival. However, to fully utilize the rumen's functions in meat sheep, they must provide high-quality feed during the lambing period to stimulate rumen development and expand rumen capacity , enabling the sheep to fully utilize its microbial protein production.

Dry matter intake: When formulating any livestock feed, the animal's daily dry matter intake must be considered to ensure the animal's daily nutritional needs are met within a limited feed intake. Livestock dry matter intake varies significantly depending on the animal's body weight, growth stage, pregnancy, nursing, lactation, housing facilities, feed type, forage fiber content, climate, social status, and whether the animal is housed or free-range. Therefore, feed supply must be adjusted accordingly based on increases or decreases in dry matter intake. Sheep are small ruminants, and smaller animals consume less, so dry matter intake has a greater impact on their growth and production traits. In Taiwan, the daily dry matter intake of sheep ranges from 1.8% to 3.8% of body weight , with 1.4% to 1.7% of dry matter intake representing the minimum requirement for survival. The dry matter intake percentage for lambs is approximately 2.8% to 3.0% . For mature sheep, it's about 2.3% to 2.4% , for older breeders or rams, it's about 2.0% to 2.2% , and for lactating sheep, it can reach as high as 3.8% . Inadequate dry matter intake in meat sheep can lead to slow weight gain at best, and weight loss and decreased milk production at worst.

Sheep require energy to meet their basic maintenance, growth, fattening, pregnancy, and lactation needs. Energy requirements vary depending on breed, weight, growth stage, and environment. For example, the energy density of a fattening mutton ration must be higher than that of a lamb or lamb. Furthermore, in the summer, when mutton intake decreases, the energy density of the ration must be higher than in the winter. Corn, barley, molasses, cassava, bran, rice bran, and oils are all excellent sources of energy in concentrate feeds. Forage grasses such as bulgur, pennisetum, and Bermuda hay are excellent sources of energy in forage. These energy-rich feed ingredients are broken down in the rumen by rumen microorganisms to form glucose and volatile short-chain fatty acids such as acetate, propionate, and butyrate. Some of these short-chain fatty acids are absorbed by the rumen and synthesized into nutrients essential to mutton. Acetate and butyrate are synthesized into milk fat, while propionate is synthesized into glucose or amino acids. Therefore, the ability of mutton to utilize other nutrients in the diet depends entirely on sufficient energy supply.

When calculating mutton feed formulas, energy can be expressed as total digestible nutrients (TDN) , metabolizable energy (ME) , digestible energy (DE) , or net energy (NE) . The net energy system, due to its independent and additive nature, allows for a more accurate estimate of animal production or growth performance. However, its methodology is relatively complex. The calculation of total digestible nutrients is relatively simple and is the most accessible and accessible of the various energy systems.

Energy deficiency is a common problem when formulating mutton sheep feeds. This is often due to insufficient feeding or a diet with low energy concentration. Most sheep farmers don't pay much attention to the problem of low-energy diets, but they are unaware that when mutton sheep consume such diets, they can experience slowed growth, weight loss, ineffective estrus, a shortened lactation period, decreased milk production, and a significantly reduced resistance to parasites and disease.

Protein: Protein is a key component of meat sheep's body tissues, and they require a continuous intake of protein to renew their cells. Rumen microbes typically break down protein in feed into peptides, free amino acids, and ammonia. Rumen microbes then use the ammonia to synthesize their own bacterial protein. Rumen protein production accounts for approximately 65% to 70% of a sheep's daily amino acid requirement . Once the microbial cells enter the abomasum, they are digested and broken down by the microbes before being absorbed and utilized in the small intestine.

Bypass protein in feed flows to the abomasum, where it is broken down by gastric acid into peptides and amino acids. Bypass protein is derived from certain feed protein molecules bonded with specific chemical substances, such as tannins, lignin, and acid-washed fiber, that resist degradation by rumen microorganisms. The percentage of bypass protein in a feed varies greatly depending on the feed's characteristics, generally ranging from 30% to 40% . The quality and quantity of bypass protein in a diet significantly impacts sheep weight gain and feed efficiency. Feed manufacturers do not want essential amino acids in feed to be broken down in the rumen. Therefore, they often use physical or chemical methods to increase the proportion of bypass protein in feed formulation. Examples include flaking corn, cooking soybean meal, spraying tannins on pasture, saponifying fats or minerals, and encapsulating amino acids. However, higher levels of bypass protein in a diet can reduce rumen ammonia concentrations, which in turn reduces rumen microbial production and, in turn, affects the total amino acid content reaching the sheep's small intestine.

In formulating mutton feeds, protein is expressed as either crude protein or digestible protein. Soybean meal, fish meal, blood meal, feather meal, brewer's meal, and sorghum distillers' meal are all excellent sources of protein supplements. The protein percentage of mutton concentrates typically ranges from 18% to 14% . Lamb concentrates typically contain 18% protein. Adult sheep typically have a protein percentage of 16 % , while dry breeder sheep or rams typically have a protein percentage of 14% .

Protein intake must not only meet the daily metabolic needs of sheep, but also their needs during growth, fattening, reproduction, and lactation. Therefore, protein requirements vary for sheep of different breeding objectives and weights. When dietary protein is insufficient, sheep utilize amino acids in their blood and protein in their muscles to balance these needs. However, chronic protein deficiency can lead to slower growth, decreased appetite, weight loss, coarse fur, decreased lactation, irregular estrus, and reduced conception rates. Therefore, sheep farmers must pay close attention to protein intake when balancing their diets.

Minerals: Generally speaking, grazing sheep are not deficient in minerals, as most of the minerals in the soil are absorbed by plant matter and subsequently consumed by the grazing sheep. However, since Taiwan's sheep farming has shifted from free-range to confinement, mineral deficiencies often occur due to insufficient minerals in the concentrate feed. This mineral deficiency often leads to physiological and metabolic disorders in sheep, thus affecting their production efficiency.

Calcium: Calcium is a vital mineral that helps animals grow and repair bones and teeth, regulates nerve and muscle function, regulates acid-base balance, aids blood clotting, activates coenzymes, and generally provides essential nutrients for growth and production. The plasma calcium level in meat sheep is typically maintained at a stable 10mg/100ml . A rapid drop in the plasma calcium level in ewes can lead to milk fever . Because lambs require and absorb more calcium than adult sheep, calcium deficiency during pregnancy can result in rickets or osteomalacia in their offspring. Calcium deficiency in adult sheep can lead to osteoporosis, making bones porous, brittle, and prone to fractures. While meat sheep can tolerate high-calcium diets, excessive calcium intake not only impairs energy and protein utilization but also reduces the absorption of manganese and zinc ions, ultimately affecting their efficiency in weight gain. Generally, calcium in meat sheep feeds is primarily sourced from legumes, dairy products, and fish meal. Due to the influence of grass fiber bonding, the utilization rate of calcium in grass is not high for meat sheep, and the calcium concentration in cereals is also low. Therefore, when preparing meat sheep concentrate, an appropriate amount of shell powder, calcium hydrogen phosphate or limestone powder is often added to make the meat sheep concentrate contain about 0.3~0.8% calcium to meet the calcium needs of meat sheep.

Phosphorus: Phosphorus also helps with the growth and repair of bones and teeth. It is a component of proteins and phospholipids in animals. It participates in various animal metabolisms, activates B vitamins, and maintains blood acid-base balance. It is a fundamental unit of energy transfer in mutton sheep and is essential for their reproduction and production.

Phosphorus deficiency can lead to loss of appetite, poor weight gain, reduced milk production, emaciated appearance, and emaciated appearance in mutton sheep, thus affecting their reproductive efficiency. Furthermore, severe phosphorus deficiency can lead to pica, a behavior characterized by wool-gnawing, skin-eating, and other edible items. Sheep fed a high-phosphorus diet experience elevated plasma phosphorus concentrations, and the excess phosphorus reacts with calcium and magnesium ions to form phosphate precipitates. These precipitates can accumulate in the kidneys or urinary system, forming urinary stones. Wethers fed a high-phosphorus diet can develop severe urinary stones. Sheep with urinary stones experience abdominal and urethral pain, loss of appetite, weight loss, and, in severe cases, even death. To prevent urinary stones, a calcium-to-phosphorus ratio of 1.5:1 to 2.0:1 is ideal for mutton sheep to facilitate their absorption and regulation of calcium and phosphorus.

Grass typically contains a lower percentage of phosphorus, while cereals contain a higher percentage. Therefore, grazing sheep only need to pay special attention to phosphorus deficiency, while confined sheep must pay attention to an oversupply of phosphorus. Meat sheep feed can contain approximately 0.3-0.7% phosphorus, which can be supplemented with calcium dihydrogen phosphate, calcium hydrogen phosphate, fish meal, etc.

Magnesium: Magnesium is one of the elements that promotes enzyme activation and plays a vital role in coenzyme function, carbohydrate metabolism, energy transfer, and nervous system transmission. It also affects calcium and phosphorus metabolism. Magnesium deficiency can lead to poor digestibility, cramps, and coma in mutton sheep. In severe cases, mutton sheep may develop grass tetamy , which can lead to death. Excessive magnesium intake can also cause toxicity in sheep. The magnesium content of cereal feed generally ranges from 0.12% to 0.18% , while that of forage ranges from 0.3% to 0.6% . Inadequate magnesium in mutton sheep feed can be supplemented with magnesium carbonate, magnesium oxide, and magnesium sulfate.

Sulfur: Sulfur is a key component of sulfur-containing amino acids and a building block for many coenzymes. It is also a crucial factor influencing protein, fat, and carbohydrate metabolism. Sulfur deficiency can lead to decreased appetite, weight loss, salivation, metabolic disorders, and even death in sheep. It also affects the interaction between copper and molybdenum ions. However, excess sulfur can also cause diarrhea, respiratory distress, and even death in sheep.

Feed protein is rich in sulfur ions, making it less susceptible to sulfur deficiency in mutton sheep. If deficiencies occur, supplementation with copper sulfate, calcium sulfate, and zinc sulfate can be used. Rumen microbes synthesize inorganic sulfur into organic sulfur. Therefore, if adequate sulfur is provided, rumen microbes will synthesize essential amino acids such as methionine. Rumen microbes are most efficient in producing sulfur-containing amino acids when the nitrogen-to-sulfur ratio in mutton sheep feed is 10 : 1 .

Potassium: Potassium ions play a physiological role in sheep, balancing cellular fluids. Potassium deficiency can lead to nutritional and endocrine imbalances in mutton sheep, resulting in weight loss. High-potassium diets can impair magnesium utilization, causing grass cramps and lamb mortality. Because pastures contain high potassium levels, mutton sheep are less susceptible to potassium deficiency. If potassium supplementation is needed in mutton sheep feed, potassium chloride can be added.

Sodium and Chloride: Sodium and chloride ions play a physiological role in sheep, balancing osmotic pressure, pH, and body fluids. Sodium ions activate glucose-carrying proteins, while chloride ions, in addition to activating amylase, also promote gastric acid synthesis. Herbivores in tropical regions can suffer from sodium deficiency due to the low sodium content of pasture and the loss of body fluids caused by the hot climate. Sodium and chloride deficiency in mutton sheep reduces their appetite, growth rate, and protein metabolism efficiency. Therefore, 0.5% crude salt should be added to the mutton concentrate. Most animals can tolerate excessive salt intake if they are adequately watered. However, excessive salt intake can cause weight loss and physiological disorders in mutton sheep.

Trace minerals: Cobalt, copper, iodine, iron, manganese, selenium, and zinc are all trace minerals. Although the daily requirement for meat sheep is very small, they are essential components for their metabolism. A deficiency in any trace element often affects the absorption and utilization of other trace minerals, minerals, and vitamins. However, excessive trace minerals can cause toxicity in meat sheep, so they must be supplemented with caution and in moderation.

Cobalt: One of the main elements of vitamin B12 , it also activates coenzymes. Rumen microorganisms in adult sheep utilize cobalt to synthesize vitamin B12 . A deficiency can lead to weakness, slow growth, and low reproductive efficiency. The optimal cobalt concentration in a sheep's diet is 0.1 ppm per dry matter . Deficiencies can be supplemented with cobalt sulfate or carbonate. However, excessive cobalt intake can also cause symptoms such as excessive urination, salivation, and rapid breathing.

Copper: Its primary functions include hair synthesis, iron absorption, hemoglobin formation, and metabolism. Since many coenzymes require copper ions for activation, copper deficiency can lead to metabolic abnormalities. Excessive copper intake can also cause nausea, vomiting, salivation, abdominal pain, and even death in sheep. The optimal copper concentration for domestic sheep is 8 to 10 ppm per dry matter . Since most cereals are copper-deficient, copper sulfate can be added to concentrates.

Iodine: Its primary function is to synthesize thyroid hormones. Animals can become deficient in iodine if they consume forage or grains grown in iodine-deficient soil. This can lead to goiter, which is most common in young animals. Sheep require 0.4mg to 0.6mg of iodine daily. Potassium iodide can be added to concentrate feed to address iodine deficiency, but excessive iodine intake can also reduce feed intake and weight gain.

Iron: Iron primarily provides hemoglobin synthesis and acts as an enzyme activating catalyst. Therefore, animals deficient in iron can develop anemia. Iron deficiency typically occurs in lactating young animals due to the low iron content in milk. Adult animals are less susceptible to iron deficiency because their feed contains sufficient iron. Sheep require 30 to 40 mg of iron daily. Iron deficiency can be addressed by adding ferrous sulfate to the concentrate premix. However, excessive iron intake can also reduce feed intake and weight gain.

Manganese: Mainly found in the coenzyme system of meat sheep, a deficiency can affect growth, reproductive efficiency, and the functioning of the animal's central nervous system. The manganese content in breeder sheep feed is approximately 40 ppm per dry matter diet. Because manganese content varies greatly between forage and cereals, and manganese utilization by animals also varies depending on factors such as soil pH and fertilization, manganese oxide can be added to meat sheep feed.

Selenium: It is primarily related to the absorption and utilization of vitamin E. Animals deficient in selenium have low conception rates. In severe cases, afterbirth can be retained in the uterus, leading to uterine inflammation and affecting future reproductive performance. Therefore, sodium selenite and other supplements can be added to concentrates. The selenium concentration in mutton feed is approximately 0.1 mg/kg of dry matter. Excessive selenium can cause loss of appetite, tail hair loss, and death in mutton. Acutely poisoned animals may develop symptoms such as blindness and unsteady gait.

Zinc: It primarily supports the development of wool, bones, and keratin in mutton sheep. It also affects the synthesis of nucleic acids, proteins, and carbohydrates. Therefore, zinc deficiency not only affects reproductive performance but also weakens the keratin in the feet of mutton sheep. In the later stages of fattening, mutton sheep are more susceptible to hoof injuries due to their weight, which in turn affects their mobility and appetite. The zinc concentration in mutton sheep feed is approximately 50 mg/kg of dry matter. Zinc oxide or zinc sulfate can be added to mutton sheep feed.

Vitamins: Vitamins are essential for the physiological metabolism of meat sheep. Generally speaking, fresh grass contains a high amount of these vitamins, so grazing sheep are not prone to vitamin deficiencies. However, for confined sheep, when their feed consists primarily of concentrates or hay, the vitamins in these feeds are often destroyed during feed processing and hay making. Therefore, vitamin supplementation is necessary to meet the physiological needs of meat sheep.

Vitamin A is a fat-soluble vitamin, chemically called retinol. Retinol is found only in animals, with the liver being the most abundant source. Plants, on the other hand, do not contain retinol, but they do contain beta-carotene. Research has shown that beta-carotene is a precursor to retinol. When animals ingest beta-carotene, it is converted into retinol in the intestines and other organs. Vitamin A plays a vital role in the growth and development of cells and tissues. Vitamin A deficiency in mutton sheep can lead to a decreased appetite, a lack of libido in rams, and poor semen quality. In ewes, estrus is difficult and reproductive efficiency is poor. More severe deficiency can lead to eye problems and night blindness. Therefore, mutton diets should contain at least 3,500 international units (IU) of vitamin A per kilogram of concentrate.

Vitamin D: Also a fat-soluble vitamin, it primarily helps animals efficiently absorb and utilize calcium and phosphorus. Vitamin D deficiency in mutton sheep can lead to an imbalance in calcium and phosphorus absorption, affecting bone development and causing rickets and osteoporosis in both lambs and adults. Sunlight's ultraviolet rays, when applied to animal skin, convert 7-dehydrocholesterol into vitamin D3. Therefore, grazing sheep are not prone to vitamin D deficiency, but confined sheep require vitamin D supplementation. Therefore, when preparing mutton concentrate, it is important to ensure it contains at least 250 International Units (IU) of vitamin D per kilogram.

Vitamin E: Vitamin E (a-Tocopherol), also known as a reproductive alcohol, is essential for animals to maintain normal physiological functions. Vitamin E is a fat-soluble vitamin found in adipose tissue, particularly in the brain, liver, heart, adrenal cortex, and gonads. Vitamin E deficiency can lead to white muscle disease in lambs, miscarriage in ewes before birth, and retained placenta after birth. Lambs can supplement their needs with vitamin E injections, while adult sheep can supplement it with concentrate feed. Therefore, domestic sheep diets should contain at least 5 mg of vitamin E per kilogram of concentrate feed. Previously, vitamin E was added to address reproductive issues in ewes, but recent studies have shown that adding vitamin E to finishing feeds for mutton sheep not only increases feed conversion and improves growth traits, but also significantly improves meat quality, color, fatty acid rancidity, and preservation.

Vitamin K: Vitamin K can activate coagulation factors. It exists in many feed raw materials and can also be synthesized in the rumen, so it is not easy to be deficient.

Vitamin B complex and vitamin C: These are water-soluble vitamins that can be synthesized by rumen microorganisms. Therefore, vitamin B complex and vitamin C deficiency is not common in adult sheep. However, during lactation, as the rumen is not yet fully developed, vitamin B complex and vitamin C supplementation is necessary for lambs.

Water: Water is crucial for meat sheep, essential for all physiological and metabolic processes. While water content varies depending on breed, age, and environment, generally speaking, water accounts for approximately 75% of all body tissues. Water intake primarily comes from drinking water, feed water, and metabolic water. An adult sheep consumes approximately 4 to 12 liters of water per day. Lactating sheep require an additional 2.5 liters for every liter of milk produced. Factors that influence water intake include feed type, activity level, environment, temperature, and the protein and salt content of the diet. Water quality is also a key consideration when supplying meat sheep. Clean, uncontaminated water is essential. Farmers relying on groundwater as their primary water source are advised to have the water tested for contamination by heavy metals, pesticides, and nitrates. Drinking fountains are recommended as water supply equipment. If you place a bucket in the sheep pen and allow the sheep to drink freely, the bucket must be cleaned and disinfected regularly to prevent feed from falling into the bucket, contaminating the water and causing bacterial growth. Lack of water can lead to reduced feed intake, slowed growth, and decreased milk production. Therefore, sheep farmers should not neglect water supply in the sheep pen simply because it is easier to obtain.

The microorganisms in the rumen of mutton sheep are capable of synthesizing microbial proteins, but they still require adequate daily nutrition for effective utilization. A reasonable and balanced supply of nutrients, such as energy and protein, is crucial. Insufficient or uneven nutrition can lead to stunted growth and reduced reproductive efficiency, while excessive amounts of nutrients are wasted because they cannot be utilized. Therefore, formulating an appropriate feed requires a balanced diet.

In fact, over the years, advanced livestock farming countries around the world have completed numerous nutritional studies, providing a deep understanding of the energy, protein, mineral, and vitamin requirements of domestic sheep of varying weights and physiological conditions. These data serve as feeding standards, and this article adopts the 1981 standards for mutton sheep established by the US National Research Council (NRC). Based on these NRC standards and reference to domestic mutton sheep farming practices, domestic sheep farming is divided into eight stages. These stages are organized based on their dry matter intake and nutrient requirements, facilitating the calculation of balanced rations for each stage.

All grains are suitable as concentrates for goats; however, extremely high amounts of grains, especially flaked corn, can cause conditions such as acidosis. With the exception of corn and oats, it's unnecessary and undesirable to crush or process grains before feeding them to goats, as goats break them up while chewing, and crushed or processed grains often ferment too quickly. Whole grains have higher milk fat content than ground or threshed grains. Soybean meal is a common protein supplement, while bran is also commonly used in dairy goat diets. Bran has a high protein content, provides bulk, and has a laxative effect. Urea and other non-protein nitrogen sources are also commonly used in goat diets, generally at a level of less than 1% of the total concentrate. Goat concentrate preparation requires particular attention to palatability; any moldy or spoiled feed will be wasted. Therefore, even if it can be purchased very cheaply, it will be uneconomical compared to high-quality, well-manufactured materials. Bulk concentrates are prone to waste because goats will pick out ingredients they particularly like and reject others, whereas this doesn't happen with pellets or blocks. However, goats accustomed to a bulk concentrate diet may take some time to accept blocks. Giving goats ample time to adjust to a new diet is crucial, as they may refuse to eat a new concentrate for several days. In dairy goats, this can cause an immediate drop in milk production, so it's best to change the diet during the dry season or late lactation to avoid a loss in milk production. Whenever possible, it's best to start a new diet with younger goats, as these are less picky and will be well accustomed to the new diet by the time they reach production.

Formulating process for concentrated feed for dairy sheep: For a group of lactating sheep with an average weight of 60 kg, producing 3 kg of milk per day with a milk fat percentage of 4.0% , the concentrated feed consists of corn and soybean meal, and the coarse feed consists of pangla hay and corn silage. The concentrated feed is formulated as follows: 1. Calculate the nutrient requirements for each group or sheep: Net energy: 1.54 (Mcal) for maintenance, 2.1 (Mcal) for milk production ; crude protein: 105 (kg) for maintenance, 216 (kg) for milk production ; calcium: 4.0 (g) for maintenance, 9.0 (g) for milk production ; phosphorus: 2.8 (g) for maintenance, 6.3 (g) for milk production . 2. Estimating Forage Dry Matter Intake: According to NRC (1981) , a 60 kg lactating sheep requires 4% of its body weight ( or 2.4 kg ) of dry matter intake , of which feed intake is approximately 2% of its body weight. This means that 1.2 kg of forage (60 x 0.02 kg) should be provided, consisting of 0.6 kg of pangolin hay and 0.6 kg of corn silage. 3. Calculating Concentrate Requirements: After deducting the hay requirement, the remaining nutrient deficit is met by concentrate. This primarily balances energy needs. Since a net energy deficit of 2.32 Mcal is observed after providing 1.2 kg of forage, the energy requirement can be met by adding 1.14 kg of corn (2.03 Mcal/kg of DM). However, the protein content is insufficient, so it should be calculated with soybean meal. Assuming X kg of corn and Y kg of soybean meal, the following equation can be used: ( Corn net energy 2.03 Mcal/kg, crude protein 10%; soybean meal net energy 1.86 Mcal/kg, crude protein 50%): Energy: 2.03X + 1.86Y = 2.32; Protein: 0.10X + 0.50Y = 0.21. Therefore, adding 0.97 kg of corn and 0.23 kg of soybean meal can meet both energy and protein requirements. 4. Check the mineral balance: If calcium or phosphorus is insufficient, mineral supplements can be used to meet the needs. After supplementing the feed and concentrate, it was found that the calcium and phosphorus levels were 8 grams and 0.6 grams respectively. After adding 0.004 kg of calcium phosphate (each kg of calcium phosphate contains 210 grams of calcium and 180 grams of phosphorus, respectively), and 0.022 kg of limestone, the needs were met. 5. Check dry matter intake and pickled fiber intake and make sure they meet the requirements after adding them up.

Lamb concentrate formulation process: For a flock of lambs weighing an average of 20 kg and gaining 50 grams daily, the concentrate consists of corn and soybean meal, and the roughage consists of pangolin grass. The concentrate formulation is as follows: 1. Calculate the nutrient requirements for each flock or head of lamb: Digestible energy: 1.18 Mcal for maintenance, 0.44 Mcal for milk production; Crude protein: 38 kg for maintenance, 14 kg for milk production; Calcium: 2.0 g for maintenance, 1.0 g for milk production; Phosphorus: 1.4 g for maintenance, 0.7 g for milk production. 2. According to the NRC (1981), dry matter intake is 2.2% of body weight, or approximately 440 grams, of which 70 grams (0.35 % of body weight) of forage is provided . 3. Calculate concentrate requirements: After deducting hay requirements, the remaining nutrient deficit is provided by concentrate. This section primarily addresses energy needs. After providing 70 grams of forage, a net energy deficit of 1.45 Mcal and a protein deficit of 45 grams were observed. Assuming X kilograms of corn and Y kilograms of soybean meal , the following equation can be used to solve these problems: (corn digestible energy 4.0 Mcal/kg, 10% crude protein; soybean meal digestible energy 4.16 Mcal/kg, 50% crude protein): Energy: 4.0X + 4.16Y = 1.45; Protein: 0.10X + 0.50Y = 45. Therefore, adding 340 grams of corn and 22 grams of soybean meal would meet energy and protein requirements . 4. Checking the mineral balance: After providing forage and supplementing concentrate, calcium and phosphorus deficits of 2.56 and 0.79 grams, respectively, were observed. These deficits can be addressed by adding 5 grams of calcium phosphate and 5 grams of limestone. 5. Total daily dry matter intake should meet requirements.

Formulating a concentrate feed for mutton sheep: A flock of fattening wethers weighing an average of 30 kg and gaining 150 g daily is fed a concentrate diet consisting of corn and barley, and a roughage diet consisting of pennisetum. The concentrate feed is formulated as follows: 1. Calculate the nutrient requirements for each flock or head of sheep: Metabolizable Energy: 2.91 Mcal ; Crude Protein: 93 kg ; Calcium: 4 g ; Phosphorus: 2.8 g . 2. According to the NRC (1981), dry matter intake is 3.2% of body weight , or 960 g. Assume that 500 g of forage ( 52% of the ration ) is provided . 3. Calculate concentrate feed requirements: After deducting the hay requirement, the nutrient deficit is provided by concentrate feed. This portion primarily balances energy requirements. After providing 500 g of forage, a digestible energy deficit of 1.72 Mcal and protein of 49.5 g is found. Adding 400 grams of corn and 80 grams of barley can meet both protein and energy requirements (corn has a digestible energy of 3.75 Mcal/kg and crude protein of 10%; barley has a digestible energy of 3.70 Mcal/kg and crude protein of 13.5%). 4. After providing both forage and concentrate, a calcium deficiency of 0.84 grams and an increase of 0.71 grams of phosphorus were found. To meet the calcium-to-phosphorus ratio of 1.48, 6 grams of limestone (containing 340 grams of calcium per kilogram) were added to meet this critical calcium-to-phosphorus ratio.

Forage, or fodder, is the primary feed for herbivores. Whether the quality of forage meets the animals' needs and whether a sufficient and stable supply is available are key factors influencing the development of the herbivore industry. According to the 2003 Agricultural Annual Report of the Council of Agriculture, Executive Yuan , Taiwan's total annual dry matter forage production is approximately 257,975 metric tons, which only meets approximately 51.4% of the total annual demand for herbivores. The shortfall requires the use of imported hay. In recent years, Taiwan's average annual hay imports have been approximately 230,113 metric tons. Although Taiwan is located in the subtropics, the island is mountainous and experiences a variety of climates. Numerous forage grass species are cultivated or experimentally grown, including sedge, pennisetum, South African pigeon grass, bermuda grass, creeping clover, geranium, bahia grass, kayu grass, ryegrass, oats, green corn, sorghum, alfalfa, tropical kudzu, mountain pea, sedge bean, Java soybean, and Egyptian clover. However, commercial production primarily focuses on sedge, pennisetum, and green corn. In recent years, the Nile grass, developed at the Animal Husbandry Research Institute, has also been gradually promoted. Smaller amounts of alfalfa and Egyptian clover are also produced. Foreign forage grasses offer even more varieties. In addition to the aforementioned, there are dozens of other species, including red clover, white clover, timothy, bluegrass, perennial ryegrass, orchard grass, tall foxgrass, and paspalum. However, the main forage grasses imported into Taiwan are alfalfa, bermuda grass, timothy, and oats. The following will provide an extensive discussion of domestic and imported forage grasses, focusing on their characteristics, quality evaluation, and quality variations and grading, providing a reference for forage selection and utilization.

Forage Species Characteristics: A rich variety of forage resources are used worldwide, encompassing hundreds of species, ranging from grasses, legumes, broadleaf weeds, and sedges. However, grasses from the Poaceae and Legume families are predominant. Grasses are divided into two categories based on their growth characteristics : cool-season grasses ( C3 grasses) and warm -season grasses ( C4 grasses) . These differences primarily stem from their photosynthetic patterns and temperature adaptability. Cool-season grasses and warm-season grasses have C3 and C4 photosynthetic patterns , respectively. C4 grasses have taller leaves. Under light intensities exceeding 1200 umol m² s' , they have higher fiber content than C3 grasses. Their photosynthetic efficiency, however, can still increase with increasing light intensity, compared to C4 grasses. However, photosynthesis in C3 grasses approaches saturation. Temperate grasses are better adapted to low temperatures, photosynthesizing below 10 °C and inhibited above 30 °C. Tropical grasses, on the other hand, are better adapted to high temperatures. Therefore, temperate grasses are naturally distributed in cool temperate regions, while tropical grasses are more common in lower latitudes. Furthermore, tropical grasses have better water use efficiency than temperate grasses and are generally more drought-tolerant . Because C4 grasses have specialized vascular bundle sheaths, the proportion of mesophyll cells per unit leaf area in tropical grasses is lower ( mesophyll cells have a higher protein and digestible content ) . Consequently, at the same maturity, tropical grasses have lower crude protein content and slower digestion rates than temperate grasses. Legumes also follow a C3 photosynthesis pattern, but photosynthesis rates vary significantly among species, and they also have varying temperature adaptability. Alfalfa has a relatively high photosynthetic rate among legumes, with a rate between C3 and C4 grasses. It also has a wide temperature adaptability, exhibiting both temperate and tropical characteristics. Trifolium repens is a typical temperate legume, with photosynthesis inhibited by high temperatures, while kudzu is adapted to tropical climates and is a typical tropical legume.

In addition to photosynthesis patterns, various grass species, varieties, and ecotypes each possess unique characteristics due to long-term adaptation and human selection, resulting in distinct growth advantages and limitations. Consequently, different grasses have specific distribution areas and nutrient compositions, which in turn influence their nutritional value. However, overall, legumes are superior in quality to temperate grasses, which in turn are superior to tropical grasses. Pangu grass and Pennisetum, which make up the majority of domestically produced grass, are both typical tropical grasses characterized by low digestibility and protein content. Consequently, large quantities of alfalfa are imported annually to supplement these deficiencies . Another major imported grass is Bermuda grass, also a tropical grass. Although its price exceeds its actual nutritional value, it is also popular with livestock farmers due to its commercial availability.

Overview of forage quality: Forage quality is determined by two factors: "nutritional value" and "feed intake". The nutritional value is determined by the composition of the forage and the proportion that can be used by animals (digestibility). Plant cells consist of two parts: highly digestible cell contents (protein, sugar, fat, starch, pectin, etc.) and less digestible cell wall structures (cellulose, hemicellulose, lignin, etc.). High-quality forage has thin cell walls and a high proportion of cell contents. Therefore, it has high nutritional value, is easy to digest, has good palatability, and has a higher feed intake. Low-quality forage has a higher proportion of cell walls and contains more indigestible substances (such as lignin), resulting in low nutritional value and animal feed intake.

The quality requirements are determined by the grade of livestock, and different animals have different forage requirements. High-yielding lactating cows require forage with high digestibility, high energy, and high protein. If high-yielding lactating cows are fed low-quality forage, their milk production will never match that of cows fed high-quality forage. Compared to high-quality forage, low-quality forage remains in the rumen longer, resulting in reduced feed intake and output. This loss cannot be fully compensated even with supplements. Compared to high-yielding lactating cows, low-yielding or dry cows have lower nutritional requirements and do not require the highest-grade forage for efficient feeding.

Silage is a commonly used method of preserving forage. If the conditions are properly controlled, the nutritional value of forage can be effectively preserved. However, if the silage fermentation is poor or the feeding management is improper, it will not only reduce the nutritional value of the forage, but also reduce the palatability and digestibility, seriously affecting the utilization efficiency of the forage.

Forage intake is a lesser-studied and more complex issue. Measuring intake involves factors other than the forage itself, as well as factors related to the animal and the effects of management. Strictly speaking, aside from direct intake measurements, there is currently no simple and rapid method that can fully represent ad libitum intake. However, in certain applications, a negative correlation exists between medium-wash fiber content and ad libitum intake; therefore, for the same forage, medium-wash fiber content is often used as an indicator of ad libitum intake. In addition to the aforementioned factors, palatability is also a factor that cannot be ignored. The inclusion of dust in baled hay or its deterioration due to moisture during storage can easily affect palatability and reduce ad libitum intake.

Factors influencing forage quality: The primary factors are forage type, maturity at harvest, and preparation and storage methods. Secondary factors include soil fertility or fertilization, temperature during the growing season, and variety. The impact of forage type has been discussed above; the remaining factors are explained below: (1) Temperature: Forages grown at high temperatures have lower quality than those grown at lower temperatures. Forages grown at 10-15°C (10-15°C) have a leaf percentage of 59%, but at 20-25°C (20-25°C), this percentage is only 36%. (2) Maturity: Harvest maturity is the primary factor influencing a particular forage. Forage quality decreases with maturity. Dry matter digestibility of forage exceeds 80% in the first two to three weeks of growth, then decreases by 0.3-0.5% per day until it reaches approximately 50%. Harvest maturity also affects animal feed intake. As forage matures, its fiber content increases, causing animal feed intake to decline rapidly. As plants age, the mid-fat fiber content increases and the feeding potential decreases because the digestion rate of mid-fat fiber is lower than that of non-fiber parts. At the same time, the more mature the plant, the lower the fiber digestion rate. (3) Leaf/stem ratio: As forage ages (or matures) and is conditioned under adverse conditions, the leaf-stem ratio decreases and the forage quality decreases. The quality of the leaves of forage is higher than that of the stems, and the proportion of leaves decreases as the forage matures. For example, the crude protein content of the old stems of alfalfa may be less than 10%, while the content of the leaves can be as high as 24%. The quality of the upper leaves (young leaves) and lower leaves of alfalfa is similar, but the quality of the young stems and old stems is very different. Reproductive growth significantly reduces the leaf/stem ratio, which means that the quality is reduced. (4) Mixed growth of grasses and beans: Mixed forages of grasses and beans often have higher crude protein content and lower fiber content than pure grasses. Bermuda grass mixed with various legumes (without nitrogen fertilizer) produces forages with a crude protein content of 11% to 13%, while areas planted solely with Bermuda grass receiving 90 kg/ha of nitrogen fertilizer have a crude protein content of only 11%. Alfalfa mixed with 30% timothy hay has a crude protein content of 17.5%, while hay grown solely with alfalfa has a crude protein content of 25%. (5) Fertilization: Nitrogen fertilizer can indeed increase the crude protein content of grasses, but legumes do not require nitrogen fertilizer. Fertilization generally has no effect on improving digestibility. Phosphorus or potassium fertilizers can increase grass yield, but due to rapid growth, they may slightly reduce forage quality. Excessive amounts of certain elements in forages, such as potassium, may reduce the availability of magnesium in the diet. (6) Diurnal Variation: The water-soluble carbohydrate content of forages varies significantly throughout the day. During the day, plants accumulate water-soluble carbohydrates and consume them at night.
