Therefore, water-soluble carbohydrate content is lowest in the early morning and reaches its highest point after a period of intense light. Therefore, in low-rainfall climates, alfalfa harvested in the afternoon is of better quality than that harvested in the morning; and Pennisetum harvested after noon is more suitable for silage preparation. (VII) Variety Efficacy: Breeding may help improve forage quality. The digestibility of the Coastcross1 Bermuda strain can be increased by 12% compared to the Coastal strain, and the daily weight gain of beef cattle can be increased by 30%; the digestibility of late-maturing timothy grass decreases more slowly than that of early-maturing varieties; silage-type corn varieties have a higher grain content and a higher stem digestibility than other varieties; Pennisetum Taixucao No. 2 is easier to ensilage than older varieties due to its high water-soluble carbohydrate content. (VIII) Harvesting and Storage: In the early stages of forage harvest, the hydrolytic and respiratory enzymes in the cells are still functional, and nutrients are continuously decomposed and consumed, resulting in dry matter loss and the conversion of some protein into non-protein nitrogen. Prolonged curing time due to rain also significantly depletes nutrients in the plant. During hay storage, the digestibility of dry matter and other nutritional components decreases. Hay with a high moisture content will generate heat, forming more acid-insoluble nitrogen through the Millard reaction (polymerization of sugars or carbohydrates with amino acids due to respiratory heat), reducing the efficiency of protein utilization.

Sensory Assessment of Forage Quality: Observing the appearance of harvested forage on-site, such as leaf ratio, bale color, and the presence of spikes and other debris, can provide a rough assessment of forage quality. A high leaf content and greener leaf color indicate higher quality, but color alone is not a reliable indicator of quality. Different hay varieties vary in color, and excessive exposure to the sun or light rain can also affect hay color, but it can still be considered high-quality hay. Generally, the fiber content of forage increases after flowering, so a high spike content indicates lower quality. However, the relationship between ripeness and quality differs from that of standard forage, as green-cut corn kernels are a key source of energy. Weeds and grasses have low feeding value, while wire, nylon rope, and other impurities can easily cause harm to animals. Other impurities can also reduce palatability. Therefore, the presence of debris in hay significantly reduces its quality. Odor is also an important indicator. The presence of a musty or rancid odor indicates lower quality, and animals are less receptive to forage with a poor odor.

Nutrient Analysis: The nutritional content of forage grass varies depending on its variety, maturity, and growing environment. Therefore, accurate quantitative descriptions of its quality require compositional analysis. Traditional compositional analysis is performed using chemical analysis methods, primarily focusing on dry matter percentage, crude protein, acid-washed fiber, and medium-washed fiber. Various nutritional indicators can be derived from these results.

Dry matter (DM): Dry matter is the percentage of forage weight after removing moisture (DM% = 100 - % moisture). Understanding dry matter is important because, firstly, rations are formulated on a dry basis, and animal feed intake is expressed as dry matter intake, not feed volume. Secondly, forage nutrients are present in the dry matter portion. Furthermore, dry matter can serve as an indicator of forage storage conditions. Forage that is too dry for silage or too wet for baling can easily heat up, reduce protein availability, and cause mold. Forage that is too wet for silage can easily leak and cause improper fermentation losses.

Crude protein (CP): Crude protein includes true protein and non-protein nitrogen. The measurement method is to analyze the total nitrogen content and then multiply it by 6.25. The crude protein content can show whether the feed has the ability to meet the protein needs of animals. Feeding forage with medium to high crude protein content (14~20%) can reduce the purchase of protein supplements. The crude protein content will change with the harvest period of the forage. The higher the maturity, the lower the crude protein content, but the total crude protein quality per unit production area can increase with maturity. With proper management and harvesting, forage quality that is sufficient to meet the nutritional needs of animals can be obtained while also having appropriate yields.

Available Crude Protein (ACP): Available crude protein refers to the percentage of crude protein that can be effectively utilized by animals. For non-heating forage, no more than 12% of crude protein is contained in acid-washed fiber. For example, alfalfa with a crude protein content of 20% has an acid-washed fiber that contains no more than 2.4% crude protein. When the crude protein content of acid-washed fiber exceeds 12%, it indicates that the harvesting or storage process was improper, resulting in a decrease in the availability of crude protein. The more crude protein contained in the acid-washed fiber, the lower the crude protein availability.

Acid Detergent Fiber (ADF): Acid Detergent Fiber refers to the proportion of indigestible or slowly digestible substances in a forage or feed, including cellulose, lignin, and pectin. A low ADF content indicates a high digestibility ratio, indicating higher quality forage.

Neutral Detergent Fiber (NDF): Neutral Detergent Fiber refers to the proportion of cell walls or fiber in a forage or feed, including acid-detergent fiber (excluding pectin) and hemicellulose. NDF is inversely proportional to an animal's feeding potential; low NDF levels indicate high feed intake. However, ruminants must have a certain amount of fiber in their diets to facilitate this process.

Total Digestible Nutrients (TDN): TDN is commonly used to express the energy content of feed or forage. The TDN reported in the analysis is calculated from crude protein, acid-washed fiber, and other components.

Net Energy (NE): Net energy is a more detailed calculation of energy than gross digestible nutrients. It includes net energy for lactation, net energy for maintenance, and net energy for growth, all expressed in Mcal/kg. Net energy for maintenance and growth is primarily used in formulating beef cattle rations, while net energy for lactation is used in formulating dairy cattle rations. Dietary energy is equally effective for maintaining and lactating milk production, but is less effective for weight gain (growth).

Relative Feed Value (RFV): RFV combines key nutritional factors, including feed intake potential and digestibility, into a single index, allowing for a quick and easy assessment of diet quality and feeding effectiveness. The formula for calculating RFV is derived from the estimated digestibility of acid-washed fiber and medium-washed fiber, respectively, and feed intake.

Minerals: All forages should be analyzed for calcium and phosphorus. Many routine analyses also include other minerals, especially in areas prone to mineral deficiencies. The mineral content of forages can reduce the need for expensive mineral supplements.

Traditional chemical analysis is time-consuming and expensive. Accurately adjusting diets, monitoring nutritional status, and selecting forage raw materials based on forage quality fluctuations requires rapid analysis. In recent years, the use of near-infrared spectroscopy for rapid forage quality analysis has become a trend. In addition to its rapid analysis speed, near-infrared spectroscopy offers advantages such as simple sample preparation, non-destructiveness, the ability to analyze multiple components simultaneously, and high accuracy and reliability. Domestically, we have developed a system for analyzing the general quality components of domestically produced sedge grass, pennisetum, green cut (silage) corn, and major imported grasses.

Hay Sampling Method: Hay sampling and analysis aims to represent the quality of the entire batch, so careful sampling methods are crucial to ensure that the sample is representative. A batch of hay is defined as hay of the same seed composition, same pasture, and maturity, cut within a certain timeframe (generally no more than 48 hours), and baled under the same weather conditions. If the same large pasture has different seed compositions, soil properties, weed infestation, maturity, cutting time, weather damage, or other factors that may alter hay value, each batch should be separated into different batches. If the stacking conditions of a batch vary significantly, more bales should be inspected or the hay should be divided into smaller batches. At least 15-20 bales should be randomly sampled and mixed to represent the entire batch. The Canadian Grassland Association's certification system stipulates that for small square bales under 1,000 bales, 20 bales must be sampled; for bales between 1,000 and 10,000 bales, 30 bales must be sampled ; and for bales over 10,000 bales, 50 bales must be sampled . For round bales under 50 bales , 20 bales must be sampled; for bales between 51 and 500 bales, 30 bales must be sampled ; and for bales over 500 bales, 50 bales must be sampled. To ensure uniform sampling of all layers, sampling should be done with a corer. The corer should be at least 12 inches long, with an inner diameter between 0.5 inches and 1.25 inches. It should be capable of obtaining 20 core samples, totaling at least one pound or one liter. Core samples should be obtained from bales at varying heights and depths along the pile. Insert the corer vertically from the side of the bale toward the center.

Pangolagrass ( Digitaria decumbens) is a tropical grass and one of the highest-quality tropical grasses. However, there's a significant difference in quality between high-quality pangolagrass harvested at the right time and stored well, and low-quality pangolagrass harvested after aging and stored poorly.

Pre-harvest quality changes of pangu grass: As pangu grass plants mature, their medium and pickled fiber content increases and their crude protein content decreases. Yield and quality are negatively correlated; delayed harvesting can yield higher yields, but not high-quality hay. Pangu grass grows rapidly in high temperatures, accumulating indigestible parts. Furthermore, pangu grass tends to flower in the summer, further accelerating aging. Consequently, seasonal variations in pangu grass quality are evident. Summer-produced pangu grass must be harvested as early as possible (within six weeks), ideally before heading, to ensure quality. Furthermore, summer coincides with Taiwan's rainy season, making timely harvesting challenging in the field. This is especially true with insufficient harvesting machinery. Many fields cannot seize sunny days to harvest, often delaying harvest for months. This results in a lower nutritional value and should be classified as poorer quality, as can be determined by grass length and leaf-to-stem ratio.

Although Taiwan is a small region, hay harvesting seasons vary from region to region, and the optimal harvest period also varies. In northern Taiwan, winter temperatures are lower, and pan-ko grass stops growing, with the production season concentrated between May and September. In southern Taiwan, due to the rainy summers, hay preparation is difficult, requiring careful timing for preparation or silage. Winters are still mild, and in areas with irrigation, autumn, winter, and spring are the prime production seasons. During these periods, pan-ko grass grows slowly, preventing its nutritional value from rapidly declining. Furthermore, the lower humidity allows for the production of higher-quality hay.

Impacts of Pangu hay curing: Pangu hay has a moisture content of approximately 80% at harvest. Before drying to a certain moisture content (approximately 30%), respiration, saccharification, microbial activity, and other physiological reactions continue, resulting in material losses. Most of this loss is in the digestible portion, and the decomposed nitrogen is easily lost through evaporation or leaching. This results in an increase in mid- and acid-washed fiber and a decrease in crude protein. The decrease in crude protein is particularly significant at high temperatures. Rain during curing is a common adverse condition for domestic Pangu hay. Heavy rain can cause leaching of soluble carbohydrates, soluble nitrogen, soluble minerals, and fat, resulting in significant dry matter losses. This increases the percentages of mid- and acid-washed fiber and decreases the percentage of crude protein. If the rain is light, the impact on hay quality is primarily due to the prolonged drying time, during which microbial activity within the hay continues, consuming nutrients. Freshly cut hay is less affected by rain. A light rain exposure, followed by rapid sun-drying, has minimal impact on quality.

Hay quality changes during storage: Hay quality continues to change during storage, with the degree of change varying depending on the moisture content at the time of baling and the temperature and humidity conditions during storage. The most rapid quality deterioration typically occurs within one month of baling. High moisture content at the time of baling, as well as high temperature and humidity in the storage environment, can easily lead to deterioration of hay quality. In Taiwan, under good storage conditions (ventilated and roofed), the moisture content of hay bales can drop below 11% after two months of storage, regardless of the initial moisture content, and the quality tends to stabilize. Poor storage conditions can lead to quality deterioration. The bales may appear yellow-green to yellow-brown or dark brown, or moldy, and may develop a burnt, alcoholic, or musty odor. Regarding compositional changes, crude protein content does not change significantly, but the indigestible fraction increases. Sometimes, high-moisture bales, due to greater dry matter loss, may actually increase the crude protein percentage, increase the percentage of medium-washed fiber and acid-washed fiber, and decrease digestibility. Taiwan's summers are hot and rainy, so the ideal moisture content for round bales is below 15%. If the bales are packaged at 20%, they will immediately heat up. If the climate is dry during packaging and storage (such as in autumn, winter, and early spring in southern Taiwan), an 18% moisture content is considered a safe moisture content for packaging . The safe moisture content for square bales can be 2% higher than for round bales.

Pennisetum purpurreum: Pennisetum purpurreum is a perennial, C4 , upright tropical forage grass with a tall plant structure. Its fresh grass yield can reach 250-400 metric tons per hectare, primarily used for forage and silage. In recent years, thanks to the popularization of the name "Taixu Grass No. 2," Pennisetum has seen significant increases in both yield and quality. Several high-quality dwarf and semi-dwarf varieties of Pennisetum are still available, but Taixu Grass No. 2 remains the primary variety currently cultivated. Because Pennisetum can be harvested year-round, its quality varies significantly depending on local climate, cultivation conditions, and maturity at harvest. The following describes the impact of various quality factors on green-cut quality.

Differences between Pennisetum strains: There are numerous Pennisetum strains, each with its own distinct plant shape and growth characteristics. Consequently, quality varies depending on the strain. Currently, the main cultivated strain is the Taichu No. 2.

The Impact of Harvest Maturity on Pennisetum: Maturity is the most important factor affecting Pennisetum quality. Regardless of whether the harvest indicator is days or height, delaying harvesting increases height and dry matter content, which may increase yield. However, this can significantly reduce quality, leading to a decrease in leaf-to-stem ratio, reduced crude protein content, increased fiber content, and decreased in vitro dry matter digestibility. Furthermore, the stem ages more rapidly than the leaves. While the content of water-soluble carbohydrates is higher in mature plants, this does not increase with delayed harvesting.

The impact of Pennisetum on seasons: As temperatures rise in summer, Pennisetum plants grow faster, resulting in taller plants and lower dry matter content. At the same time, the proportion of plant cell walls increases, the fiber content increases, the leaf-to-stem ratio decreases, and aging is also faster. Therefore, the green feed quality of Pennisetum in summer is lower; the quality of Pennisetum in other seasons is relatively better than in summer. However, adjusting cultivation and harvest management can also achieve better feed quality in summer, such as shortening the harvesting days and reducing the amount of fertilizer.

The Impact of Pennisetum on Fertility: Fertility significantly impacts the yield and yield traits of Pennisetum, particularly nitrogen fertilizer application. Increasing nitrogen fertilizer application rates for both tall and short-stemmed Pennisetum species helps increase leaf area, facilitates herb distribution, and boosts both fresh and hay yields. Phosphorus and potassium fertilizers have less of an impact. Regarding Pennisetum quality, nitrogen fertilizer application may increase crude protein content but has a limited effect on fiber content. Phosphorus and potassium fertilizers also have little effect on crude protein and fiber content. However, excessive potassium fertilizer application may result in an excessively high K/(Ca+Mg) ratio, which can easily lead to adverse physiological symptoms such as convulsions.

Green-cut corn: Green-cut corn refers to corn that has been harvested whole for animal consumption. It can be standard feed or food corn, or specialized green-cut corn varieties. Currently, the main specialized varieties in Taiwan include Tainan No. 19, Tainong No. 3, and Tainan No. 21. Corn is a single-year tropical grass plant that is adaptable to a wide range of temperatures and can be cultivated in both temperate and tropical zones. It is one of the most widely used forage crops worldwide. Because green-cut corn has cobs and contains large amounts of starch and sugars, it provides a higher-energy, high-quality forage crop than regular grasses, earning it the reputation of "king of forages." Harvested at the right time, it has an appropriate moisture content (approximately 65-70%) and sufficient water-soluble carbohydrates, making it easy to prepare into silage.

The vegetative growth phase of corn is from seed germination to flowering. During this period, yields are still low, and harvesting is not typically carried out. The reproductive growth phase begins with flowering and silking. The United States divides the reproductive growth phase into six stages: R1, R6 . These stages correspond to the silking, filling, milky, paste, yellow, and mature stages in China. The length of each growth stage is greatly affected by temperature. Temperate countries generally have one harvest per year, with both the vegetative and reproductive phases being longer. Taiwan generally has two harvests per year, with a relatively short reproductive phase, making it more difficult to determine the optimal harvesting time for silage. From the kernel filling stage on, the moisture content of the corn plant decreases dramatically daily from 80-85% to approximately 50-60% at mature stage . The rate of moisture reduction is affected by maturity and rainfall. The recommended harvesting period is around the yellow-ripe stage, but silage material requires more precise moisture control. The whey line, the dividing line between the whey and the hard part of the corn kernel's endosperm (referred to as the whey line), can be used as a harvest indicator, as it correlates well with the overall plant moisture content (although there is still some error).

Green-cut corn gradually increases in seed-to-grain ratio from the filling stage onward. After reaching the yellow ripening stage, the digestibility of the whole plant increases slightly, while the crude protein content decreases. Starch content in the whole plant gradually accumulates from the filling stage. During the early stages of ear ripening, water-soluble carbohydrates predominate in the non-structural carbohydrate content of the whole plant. However, starch becomes the primary component in later ripening, with starch accumulation peaking from the early milky to pasty stages. Starch digestibility decreases after yellow ripening, and rupturing the corn kernels during harvesting with a press can improve digestibility.

Nilegrass: Nilegrass, named Taichucao No. 1 , is a newly developed forage grass strain developed by the Animal Products Research Institute in 1999. Nilegrass (Acroceras macrum , nilegrass) is an upright, clumping temperate forage grass with a plant form somewhat similar to that of sedge, also with a creeping nature. It has strong growth vigour, is rust-resistant, and is relatively drought-tolerant.

The crude protein content of various Nilegrass strains decreases with increasing cutting time, while the medium-washed fiber and acid-washed fiber contents increase. The factors influencing and varying characteristics of Nilegrass quality are generally similar to those of Pangu grass, while the crude protein content is slightly higher than Pangu grass, and the medium- and acid-washed fiber contents are similar to those of Pangu grass. In terms of yield and quality, a cutting time of approximately 5-6 days is recommended for high yield and good hay quality. Using the height of the top leaf collar as an indicator trait provides a simple and effective way to estimate Nilegrass yield and quality.

Alfalfa ( Medicago sativa), native to southwestern Asia, is a highly nutritious legume with a long history of cultivation. Many countries around the world consider it a primary forage resource for livestock farming, earning it the reputation of "after fodder." Alfalfa is a perennial legume that grows in upright, clumps. Its leaves are pinnate, trifoliate, and arranged in whorls. The plant is 60 to 90 centimeters tall, with 5 to 25 branches and a taproot that can reach depths of 7 to 9 meters. Its inflorescence is sparsely racemose, ranging in color from purple to yellow, with 2 to 5 whorls. Each pod contains several kidney-shaped seeds. There are many varieties of alfalfa. Cultivated alfalfa can be broadly divided into four groups: Common , Variegated , Turkistan , and Nonhardy. Common , with its purple flowers, is a composite variety resulting from years of adaptation and selection of strains from multiple regions. It is often distinguished by its growing region, such as Montana Common and Kansas Common , and has different regional adaptability characteristics depending on the breeding area . Variegated , characterized by its variegated flower colors, ranging from purple to blue and white to yellow, is a hybrid of purple and yellow alfalfa and is extremely cold-resistant. Variegated varieties include Grimm , Ladak , Ranger , Atlantic , and Narragansett . Turkistan , like Common , belongs to the purple-flowered group and is cold-resistant, drought-resistant, and resistant to bacterial wilt. However, it also has unfavorable characteristics such as slow regeneration, low yield, low seed production, and susceptibility to leaf spot, making it a common source of material for disease-resistant breeding . Alfalfa is hardy, fast-growing and regenerating, but not cold-tolerant. Indian and African varieties are introduced from India and Africa, respectively, and are suitable for cultivation at lower latitudes. Generally speaking, alfalfa has a wide temperature adaptability and is well-suited to cultivation in well-drained areas. It is drought-tolerant but not wet-tolerant. Compared to other forage grasses, alfalfa contains a high amount of digestible protein and has a high feeding value. However, its quality varies considerably depending on the production region, harvest maturity, and variety characteristics.

Timothy (Phleum pratense) is native to northern Eurasia and is now found throughout temperate regions worldwide. It is a short-lived, perennial temperate grass that thrives in cool, wet climates. It is widely cultivated in the northern United States and Canada, either alone or in admixture with other grasses. Timothy is an upright, clumping grass, 60 to 120 cm tall, with densely packed spikelets forming a long, cylindrical shape. It has a shallow, fibrous root system and lacks underground roots or runners, making it incapable of vegetative propagation. New meadows are established by sowing, resulting in high seed yields and rapid, easy reestablishment. Timothy is primarily used for hay production. In meadows mixed with clover or alfalfa, a single cutting is often used to make hay, which is then used for grazing.

Bermudagrass (Cynodon dactylon) is a group of deep-rooted, tropical perennial grasses found in tropical and subtropical regions worldwide. They thrive in temperatures above 23°C and are drought-tolerant. While they tolerate flooding, their growth is limited by waterlogging. Bermudagrass exhibits significant variation within species, with numerous varieties including Common, Giant Coastal, Tifton44, and Tifton85. Common is a local variety also used for turfgrass. It has small stems and leaves and is harvested for hay or as the remaining stalks after turfgrass seed harvesting. Its nutritional value is low. Both the Coastal and Tifton series are improved forage grasses with taller plants and larger leaves, but their cultivation has been limited. Bermudagrass production in the southern United States still primarily relies on local varieties. Bermudagrass imported into Taiwan is primarily a thin-stemmed local variety. Bermuda grass is the largest imported grass in the family Poaceae. Its advantages are that it is finely chopped, dried and well packaged. Although the acid-washed fiber content is lower than that of Bermuda grass, its dry matter digestibility is not higher.

Imported Hay Quality Grading: Taiwan imports a vast amount of hay annually. Each type of hay has varying quality grades, and even batches of the same type can vary significantly. Analytical data from the Hengchun Branch of the Animal Research Institute shows that the mean and standard deviation values for crude protein, medium-washed fiber, and acid-washed fiber content of imported alfalfa, Bermuda grass, oats, and Timothy are close to those in the United States, indicating that various hay grades are likely entering Taiwan. Further understanding of the quality fluctuations of imported hay and the grading system for exported hay based on its origin is crucial. The United States currently lacks a nationally consistent grading system, and the single standard proposed by the American Grass and Pasture Association, encompassing legumes, grasses, and mixed grass-legume forages, has not been widely adopted. The grading system for alfalfa is the most comprehensive. As early as 1933, the United States had a federal standard based on appearance. Although it has been revised many times, it has not been widely used because the appearance grading lacks objectivity and has little correlation with animal performance. Later, with the development of component determination, grading systems that can reflect feeding value have been established. However, the grading systems are currently inconsistent between states, and most are based on feeding value.

Grading of grasses is less sophisticated than alfalfa. Currently, most grasses are graded based on appearance. The USDA's unified standard only measures crude protein, while individual producing regions have their own standards based on export needs. Bermuda grass can be broadly divided into straw and hay. Bermuda grass, a byproduct of lawn seed harvest, has lower nutritional value and is rarely exported. A survey of distributors in Southern California found that Bermuda hay is classified into grades A , B , C , and D based on length, color, texture, weed content, and the presence of old grass. Each grade is further subdivided into grades of + , 0 , and - . Only grades C and above are intended for export . Canadian export timothy hay is also graded based on appearance, with color being the primary criterion. Recently, optical color scanning systems have been developed as an objective grading standard. In summary, current grass grading standards, which primarily rely on appearance, are subjective and fail to reflect feeding value. Differing standards and preferences in importing regions further complicate grading. With the advancement of rapid quality measurement, rapid methods that can reflect feeding value and objectively grade grass hay will play a crucial role.

Behavior of dairy goats: Based on the available data, only a brief introduction can be given to the study of goat behavior. Many inferences about goat behavior patterns are mostly derived from animals of more closely related genera such as sheep, deer, antelope or elk. Among these animals of similar genera, many behavioral characteristics are indeed similar, but it is also important to understand some basic behavioral differences between them. Understanding the unique behavior of goats can help managers establish a flock management system in an effective manner. Understanding goat behavior patterns can achieve at least the following two advantages. First, it enables managers to provide a more consistent and efficient management system to increase economic benefits. Second, the more knowledge related to goat behavior is learned, the more it will help promote a deeper understanding of goats and increase the enjoyment of raising goats. Goats have nine recognized behavioral patterns, of which ingestive and allelomimetic behaviors (imitating) are the most interesting. Sexual behavior , agonistic or antagonistic behavior , epimeletic behavior (care-giving maternal) , and et-epimeletic behavior (care-seeking ) are also important, and all of these behaviors are seasonal. The true importance of specific eliminative behaviors is still less understood.

Grazing : Goats differ from other ruminants in their preference for browsing , contrary to other herbivores. Naturally, goats are not major destructors of vegetation, but rather roam large pastures, selectively grazing on grasses and twigs. When confined, due to the reduced availability of grass, goats are less selective, consuming large quantities of twigs and small shrubs. Goats display distinct preferences for certain feeds and often consume over 25 plant species. This diverse preference is beneficial for goats, as many of the wild grasses favored by goats are high in minerals and protein, primarily due to their deep roots. This grazing behavior provides a valuable compensation for livestock managers, as pastures previously grazed by cattle or other livestock can be used by goats for grazing, increasing production efficiency and providing managers with the added benefit of utilizing the land with a diverse range of animals. This is also why goats are less prone to bloat than other ruminants. Goats graze on juicy leguminous pastures without the problem of overgrazing, and the grass and young branches they eat are enough to provide the roughage needed for normal fermentation in the goat's rumen.

Under extensive grazing systems, goats will almost universally refuse to eat any plant matter contaminated by the odor of urine or feces from their own kind. From an evolutionary perspective, this unique trait prevents the spread of parasites. However, in confined areas with highly contaminated soil, goats may still be forced to eat such contaminated plants, with undesirable consequences.

The amount of grass a goat consumes depends on its metabolic rate and body weight, and is also affected by the breed and age of the goat. The type and growth stage of the grass also affect the amount of grass a goat consumes. A goat's feed intake varies with the ambient temperature, and appetite is controlled by the brain's thermoregulatory mechanism. When the ambient temperature drops, both the time and rate of feeding tend to increase. However, when the ambient temperature drops below 10°C, feeding activity decreases. There is a correlation between low temperatures and reduced water intake, which leads to a decrease in dry matter intake. When the crude fiber digestibility of the feed increases, the actual digestibility of the dry matter increases.

Goats can distinguish bitter, salty, sweet, and sour tastes, and their tolerance for bitterness is higher than that of other ruminants. Bark , leaves, roots, small shrubs, and branches are more bitter than grasses, root crops, or forage, which may explain their preference for tender shoots. Goats also have a good sense of sweetness. While a moderate amount of salt is crucial for goats, they generally refuse to eat anything above 5g/100cc .

Goats appear to spend more time feeding each day than other ruminants, often lasting up to 11 hours. This is often misunderstood because goats spend more time and travel longer distances searching for food each day than cattle or sheep. The length and regularity of rumination are negatively correlated with the goat's alertness. Goats typically have longer, more regular ruminations when they are semi-asleep. Sudden noise exposure can cause rumination to become irregular. Rumination usually ceases when a goat is fully awake.

Drinking Behavior: Goats are well-adapted to water restriction and deprivation, as their water turnover rate is only 188cc/kg/24 hours. This is comparable to camels, which can survive prolonged water deprivation, with a water turnover rate of 185cc/kg/24 hours. Sheep and cattle have water turnover rates of 197cc/kg/24 hours and 347cc/kg/24 hours, respectively, both higher than goats. In certain seasons, when sweating or respiration are not necessary to maintain body temperature, goats can generally obtain adequate water from their feed if the moisture content of the forage is 60% or above. When the ambient temperature is about 38°C, the goat's respiration rate (respiratory cooling) is only about half that of a sheep. At this time, the goat's sweating is quite limited, and the water loss through feces and urine is greatly reduced. When the amount of water consumed is reduced for several consecutive days, the goat's urine output will be relatively reduced, and the urea concentration in the blood will increase. Lactating goats drink more water than ordinary sheep because milk contains about 85% water. Therefore, the higher the milk production of the ewe, the more water it drinks. A ewe that produces 8 pounds of milk per day needs the same amount of water every day to meet its lactation needs.

Suckling lambs: Within a short period of time after birth, lambs will try to find any part of the ewe's body that they can bite. Usually, newborn lambs will try to suckle between the ewe's two front legs, and finally they will find the nipple and actually start suckling. In the first one or two days after giving birth, when normal lambs are hungry, they can usually find the nipple quickly to suckle. When suckling, lambs usually bump against the ewe's udder, which can promote milk let-down and increase the amount of milk supplied to the lambs. The lamb's suckling reflex can be easily transferred to a bottle or milk bucket, so after the lambs have consumed an appropriate amount of colostrum, the ewe can return to the normal lactating flock.

Chasing or Escape Behavior: The development of specific chasing or escape behaviors among goats also appears to have some evolutionary significance. While many other animals commonly use urine or defecation as territorial marking, there is no clear evidence of this behavior in goats. However, male goats can detect estrus by sniffing the urine of ewes. In the wild, chasing behavior is sporadic, and when grazing, goats avoid areas where other animals urinate or defecate. This escape behavior is suppressed in confinement and in areas with extensive soiling. Ewes adopt a half-crouching position similar to that of female dogs when urinating, while males arch their backs and bend their legs, but adult males lack this behavior. Goats often wag their tails back and forth during defecation, but the significance of this behavior remains unclear.

Sexual Behavior: For goats, at least most of those from temperate regions, sexual behavior is affected by the seasons. The estrus cycle ceases in late spring and summer, and the libido of rams is very low during these months. Semen volume reaches its lowest point in early spring, and sperm motility is also poor in winter. Semen volume and sperm motility are highest in late summer and autumn. Rams typically resume sexual activity more quickly, about two weeks before the return of estrus in ewes . It is believed that the courtship stimulation of rams accelerates the onset of the ewe's breeding season. The length of a goat's breeding season is influenced by factors such as light, temperature, and latitude. Goat breeds from higher latitudes and mountainous regions have a shorter breeding season. The estrus cycle of all goats peaks in autumn, allowing most lambs to be born in the spring when the weather is favorable.

During the breeding season, ewes have an estrous cycle of 21 days, but this can vary slightly from individual to individual. The duration of estrus, or standing estrus, also varies from individual to individual. The estrus period typically lasts 18 to 24 hours, but can last considerably longer. At the beginning and end of the breeding season, ewes may experience silent heat, a state of ovulation without estrus. In other animals, this is due to insufficient estrogen in the blood during estrus.

Ram odor plays a significant role in sexual behavior because it serves as a medium for stimulating ewes. Through conditioning and prior experience, the ram's scent triggers a series of responses in ewes that facilitate courtship and mating, such as rubbing the ram's neck and body against the ram and standing still to receive his advances. During the mating season, rams exhibit distinct and complex behavioral patterns between the two sexes. Rams can become aggressive and strive to achieve and maintain their position as the highest social rank. During the mating season, rams are extremely active and noisy, constantly guarding ewes in estrus and intruding rams. Rams often sniff ewe urine and extend their heads and necks, curling their upper lips in a "flehmen" posture, seeking out olfactory and gustatory stimulation from estrus ewes. Upon identifying an estrus ewe, rams will follow her and attempt to isolate her from the flock. Once a ewe is separated from the flock, the ram will scratch the ground around her, displaying masculine behavior. He will then frequently bleat with a hoarse voice and constantly stick out his tongue, demonstrating highly excited pre-mating behavior. He will then sniff and rub the area around the ewe's vulva with his nose, intermittently rubbing the ewe's side. Once the ram mounts the ewe, mating is accomplished through repeated thrusts of the hindquarters and the insertion of his penis. If the ewe has fully accepted the ram and has sexual experience, she will stand still and accept the ram's mounting and mating. If the ewe refuses the ram's mounting or even runs away, the ram will be unable to mount or mate. Therefore, if the sexual behavior of rams and ewes in the flock is not compatible, it may become a potential reproductive problem.

Maternal Care Behavior: As far as birth is concerned, expectant ewes are often isolated from the flock. After the lamb is born, the ewe licks the fetal membranes and fluids clean, and may even eat the placenta. This licking has a systemic stimulating effect on the lamb, and this stimulation can even be achieved by ranchers wiping the lamb with a dry cloth or straw. After giving birth, ewes typically emit brief, low-frequency bleating calls to call their lambs. If a stranger lamb approaches, the ewe will push it away.

Sustained contact between ewe and lamb, through sniffing and licking, is essential for establishing a strong bond and imprinting between mother and lamb. If a lamb is separated from its mother immediately after birth but returned to its mother within two hours, the mother will still accept it. However, if the lamb is returned more than two hours later, the mother may not accept it, and if it is returned more than three hours later, the mother will definitely not accept it. Tricks such as immobilizing the ewe or dousing the lamb with her scent can force her to accept the lamb, but this process is time-consuming, sometimes taking up to ten days to fully establish acceptance. In sheep flocks, surrogate ewes can even accept a stranger lamb after only a brief contact of about ten minutes, establishing this bond primarily through olfactory cues.

Protection (care) behavior: When lambs are captured, restrained, or injured, they emit a high-pitched wail, signaling their distress. This wail is recognizable even to inexperienced handlers. Adult sheep also bleat when isolated from the rest of the flock. In other words, when sheep from different flocks mix, they often create a rumble as they move, and these noises signal the location of fellow flock members.

Cooperative Behavior (or Resistant Behavior): When rams are kept together, they butt each other's horns to establish their pecking order within the flock. When sheep are mixed together, they butt each other's heads. Goats, on the other hand, first stand about 4 to 6 feet apart, then step back, angle their bodies toward each other, and then raise their heads to face each other. They then rotate their heads and lunge forward, forcefully butting each other downward. This difference in behavior between sheep and goats allows them to be kept together with minimal fighting.

The establishment of a social hierarchy among rams allows for the selection of dominant males to maintain flock security and provide breeding stock for ewes. Rams other than the dominant male in the flock, not used for breeding, are called peripheral males. If the dominant male in a flock is infertile or has low fertility, the flock's littering rate will be poor. However, a flock with only a few fertile males is sufficient.

During the breeding season, dominant rams are aggressive, but outside the breeding season, they may even be jostled by alpha ewes (the ewes with the highest social rank). Alpha ewes are the true leaders of the flock, and due to their rank, they produce the most offspring. Throughout their lives, ewes maintain a dominant position over their offspring. The establishment of a dominance hierarchy is often influenced by factors such as the relative age of the sheep, their fighting ability, and whether they have horns. Horned ewes may even rank higher than hornless rams. Overcrowding and small housing areas promote the development of a dominance hierarchy due to increased competition for food and space.

Although the composition of a flock may change due to factors such as the birth of newborns, death, and maturation, once a dominance hierarchy is established, it can typically last for several years. When a new animal joins the flock, aggression often occurs within a short period of time to establish its position within the flock. When sheep fight, it is usually one-on-one, never a group-on-one fight.

When goats encounter an emergency, they push their front legs hard and make a sound very similar to a sneeze. The flock will move slightly in a small area and form a sparse line in front of the disturbance. If they are further pursued, the flock will tend to be dispersed. This prevents them from gathering together like sheep when attacked. This is one of the reasons why timid dogs can never be good at watching over sheep.

When grazing, lambs typically do not follow their dams, remaining in a fixed location and timidly responding to any signs of danger. This timid behavior can prevent predators from noticing the lambs and leaving them alone. Adult sheep occasionally enter a state of stress when faced with danger or threat. This response, similar to that of kangaroos, was first discovered by Russian scientist Ivan Petrovich Pavlov , who pioneered traditional conditioning experiments with dogs. Pavlov believed that the goat's conditioning was related to a suppression of its natural self-protection impulses. This unique behavioral response was replicated in 1961 at Cornell University, Duke University, and the Muscle Research Institute at the Woods Hole Fish Biology Laboratory in Massachusetts. The results showed that goats exhibited less stress when water was restricted. In breeding, this response is thought to be genetically controlled, but current knowledge is limited.

Imitative Behavior: Goats, like other farm animals, exhibit social facilitation behaviors that are induced by the shared stimulation and security of the flock , allowing individuals to maintain optimal feed intake, growth, and health.
When sheep are herded, they typically follow the first sheep, with the rest of the flock following closely. An older ewe, usually leading the pack, selects a grazing spot and leads the flock there. The rest of the flock slowly gathers and follows. As the lead ewe moves forward, the rest of the flock follows in a sequential pattern, though unlike sheep or cattle, which often huddle together in front, the entire flock follows.

Sheep typically maintain contact with each other through vision. When grazing, individual sheep look up and around every few steps. This allows them to understand the relative positions of other sheep and to determine if they are in danger. Therefore, maintaining visual contact with other sheep is crucial for goats. When sheep visually detect being isolated from other sheep, they can become panicked. Therefore, if a sheep must be separated from the flock for reasons such as injury, birthing, or medication, it is best to place it within visual range of the flock.

Imitation behavior also occurs between different species of animals, such as sheep and goats. Because goats are more independent, they often quickly become leaders in mixed flocks. This benefits sheep farmers, as they can teach goats to seek them out when needed. Other sheep or flocks then imitate their behavior, reducing the time and effort required to gather or disperse the flock. Goats that can guide sheep to slaughter on a farm are called Judas goats.

Shelter-Seeking Behavior: Goats are generally quite hardy animals, well-adapted to both hot and cold weather, provided they are provided with shelter. Goats dislike rain, so before the first drop of rain falls, they often seek shelter under the nearest available shelter. Perhaps due to evolutionary reasons, goats also dislike puddles and muddy areas, avoiding wet areas as much as possible, thus reducing their risk of parasite infection. This is a valuable lesson to learn in goat management. While heat is not a major problem for most goats, excessively high temperatures can cause stress, leading to reduced milk production, weight loss, and even increased aggression within the flock.

Goats living in hot, dry, and treeless areas have an interesting adaptation known as shelter-seeking behavior. During the hot midday heat, when it would seem preferable for the goats to disperse individually, they instead gather together and jostle each other. This clustering occurs when the goats' ability to dissipate heat is lower than their ability to absorb it. The purpose of this clustering is to reduce their absorption of both direct and reflected solar energy. In the midday heat , the lack of shade or shelter prompts the goats to develop this unique shelter-seeking behavior to cope with the challenging conditions. This clustering and jostling also occurs in extreme cold or to repel flies.

Exploratory Behavior: Goats are naturally curious, especially young goats. When encountering something new or unfamiliar, their initial reaction will be to dodge or run for a short distance, depending on the degree of novelty, before turning to face the object. This exploratory behavior is most likely to occur when the object suddenly appears, causing panic in the flock. For example, an animal suddenly jumping out from hiding in the grass, or a person suddenly entering the pasture, can both trigger this reaction. Once the goat turns around, all senses are directed toward the distraction, and it quickly raises its head, focusing its eyes, nose, and ears on the distraction. If the distraction poses no further threat, the goat will cautiously move forward, followed by other goats, and eventually the entire flock will slowly move toward it. If the threat reappears, the goats will typically scatter and prepare to flee. The dominant male sheep usually approaches the threat first to search for it, but sometimes the dominant ewe makes the initial contact. Because young animals are more curious, lambs' exploratory responses are often bolder and more intense. This natural curiosity, combined with their smaller size and greater agility, results in unique movements and gestures. As lambs reach adulthood, their exploratory behavior gradually decreases due to natural maturation and accumulated experience. However, goats still rank among domestic animals in terms of risk-taking, adaptability, and exploratory behavior.

Feeding of lambs: 1. Feeding methods: (1) Natural lactation: lambs are fed by the ewe from birth until they are weaned. This method is generally used more often for meat goats. (2) Natural lactation and artificial feeding: lambs are fed by the ewe for colostrum for three to five days after birth, and then the lambs are separated from the ewe and fed with artificial milk substitute until they are weaned. This method is rarely used. (3) Artificial feeding: lambs are separated from the ewe from birth and fed with colostrum for three to five days, and then fed with artificial milk substitute until they are weaned. This method is generally used for dairy goats. 2. Artificial feeding methods: including milk bottles, shallow trays, multi-nipple feeding devices, multi-bottle feeding devices, tubular nipple feeding devices and fully automatic feeding machines. 3. Key points of feeding management: (1) The importance of colostrum: Colostrum contains high amounts of vitamin A and has a mild laxative effect that can help with the excretion of meconium. It also contains rich immune antibodies that can improve the disease resistance and growth rate of lambs. Therefore, newborn lambs must be fed colostrum, and the earlier they are fed, the better. (2) All feeding utensils must be thoroughly cleaned and disinfected before and after feeding to avoid digestive disturbances or diarrhea. If an automatic breastfeeding machine is used, the pipe system should be cleaned once a day. (3) Appropriately prepare substitute milk and feed it in a warm state. The ratio of milk powder to warm water is about 1:7-8, and the milk temperature for feeding is about 38-39℃. (4) The feeding time should be regular, and the number of feedings can be gradually reduced from five to six times a day at birth to two to three times a day at two weeks of age, and then gradually reduced to once a day before weaning. (5) Provide creep feed and green grass or hay to lambs as early as possible, and wean lambs early depending on their development to reduce feeding costs. Creep feed: It can be a mixture of cereals and minerals, or commercially available complete formula feed can be used directly. (6) Related management work such as numbering, dehorning, castration or removal of scent glands should be completed as early as possible before lambs are weaned to save feeding space. (7) Avoid overfeeding or underfeeding, which may cause excessive obesity or malnutrition, or cause digestive problems, thereby inhibiting the growth of lambs.

The formula of self-prepared creep feed is as follows: 13.6 kg crushed yellow corn, 9.1 kg crushed oats, 4.5 kg crushed barley, 13.6 kg bran, 4.5 kg soybean meal or flaxseed meal, 0.5 kg salt, and the crude protein content is about 18-20%.

Weaning time: generally one and a half to three months old.

Feeding guidelines for lambs: (1) From birth to three days old, only colostrum is fed, with milk fed 4-5 times a day, each time about 114-227 ml. (2) From three days old to one week old, whole milk or milk substitute is fed, with milk fed 4 times a day, each time about 227-284 ml. (3) From one week old to three weeks old, whole milk or milk substitute, creep feed, hay, drinking water, salt are fed, with milk fed 3 times a day, each time about 284-454 ml. Other foods are optional. (4) From three weeks old to two months old, whole milk or milk substitute, creep feed, hay, drinking water, and mixed minerals are fed 2 or 3 times a day, with a total daily amount of about 1.8 kg, of which creep feed is limited to 0.5 kg per day. (5) For children aged 2 to 4 months, the diet consists of whole milk or milk substitute, creep feed, hay, drinking water, and a combination of minerals. The dosage is approximately 0.5-1.0 kg twice daily. (6) For children aged 4 months to 1 year, the diet consists of growing season feed, hay, drinking water, and a combination of minerals. The dosage is approximately 0.5 kg twice daily. Other foods are ad libitum.

Feeding in the early stages of pregnancy: The feeding of ewes in the early stages of pregnancy can be done in the same way as for adult sheep. There is no need to increase the amount of concentrate feed. However, attention should still be paid to the quality of the pasture, clean drinking water, the supply of comprehensive minerals, and moderate exercise.

Feeding at the end of pregnancy: (1) During the last four to six weeks of pregnancy, 70% of the fetal development is completed at this time . Therefore, pregnant lactating ewes must have a dry period of four to six weeks to promote fetal development and allow the ewes to store appropriate nutrients in preparation for the next lactation period. (2) At the end of pregnancy, the ewes should be provided with good quality pasture, such as alfalfa combined with Bermuda or Timothy pasture as much as possible ; and when legume pastures such as legumes are used as the only source of forage, because legumes have a high calcium content, they should be replaced with Bermuda or Timothy pastures at least three weeks before delivery, or the amount of alfalfa fed should be reduced and supplemented with some Bermuda or Timothy pasture to reduce the occurrence of milk fever. (3) Depending on the physical condition of the ewe, the amount of concentrate fed should be adjusted as appropriate. If the ewe looks thin and has a low body weight, the daily concentrate feed should be increased to 0.5-0.8 kg. (4) Provide clean drinking water and mineral-rich salt bricks for the ewes to eat freely. (5) Provide sufficient space or exercise grounds for the ewes to exercise moderately. (6) Two weeks before the expected date of delivery, a mixture of equal amounts of table salt and dicalcium phosphate should be prepared for the ewes to eat freely to avoid milk fever. (7) Four to five days before delivery, the amount of concentrated feed should be reduced and replaced with a mild laxative feed such as bran. In addition, the grass fed should be as short as possible to facilitate digestion and help smooth delivery.

Feeding Lactating Ewes: The nutritional requirements of lactating ewes must cover all their needs for maintenance, activity, and lactation to meet their normal lactation needs. Improper feeding of lactating ewes will not only affect milk production but also reduce their disease resistance, reproductive rate, and future lactation capacity. Whenever possible, provide lactating ewes with high-quality forages, such as alfalfa, Bermuda grass, and Timothy . A mixture of grasses and legumes is preferred. For example, a mixture of alfalfa with Bermuda grass or Timothy grass is better than feeding only one forage. Some legumes contain high levels of phytoestrogens, acting as natural contraceptives. Therefore, if legumes are the only forage available and reproductive performance is poor, the ewes should be carefully investigated or replaced.

The amount of concentrate fed should vary depending on the amount of milk produced and the stage of lactation. In early lactation, give the ewe one kilogram of concentrate for every four kilograms of milk produced. As lactation progresses, gradually increase this to one kilogram for every three kilograms of milk produced and one kilogram for every two kilograms of milk produced. By the end of lactation, the amount of concentrate fed should be adjusted to one kilogram for every three kilograms of milk produced and one kilogram for every four kilograms of milk produced. The crude protein content of the concentrate can be adjusted based on the quality and type of forage, but is generally around 12-16%.

Clean drinking water and mineral-rich salt bricks should be available to the ewes as they please. Sufficient space or exercise areas should be provided to allow the ewes to exercise moderately.

Lamb Care from Birth to Weaning: The choice of lambing method depends on flock size, labor availability, and personal preference. Natural nursing is generally the most common method, but artificial feeding also includes bottle feeding, tray feeding, and automatic feeding machines. 1. Natural nursing: Lambs nurse from their dams, feeding small, frequent meals, saving labor, but can be more challenging to manage during weaning. 2. Bottle feeding: This method is more labor-intensive, but requires more care and is easier to manage after weaning. 3. Tray feeding: This method is more labor-intensive than bottle feeding, but requires training, and the lambs often lose weight during training. 4. Automatic feeding machines: This method can save considerable labor in larger flocks, but the ideal milk temperature is 35 °C, limiting the amount of milk the lambs consume at any one time. Small, frequent feedings can be used to increase the lambs' digestibility and reduce digestive disturbances. Excessive milk intake can increase the risk of diarrhea.

When feeding lambs with milk substitutes or artificial milk, since milk substitutes often use whey instead of skim milk, and whey has a high lactose content, it is more likely to cause diarrhea and bloating in lambs. Therefore, milk substitutes are best used on lambs over two weeks old or mixed with other milk powders in equal amounts. The concentration of milk substitutes should be appropriate, not too thick or too thin. When switching to milk substitutes, a gradual approach should be adopted, and a complete change should be made over several days.

Lambs over two weeks of age should be fed short-cut hay or grass to promote rumen development. Lambs over four weeks of age should be fed crushed concentrates or cereals containing 14-18% crude protein to accelerate their physical development. The weaning time of lambs should be determined based on their physical health, weight, and age. Generally, weaning is between two and three months of age, when the lamb weighs approximately ten to fifteen kilograms. Horn buds should be removed between three and fourteen days of age, but the lamb's physical condition and the growth of the horn buds must also be considered. After removal, the horn buds should be sprayed with fly repellent or iodine solution to prevent inflammation or infection. Various vaccinations can be given between three and four weeks of age. Male lambs intended for fattening should be castrated between two and four years of age to promote growth and reduce sheep odor and hygiene issues.

Management of lambs from weaning to breeding: (1) Provide high-quality legume and grass forage, and supplement with an appropriate amount of concentrate to promote normal physical development. (2) Provide adequate minerals and vitamins. (3) Provide appropriate exercise. (4) Regularly trim hooves and deworm. (5) Maintain a clean, sanitary, dry, well-ventilated sheep shed, and disinfect regularly.

Management of pregnant ewes: 1. The gestation period of ewes is about 150 days. The expected date of birth can be estimated based on the breeding date. 2. The most commonly used methods for diagnosing pregnancy in ewes are as follows: (1) Repeated estrus: If the ewe comes into estrus again 21 days after breeding, it means that the ewe is not pregnant; otherwise, it means that the ewe is pregnant. (2) Rectal and abdominal palpation: Used 70-110 days after breeding. (3) Abdominal wall palpation: Used after 110 days after breeding. (4) Ultrasound examination: It can be used for early diagnosis around 60 days of pregnancy. (5) Blood or milk progesterone concentration test: It can be used for early diagnosis. 3. Infectious reproductive tract diseases are rare in ewes, and goats are also more resistant to Brucella abortion. 4. During the last 1.5-2 months of pregnancy, ewes should be dried off and should pay attention to balanced nutrition, moderate exercise, stop feeding alfalfa hay or alfalfa pellets, and supplement with dicalcium phosphate to reduce the occurrence of ketosis and milk fever. 5. Management focus of pregnant ewes: (1) Early pregnancy - the first three and a half months, still follow the management method of general sheep. (2) Late pregnancy - the last one and a half months, pay attention to their post-natal care needs to maintain the health of the ewe and the development of the fetus. (3) Stop feeding alfalfa hay or alfalfa pellets in the late pregnancy. (4) Supply clean drinking water and supplement trace minerals. (5) Give moderate exercise. (6) Provide deworming and hoof trimming. 6. Signs and care of pregnant ewes approaching delivery: (1) When the ewe is approaching delivery, the udder will swell, the ligament near the base of the tail will relax, and the vulva will be extremely swollen. (2) 8-12 hours before delivery, the cervix begins to contract and discharge mucus that sticks to the back of the body. When the water bag appears, the lamb will be born in a short time. (3) The ewe should be observed during delivery so that timely assistance can be provided if necessary. (4) The ewe should be allowed to give birth in an individual pen to avoid being disturbed by other sheep, because if the ewe is disturbed during delivery, she may refuse to take care of the lamb. If the ewe starts to lick the lamb after delivery, it means that she should take care of the lamb. (5) The umbilical cord of the lamb after delivery should be soaked in iodine solution and tied with silk thread to prevent inflammation or infection of the umbilical cord. (6) The lamb should be fed colostrum as soon as possible, especially thin or weak lambs should be assisted in suckling. (7) The afterbirth is usually discharged within 6 hours after delivery, but sometimes it may be discharged 3-5 days after delivery. (8) Within 7-14 days after giving birth, the ewe will have a dark red mucous discharge from the vulva. This is a normal phenomenon. However, if a large amount of bright red blood or foul-smelling discharge is discharged, a veterinarian should be consulted. 7. Management of pregnant ewes during the delivery and lactation period (1) One week before the ewe is scheduled to give birth, move it to a clean individual delivery room to wait for delivery, and pay attention to the ewe's delivery signs, such as swelling of the vulva and discharge of mucus, swollen udders, bleating and restlessness, etc. (2) Install a heat lamp in the delivery room, especially in winter. (3) When the ewe is giving birth, you should observe and wait so that you can provide timely assistance in case of difficult labor. (4) After the lamb is born, the mucus in its mouth and nose should be cleaned first, and then its body should be wiped dry or blown dry. (5) The umbilical cord should be soaked in iodine solution, and then about 7-8 cm should be left, and then tied with silk thread. The ligature should be sprayed with iodine solution once or twice a day until the umbilical cord dries up to avoid inflammation or infection. (6) Lambs should be fed colostrum as soon as possible to maintain their health and improve their growth rate. (7) If the ewe is a carrier of caprine arthritis encephalitis (CAE), the colostrum should be sterilized at 57°C for 1 hour before feeding to the lamb to avoid the lamb being infected with caprine arthritis encephalitis. (8) To promote the health of the lamb, iron, vitamin A, vitamin D, vitamin E or selenium can also be injected. (9) Lambs should be kept warm and the sheep bed should be kept dry at all times to avoid the lamb from contracting pneumonia, as pneumonia is the number one killer of lambs. (10) The first ten days after the birth of the lamb are the period with the highest mortality rate. The main causes of death include low birth weight, premature birth and malnutrition of the ewe during pregnancy.

Lactating Ewe Management: Lactating ewes should be fed an appropriate amount of feed based on their milk production to maintain their milk production and health. Additionally, adequate access to clean drinking water, a healthy hygiene plan, and adequate exercise are crucial. A well-produced lactating ewe can lactate for up to 305 days per year and produce one to two lambs annually. Proper management and care are essential for lactating ewes to reach their full potential.

The most important management tasks for lactating ewes include the following two items: 1. Constructing high-quality and hygienic milking equipment, such as milking machines, stainless steel milk barrels, milk storage tanks and related milking equipment. All milking equipment should be thoroughly cleaned after use, drained and kept clean at all times. 2. Adopting correct milking procedures to maintain the health of the ewe's udder and the quality of the goat's milk. The correct milking procedures are as follows: (1) Put a small amount of concentrate in the feed trough of the milking platform. (2) Wash your hands. (3) Drive the lactating ewe to the milking platform. (4) Wash and massage the udder. (5) Express the foremilk and check whether the goat's milk is abnormal. (6) Milk manually or mechanically. Usually milking is done once in the morning and afternoon, and it is best to separate them by 12 hours. However, it can be adjusted according to the labor conditions of each dairy farm, but a fixed milking time should be maintained every day. (7) After milking is completed, soak the nipples in iodine solution and wait for the nipple soaking solution to dry slightly before returning the lactating ewe to the sheep pen. 3. Drying of lactating ewes in the late pregnancy The ewe should be dried in the last 4 to 6 weeks of pregnancy. The purpose of drying is to allow the fetus to grow and develop fully and to allow the ewe's body to store an appropriate amount of nutrients in preparation for the next lactation period. 4. Control of mastitis Mastitis is a disease caused by bacterial infection of the breast, and good hygiene management is the best way to prevent mastitis. Therefore, before milking, be sure to wash your hands clean, and after each milking, be sure to soak the nipples in iodine solution. The characteristics of mastitis are fever, pain and swelling of the breast, lumps in the breast, and some discoloration of the breast. The milk is watery, dark yellow, lumpy, green or contains blood.

Occasionally, young ewes may experience hardened udders, but this is not necessarily mastitis. Sometimes, after giving birth, the condition gradually returns to normal after a period of nursing or several artificial milking sessions. If a lactating ewe develops mastitis, the milk can be squeezed out, and then half a tube of cow's mastitis ointment (antibiotic) can be pressed into the nipple, followed by gentle massage. Applying a warm compress to the udder can also be helpful. Milk from ewes with mastitis should be discarded while they are being treated with antibiotics. It can only be consumed after 3-4 days of discontinuing the antibiotics and the milk has returned to normal.

Health Management of Dairy Sheep: Commercial dairy sheep farms typically employ a closed flock model to minimize disease. Therefore, participating in shows or competitions is discouraged. If participating in a show is necessary for the purpose of selling breeding sheep, the sheep should be isolated and quarantined after the show. A three-week quarantine period is considered safe. From a disease prevention perspective, artificial insemination is preferable to importing breeding rams or transporting ewes to other farms for breeding.

Goats are naturally intelligent and will open collars and fence latches on their own. Therefore, collars and latches must be extremely secure to prevent escape and accidental access to feed or metal storage areas, which could lead to engorgement toxemia, bloating, or poisoning. Goats naturally chew fencing materials, so paint containing lead should be avoided in old pens, and new pens should avoid the use of toxic chemicals such as pentachlorophenol as a wood preservative.

Goats' tendency to climb makes fences quite challenging to construct, and poorly chosen materials can be quite expensive. Fences with horizontal bars or slats are easy for goats to climb, making them vulnerable to damage. Chain-link fences often snag sheep's front and hind legs, resulting in fractures. If fences or pen gates use upright poles, their spacing must be carefully considered to prevent entanglement or entanglement, especially for horned sheep. Consequently, electric fences are becoming increasingly popular.

Because goats and sheep are small and lack strong defenses, they are extremely vulnerable to predators. Lambs are particularly vulnerable to predators, as they lack the ability to defend themselves. Small ruminants' primary defense is flight, and tethered sheep are doomed to die when predators invade.

In certain regions of the world, soil composition can cause plant deficiencies in certain minerals, leading to poor performance or disease in goats. Therefore, veterinarians and producers need to be aware of regional mineral deficiencies. Documented mineral deficiencies that can cause economic losses in goats include selenium, iodine, cobalt, and copper. Mineral deficiencies can be addressed by fertilizing pastures, supplementing the feed with minerals, providing trace mineral salt bricks, or injecting the missing minerals. Another potential regional concern in goat husbandry is damage from toxic plants. Among the most significant impacts of toxic plants on goats are sudden death, primary or secondary photosensitization, and an unpleasant flavor in goat milk.

Veterinarians interested in goats should encourage owners to participate in regular flock health checks. When knowledgeable veterinarians are familiar with regular flock health management measures, they can readily identify potential health problems in the flock and address them appropriately to reduce future losses. Through reproductive consultation with veterinarians, owners can improve the production efficiency of their flocks through methods such as pregnancy diagnosis, estrus synchronization, and out-of-season breeding. Mastitis control, nutritional management of metabolic diseases (such as pregnancy toxemia), and prevention plans for caprine arthritis encephalitis (CAE) are all related to flock production efficiency. In addition, autopsies of culled animals are also an effective way to identify potential health problems in the flock.

Disease issues in lambs from birth to weaning: During farrowing, the greatest challenges to lamb survival are hypoxia, hypothermia, hypoglycemia, and susceptibility to infection. Delayed or inadequate feeding of colostrum, rich in immunoglobulins secreted by the ewe, increases the risk of disease. Failure to passively transfer maternal antibodies to the lamb increases neonatal survival and morbidity, particularly sepsis in the first week after birth and pneumonia during the weaning period. Lambs also have lower levels of immunoglobulins produced through active immunization. Failure to properly handle and disinfect the umbilical cord at birth, and exposure to unclean environments, increases the incidence of omphalophebitis, sepsis, polyarthritis, and pneumonia.

Diarrhea is a common disease in newborn lambs, and many pathogens can cause diarrhea in lambs before they are two weeks old. After two weeks of age, the threat of coccidiosis gradually increases. Pneumoenteritis complex also occasionally occurs in lambs, likely due to improper care, severe environmental contamination with pathogens, and inadequate colostrum intake. Furthermore, contagious ecthyma may occur in lambs, especially in areas where it occurs or has recently been reported.

Newborn lambs should be inspected for congenital defects such as cleft palate, rectovaginal fistula, anal atresia, hydrocephalus, umbilical hernia, and ventricular septal defect. Intersex is common in goats. Beta mannosidosis is a hereditary disease in Nubian lambs. Lambs afflicted with this genetic defect should be culled or raised for meat.

Arthritis encephalitis (CAE) is a disease of concern to goat producers worldwide. Although clinically rare in lambs under two months of age, CAE is primarily transmitted through colostrum and milk from ewes, making lambs a key focus of CAE prevention and control programs. Recent lamb husbandry techniques have developed techniques to reduce the risk of CAE infection in newborn lambs .

Management measures for lambs from birth to weaning: Before the ewe gives birth, adequate preparations should be made in advance and a comprehensive emergency treatment process should be planned to reduce the loss of newborn lambs. It is very important to plan and build a clean, warm, dry, well-lit, adequately bedding, and well-ventilated farrowing pen for the ewes to give birth, and related supplies such as towels, milk bottles or iodine solution for treating the umbilical cord should also be prepared. In addition, plans for preventing and controlling CAE infection in lambs should also be planned and prepared in advance so that they can be effectively implemented immediately after the lambs give birth. The technology of using prostaglandin treatment to induce lambs to give birth at a predetermined time has been widely used, and its details can be found in relevant books.

A well-managed sheep farm should have a comprehensive sheep identification and recordkeeping system. Newborn lambs can be identified with neck collars, while ear tattooing is recommended for lambs over three months of age. Ear tattooing should not be performed too early, as the markings will expand and separate as the lambs grow, making them unreadable. Green ink is preferred, as some sheep have black skin, making black tattoos difficult to read. Additionally, the external ears of Lai Manjiao dairy lambs are very small, so the tattoos can be placed on the inside of the tail.

Lambs can be raised in separate groups, but they should not be overcrowded. Sheep pens should be designed for easy and thorough cleaning, as poor hygiene and overcrowding increase the risk of spreading diarrheal diseases . Excess rams not intended for breeding should be culled or sold as soon as possible to reduce the number of lambs and avoid increasing environmental pollution and the risk of spreading infectious diseases.

Disbudding lambs is a routine task on sheep farms, preventing harm to both sheep and humans. In the United States, purebred goats with horns are not permitted to participate in shows. Lambs are typically dehorned between 3 and 14 days of age, but the timing varies depending on the breed, size, and sex. Dehorning male rams intended for breeding often damages the pheromone scent glands adjacent to the horn buds, potentially reducing the ram's future breeding efficiency. For male rams intended for domestic use or pets, the scent glands are typically removed along with the dehorning.

Ewes often have supernumerary nipples, which can be removed with curved scissors during dehorning. However, if the function of the supernumerary nipples cannot be confirmed, removal should be delayed. While wattles can be pinched or bitten, these are rare and can be removed with curved scissors during lambing, resulting in minimal wounding. Watt removal is usually performed for cosmetic reasons.

It's best to castrate young male rams between 4 and 14 days of age, but the reason for castration should be clear. For male rams intended for meat, castration should be determined based on market demand and the age at which they are marketed, as some markets prefer intact male rams. If the market requires rams slaughtered at 8 weeks of age, castration is generally not necessary. However, if fattening is required for a longer period, castration is necessary, as the market cannot tolerate the odor of ram meat. If castrated male rams are intended for pet use, castration should be delayed until 6 to 8 weeks of age to reduce the risk of obstructive urolithiasis later in life.

The timing and timing of vaccination for newborn lambs depends on the overall flock vaccination program. If ewes are vaccinated against a disease during the dry period and newborn lambs are fed an appropriate amount of colostrum from vaccinated ewes, they will acquire protective maternal antibodies during the first six weeks of life. Because the titer of maternal antibodies decreases depending on their initial concentration, it is best to give lambs a booster vaccination at four or five weeks of age to enhance their protective effect. If the ewe is not vaccinated, lambs born to unvaccinated lambs should be vaccinated within the first week of life, with additional booster vaccinations after a certain period, depending on the vaccine type. During the first week of life, when active antibodies are developing, prophylactic antiserum can be administered concurrently with a bacterin-toxoid vaccine to enhance protection. Products such as tetanus antitoxin and Clostridium perfringens type C and type D antitoxins are commonly used in lambs. While the vaccines required for lambs vary by region, globally recommended vaccines include tetanus and Clostridium perfringens types C and D. The tetanus vaccine is also widely used in lambs. After the initial vaccination, all lambs should receive a booster shot ; subsequent vaccinations are only required in lambs, as adult lambs already have persistent antibodies. Lambs should not be vaccinated too early, as maternal antibodies may interfere with their immune response.

Vaccinations for paratuberculosis, foot and mouth disease, and rinderpest are administered based on the needs of the national disease prevention program. Vaccines for anthrax, blackleg, leptospirosis, and rabies are administered based on regional needs. Vaccinations should not be administered arbitrarily, especially when they are live viruses that are not specifically designed for sheep. For example, in the United States, live intranasal infectious bovine rhinotracheitis (IBR) vaccines are commonly used to control respiratory diseases in sheep, but their effectiveness is not documented.
