Nutritional muscular dystrophy can develop in sheep from selenium-deficient soils; this condition can be treated with intravenous vitamin E and sodium selenite.

The timing of weaning lambs varies depending on the feeding system and management methods, and can be as early as 6 weeks of age or as late as 12 weeks of age. If milk feeding is not an option, lambs should be fed hay and concentrate as early as possible to promote rumen development. Abrupt weaning lambs before they are fully adapted to dry feeds can lead to indigestion, bloating, and possibly polioencephalomalacia.

Disease issues in lambs from weaning to breeding: Coccidia and pneumonia are the primary problems during this period, especially when housed. Regarding coccidia, the transition from milk to solid feed encourages lambs to ingest more coccidia eggs. Regarding pneumonia, after eight weeks of age, the gradual decline in maternal antibodies, coupled with the stress of weaning, makes lambs more susceptible to respiratory infections. During their first summer of grazing, lambs are highly susceptible to gastrointestinal parasites and lungworm infections because their digestive and respiratory tracts are not yet fully developed. The severity of these parasitic infections depends on the concentration of parasite eggs in the pasture, the stocking rate , climatic conditions, and other factors. Overfeeding lambs with concentrate feed between eight and 16 weeks of age can lead to recurrent enterotoxemia . Furthermore, progressive limb paralysis caused by infection with the neuroarthritis encephalitis (CAE) virus is also common.

Management measures for lambs from weaning to breeding: When keeping lambs in pens, it is crucial to maintain the integrity of the pen and feeding system to reduce the incidence of pneumonia and coccidiosis. Even farms with excellent feeding practices can experience clinical symptoms of coccidiosis. Therefore, in addition to maintaining good farm hygiene, it is essential to add anticoccidial drugs to the feed or drinking water.

Currently, there is no vaccine available to control respiratory diseases in lambs. In affected countries, the use of vaccines against mycoplasma pneumonia is increasing. In countries where vaccines for caprine brucellosis have been developed , lambs are typically vaccinated between three and eight months of age.

Regarding parasite control in pastures, pastures used for lamb grazing should be closed for at least one year and should be grazed separately from adult sheep whenever possible. Lambs should be given broad-spectrum anthelmintics before grazing, and fecal samples should be collected during the grazing season to monitor parasite egg concentrations. Contact anthelmintics may also be used as needed.

Once weaned, spring lambs should be fed appropriately to maintain their health and reach a suitable weight for breeding by summer of the same year, allowing for successful farrowing the following year. In North America, for example, target weights of 70 pounds by seven months for larger breeds such as the Saanen , Alpine , and Nubian , and 60 pounds by seven months for smaller breeds such as the Lamancha and Toggenburg . At three months of age, male and female lambs should be housed separately to prevent unintended breeding. Prior to breeding, all lambs should be carefully inspected for any intersexed lambs.

In temperate regions, goats are seasonal breeders, with their estrus cycle triggered by shortened daylight hours. A common area of interest is manipulating endocrine changes to induce estrus in goats, thereby achieving year-round reproduction. If goats can reproduce year-round, this significantly helps maintain a balanced milk production and sales. During the normal anestrus season, there are two methods for inducing estrus in new ewes and older goats: supplementation with fertilizing hormones, and control of daylight duration through penning and artificial lighting.

Disease Problems in Bred and Dry Doe Ewes: The primary concerns for this flock are pregnancy toxemia and abortion. False pregnancy is common in goats, but can be diagnosed by ultrasound or spontaneous uterine fluid release without the presence of a fetus or placenta. Obese ewes are at increased risk for pregnancy toxemia during the dry period, vaginal prolapse in late pregnancy, and dystocia during delivery.

Management of bred doeling and dry doe ewes : Lactating ewes are typically dry during the last two to three months of pregnancy. Since new ewes have not yet produced lambs, dryness is not an issue in late pregnancy . New and dry ewes should be properly managed to avoid obesity and receive adequate nutrients to support the rapid growth of the fetus in late pregnancy. This is crucial for preventing preeclampsia. To prevent preeclampsia, reduce the amount of grain fed during early pregnancy when milk production stops increasing . In mid-pregnancy, feed high-quality legumes and small amounts of grain or no grain . Grain intake should be gradually increased from the last two months of pregnancy until farrowing. When a flock of sheep has a problem with pregnancy toxemia, the ketone levels in the urine of new ewes and old sheep should be tested regularly with commercially available reagents or test strips to screen out sheep with early symptoms and provide appropriate treatment immediately.

There are many infectious and non-infectious factors that can cause abortion in ewes during late pregnancy. To prevent infectious abortion in pregnant ewes, no new sheep, not even rams, should be introduced to the flock once a pregnant ewe is already pregnant. Any sheep experiencing an abortion should be immediately isolated, and the area where the abortion occurred should be isolated and thoroughly disinfected. The cause of the abortion should be carefully investigated, and all fetal tissue or placenta obtained for diagnosis should be cremated. In flocks with a history of chlamydial abortion, new ewes should be vaccinated annually before breeding. The dry period is an appropriate time to vaccinate pregnant ewes, as this increases the concentration of specific antibodies in the colostrum and enhances the passive immunity of the lambs. Vaccination should be administered three to five weeks before the dry ewe farrows.

Pregnant ewes infected with gastrointestinal nematodes may experience an increase in the number of parasite eggs and eggs excreted near the time of delivery. This is a survival strategy for the parasites and increases their chances of infecting the next generation. Therefore, pregnant ewes should be given a broad-spectrum anthelmintic two to three weeks before delivery. While there are no reports of anthelmintics causing miscarriage or fetal malformations at specific doses in goats, two anthelmintics, levamisole and albendazole, are suspected of carrying these risks and should be avoided in pregnant ewes.

Disease problems in lactating ewes: (1) Mastitis is the biggest challenge for lactating ewes, and many pathogens can be isolated from lactating ewes. Mycoplasma mastitis is common in some flocks and causes serious damage to the udder. Mastitis caused by Staphylococcus aureus is also very expensive to treat, and it is often difficult to control if infected sheep are not strictly culled. (2) Caseous lymphadenitis occurs in sheep of any age, but the problem is most serious in lactating ewes. Lymph node abscesses are most common in the head and neck of sheep, and when lactating ewes are fixed in a neck clamp, the risk of abscess rupture increases and the disease may be transmitted to other sheep. (3) Mastitis-type arthritis encephalitis virus (CAE) infection often occurs at the beginning of lactation and is characterized by low milk production (hypogalactia) or no milk production (agalactia) and firm udders. This form of arthritis can severely reduce the milk production of ewes and has a significant impact on the production performance of sheep farms. Therefore, special attention must be paid to it and its prevention and control methods must be strengthened.

Once ewes begin lactating, infections with the arthritis-type encephalitis virus become more frequent. According to French epidemiological studies, sheep are more susceptible to infection and carpal swelling when exposed to conditions that increase their risk of joint damage. Conditions that increase the risk of joint damage include jumping on and off the milking platform during milking, poorly designed pens or feed troughs that cause repeated impacts on the carpal bones, hard flooring, and confinement or tethering that forces the sheep's carpal bones to rest for extended periods. Excessive hoof growth and improper hoof trimming are also contributing factors.

Some weight loss is normal in lactating ewes during early lactation, especially in high-yielding ewes. Lambs infected with subclinical Mycobacterium paratuberculosis (MPP) may experience clinical weight loss during the stress of farrowing and subsequent lactation. Therefore, persistent weight loss in lactating ewes should be monitored for MPP.

Gastrointestinal parasites are also a potential problem for lactating ewes when they are pastured. Although sheep's resistance to parasitic infections increases with age and exposure, it is not absolute. Clinically, even in adult lactating ewes without obvious parasitic symptoms, secondary clinical symptoms caused by parasites can significantly affect milk production.

Milk fever or hypocalcemia is less common in dairy sheep than in dairy cattle, but recurrences have occurred in some herds. Recommended management practices for controlling hypocalcemia in dairy cattle have not yet been proven effective in dairy sheep. Abomasal displacement is a common problem in intensively farmed dairy sheep, but its application in dairy sheep remains unknown.

Lactating Ewe Management: The primary goal of lactating ewe management is to maximize milk production at the lowest production cost. To achieve this, attention must be paid to the general and udder health of the lactating ewe. Feeding plans must be tailored to the lactation period and milk production of individual ewes to achieve optimal lactation. Furthermore, breeding and culling programs must be implemented to select high-yielding ewes, necessitating the continuous and thorough maintenance of relevant health and production records.

Preventive medicine for lactating ewes includes vaccinations, hoof care, and deworming. Lactating ewes should be vaccinated against enterotoxemia at least twice a year, with problematic farms requiring three vaccinations. Because a single vaccination boosts the antibody titer of colostrum in dry ewes, one or two more vaccinations are recommended during the lactation period. Vaccines to prevent abortion, such as the clostridial virus vaccine, should be administered prior to breeding.

Regular hoof trimming is crucial for maintaining a sheep's hooves. It can reduce the incidence of hoof rot, teat damage, gait disturbances, and reduced feed intake due to hoof pain. The frequency of hoof trimming depends on the sheep's level of activity, and there's a wide range of requirements. For example, sheep kept on rough or rocky ground can achieve a natural hoof trimming effect when they move around.

The feces of lactating ewes should be regularly tested for nematode infestations. A deworming program should be implemented as needed for lactating ewes, with two key points in mind. First, some anthelmintics have antifungal and antibacterial properties, which can interfere with cheese production. Second, it is important to select an anthelmintic suitable for lactating ewes and ensure proper milk collection after administration.

Maintaining the health of lactating ewes' udders requires selecting ewes with well-formed udders and good attachment, providing them with a clean and comfortable living space, properly operating milking equipment, and establishing procedures for maintaining udder hygiene during milking. When lactating ewes begin drying off, a drying ointment should be applied to the udder to prevent active mastitis. Additionally, trimming the udder and hindquarters can help reduce sediment in the milk.

For dairy goats, milk quality is a major issue. In the past, the odor and goatish taste of goat milk significantly reduced consumer acceptance of goat milk. Therefore, contact between rams and ewes should be minimized, and rams should not be kept near milking parlors or storage troughs to prevent the goatish odor from entering the milk. Lactating ewes should avoid being fed coarse feed with foreign odors, as this will impart an unpleasant flavor or odor to the milk. Proper handling of goat milk after milking is also crucial. Excessive stirring and delayed sterilization will cause the fat in the milk to break down, producing a goaty flavor. The high somatic cell count in goat milk is also a source of serious concern to the public. The somatic cell count standard for cow's milk does not apply to goat milk unless the goat's udder is infected.

When raising lactating ewes, adequate troughs or space should be provided for each ewe to feed, as much as possible, to prevent ewes with lower social status from being excluded by more dominant ewes and deprived of feed. Furthermore, dominant ewes often bully other sheep, preventing them from getting enough feed themselves. Such dominant ewes should be culled. Another issue in goat husbandry is the feeding of silage . Although this issue is still unresolved, it may be linked to listeriosis in goats .

Ram Health Management: Rams are often considered last in sheep health management plans. This reflects their unique position within the flock, signifying that overall flock health management measures are nearing completion. Ram health management plans are often abandoned due to their unpleasant odor. However, since rams play a critical role in improving reproductive efficiency and overall flock performance, ram health management plans must not be abandoned lightly.

Ram Health Issues: There are relatively few specific ram-related diseases, with obstructive urolithiasis being the most significant. While obstructive urolithiasis is more common in castrated rams, urethral stones can also cause obstructive disease in healthy rams, and the damage is similar to that seen in breeding rams. During the breeding season, rams can become quite aggressive, and fighting can cause injuries to one another. Rams can also injure humans, so staff should exercise extreme caution when approaching breeding rams. The most common problem in fighting rams is horn base fracture, resulting in profuse bleeding. This condition is caused by improper dehorning of young rams, resulting in abnormal growth of the remaining horn bud tissue, making the horn base less securely attached. While profuse bleeding is generally not serious, it is a warning sign. Therefore, dehorning of young lambs requires strict supervision to prevent the profuse bleeding associated with horn base fracture. During the breeding season, rams tend to urinate on themselves, particularly on their faces and the outside of their forequarters. This can cause urine burns and subsequent bacterial dermatitis. Applying petroleum jelly to the outside of the goat's forequarters can help prevent these injuries.

Ram Management Measures: To ensure optimal performance, rams should be conditioned and in peak condition before the breeding season. Therefore, as the breeding season progresses, they should show a noticeable trend of weight loss. This change is physiological, not pathological, and is particularly important before breeding. To maintain optimal physical condition, rams should be regularly dewormed and properly fed. Proper care of their hooves is also crucial. During breeding, the ram's riding exerts considerable pressure on the ewe's back and legs. If the ewe's hooves are not properly trimmed, the ram's riding can be painful, making it difficult for the ram to complete the ride, especially when using larger rams for breeding. Ram hoof care is often neglected because managers are often reluctant to trim rams that have a foul odor. A similar situation exists with vaccinations; rams are often under-vaccinated because they are more difficult to control.

Before breeding, rams should undergo a thorough reproductive examination, including a general physical examination, reproductive examination, semen analysis, and libido testing. These examinations should not be conducted too long after the breeding season, as the quality of a ram's semen will decline during the anestrus period. If rams are group-housed year-round, they will be more familiar with each other, and even during the breeding season, violent aggression and serious injuries will be avoided. However, introducing a young or small ram into the herd during the breeding season can cause fatal injuries and should be avoided. Trimming a ram's beard and hair can remove unpleasant ram odor, especially trimming urine-contaminated hair on the forequarters. Trimming hair is best performed before breeding. Placing rams close to ewes can help detect estrus. Rams and ewes can be induced to breed by simply being in contact through a fence, without being confined together.

Taiwan's sheep farming industry can be distinguished from its predecessor since 1985. Prior to this, it was almost entirely family-run, small-scale operations, with little emphasis on sheep breed and size. Sheep pens were simple, and the animals were kept free range. Sheep traders purchased sheep from various sheep pens, resulting in a small market for mutton. After 1985, mutton producers began importing Nubian breeders and crossbreeding them with local goats to improve their physiques. They also shifted from free range to pen-raising, increasing the use of concentrated feed, accelerating weight gain, and shortening time to market. Private producers provided a distribution channel for high-quality breeding stock through exhibitions and sales. Sheep pens were also improved, with the use of mechanical manure scrapers, saving labor costs and improving the sheep's breeding environment. Regarding mutton industry organizations, sheep farmers established the Republic of China Sheep Raising Association in 1997 to provide goat-related information. Agricultural authorities assisted various regions in establishing mutton production and marketing teams to enhance mutton producers' production techniques and strengthen their alliance production and marketing capabilities. In addition to regional farmers' associations assisting in the joint marketing of mutton, mutton auctions have been established in meat markets in Yunlin, Fengshan, and Changhua since 1988. These have fostered favorable conditions for sheep sales, including open trading, transparency, guaranteed payment, and market services. In recent years, given that imported sheep account for over 85% of domestic mutton consumption, the Taiwan Sheep Association established the Domestic Mutton Promotion Committee in 2002 to promote domestic mutton production and consumption. The recent development trend of the mutton industry has been to actively introduce new breeds of pure meat-producing Boer goats from abroad to explore sustainable development opportunities for the domestic goat industry.

Sheep husbandry and management are one of the most important factors in determining the success of a successful sheep operation. Good management can maintain flock health, improve production efficiency, reduce costs, and increase profits. However, management encompasses a wide range of factors, including sheep behavior, climate, the environment, shed construction, husbandry methods, scale, and the quality of the staff. Therefore, while there are principles for sheep husbandry and management, owners must make appropriate adjustments within their capabilities to achieve optimal results.

Goats are gregarious animals, maintaining a strong hierarchy or social status within their flocks. Males and ewes typically establish their social standing within the flock through head-to-head combat. Furthermore, a sheep's social status within the flock is influenced by age, sex, and whether it possesses horns or not. Older, male, and horned sheep have a comparative advantage. Once a social order is established within a flock, the sheep can coexist peacefully, but social status remains a dynamic equilibrium and is subject to frequent fluctuations. To minimize the dominance of horned sheep and to protect the safety of both humans and animals, it is best to avoid mixing horned and hornless sheep within a flock.

Goats are more defensive than sheep. When threatened by other animals, they emit a distinctive sneezing sound to resist. Isolated sheep often become anxious and vocalize, adding to the management difficulties.

Goats are very curious and enjoy using their mouths to inspect any novel objects around them, such as handlers' clothing, medical equipment, and record books. These items can be easily chewed or knocked over. There have even been cases of goats accidentally ingesting plastic bags or other indigestible items, causing gastrointestinal obstruction and death. Penned goats also often chew on the wood of their pens. Therefore, equipment that sheep may come into contact with should be made of sturdy materials and avoid using paint to prevent lead poisoning.

Goats prefer dry ground to muddy terrain. They would rather jump over ditches than wade through them, and when it rains, they eagerly seek shelter. Goats are agile and have a knack for climbing. Pile rocks or create rough concrete platforms in the grazing area or exercise yard of the sheep pen. This not only provides recreational exercise for the goats, but also reduces the need for regular hoof trimming. Goats are adept at jumping and climbing, easily unlocking fence latches and making them skilled escapees. Therefore, gate design, fence height, and fenceline or rail spacing should differ from those used for sheep pens.

Goats have a habit of browsing. Shrub leaves generally have higher nutritional content than grasses. Given a choice, grazing goats will skip grasses and prioritize shrub leaves. Sometimes they even stand or climb tree trunks to pluck leaves from higher trees, or even gnaw on the bark. Due to these feeding habits, goats can be used to help clear brush during wasteland development. In general grazing management, cattle and sheep can be combined for integrated grazing or the grazing order can be adjusted to fully utilize the fodder resources in the pasture. However, due to the feeding characteristics of goats, overgrazing that damages soil and water conservation must be avoided when grazing in small pastures. Goats have a strong ability to identify poisonous wild plants in pastures, but once the poisonous plants dry or wither, the goats' ability to identify them decreases.

Sheep grazing in the wild enjoy a diverse diet of over 25 plant species, but in intensive confinement, this diverse forage habit is significantly limited. Because goats have a unique habit of picking leaves, often roaming among shrubs to gather them, they spend longer foraging than sheep. Goats are very particular about the freshness of their forage, and often refuse to eat or drink if the feed or water in the trough or pasture is contaminated by sheep feces or urine. From an evolutionary perspective, this behavior serves to prevent parasitic infections.

Goats have the ability to distinguish bitter, salty, sour and sweet tastes. Since they are born with the habit of picking leaves and eating them, and leaves and bark are generally more bitter than grass, goats have a higher tolerance for bitterness than other ruminants.

Goats' feed and water intake are inversely proportional to temperature, but feed intake decreases when temperatures fall below 10°C. Penned goats often waste forage by pulling or pushing it out of the trough. Therefore, in addition to specially designed troughs, forage must be cut short before feeding. Goats spend approximately 6 to 9 hours daily feeding. Like other four-gastric animals, they ruminate, primarily at night, with each rumination lasting about one minute. Free-range goats spend approximately 28% of their daytime feeding, one-third of which is spent grazing on the ground and two-thirds on picking leaves. The remaining 50% is spent resting, 10% ruminating, and 12% traveling. For intensively housed Saanen goats, the daytime distribution is 20% feeding, 25% ruminating, 20% traveling, 11% sleeping, 14% lying down, and 8% resting. They defecate 11.2 times and urinate 8.3 times daily. In situations of urgency or alertness, the frequency and duration of rumination decreases relatively.

Goats easily adapt to water shortages because their water turnover rate is only 188 mL/kg/24 hours, similar to camels' 185 mL/kg/24 hours and far lower than cattle's 347 mL/kg/24 hours. Therefore, they are more tolerant of short periods of water deprivation than cattle. Goats are known for their mysophobia and will refuse to eat hay contaminated by fallen soil. They prefer clean, cool water. If fecal particles or urine fall into the water trough, or the water temperature rises due to sunlight, they will rather thirst than drink. This is one of the main causes of urinary stone blockage, so the water trough must be cleaned daily and clean, cool water must be provided.

Goats lift their tails and wag them when defecating, producing pellets of feces. Ewes and rams urinate in a semi-squatting position. During the non-breeding season, the penis of rams rarely, if ever, protrudes beyond the foreskin. However, during the breeding season, the situation is different: urination is accompanied by an erection and sexual activity.

Sheep in temperate regions breed seasonally, beginning in late summer and early autumn as daylight length gradually decreases, with spring and summer being the anestrus season. Ewes native to tropical regions, however, are not restricted by season and estrus occurs year-round. Rams follow a similar breeding pattern to ewes, but enter the breeding season approximately two weeks earlier. During the anestrus season, rams have lower testosterone levels in their blood and exhibit less body odor.

During the anestrus season, libido and sperm quality may decrease. However, when faced with hormonally induced estrus ewes, rams will be stimulated to mate even during the anestrus season. The behavior and body odor of rams during the breeding season are a strong aphrodisiac, which can induce obvious estrus symptoms in ewes. The body odor of rams is caused by at least two factors: (1) Rams repeatedly spray urine on their heads, necks, and forequarters. Sometimes they ejaculate urine into their mouths and open their mouths and curl their upper lips to produce the Flehmen reaction. (2) Rams have sebaceous glands on their heads, located behind the horns, which may be the source of the odor (6-Grans nonenal) during the breeding season. This substance is also likely secreted from the sebaceous glands at the base of the tail.

Sexual behavior in meat sheep includes courtship and mounting, controlled by sex hormones. When the ovarian follicles of ewes mature, they begin to show symptoms of estrus, including restlessness, bleating, tail wagging, frequent urination, redness and swelling of the vulva, mucus discharge, and attempts to mount other ewes or be mounted by others. If a ram is in the flock, the ewe will approach him, rubbing her head against his neck or body, vigorously wagging her tail, and urinating. When the ram is in its most favorable estrus phase, the ewe will frequently point her vulva toward the ram in an attempt to mate. If the ram is not in its most favorable estrus phase, the ewe will immediately run away when mounted. When the ram is in its most favorable estrus phase, the ewe will stand still for several seconds, ready to accept the ram's mating. After the most favorable estrus phase, even if the ram mounts, the ewe will no longer be willing to mate and will run away.

At the beginning or during the breeding season, rams may exhibit aggressive behavior to establish dominance. Their standing posture during a fight can injure caretakers, so rams of different sizes should be kept separately. When encountering a ewe in estrus, they will approach and follow her, placing one of their forelegs on her back to tease her. Upon smelling the ewe's vulva or urine, they will exhibit a flamboyant reflex, extending their neck and raising their head, parting their lips and exposing their teeth and gums. Occasionally, they may bellow, raise their head, or lower their neck to the ground. If the ewe is in stable estrus, the ram can successfully mate, with copulation taking only a few seconds before ejaculation. If the number of ewes in estrus is small, a single ram may mate with a ewe several to dozens of times per day. Furthermore, ram courtship is often accompanied by frantic vocalizations and rapid tongue movements. A sexually active ram often loses weight during the breeding season due to inattention to feeding and excessive breeding.

Maternal Behavior: Healthy lambs will attempt to stand approximately 10-20 minutes after birth and can stand independently approximately 30 minutes to an hour after birth. The lamb's first action after standing is to locate the ewe's teat. The ewe often rotates her body, bringing her udder closer to the lamb to help it find the teat quickly. Around 50 minutes to two hours after birth , the lamb will successfully locate the teat and begin suckling colostrum. Initially, the ewe allows the lamb to nurse at any time and allow the lamb to fully absorb the milk. However, after one to two weeks, the ewe will leave the lamb, limiting the frequency and duration of the lamb's nursing session to encourage the lamb to begin consuming solid food. While nursing, the lamb will occasionally push the tip of its nose against the ewe's udder, seemingly to stimulate milk production, and will noticeably wag its tail while doing so.

As birth approaches, ewes will become restless and scratch the ground with their front paws, engaging in primitive nesting behavior. A typical sheep gives birth to one to three litters. After the first birth, the ewe will first smell the newborn lamb and lick the amniotic fluid to protect it from the cold weather. Occasionally, the ewe will ingest the afterbirth left behind by the fetus. This behavior is repeated after the second or third birth. After birth, ewes generally display strong maternal instincts. They can distinguish between their own lambs and non-born lambs through their sense of smell, which is crucial for fostering parent-child bonding. If a non-born lamb approaches or attempts to nurse, the ewe will often immediately move away or push it away with her head. Abandoning a lamb, leaving it unattended when it attempts to nurse, or pushing it away with her head are all considered abnormal behavior.

Sheep grazing in the wild often leave the flock to give birth and hide their lambs. Even sheep kept in pens tend to try to hide when possible. If the ewe is startled, disturbed, or delays licking the lamb for more than an hour after giving birth, parent-child recognition will be impaired. If it lasts more than three hours, the lamb may be abandoned or receive less care from the ewe. Lambs grazing in the wild may be kept in a safe place for at least three days. Ewes that forage away return to nurse four to five times daily. If the ewe and lamb have a strong bond, they will be very sensitive to the bleating of the lamb if it is in danger from a distance and will immediately return to check on it. In penned sheep, a small box is set up within the pen for easy entry and exit. Lambs under one week old will also use the box as a hiding place, leaving only to nurse.

Lamb Care and Management: The average gestation period for ewes is approximately 150 days. As lambs approach their due date, it's best to isolate them in the same shed to prevent the lambs from being trampled and killed due to crowding. The delivery room should be dry, warm, and well-lit. If the lambs are born naturally and smoothly, the ewe can lick the amniotic fluid off the lambs. If the ewe shows signs of labor pains but still hasn't delivered a lamb within 1.5 hours, consult a veterinarian to determine if a dystocia is occurring. If necessary, perform an assisted birth. Before assisting, the midwife should wash and disinfect both arms and clean the vulva and surrounding area with disinfectant. After securing the lamb, insert a hand into the vagina and uterus to check the fetal position, posture, and orientation. If abnormal, push the fetus toward the uterus to create space near the vagina, making it easier to correct the fetal position. Any fetus misplaced in the uterus should be corrected to a standard position with the head above the forelimbs and the back upward. The midwife grasps the fetus's two front limbs with the fingers of one hand and, in time with the labor pains, assists in the birth of the lamb. After the lamb is delivered, the amniotic fluid can be wiped dry with a clean towel, and as much amniotic fluid and mucus as possible removed from the mouth and nasal cavity. If it is difficult to remove amniotic fluid from deep within the mouth and nose, the midwife can hold the lamb's body and head with both hands, tilting the head downward and gently shaking the fluid downward to prevent suffocation. After the lamb is born, the umbilical cord is smeared with iodine tincture or betadine. The umbilical cord is tied with iodine-soaked cotton string 1-2 centimeters from the abdominal wall. Finally, the umbilical cord and cotton string are cut at the lower end of the ligature. This prevents the umbilical cord from rotting due to prolonged moisture during the rainy season, which can lead to flies laying eggs and maggots in the umbilical cord, causing umbilical corditis, and prevents bacteria from ascending from the umbilical cord to infect internal organs.

About 30 minutes to an hour after birth, the fetus can stand on its own and begin seeking the ewe's teat to suck colostrum. Colostrum is rich in antibodies, produced by the ewe in response to environmental pathogens. These antibodies accumulate in large quantities in the ewe's milk during the latter stages of pregnancy. Lambs should consume colostrum within 12 hours of birth. This allows antibodies against large proteins in the milk to pass through the lamb's small intestine and be absorbed. This process is called migratory antibodies. After 12 hours, the lamb's ability to absorb these antibodies gradually decreases, and after 36 hours, colostrum antibodies are virtually unavailable. These migratory antibodies from colostrum protect the lamb for approximately six weeks, protecting it from common environmental pathogens. After six weeks of age, the lamb's own active immune system takes over. Colostrum also has a mild laxative effect, facilitating the excretion of meconium. It also contains a high level of vitamin A, providing sufficient nutritional support for the lamb's early years.

The fetal temperature within the ewe's body is approximately 39°C (104°F). In winter, the ambient temperature after birth is approximately 15°C (61°F). The lamb is exposed to a temperature drop of 24°C (15°F). This, combined with the damp amniotic fluid in the lamb's body, can cause significant cold stress, leading to weakness and increased susceptibility to pathogens, such as upper respiratory tract infections, pneumonia, or gastroenteritis. Therefore, during the late stages of pregnancy, ewes should be housed in a farrowing pen protected from northerly winds. After birth, the ewe should be checked to ensure she has licked the amniotic fluid from the lamb. If the ewe is not maternal and fails to do so, assistance should be provided to quickly dry the lamb with a clean towel or a hairdryer. If a lamb is hypothermic, it should be wrapped in a blanket or towel and placed under a heater or heat lamp. Straw bedding can be placed on the raised bed in the farrowing pen, and a heat lamp can be hung in a corner to provide the lamb with a resting and warming area. This insulation measure to protect lambs from cold stress should be maintained until the lambs are at least 2 weeks old. In the summer, when the temperature is high, the farrowing house may not need insulation equipment, but ventilation and dryness are the principles. A hot and humid environment is still one of the stress factors for lambs.

After calving, ewes begin lactating. Lambs are not producing much milk at this time. Owners can express excess milk, divide it into 100cc plastic bags, and freeze it for storage. This can last for over a year. If other ewes are unable to nurse for some reason, the frozen colostrum can be thawed in 40°C warm water and fed to the lambs, which also provides antibodies. Lambs that require artificial nursing should be fed 50-100cc of colostrum three to four times daily. After three days, artificial milk can be used instead. For example, to control infection with the caprine arthritis encephalitis (CAE) virus, lambs born from CAE-infected ewes should be immediately separated from the dam and not exposed to colostrum directly to prevent infection. Colostrum must be sterilized at 56°C for one hour after expression before it can be fed to lambs. If the sterilized colostrum cannot be consumed in one sitting, it can be divided into small plastic bags (approximately 100cc per bag) and frozen. Thaw at 40°C before feeding to lambs. Lambs that have consumed low-temperature sterilized colostrum should then be fed artificial milk instead of the ewe's milk until weaning to control CAE infection.

begin to learn to consume concentrate and hay on their own around two weeks after birth . Provide a creep feed containing 18% crude protein and 75% total digestible nutrients. Provide 50 grams of creep feed per lamb daily, along with free-flowing high-quality hay, to promote proper gastric development. Lambs are weaned between 10 and 12 weeks of age. Abrupt weaning of lambs that have not adapted to solid feeds can lead to digestive problems such as indigestion and bloating. To prevent creep feed from being consumed by the ewes during the creep period, construct a vertical wooden slatted fence in a corner of the sheep pen and place the crate inside, allowing only the lambs access to feed. A low-lying trough can also be installed within the fence to provide drinking water for the lambs.

Well-managed ranches should have a reliable sheep identification system for record-keeping purposes. Newborn lambs can be temporarily marked with tape around their necks or feet with a marker. Ear tags can then be attached after dehorning. Alternatively, ears can be clipped 24 hours after birth for permanent identification. If a tattoo is required inside the lamb's ear for logging purposes, it should be done after the lamb is three months old to prevent further ear growth, which could enlarge or separate the tattoo and affect future identification.

Dehorning, castration, and removal of accessory teats can be performed simultaneously on lambs between one and two weeks of age. The purpose of dehorning is to reduce injuries from fighting as the lambs grow and to facilitate management. Common dehorning methods include caustic soda or cautery sticks, dehorners, and cautery devices. However, any dehorning method has the disadvantage of causing localized damage. Dehorning with caustic soda sticks uses strong salt to corrode and destroy the growth points around the horn base, halting horn growth. Afterwards, wipe away excess fluid with a wrung-out wet towel to prevent dripping and eye injury. Mechanical dehorners use opposing sharp blades to cut the horn base and growth points, causing significant bleeding. After dehorning, apply pressure to the bleeding area with cotton dipped in ferrous chloride solution to stop the bleeding. Electric or flame-heated dehorners are most commonly used. After heating the metal tip of the dehorner to red, temporarily unplug the power cord or remove the flame. Once the tip turns black, the horn and surrounding growth points can be burned. However, incomplete burning often occurs, potentially leading to horn deformities in the future. Technically, after placing the tip of the dehorner at the base of the horn, rotate the handle in a circular motion to heat all the growth points around the base. The burning process takes approximately 7-10 seconds. Afterward, apply an ice pack to cool the area and then apply sulfa ointment. Keep flies away for two weeks to prevent maggots. Dehorning rams for breeding is generally not performed. If dehorning is performed, it is important to verify that the ram is naturally hornless. Naturally hornless rams should not be used as breeding rams, as mating with ewes may result in offspring with intersex characteristics. After dehorning, check the teats of young sheep. If there are any extra small teats around the two normal teats, these are called accessory teats and can be removed with curved scissors and coated with iodine. If it is difficult to distinguish between the primary and accessory teats, wait until the sheep grows up before deciding whether to keep them.

The purpose of castration of young rams is to prevent a strong mutton odor when they are marketed after fattening. At the same time as dehorning, non-breeding rams can be castrated surgically or by rubber banding. The surgical method involves first disinfecting the testicles and the surrounding area with iodine. Then, the scrotal skin and testicular capsule are incised at the base of the testicles, the testicles are squeezed out, the mesentery is peeled off, and the blood vessels and vas deferens are severed. The rubber band method uses a retractor to stretch a special rubber band, which is then inserted into the scrotum and tightly bound around the testicles. This causes the tissue below the band to dryly necrotize and fall off, achieving castration. In recent years, surgical castration has been performed at 4-5 months of age. This aims to extend the breeding period of breeding rams, improve the accuracy of breeding ram selection, accelerate weight gain, and reduce the risk of urinary stone blockage. However, the disadvantages of this method include larger incisions, more bleeding, labor-intensive procedures, and an increased risk of tetanus. The surgical procedure involves an assistant first placing the sheep in a side-lying position, or standing face-to-face with the sheep, holding the sheep's neck with their feet and grasping the tibiae of both hind legs with their hands to force the sheep into a standing position. After the sheep is secured, the exposed testicles are smeared or sprayed with strong iodine tincture for disinfection. An incision is made below the testicles, one on each side, down to the testicular capsule. The testicle is first expelled from one side, and the capsule is peeled away from the epididymis, exposing the vas deferens and blood vessels connecting to the testicle. These are then severed by blunt force pulling at the groin. After both testicles are removed, 5cc of betadine solution is injected into each incision, the wounds are sprayed with fly repellent, and 5cc of streptopenicillin and 0.5cc of tetanus toxoid are injected intramuscularly. After castration, unless heavy bleeding from the incised scrotum is observed, requiring veterinary attention, a small amount of blood dripping from the incision is normal bleeding from the scrotal skin. The sheep only need to be raised on a high bed for one week to avoid going down to the playground to contaminate the wound. The wound will dry up after about one week and heal in 2 to 3 weeks. When fattened to 11 months of age and sold, the scrotum will have completely atrophied and the meat will not have a strong fishy smell.

After birth, lambs should be screened for congenital defects such as cleft palate, rectovaginal gall, imperforate anus, hydrothorax, and umbilical hernia, and culled. Common diseases in lambs from birth to weaning include omphalitis, pneumonia, diarrhea, and coccidiosis. Providing a warm, dry, and clean environment and implementing proper husbandry and management methods are the best ways to reduce the incidence of lamb diseases during this period.

Growing Sheep Management: Lambs should be weaned at approximately 10-12 weeks of age or weighing 15-18 kg. Weaning should be done decisively, and intermittent feeding should not be interrupted by the lambs' cries. Lambs from the same flock that continue to suckle at each other's teats after weaning should be housed separately to prevent future lambs from turning to suck their own milk or that of other ewes. Breeding lambs should be fed high-quality hay as their primary diet during this period, supplemented with appropriate concentrates to build rumen capacity and function and prevent excessive obesity. Daily weight gain should be controlled at 0.1-0.12 kg for female lambs and 0.12-0.15 kg for male lambs .

At weaning, male and female lambs are separated to prevent uncastrated male lambs from accidentally mating, which could lead to premature pregnancy and affect the lambs' subsequent growth and development. Male lambs culled early can be surgically castrated at 4-5 months of age and fattened to be sold at 10-11 months of age. The period from weaning to 8 months of age is a critical period for the development and growth of both male and female lambs. In addition to weight gain ranking, parental performance and the lambs' physical appearance are also important factors in selecting lambs for breeding. Male lambs culled due to not being selected should be castrated immediately after 8 months of age and fattened to 12-13 months of age before being sold. This is because castrated males require 4-5 months to lose their mutton odor. Female lambs are sold when they reach 45-50 kg after fattening.

The appropriate breeding period for female sheep depends on the breed, birth season, nutritional status, etc., and breeding is carried out when the female sheep is over 8 months old and the weight reaches more than 60% of the weight of mature ewes of the breed. If female sheep are bred too early and become pregnant, it will hinder the growth and development of the female sheep, increase the chance of dystocia, reduce the twin rate, and reduce the amount of milk produced after delivery. Female sheep should not be too fat or too thin before breeding to avoid abnormal estrus or infertility. During these two periods, the ewes should be provided with concentrate feed containing 14% crude protein and 70% total digestible nutrients. One week before breeding, the concentrate feed supply should be adjusted to 0.5 kg per head per day to stimulate the ewe's ovulation.

After weaning, lambs can be dewormed for intestinal parasites between 4 and 6 months of age. Farms implementing caprine arthritis encephalitis (CAE) control procedures draw blood from lambs at 6 months of age to test for CAE antibodies. Lambs that test positive for antibodies are immediately isolated and culled. Lambs infected with CAE during this period are prone to developing progressive paralytic encephalitis between 8 and 16 weeks of age. Weaning is a stressful time for lambs, and they must be mindful of respiratory and digestive tract diseases. To prevent respiratory diseases, sheep sheds should be well ventilated and temperature fluctuations should be avoided to minimize stress. Proper nutrition should also be provided to enhance the lambs' resistance and reduce the incidence of respiratory diseases. During this period, lambs grow rapidly and have high nutritional requirements, so they must be careful not to overfeed with concentrate, which can cause enterotoxigenic disease. Weaned lambs, no longer protected by lactic acid in milk, are prone to coccidiosis, especially in pastures that have had coccidiosis. Complete eradication of coccidia is difficult, especially in humid climates. Weaned lambs are particularly susceptible to widespread coccidia infection, leading to severe diarrhea. Maintaining a clean and dry sheep shed is crucial to prevent coccidiosis. Rams with calcium-phosphorus imbalances in their feed, chronic vitamin A deficiency, and insufficient water intake are more susceptible to urinary stone blockage during this period. Ewes with masculine characteristics should be examined for intersex traits and culled, and their sires should be identified to determine if they are naturally hornless.

Fattening Sheep Management: The fattening program for meat sheep generally begins with early fattening between weaning and six months of age. Lambs weigh approximately 15-18 kg at three months of age. After weaning, they are fed creep feed for an additional 7-10 days before gradually transitioning to lamb feed. The concentrate feed consists of 14-16% crude protein and 70% total digestible nutrients. Average daily gain (ADG) is set at 0.15 kg, and by six months of age, they reach approximately 30 kg. After six months of age, castrated rams begin late fattening, with a concentrate feed consisting of 16% crude protein and 75% total digestible nutrients. The concentrate:roughage ratio is adjusted to 8:2, achieving an ADAG of 0.15-0.2 kg. By 11 months of age, lambs weigh 53-60 kg, making them suitable for market. During this fattening period, confinement is the primary method of feeding to reduce the energy consumption of grazing, which can negatively impact fattening. In terms of nutrition, since lambs are provided with free roughage daily during the training period, after weaning and starting fattening, the roughage intake should be very slowly reduced and the concentrate intake gradually increased, eventually reaching a concentrate to roughage ratio of 8:2. This allows the rumen to adapt to the concentrate environment and avoid rumen acidosis. This adaptation period takes about 2-3 months. To understand whether the fattened lambs are meeting the actual predicted weight gain requirements, the lambs can be weighed monthly to calculate the average daily weight gain and adjust the nutrition intake accordingly.

Feed costs for meat sheep account for approximately 60-70% of total production costs. To reduce production costs, some agricultural by-products, such as peanut vines and distiller's grains, can be incorporated into mutton sheep feed. Peanut vines are high in protein and can replace some concentrate feed, but due to their laxative properties, their use should not exceed 40% of the daily diet. Care should be taken with peanut vines to avoid the formation of aflatoxin. Brewer's grains and sorghum meal are also suitable for mutton sheep at all stages of their production, but due to storage difficulties, they can be mixed with corn to make silage for long-term use, replacing 30-40% of the daily feed intake and reducing costs by approximately 20-30%. However, due to the high phosphorus content of distiller's grains by-products, excessive intake should be considered, as it can lead to dietary calcium and phosphorus imbalances.

During the fattening of castrated rams, care should be taken to prevent bloating, enterotoxemia, and urinary tract obstruction. Bloating is caused by moldy feed or excessive consumption of fermentable feed. Concentrate should be kept fresh and dry, and moldy coarse feed should be avoided. Enterotoxemia is caused by Clostridium species, which are found in the environment and are common in the sheep's digestive tract. Normally, they pose no threat to the sheep. However, when excessive amounts of concentrate are consumed, the digestive tract becomes acidic due to the fermentation of high carbohydrates in the feed. Clostridium species multiply in this acidic environment and produce bacterial toxins. These toxins are absorbed by the intestines and enter the bloodstream, causing enterotoxemia. In severe cases, the sheep may die within 4-6 hours. Therefore, in the management of mutton sheep, a stable feed supply should be maintained to avoid sudden overdose of concentrate or allowing the sheep to steal excess concentrate to prevent the occurrence of this disease.

Castrated rams often develop urinary tract obstruction caused by stones. Stones typically develop in the bladder. During urination, stones flow with urine into the S-shaped bend of the urethra or the urethral protuberance at the urethral outlet, causing obstruction and urinary retention. This condition is associated with chronic vitamin A deficiency, a small urinary tract, a chronic imbalance of calcium and phosphorus in the feed, and insufficient water intake. In addition to ensuring the calcium and phosphorus content of the feed and vitamin A, care must be taken to ensure the cleanliness of the sheep's drinking water. Because sheep are known for their mysophobia, they tend to refuse dirty water from the trough. Therefore, daily cleaning of the trough is essential to ensure cool, clean water is provided. Preventive measures can be taken to increase the crude salt content in the concentrate to 1-1.5%. This will encourage the sheep to drink more water after consuming the concentrate, indirectly increasing urine output and reducing the risk of bladder stones. In order to reduce the consumption of exercise for fattening meat sheep, when they are kept in a pen for a long time, attention should be paid to the cleanliness and dryness of the sheep house, and the ventilation equipment should be improved to reduce the accumulation of ammonia in the sheep house, which may cause stress in the flock.

Breeding and Management: Meat-producing ewes are primarily selected for their size, excellent meat production, high reproductive efficiency, excellent maternal qualities, and abundant milk production. Since their primary function is breeding and lambing, reproductive capacity is paramount. The gestation period for ewes is approximately five months, and the uterus requires approximately 35 days to recover after giving birth before breeding again. A maximum of two pregnancies per year are possible, but due to the breeding season (September to February) and the non-breeding season (March to August), achieving two parities per year is not always feasible. A breeding plan of three parities every two years or four parities every three years is recommended, with a spacing of eight or nine months between parities. This allows the ewes three to four months to prepare for another pregnancy after giving birth, minimizing the burden on the ewes. However, if a 2-year, 3-parity breeding system is used as an example, there will be one non-breeding season within those two years. For example, if a ewe is bred in September and gives birth the following February, the next breeding time will fall during the non-breeding season in May. If a successful breeding in May and October birth is achieved, breeding can be performed during the breeding season in January of the following year . To achieve effective breeding during the non-breeding season, estrus induction can be used. A corpus luteum release device is placed in the ewe's vagina at an appropriate time. On the 9th day, 504g of prostaglandin (PGF2a) and 400 units of good horse serum gonadotropin (PMSG) are injected intramuscularly. The corpus luteum release device is removed from the vagina on the 11th day. On the 12th or 13th day, the ewe will be in estrus and receptive to ram breeding or artificial insemination. This shortened calving effect ensures the ewe's full reproduction and birthing.

There are two methods for mating ewes: natural mating with rams and artificial insemination. Natural mating simply involves placing a ram permanently in the flock, allowing it to seek out ewes in estrus for mating. Alternatively, hand mating is used, with rams being released to mate ewes once daily, in the morning and afternoon, to conserve the ram's energy. Generally, estrus is more pronounced when stimulated by a ram. To monitor estrus in the flock, rams can be released once daily, morning and evening, and inspected pen by pen along the ewe aisle. Ewes in estrus will be drawn to the rails and exhibit estrus behaviors toward the ram. Once a ewe is confirmed to be in estrus and ready for mating, the ram is brought in for mating. If different rams are used for mating during the breeding season, detailed records must be kept to ensure the accuracy of the pedigrees of future lambs.

The use of artificial insemination in goats has the following advantages: (1) the amount of semen ejaculated by rams is about 1cc, containing about 3 to 6 billion sperm, while the number of sperm required for fertilization of ewes is about 60 to 100 million. If ewes conceive through natural mating, it is a waste of sperm. If the semen can be diluted and fully utilized after collection, it can expand and accelerate the breeding and improvement of the flock for excellent rams. (2) It can save feed and management costs for raising rams. (3) There are more opportunities to choose rams, avoiding the trouble of inbreeding or frequently introducing breeding rams, which has positive value in breeding. (4) It speeds up the inspection and selection of ram offspring. (5) It prevents the possibility of reproductive tract diseases being transmitted during mating. Rams can mate naturally regardless of whether the ewes are in estrus or not, and can conceive after several matings. When implementing artificial insemination, the correct estrus stability time of the ewes should be mastered to improve the effect of conception. The onset of estrus in a ewe is generally called proestrus, lasting approximately 6-10 hours. The ewe exhibits signs such as approaching the ram, experiencing redness and swelling of the vulva, and mucus discharge, but still refuses to be mounted. This phase then enters stable estrus, lasting approximately 18-24 hours. During this phase, the ewe approaches the ram, points her vulva toward him, shakes her tail vigorously, and stands firmly, ready for mounting and breeding. During the 0-6 hour stable estrus phase, the ewe's vaginal mucus is clear. Between 9-24 hours, the mucus becomes turbid and thick, with a cloudy or creamy color, indicating the ideal time for breeding and conception. Metestrus, 18-28 hours after stable estrus, enters metestrus. While signs of estrus persist, the ewe gradually refuses to be mounted. Ovulation occurs approximately 28 hours after stable estrus. To determine whether a ewe is in stable estrus, a vasectomized ram can be used, or a belly band can be placed around the ram's chest or penis. This will prevent the ewe from being successfully bred when being ridden by the ram. If the ewe remains motionless for several seconds while being ridden by the ram, it indicates that she is in stable estrus. For ewes in natural estrus, artificial insemination should be performed 12 to 18 hours after the first stable estrus is observed. For ewes that are synchronized with a gestational hormone releaser (CIDR), prostaglandins, or serum gonadotropin (pregnant mare serum gonadotropin), artificial insemination can be performed 22 to 24 hours after stable estrus is recorded.

During artificial insemination, an assistant can stand with their legs clamped around the ewe's neck. The inseminator uses their right hand to push upward from the ewe's ischium. The assistant immediately grasps the ewe's hind shins and places their hands on her flanks, positioning the ewe on her front legs with her body at a 90 -degree angle. Alternatively, an artificial insemination rack can be used, with the ewe's neck clamped and her hind legs elevated and tied to the rear crossbar of the rack, positioning her body at a 45- degree angle to facilitate artificial insemination. The ewe should be secured as gently as possible, avoiding causing panic or stress, to maximize conception success. After the ewe is secured, the frozen semen is removed from the liquid nitrogen tank and immediately thawed in 37 °C warm water for 30 seconds. The semen tube is removed, dried, and the non-cotton-stoppered end of the straw is cut to seal it. The semen tube is then loaded into the artificial insemination gun for later use. Then, hold the vaginal speculum open and illuminate the interior of the vagina to locate the cervical opening. Insert the insemination gun and aim it at the external cervical opening. Gently and gently, move the gun back and forth and side to side, allowing it to gently pass through the cervical folds. When the gun enters the uterine body, you should feel no resistance at the tip of the gun. Then, slowly inject the semen from the straw into the uterus, completing the artificial insemination. If the insemination gun has difficulty passing through the cervix, injecting the semen into the mid-cervix can also achieve a near-ideal conception rate. Avoid forcing the gun through the cervix. Injury and bleeding will reduce the ewe's chances of conception and may even affect future fertility. Also, avoid inserting the gun too deeply to avoid injuring or puncturing the uterine horns.

stops estrus after 17 to 20 days of mating, it can be preliminarily determined to be pregnant. However, some ewes may go into estrus during pregnancy, or may be about to enter the non-breeding season. Therefore, non-pregnant ewes may not return to estrus, which may lead to misdiagnosis. 3 to 4 months after mating, pregnancy can be determined by the appearance of the ewe's enlarged abdomen and gradually fattened body. Common methods for confirming pregnancy include : (1) abdominal wall palpation, which is suitable for 3 months of pregnancy. Use both hands to circle the ewe's lower abdomen and move inward and upward. If you feel the fetus moving in the ewe's abdominal cavity, it indicates pregnancy. (2) Ultrasound auscultation is suitable for the early 35-40 days of pregnancy. Insert the ultrasound probe into the rectum along the lower side of the rectal wall to avoid interference from feces between the probe and the rectal wall. Rotate the probe about 35-40 degrees to the left and right with the probe pointing downward . Listen to the sound of blood flow in the uterine artery of the ewe. The uterine artery of the pregnant ewe expands due to the need to increase the blood flow into the uterus. When the blood flows, it will make a clear and intermittent whooshing sound. In non-pregnant ewes, the sound is weak or there is no echo. (3) Ultrasound imaging scanning is used for the 25-45 days of pregnancy. The accuracy rate can be as high as over 95% . Use an ultrasound scanner. After shaving the abdominal wall, apply gel. Place the ultrasound probe close to the abdominal wall and scan upwards. Alternatively, insert the rectal probe into the anus and scan downwards towards the uterus. The fetal image can be seen directly on the screen. In the early stages, the fetus is not yet formed and only black, hollow amniotic fluid and white, dot-like fetuses are visible on the image. From 45 to 60 days of pregnancy, the hollow amniotic fluid, fetal bones and button-shaped uterine mounds can be easily seen.

After calving, the ewe's concentrate intake can be increased to accelerate postpartum recovery and ovulation, thereby ensuring multiple births at the next breeding. From breeding until 3.5 months of gestation, the concentrate intake should be limited to approximately 0.3 to 0.5 kg per day to prevent obesity, which can become a predisposing factor for pregnancy toxemia. After 3.5 months of gestation, the concentrate intake should be increased to approximately 0.5 to 0.8 kg per day to prevent pregnancy toxemia, which can occur 7 to 10 days before birth when the uterus is carrying two or more fetuses, resulting in loss of both the ewe and the fetus.

During the middle and late stages of pregnancy, as the fetus grows, the ewe needs not only an increased nutritional requirement but also additional vitamins and trace minerals. Therefore, vitamins ADE should be added to the feed, or the ewe should be given an injection of oily vitamins ADE one month before and one week after giving birth. This supplement can improve the vitality of the lambs born later and help the recovery of the ewe's uterus after giving birth. Trace minerals can be provided by hanging salt bricks in the ewe's house and allowing the ewe to lick them, or by mixing half coarse salt and half dicalcium phosphate in a plastic basin and placing it in the barn for the ewe to feed freely when needed. This can prevent the fetus from developing rickets due to calcium deficiency, or the lambs from being less energetic due to insufficient trace minerals.

If ewes are confined for long periods of time and have limited exercise, their hooves often grow too long or become severely deformed. This, combined with an improper standing posture, can lead to foot rot or lameness, and even reduce feed intake, affecting body condition and reproductive efficiency. Excessive hoof growth can also cause scratches on the nipples or udders, causing mastitis. Hoof trimming can be done with hoof shears or tree shears. Secure the sheep's neck to the barn rail. With your left hand, grasp the ankle and lift it upward, keeping the hoof facing upward. Use the hoof shears to level the hoof sole. If trimming causes bleeding, use a soldering iron to stop the bleeding, then apply iodine, or apply pressure to the bleeding area with cotton soaked in iodine, then secure the cotton with tape. Both hooves should be trimmed to the same height. Significant unevenness can lead to hoof deformation. Hoof trimming should be done twice a year.

Confined ewes require deworming approximately twice a year, and three to four times when regularly grazing, to prevent parasitic infections. Deworming should ideally be performed when the ewes are empty to avoid stress. Some anthelmintics can cause miscarriage in late pregnancy. If deworming is necessary during pregnancy, choose the right anthelmintic carefully.

Excellent rams play a crucial role in improving the reproductive efficiency of a flock and its overall productivity. Selecting a high-quality ram requires not only considering the genetics of its parents, but also selecting one with a good appearance, body shape, and sharp, sensitive eyes. Testicles are also a key consideration in selecting rams. They should be well developed, with a large circumference and symmetry. Some height variation is normal. Testicles should be reasonably firm when palpated. If they are too firm or too soft, or contain lumps of varying sizes, they may be pathological and could affect spermatogenesis.

1.5 years old, breeding rams can perform light breeding duties with approximately 20 ewes. After 2 years of age, they begin breeding 30-50 ewes. Rams over 5 years of age may be considered for culling. Normally, breeding rams should be kept in a spacious pen or pasture to allow for adequate exercise and build strength. Rams with insufficient strength, poor hind leg support, or excessive breeding will affect their breeding ability. Before the breeding season begins, rams should undergo health, physiological, semen, and libido examinations and assessments. At the beginning of the breeding season, rams can be housed in a pen adjacent to the ewes; their body odor can stimulate estrus in the flock. Avoid using two or more rams simultaneously with the same flock of ewes during breeding, as this may lead to aggression and fighting.

During the non-breeding season, rams are fed approximately 0.5-0.7 kg of concentrate feed daily in addition to free-flowing hay. During the breeding season, additional concentrate feed may be provided depending on the individual's nutritional status. During the breeding season, some rams urinate on their chests or forelimbs. Urine stains can occasionally cause dermatitis or secondary bacterial infections. This can be addressed by shaving the chest and forelimbs. Because rams' testicles hang between their thighs, they can occasionally become injured and infected due to fighting, insect bites, or punctures from shed building materials, leading to suppuration or fibrosis, ultimately resulting in loss of spermatogenesis. High ambient temperatures can also affect testicular spermatogenesis, requiring management improvements and preventative measures.

A culling system for meat sheep breeding stock needs to be established. Ewes that are too old, do not meet the required size, have low reproductive rates, or suffer from CAE infection with swollen wrists and severe udder damage should be culled early. Female lambs retained for breeding can be selected twice at three and six months of age, with a retention rate of 25-40%. To expand the ewe herd, 40% of the female lambs can be retained annually for replacement, thereby increasing the ewe population. If the retention rate is set at 25%, 20% of the older ewes and 5% of those unsuitable for breeding for other reasons can be culled annually. The ewe population can be kept constant at approximately five years of age. The selection of rams is more rigorous, generally with a retention rate of less than 5%. A sheep identification system should be established on the ranch to facilitate management, and color zoning can also be used. Except for tattoos used for breeding stock registration or epidemic prevention purposes, ear markings and ear tags or neck tags should be consistent to avoid confusion. Pedigree information should be established for breeding male and female sheep for breeding sheep registration.

Sheep shed construction and the space provided for sheep: Due to limited and expensive land resources in China, coupled with concerns about urban environmental pollution, sheep sheds are generally built in rural areas or near mountainous areas. In principle, sheep shed planning should consider drainage and ventilation, ease of management, smooth operations, environmental hygiene, waste disposal, robustness, capital investment and recovery, and the potential for future expansion. Sheep shed size is generally determined by the scale of the herd. Sheep sheds should typically feature high roofs to reduce the accumulation of stale air. It is best to keep walls free to increase air circulation in the summer, and plastic sheeting can be used to block cold northerly winds in the winter. Sheep shed railings should be made of tungsten or stainless steel to minimize damage from gnawing. Avoid painting any objects in the shed with lead paint to prevent lead poisoning from the sheep. Raised sheep beds should be approximately 60-80 cm above the ground. The bed surface can be constructed of 1.2 x 1.2-inch wooden strips with 0.8-1.2 cm gaps, or a coarse wire mesh with 0.6-0.8 x 3 cm gaps, allowing sheep feces and urine to fall directly to the ground. An automatic manure scraper can be installed under the bed to remove feces and urine daily, saving labor on manure removal. Adult sheep on the raised bed have approximately 0.6 ping (approximately 1.2 m2) of space per head to avoid overcrowding. A play area should be located behind the sheep bed, at least equal to or larger than the raised bed. Stones or bricks can be piled in the play area to accommodate the goats' climbing habits. A feed trough can be placed at the front edge of the raised bed to protect it from direct sunlight and rain. A water trough should be located out of direct sunlight to minimize moss growth and water temperature rise. A grass rack should be placed in the play area for easy cleaning.

Sheep house cleaning and disinfection: Sheep feces and urine under the high beds in the sheep house should be scraped off daily, and waste in the playground should be swept daily to maintain cleanliness at all times. Keeping the sheep house and the surrounding environment dry is the best way to control diseases. It would be even better if the ultraviolet rays of sunlight could reach the sheep house every day. Especially for sheep farms with harmful conditions, the sheep house should be kept away from moisture to control the continued spread of coccidia. To prevent the occurrence of diseases, you can consider spraying the sheep house with disinfectant. The ranch can prepare 2 to 3 different types of disinfectant, which should be replaced every 3 to 4 months to avoid the development of microbial resistance after long-term use of the same disinfectant. The amniotic fluid and afterbirth of the ewe should be swept away after giving birth to prevent the ewe from eating them. The bedding used to keep the lambs warm in winter should also be replaced when it is wet to reduce the potential for bacteria.

Maintaining a hygienic sheep barn environment: Drainage and composting facilities around sheep barns must be carefully protected from mosquito and fly breeding and rat shelters. Besides spreading disease, mosquitoes, flies, and rats can also cause considerable harassment to sheep. To address the mosquito and fly problem in pastures, some farmers spray insecticides or hang numerous fly traps in the barns. This has been effective, killing numerous flies with poison or electrocution. However, large numbers of flies and mosquitoes still exist in the pastures. This is because these measures only address the symptoms, not the root cause. The fundamental problem lies in the conditions that allow mosquitoes and flies to breed. Clearing drains and cleaning feces and urine, eliminating the breeding grounds for mosquitoes and flies, will naturally reduce the number of flies and mosquitoes and effectively reduce pasture odor. Rodent control is not easy, and it is recommended to use multiple methods simultaneously and continuously, such as bait, traps, cages, and repellers, to achieve the desired effect.

Waste Treatment and Utilization: For example, an average goat weighing 40 kg excretes approximately 0.2-0.4 kg of feces and 0.9-1.8 liters of urine per day. For a farm housing 100 goats, the total annual manure production can reach 11 tons. Left untreated, this waste can cause serious environmental pollution and promote mosquito and fly breeding. The collection of feces and urine on a sheep farm depends on the construction of the sheep shed. Currently, the preferred design utilizes automatic manure scrapers, either net-type or scraper-type, to collect feces and urine. A net-type scraper features a revolving mesh surface between the raised bed and the ground, collecting fecal pellets that fall from the raised bed while urine falls to the ground below, effectively separating solids from liquids. The scraper is activated daily, and the rolling mesh collects feces and pellets into a manure trough at the rear of the sheep shed. Due to their low moisture content, the feces can be dried briefly, bagged, sold, or sent to a composting facility for composting. However, the urine that falls to the ground requires a gentle rinse, increasing wastewater volume and treatment costs. A scraper-type manure scraper allows sheep manure and urine to fall directly from the raised bed to the ground. Automatically controlled, it is scraped into a manure trough once or twice daily. The collected manure and urine is then moved to an open area for drying, bagging, and sale, or to a composting shed for composting. A composting shed requires a three-sided building with a rainproof roof. A shed capable of handling the manure and urine of 100 sheep should occupy approximately 45-50 square meters. Composting is an aerobic fermentation process, with an optimal moisture content of 65-70%. However, sheep manure only contains 50% moisture, so water must be added during the fermentation process. During fermentation, the compost temperature can reach over 70°C. To replenish oxygen and maintain fermentation, the compost must be turned every two weeks, either by a shovel loader or manually. Turning the compost causes the temperature to rise again. After about four to five turnings, the temperature stops rising, indicating that the compost is fermenting. During composting, cover the pile with plastic tarpaulin to deter flies and mosquitoes. Composting involves microorganisms breaking down undigested organic matter in feces and urine, reducing the inherent odor. The high temperatures generated during fermentation kill harmful microorganisms, parasite eggs, and plant seeds, and reduce the presence of substances in the manure that are harmful to crop growth. This allows the treated organic compost to be used directly as fertilizer for pasture or crops.

Feed and Water Provision: Sheep concentrate feed should be fresh. Whether home-mixed or purchased from a factory, it should be used within 15 days and stored in a dry place to minimize moisture. Hay should be kept away from moisture and mold, and high-quality hay should be fragrant. When increasing the amount of concentrate or changing the formula during sheep rearing, it should be done gradually to avoid diarrhea or even enterotoxemia due to poor adaptation. Sheep are known for their mysophobia and prefer clean, cool water. If their water is contaminated with feces or urine and has an odor, or if it's exposed to sunlight and moss grows in the water, or if the water temperature rises, they often refuse to drink, resulting in reduced urine output. This is a major cause of urinary stone blockage in castrated rams. Water troughs should be made of easily cleanable materials, placed away from feces and urine, and protected from direct sunlight. They should be cleaned daily to ensure a supply of cool, clean water.

Separate herd management: Sheep on a sheep farm must be separated for management purposes, including sex, size, and fatness. Because sheep breed seasonally, rams are typically managed separately from ewes. Rams are only introduced into the ewe flock during the breeding season, or bred by hand mating. Ewes are grouped separately for foaling, pregnant, and empty lambs to facilitate management. During the fattening period, castrated rams should also be separated by size and fatness to minimize competition for food.

Reduce stress: Sheep exposed to stress can weaken their immune system, rapidly reducing their resistance to external pathogens and making them more susceptible to disease. Sources of stress include sudden rises or falls in temperature, heat or cold, prolonged lack of ventilation in the barn, prolonged noise disturbances, overcrowding in a barn, sudden changes in the feeding environment, chronic nutritional imbalances, sudden changes in feed, sudden changes in management practices, startling sheep due to harassment by strangers or stray dogs, forceful driving or beating, long-distance transportation, pain from accidents, and poor post-operative care. Good management should prioritize animal welfare and adopt a reasonable, gentle, and gradual approach.

Disease Isolation: The domestic sheep farming industry is plagued by a shortage of breeding rams within the ranch, which often results in the borrowing of rams for breeding. Alternatively, when sheep are purchased from other ranches, they are immediately placed in the flock due to a lack of housing. If the purchased sheep carry diseases such as conjunctivitis, caseous lymphadenitis, suppurative lupus, arthritis, encephalitis, and coccidiosis, these diseases can spread to all healthy sheep on the ranch, impacting normal management. Therefore, properly managed ranches should maintain an isolation shed in a remote area. All sheep entering the ranch from outside must be kept in the isolation shed for at least two weeks, with their health monitored daily. Any abnormal symptoms should be diagnosed and treated by a veterinarian, and only released into the flock when all conditions are resolved.
