Self-defense and epidemic prevention in mutton farms: In addition to paying attention to feeding management and maintaining nutrition, attention should also be paid to hygiene management to prevent the invasion of diseases and cause economic losses. Ranch self-defense and epidemic prevention is the most basic method of self-control of diseases and needs to be implemented continuously in normal times. The key points are as follows: 1. Epidemic prevention management: (1) Personnel management: Visitors or non-staff are not allowed to enter the ranch in normal times. Staff are also strictly prohibited from entering and leaving other ranches at will. When traveling abroad, try to avoid entering local ranches. Otherwise, after returning to the country, self-isolate for at least one week and do not enter the ranch to contact animals. (2) Vehicle control: Control the vehicles entering and leaving the ranch. Feed trucks, milk trucks, sheep trucks, etc. that need to enter and leave must be sprayed with disinfectant or passed through shallow disinfection pools. (3) Herd management: Animals should be isolated when purchased in normal times. Vaccinations should be administered as required. Animals on the farm should be vaccinated on time. Animals transported to the market for sale should also provide vaccination certificates as required. Dead animals should be collected by the chemical processing plant for chemical processing, incineration, or burial, and disinfection should be paid attention to. (4) Livestock house management: Livestock houses should be cleaned, maintained and disinfected as necessary. In the event of an epidemic, appropriate disinfectants should be used to strengthen the disinfection of livestock houses. (5) Epidemic management: If an abnormal disease is found in the ranch, the local animal epidemic prevention unit such as the county or city livestock disease prevention and control center or the township office should be notified immediately so that the epidemic can be dealt with as soon as possible. 2. Disinfection method: (1) The surface of the object to be disinfected should be cleaned first for better disinfection effect. (2) For disinfectants that are effective against general bacteria and viruses, please select them according to the instructions. For foot-and-mouth disease virus, a disinfectant that can effectively kill the virus should also be selected. (3) Other acidic and alkaline disinfectants suitable for killing foot-and-mouth disease virus are as follows: Sodium hydroxide: dilution ratio 2%, dilution method is 2 kg of alkaline tablets dissolved in 100 liters of water, but it is corrosive, so be careful; Sodium carbonate: dilution ratio 2%, dilution method is 2 kg of sodium carbonate dissolved in 100 liters of water, but it is irritating; Citric acid: dilution ratio 2%, dilution method is 2 kg of citric acid powder dissolved in 100 liters of water, but it is irritating; Acetic acid: 2 parts of glacial acetic acid solution mixed with 98 parts of water, it is irritating, but it is safe for humans and animals to contact, and is relatively non-irritating. (4) Do not use acidic and alkaline disinfectants at the same time, otherwise they will lose their effectiveness after the acid and alkali are neutralized. 3. Vaccination: Currently, the only vaccine that requires vaccination for meat sheep in Taiwan is foot-and-mouth disease. The following precautions should be taken when using the vaccine: (1) Use government-approved vaccines and do not purchase vaccines of unknown origin. (2) Strictly follow the method and dosage of vaccine use. (3) Follow the instructions of the veterinarian and administer the primary vaccination and the booster shot every six months on time. (4) Follow the instructions and pay attention to the storage method and expiration date of the vaccine. Never use expired vaccines. (5) Vaccinate according to the instructions of the veterinarian and never falsify the information.

Basic Health Checks for Sheep: When raising meat sheep, it's important to carefully observe their behavior and habits to fully understand their normal feeding habits and other behaviors. This allows for early detection of illness and prompt health checks or treatment. The average normal body temperature for goats is 39.9 °C, ranging from 38.7°C to 40.7 °C, with a pulse rate of 70–80 beats per minute and a respiratory rate of 12–20 breaths per minute . Abnormalities in a goat's normal body temperature, pulse rate, and respiratory rate may indicate illness. To measure a sheep's temperature, use an animal thermometer with a 30- centimeter string and a clip attached to the rear end. First, swing the thermometer downward until the mercury column drops below 34 °C. Then, fully insert the thermometer into the anus, securing the clip to a hairy area on the back to prevent it from falling. The thermometer should be measured for at least three minutes. After removal, check the temperature indicated by the mercury column. A rise in body temperature may be affected by factors such as ambient temperature, exercise, stimulation, and age. After excluding these factors, if the body temperature continues to rise, it may indicate that the sheep is infected and ill. Body temperature can also decrease due to low ambient temperatures, old age, or nighttime measurements. The pulse rate indicates the sheep's heart activity. If it is too high or too low, attention should be paid to whether the sheep is suffering from heat stroke, bloating, other febrile diseases, or internal bleeding. The respiratory rate can be calculated by the number of times the chest and abdomen rise and fall. In cases of febrile diseases or respiratory diseases, sheep will only have symptoms of rapid, short, or difficult breathing. A stethoscope can also be used to check for respiratory and digestive tract diseases in sheep to increase the accuracy of disease diagnosis. It serves as a basic examination of the sheep's physical health.

Health management of lambs: The quality of management after lambs are born directly affects the success rate. In fact, the health of lambs after birth is related to the health status of the ewe in the late pregnancy and the care after the lambs are born. The causes of fetal or lamb death from before delivery to 28 days after delivery are as follows. In the process of raising sheep, the causes can be targeted, and the sanitary environment or feeding management methods can be improved to reduce the loss of lambs.

Prenatal death: Malnutrition in the late pregnancy of the ewe, excessive obesity, infection of the uterus or birth canal, excessive stress on the ewe caused by high temperature environment, or infection by pathogens can cause the fetus to die in the womb.

Death during childbirth: Dystocia caused by an overly large fetus, abnormal fetal position, malposition, or uterine weakness, which may result in uterine rupture or fetal death due to lack of timely assistance.

3 days after delivery : It may be due to malnutrition of the ewe or inability to provide milk due to old age ; the ewe has no milk to breastfeed due to mastitis ; poor maternal instinct or disturbed or difficult delivery, which makes the ewe unwilling to breastfeed, resulting in fetal hunger ; postpartum lambs are exposed to hypothermia due to excessive cooling or stress due to overheating ; the ewe lacks trace minerals such as iodine, selenium, and manganese, causing the lambs to be excessively weak after delivery ; accidents or attacks by wild dogs or cats, etc., can easily cause the fetus to die within a short period of time after delivery.

Death within 7 days after birth: Abandonment by the ewe, mastitis, nipple injury, unwillingness to nurse, starvation; congenital defects such as anorectal lock or cleft palate; infection leading to diarrhea, pneumonia, sepsis, or white muscle disease caused by a deficiency of trace minerals such as iodine and selenium. Death within 1 month after birth; severe parasitic infection, pneumonia, gastroenteritis, and other diseases.

Ewe Health Management: Pregnant ewes not only need to maintain their own nutrition but also maintain systemic function to prevent the development of this disease. Providing the nutritional needs of the fetus is crucial. If the feed consumed is inadequate, metabolic diseases such as pregnancy toxemia and milk fever may develop . Pregnancy toxemia, also known as ketonemia or twin disease in sheep, is a metabolic disease that is common in late pregnancy. In ewes without effective prevention measures, the mortality rate can be as high as 80% . While general treatment is ineffective, management strategies can be used to prevent this disease. These include avoiding excessive obesity ; providing high-energy concentrate feed that meets the needs of the ewe and fetus for maintenance and growth during the last 45 days of pregnancy ; and providing free access to good forage or feeding small, frequent meals . In late pregnancy, avoid sudden feed changes, long-distance transportation, or exposure to sudden climate changes. Furthermore, other diseases should be prevented to prevent prolonged loss of appetite and subsequent disease. Milk fever, also known as peripartum hypocalcemia, is a metabolic disease that is common in ewes around the time of parturition. Symptoms include decreased milk production, poor appetite, lethargy, and occasional rigor mortis. The ewe's blood calcium concentration drops from a normal 9-10.5 mg/dL to 5 mg/dL . Treatment can include a jugular vein injection of 50-100cc of 25% calcium borogluconate. The injection should be slow to prevent cardiac shock, which can quickly improve symptoms. Prevention involves limiting the ewe's calcium intake to a low-calcium diet from 30 days before parturition until delivery, withholding alfalfa to maintain a negative calcium balance and activate calcium-releasing hormones. Abortions in pregnant ewes can be caused by both infectious and non-infectious factors. To prevent abortions caused by infectious factors, strict farm self-prevention and epidemic prevention principles should be adhered to. Newly purchased, unquarantined sheep should never be mixed with pregnant ewes to avoid introducing pathogens that cause abortion. Non-infectious causes of abortion should be carefully considered, including excessive driving or crowding, long-distance transport, collisions or fighting between sheep, prolonged noise exposure, inhalation or ingestion of large amounts of mold spores, and the misuse of medications containing steroids, estrogen, or insect repellents. When abortion symptoms appear, in addition to immediately isolating the aborted ewe, owners should wear plastic gloves and place the aborted fetus and place the afterbirth in a double plastic bag. These should be promptly sent to an epidemic prevention unit for inspection. Aborted fetuses and afterbirth that do not require inspection should be buried or incinerated.

Regular Infectious Disease Testing: Goat tuberculosis (TB) is caused by Mycobacterium spp. and is a notifiable zoonosis. It can be transmitted through droplets, contaminated milk, feces, urine, feed, drinking water, and skin wounds. Severe cases may present with symptoms such as persistent dry cough, dyspnea, enlarged lymph nodes in the head, neck, or abdominal mesenteric region, intermittent diarrhea or constipation, enlarged lymph nodes in the udder, and granulomatous skin ulcers. Regular testing can help detect TB early before symptoms appear, allowing culling. Currently, livestock disease control centers across Taiwan regularly test dairy sheep annually using intradermal tuberculin injections at the base of the tail. Positive sheep are immediately isolated, restricted from movement, and culled. Contaminated farms use intradermal tuberculin injections at the side of the neck, testing every three months. Regular testing resumes only after all sheep have tested negative three times in a row. Because this disease is a zoonosis, farms can apply for regular testing to protect the health of both humans and animals.

Prevention and Treatment of Parasitic Infections: Sheep of all ages can be infected by parasites, which can affect their growth and weight gain, causing economic losses to sheep farming. Young sheep are particularly vulnerable. Common parasitic infections in sheep include contortus, intestinal worms, trichinella, and tapeworms, causing nutritional deficiencies, anemia, diarrhea, weakness, stunted growth, enlarged abdominal girth, and a rough, dull coat. Antiparasitic drugs such as Levamisole ( Levamisole, Libiq ) and Ivomectin ( Ivomectin ) are the most commonly used for treating parasites, as they are effective and have low toxicity. Levamisole may cause miscarriage when used in ewes during late pregnancy, so its use should be avoided during this period. Ivomectin not only eliminates internal parasites but can also treat external parasites such as scabies. These two drugs are ineffective against tapeworms; Mebendazole is an alternative. Parasitic infections can be prevented through management measures. For example, sheep should not be grazed after rain or during summer morning dew to prevent the hatching of parasite larvae from being ingested by the sheep. Confined sheep should be dewormed twice a year. Grazing ewes are more susceptible to internal parasite infections and should be dewormed approximately three to four times a year as needed.

Breeding Season: Goats are a type of livestock that breed seasonally. In temperate regions, the breeding season for dairy goats begins in late summer and early autumn, when daylight length gradually decreases, and lasts from approximately August to February. Deep anoestrus occurs in spring and summer, from March to July, while August and February are transitional periods. The length of the goat breeding season varies depending on latitude, breed, and nutritional status. For example, breeds of goats from equatorial or tropical regions have no distinct breeding season year-round. Temperate breeds migrated to the tropics experience a gradually longer breeding season. Nubian goats, due to their tropical ancestry, have a longer breeding season than Swiss breeds such as the Saanen, Albain, and Toggenburg. Tropical goats are primarily herded, and seasonal forage shortages and high temperatures can significantly limit their reproductive activity, but this is unrelated to seasonal changes in daylight length. Taiwan is located in the subtropics, and the temperate dairy sheep breeds raised there, such as the Saanen, Albain, and Toggenburg, have mostly adapted to the local natural environment. As a result, the sheep do not experience severe estrus in spring. However, due to the influence of latitude, most sheep still show varying degrees of estrus in spring. For economic production, appropriate application of artificial regulation techniques to induce estrus is still necessary.

The primary factor affecting seasonal reproduction in goats is the seasonal variation in daylight length (photoperiod). In temperate regions, daylight length varies significantly year-round. As summer transitions into autumn, the shortened days and long nights stimulate light, which is transmitted via the goat's optic nerve to the brain, promoting the secretion of melatonin from the pineal gland. Melatonin, in turn, stimulates the pituitary gland to secrete gonadotropin, ultimately stimulating gonadal activity. This leads to ovulation and estrus in ewes, while rams experience a sharp increase in plasma testosterone levels. Other effects, such as spermatogenesis, body odor, and libido, also increase significantly.

Puberty: Puberty, or sexual maturity, occurs between 5 and 7 months of age in female sheep and between 4 and 6 months of age in rams, when they reach at least 40% of their mature body weight. Puberty is influenced by the breed, nutritional status, and season of birth. For example, early-maturing breeds like Pigmy or Formosan goats may see females puberty as early as 4 months of age, while larger dairy sheep like Sanen may not puberty until after 7 months of age. Puberty can occur early in female sheep raised in the presence of mature rams, or in the presence of estrus ewes in the presence of rams, due to sexual stimulation. To prevent unwanted pregnancies, ewes must be completely separated until 5 months of age.

Estrus and estrous cycle : Signs of estrus in ewes include restlessness, approaching the pen in search of rams, frequent tail wagging and bleating, frequent urination, decreased appetite and milk production, vulvar swelling and mucus discharge, and occasional mounting with other ewes. These behaviors and signs are particularly pronounced when rams are near the pen. Normally, vaginal mucus in ewes is clear and egg-white in the early stages of estrus, gradually becoming turbid or even creamy in the middle and late stages. This can be used as a reference for determining estrus during artificial insemination.

The duration of estrus in ewes is approximately 24 to 48 hours, with an average of 36 hours. Stable ram-mounted estrus lasts an average of 24 hours. Studies have shown that estrus duration is significantly shortened after ram-mounted estrus or artificial insemination .

The ram's body odor and courtship behaviors, such as calling the ewe's vulva, the Flehmen reaction , tongue thrusting and rapidly flicking, chasing the ewe, and slapping the ewe's rump with his forelimbs, together constitute the "buck effect ," which can strongly induce estrus in ewes and enhance estrus symptoms. The sudden introduction of rams into the flock at the end of the anestrus season can not only advance the breeding season but also synchronize estrus. During the breeding season, the sudden introduction of rams after a month or more of isolation can also synchronize estrus in ewes within one to two weeks.

The estrous cycle length of female goats ranges from 17 to 25 days, with an average of 21 days, but may vary depending on region, breed, and flock. Ewes often experience short cycles, lasting 5 to 7 days. This is due to early regression of the corpus luteum after ovulation. These cycles typically occur during the transition period around the start or end of the breeding season, early puberty, the first estrus after parturition, artificially induced estrus during the anestrus season, or superovulation. Ewes may also have estrous cycles exceeding 25 days, often due to embryonic death occurring 15 days after mating, i.e., after maternal recognition of pregnancy .

Ovulation: Ovulation occurs in ewes at the end of estrus, approximately 24 hours after the onset of the LH surge in the blood. Most ovulation occurs in the right ovary (53.4%). Silent ovulation, without any signs of estrus, is common in ewes before the breeding season.

Most female goats give birth to twins, but triplets are quite common. The number of ovulations increases with age, reaching a peak between 3 and 6 years of age. The number of ovulations is closely related to factors such as breed, genetic selection, season, and nutritional status. Nubian goats ovulate more frequently than other dairy goat breeds; ovulation is higher early in the breeding season than late in the season; and ewes with a medium or higher body fatness have a higher ovulation rate. For ewes with a low nutritional level or body fatness, increasing the caloric content of their diet 6 to 8 weeks before the breeding season can promote estrus and increase ovulation, a process known as flushing.

Breeding and conception: Mature rams can mate up to 20 times per day. However, in naturally mated ewes, the appropriate ratio of rams to ewes is approximately 1:50. If estrus synchronization is used for ewes, the ratio is reduced to 1:20, and hand mating is recommended. This involves manually mating ewes in estrus one by one with rams to ensure effective mating.

Approximately 72 hours after ovulation, the fertilized egg or embryo of a goat divides to the 16-32 cell stage and enters the uterus through the oviduct. Hatching occurs on the 8th day, and during elongation on the 15th day, it secretes large amounts of trophoblast protein 1 (cTP-1), which blocks the secretion of the prostaglandin PGF2a in the maternal uterine mucosa, preventing atrophy of the corpus luteum. This process is crucial for the establishment of early pregnancy and is known as maternal recognition of pregnancy. From elongation to implantation, goat embryos undergo intrauterine migration, ensuring that each embryo secures sufficient space for implantation during multiple ovulations.

Gestation, Parturition, and Postpartum Recovery (gestation, parturition, and puerperium): The average gestation period for ewes is 150 days, with a variation of ±2 days between breeds. Gestation period varies among individuals depending on the sex of the fetuses, the number of fetuses, and the age of the ewe. Gestation periods are slightly longer in males, singleton fetuses, and older ewes. Pregnancy in goats depends on progesterone secreted by the corpus luteum throughout its duration. Injection of prostaglandins (PGF2a) or removal of the ovaries at any stage of pregnancy can cause miscarriage.

False pregnancy, or hydrometra, is a common phenomenon in goats. In this condition, the ewe's corpus luteum is functioning normally, and a large amount of fluid accumulates in the uterus, as if pregnant. However, ultrasound imaging reveals no fetus or placenta. Once the uterine fluid (cloudburst) is expelled, normal fertility can resume, but recurrence is not uncommon. False pregnancy often occurs in ewes bred in spring or after artificial estrus synchronization. The cause of this condition is unknown.

Most ewes give birth during the day, with labor starting within an hour. Twins are born in a shorter time than single lambs, but the time between births can vary widely, from a few minutes to over an hour.

It takes about 30 days for the uterus to recover after giving birth. The interval from giving birth to the return of estrus in ewes is affected by the ewe's fatness, nutritional level, milk production and season. The interval from giving birth to the return of estrus is significantly increased for high-yielding lactating sheep, ewes breastfeeding multiple babies and ewes with low fatness scores . Those who give birth in the spring anestrus season must postpone the return of estrus until the next breeding season.

Age of Puberty and Breeding: Although female sheep reach puberty and sexual maturity between 5 and 7 months of age, this does not mean they can be bred immediately. Breeding female sheep too early not only reduces the twinning rate and increases the rate of dystocia, but can also hinder their normal development, reduce milk production, and delay the return to estrus after birth, ultimately outweighing the benefits. Due to the varying nutritional needs of individual sheep, the ideal age for breeding female sheep varies. Generally, breeding is recommended when the female sheep's weight reaches at least 60% of that of a mature ewe. For example, if the average weight of a mature ewe is 50 kg, the female sheep should be bred at least 30 kg.

Male rams are puberty at around 5-6 months of age. Non-breeding rams must be castrated before 5 months of age, and breeding rams should be at least 18 months old before they can be used for breeding. The ratio of breeding rams to breeding ewes is approximately 1:50.

Body fatness adjustment: Breeding ewes must be properly fed and managed according to the physiological needs of growth, pregnancy, and lactation. Being too fat or too thin will affect normal reproduction. Body fatness or body condition score is used to represent the nutritional status of the sheep. The score can range from 1 to 5, with 1 representing the thinnest and 5 representing the fattest. The body fatness of the ewe at breeding will affect the number of ovulations, prolificacy, and reproductive efficiency. In addition to reducing the estrus rate, estrus visibility, and twinning rate, too thin ewes will also affect lactation and estrus recovery after delivery. Overweight ewes, especially ewes carrying triplets, are more likely to suffer from pregnancy toxemia in the late stages of pregnancy. Generally, the body fatness of multiparous ewes at breeding must be adjusted to around 2.5, and for female sheep, it must be adjusted to 3.0. For ewes with low leanness, increasing the daily concentrate intake or increasing the caloric content of the diet 6-8 weeks before the breeding season can effectively stimulate estrus and ovulation, thereby increasing the twinning rate. Leanness also affects the interval between estrus and return to estrus after parturition. Except for lactating ewes that are too thin or have multiple births, under normal nutritional conditions, meat ewes can return to estrus around 35 days after parturition (14-67 days). Before the breeding season, rams should be adjusted to a leanness of 3.0-3.5 to avoid excessive weight loss from continuous breeding during the breeding season. However, rams in the fallow season should avoid being overweight and a leanness of 2.5 is sufficient.

Estrus Detection: Utilizing test rams is the most effective method for detecting estrus in ewes. In sheep pens with a shared walkway behind the pens , stud rams can be placed in the walkway early and late in the morning. Ewes in estrus will actively approach the rams outside the pens and exhibit estrus signs, allowing managers to detect and record estrus status pen by pen. If a shared walkway is unavailable, vasectomized rams can be placed in the pens, or stud rams wearing bibs on their groins and abdomens can be used to assist in estrus detection. If suitable rams are unavailable , culled ewes injected with intramuscular testosterone or intersex sheep can be used as test rams, both of which are effective. Estrus detection should be conducted outside the feeding period of the ewes to improve detection efficiency.

Breeding Methods: Natural mating is the most common breeding method for meat sheep, and can be categorized as free mating or hand mating. Free mating involves placing rams into a flock of ewes for free mating. Its advantage is that it reduces the labor required for estrus observation and hand mating. However, its disadvantages are that it makes it difficult to accurately record the number of estrus ewes and the date of mating, and it can be difficult to accurately determine the ewes' precise pregnancy stage for grouping and segmented management. Furthermore, if too many ewes are in estrus in a single day, rams can often over-breed or develop a preference for specific ewes, resulting in wasted semen and reduced conception rates, thus impacting the reproductive efficiency of the flock. Rams can be bred up to eight times per day, but prolonged over-breeding is not recommended, as it can reduce conception rates and the useful life of the rams. It is best not to place two rams into the same flock at once to avoid competition, delaying mating, and confusing the pedigrees of the offspring. Another way of natural breeding is monitored breeding, that is, after the ewes are confirmed to be in estrus, they are manually monitored and accepted by the rams for breeding one by one, which can avoid many of the disadvantages of the aforementioned free breeding method.

To reduce the labor involved in estrus detection and breeding during the breeding season, consider installing a temporary ram pen within the ewe pen during the breeding season. Regardless of the natural breeding method, the male-to-ewe ratio should be maintained at approximately 1:50. However, if estrus synchronization is used, the male-to-ewe ratio should be increased to 1:20 or higher.

Improved feeding and management: Sheep require different nutritional levels and management methods depending on their physiological conditions, such as growth and development stage, leanness, pregnancy stage, and lactation status. Ewes in the middle growth and development stages, those in low leanness, in the last third of pregnancy and early lactation, and especially those nursing twins or more, require separate grouping and additional rations tailored to their physiological needs to achieve maximum reproductive efficiency.

A balanced diet is crucial for proper sheep reproduction. Besides insufficient energy and protein, which can affect normal reproduction, excessive protein, a deficiency in vitamins A and E, or a deficiency in minerals such as phosphorus, cobalt, manganese, zinc, and selenium can also reduce reproductive efficiency. Currently, all domestic mutton sheep are raised in confinement. Some farmers who rely on hay as their sole source of forage for extended periods must pay special attention to vitamin A supplementation, particularly intramuscular injections of vitamin A-3E one month before the breeding season, which can effectively improve reproductive efficiency.

Low calcium levels in the diet of late-pregnant dairy sheep, or excessive amounts of alfalfa, can cause postpartum hypocalcemia or milk fever . High phosphorus:calcium ratios in the diet of rams, with a phosphorus:calcium ratio close to 1 : 1 instead of the normal 1 : 1.5-2.0 , can cause urolithiasis . In addition to adjusting the calcium-phosphorus ratio in the diet, adding 6g of ammonium chloride daily to the ram's diet can help prevent urinary stones.

To reduce the risk of miscarriage due to stress, ewes should avoid deworming, vaccinations, hoof trimming, stalling, driving, and long-distance transport during the second and third trimesters of pregnancy. Breeding rams are generally housed individually. To maintain their breeding soundness , penned rams must have their hooves trimmed regularly and be provided with adequate space for movement.

Breeding ewes should have a complete breeding record and maintain a production of more than three parities in two years. If ultrasonic pregnancy diagnostic devices can be used to detect and treat false pregnancies in ewes early, or to eliminate ewes with habitual miscarriage and reproductive disorders early, it will help improve the reproductive efficiency of the flock.

Year-round breeding and seasonal breeding: Most meat-producing ewe flocks are bred using natural breeding. Depending on the length of the breeding period between rams and ewes, year-round breeding and seasonal breeding can be categorized as either year-round or seasonal. The advantage of year-round breeding is that once a ewe is in estrus, she is ready for breeding at any time. However, the disadvantage is that it is difficult to concentrate pregnancy and farrowing, making it difficult to manage separate groups for simultaneous feeding. Seasonal breeding involves designing two to three breeding seasons per year, with each season lasting approximately 45 days. This allows each ewe to have two or more breeding opportunities during the breeding season. This not only facilitates grouping and segmented feeding management of the ewes, as well as monitoring of the ewe's reproductive efficiency after the breeding season, but also facilitates the concentrated fattening and marketing of meat sheep due to the more concentrated birth dates of the lambs.

The auction market price of Taiwanese mutton exhibits significant seasonal variations, with prices beginning to climb after August and declining after the Lunar New Year. Furthermore, castrated rams are typically raised until 12 months of age before being put on the market for auction. Therefore, if the ewe flock can be mated during spring estrus and give birth in autumn, the castrated rams can be fattened and marketed the following autumn, increasing farmers' profits. Because most of Taiwan's existing mutton breeds are hybrids of Nubian and local goats, the lack of estrus in spring is less pronounced in ewes than in dairy sheep. The goat milk market and raw milk prices exhibit significant seasonal variations, and dairy sheep breeds naturally reproduce seasonally. Sheep farmers can set their breeding seasons based on individual needs. For information on how to induce estrus in ewes in spring, please refer to the section on artificial regulation techniques during the breeding season.

Artificial regulation during the breeding season: Autumn and winter are peak seasons for goat milk consumption, and raw milk prices are also higher. However, due to the estrus of ewes in the spring, most ewes are unable to give birth and reach peak lactation in the autumn. This not only reduces the reproductive efficiency of the flock, but also leads to an imbalance between supply and demand in the goat milk market and reduces dairy farmers' goat milk revenue. During the deep anestrus season, using the ram effect alone to induce estrus in ewes has limited effectiveness. However, the ram effect can be combined with other artificial induction methods to effectively enhance the induction effect. Among the methods of inducing estrus in ewes with anestrus in the spring, light regulation and hormone treatment are the more practical.

Light regulation: This method mimics the natural daylight patterns of summer and autumn to induce estrus. Light regulation can take up to four months. For example, if a flock of ewes is scheduled for breeding in May, they must begin receiving artificial light in January. This is accomplished by installing fluorescent lights 2.5 to 3 meters above the bedding in an open-plan barn. The light intensity at eye level is adjusted to between 200 and 250 lux. A timer is used to turn the lights on one hour before sunset, off for four hours at midnight, and off one hour after sunrise. During the day, the ewes receive only natural light, for a total of 20 hours of light per day. After two months of this, artificial lighting is turned off. After another two months, a test ram is introduced into the flock. Using the ram effect, most ewes can achieve estrus within two weeks. To maximize the ram effect, the ram should be kept as far away from the sun as possible during the lighting treatment. Light regulation is not only less expensive than hormonal treatment, but also conforms to natural laws. Once induced into estrus, ewes can maintain two to three estrous cycles, increasing the chance of conception without the side effects of hormonal treatment. To ensure that rams maintain their robust breeding capacity in the spring, they must also undergo light regulation. A three- year French experiment confirmed that alternating long-day (16L : 8D) and short-day (8L : 16D) exposures every two months significantly improved semen quality, regardless of seasonal variations.

Endocrine treatment: Endocrine treatment involves inserting a silicone gel (such as CIDR®, containing 366 mg progesterone) or foam (containing 45 mg FGA) into the ewe's vagina and leaving it in place for 11 days. However, on the ninth day, 48 hours before the vaginal plug is removed, each ewe receives an intramuscular injection of 500 units of equine chorionic gonadotropin (eCG, formerly known as PMSG) and 100 micrograms of cloprostenol (a derivative of prostaglandin PGF2a, Estrumate®). The eCG injection dose can be reduced to 400 units for females and increased to 600 units for high-producing lactating sheep. Ewes enter estrus approximately 24 hours after the vaginal plug is removed. The advantages of this method include: (1) it is time-saving compared to light treatment; (2) it is effective: more than 90% of ewes can be induced to estrus, and the conception rate is more than 85%; (3) estrus is concentrated, which facilitates the implementation of artificial insemination; (4) it can increase the litter size, with an average of 2.2 piglets per litter. Disadvantages include: (1) CG antibodies may be produced, affecting the effect of subsequent eCG treatment; (2) treatment is only allowed once a year; (3) only one estrus breeding opportunity can be induced during the anestrus season; (4) the interval from the removal of the suppository to the onset of estrus in the ewe is proportional to the number of eCG treatments the ewe will receive in her lifetime, and the conception rate may also decrease with the increase in the number of treatments. Melatonin can also be used to induce estrus in ewes by subcutaneous implantation or oral administration, alone or in combination with light treatment, but the effect is not as significant as the above two methods, and commercially produced drugs are difficult to obtain, so it is rarely used.

Estrus synchronization technology: During the normal breeding season, rams are isolated from the flock of ewes for a period of time and then suddenly introduced back into the flock. This ram effect can induce estrus synchronization in the ewes. However, this type of estrus synchronization is not sufficiently concentrated to meet the requirements for artificial insemination. Commonly used estrus synchronization methods for ewes include prostaglandin (PGF2a) injection and the use of gestagens, PGF2a , and CG .

Prostaglandin (PGF2a) injection: PGF2a can cause corpus luteum atrophy, inducing estrus or abortion. Before administering PGF2a injections to ewes with unknown reproductive backgrounds, pregnancy should be confirmed. Ewes enter estrus approximately 48 hours after injection with PGF2a (e.g., Lutelyse®) or its derivative, cloprostenol (e.g., Estrumate®). Because developing corpus luteum cells are insensitive to PGF2a within five days of ovulation, and endogenous PGF2a is naturally secreted after day 16 of the estrous cycle, only one injection of PGF2a will result in only approximately two-thirds of the ewes in a flock of reproductively healthy ewes entering estrus. To synchronize estrus for the entire flock, a second injection of PGF2a must be given 11 days after the first.

gestagen, PGF2a, and eCG: Please refer to the "Artificial Regulation of the Breeding Season" section for this method. During the natural breeding season, the combined use of gestagen and PGF2a can significantly increase the frequency of estrus in ewes compared to using PGF2a alone. While CG can increase ovulation and farrowing rates, it also carries the risk of antibody production. During the normal breeding season, the use of eCG is not absolutely necessary.

Fresh semen collection and dilution: There are two methods of semen collection: electrical and artificial vaginal. The main principle of electrical semen collection is to use an electric rod to stimulate the seminal vesicle of the ram through the rectal wall to achieve the purpose of ejaculation. The quality and quantity of semen obtained by this forced semen collection method are inferior to those collected by artificial vaginal collection. It is generally only used for rams that cannot accept artificial vaginal semen collection training, are neurotic, have poor libido, or cannot use artificial vaginal semen due to limb disabilities.

The artificial vagina method is currently the most ideal method for semen collection in goats. The artificial vagina consists of three parts: a rubber-lined plastic tube that can be filled with 42 °C warm water, a silicone funnel, and a centrifuge tube for collecting semen. To minimize cold shock to sperm caused by temperature fluctuations outside the body , the equipment must be stored in a 39 ° C incubator during assembly and before semen collection. During semen collection, the artificial vagina kit must be protected by an insulating sheath to maintain the appropriate temperature. Before semen collection, the estrus ewe is secured to a neck rack. At the moment the ram's penis penetrates the vagina after mounting, the penis is inserted into the artificial vagina for ejaculation. After the artificial vagina is filled with warm water, air can be blown in through the water inlet to increase water pressure. Before semen collection, the inner wall is lubricated with a sperm-safe jelly.

Fresh semen, after being tested for appearance, volume, sperm concentration, motility, live sperm percentage, and total live sperm count, can be diluted at room temperature with skim milk (SKM) or egg yolk tris- fructose (YTF) diluent to an appropriate concentration for use. Because goat semen contains phosphatidase , which reacts with lactoglobulins and triglycerides, or metabolites produced by the breakdown of lethicin, potentially present in the skim milk diluent , reducing sperm viability and motility, centrifugal separation is recommended before dilution to remove the semen. The sperm concentration after dilution should be approximately 100 million live sperm per insemination dose (0.5 or 0.25 mL straw ) , or at least 5 x 10' . Any semen not used immediately after dilution should be placed in a water bath at a constant temperature and stored in a refrigerator at 6 °C. Freshly diluted semen should generally be used within one day.

Freezing and Thawing of Semen: After semen collection, it is diluted with Krebs-Ringer-Glucose saline to a concentration of 400 million sperm per mL. This is then centrifuged twice at 600g for 15 minutes, and the seminal plasma is removed. The washed sperm is diluted with skim milk diluent to a concentration of 1 billion sperm per mL and cooled to 4°C in a water bath. Finally, the diluted semen is diluted with skim milk diluent containing 14% glycerol at 4°C to a concentration of 7% glycerol and 500 million sperm per mL. After equilibration for 2 hours, the semen is divided into 0.25 mL straws, each containing 125 million sperm. Finally, the semen is frozen using liquid nitrogen vapor to rapidly cool the straws. The semen is then stored in a liquid nitrogen vat at -196°C for long-term storage. To thaw the frozen semen, remove the straws from the liquid nitrogen vat and plunge them directly into 36°C warm water for 30 seconds.

Artificial insemination equipment, timing, and techniques: Artificial insemination equipment includes a vaginal speculum with a light source, a semen thaw device, a straw sheath, an artificial insemination gun, and a lubricating jelly cream. For estrus synchronization using an intravaginal progestogen releaser (CIDR) using PGF2a and CG , estrus detection should be performed every three hours, starting 16 hours after CIDR removal . Artificial insemination can be performed 22 hours after estrus or 43 hours after CIDR removal . Frozen semen removed from a liquid nitrogen tank should be thawed in warm water at 36 °C for approximately 30 seconds. The straw should then be dried, the sealed end cut, and placed in the sheath. The straw should then be assembled with the insemination gun and used for later use. During insemination, the ewe must first be secured in a cervical cage. An assistant or other equipment should then be used to raise the ewe's hips to a near 90 -degree angle. Using a vaginal speculum and a light, the cervical opening is located. The insemination gun is then aimed at the cervical opening and slowly inserted. Once the gun has passed through the cervix, the semen is slowly injected into the uterus. The cervix is approximately 3.8 centimeters long and has three to five internal wrinkles. Only about 20% of estrus ewes have cervices that allow the insemination gun to pass smoothly. For most other ewes, do not force the gun; simply inject the semen into the cervix to a point within reach of the gun.

Superovulation and breeding of embryo donor ewes: Multiparous ewes with good leanness and normal reproductive background were selected as embryo donors. An intravaginal progestogen releaser (CIDR®) (AHI Plastic Molding Co., New Zealand) was inserted into the ewes' vagina (day 0) and left in place for 11 days. From day 9 to 11, i.e., 3 days before CIDR removal, a total of 20 A.U. (Armor unit) of pFSH (Tenka Pharmaceutical Co., Japan) was injected intramuscularly at 12-hour intervals in decreasing doses (4.8, 4.8; 3.4, 3.4; 1.8, 1.8). During the FSH treatment, 125 μg of prostaglandin cloprostenol (Estrumate®, Coopers, Germany) and 132 μg of LH (Sigma, USA) were administered. Another less expensive method for superovulation is to use eCG (Serum Gona, China Chemical Pharmaceutical, Taipei) instead of FSH. Each ewe requires a single intramuscular injection of 1000-1500 IU, along with a prostaglandin injection. For optimal ovulation, consider administering 1000 IU of hCG (Gona 500, China Chemical Pharmaceutical, Taipei) three days after the eCG injection. However, the number of viable embryos obtained using eCG is lower than with FSH.

Superovulated ewes will begin estrus on the day or the next following CIDR removal. Ewes in estrus should be immediately bred and mated naturally with the same ram every 12 hours, or artificially inseminated with frozen semen every 6 hours, until estrus ends. If the day of estrus is considered Day 0, embryo collection and transfer from donor ewes can be performed on Days 6-7, at which point the embryos have developed to the morula or blastocyst stage.

Synchronization of estrus in recipient ewes: Multiparous hybrid meat ewes were selected as recipient ewes. CIDR® was applied vaginally to the recipient ewes on the same day as the donor ewes and left in place for 11 days. However, on the 9th day, 48 hours before CIDR removal, each ewe was injected with 125 μg of cloprostenol and 500 IU of eCG to induce estrus synchronization with the donor ewes.

Embryo collection, recovery, and transfer: Embryos were collected from donor ewes by midline laparotomy on day 6 to 7 after estrus. The ewes were initially sedated with an intramuscular injection of 0.11 mg/kg xylazine (Rompun, Bayer, Germany). Ten minutes later, general anesthesia was achieved with an intravenous injection of 5.5 mg/kg ketamine (Shunshun, Yongxin Pharmaceutical, Taiwan). The surgical site in the midline of the abdomen in front of the udder was shaved and then disinfected with 70% alcohol and betadine. The uterus and ovaries were pulled out of the abdominal wall through a midline incision. After visual inspection of the number of ovulated eggs, the uterine horns were clamped near the uterine body with intestinal forceps to block the flow of irrigating fluid to the uterine body. Using a self-developed irrigating needle, the uterine horns were repeatedly irrigated twice using a one-way embryo flushing method, using 6-10 ml of PBS each time. The recovered irrigating fluid was briefly allowed to settle in a 15 ml centrifuge tube, and approximately 1-2 ml of the liquid at the bottom was aspirated with a pipette. Goat embryos were recovered under a stereomicroscope, and the embryo recovery rate (embryo recovery rate (%)) was calculated as (number of recovered embryos/total number of corpora lutea) × 100%. Recovered goat embryos were evaluated and screened for appearance quality according to the criteria of Lindner and Wright (1983).

Embryo transfer in goats: Embryos are transferred into the uterine horn on the same side as the corpus luteum through a midline laparotomy on day 6-7 after the recipient goat comes into estrus. Transferring two to three embryos per recipient goat increases the twinning rate. Pregnancy is diagnosed by ultrasound approximately 40 days after embryo transfer.

Diagnosis by external observation: During the breeding season, if a female goat does not return to estrus within 25 days after mating, it may be pregnant. Pregnancy can be diagnosed by observing mammary gland development in female goats around three months into their pregnancy. However, in multiparous ewes, especially lactating goats, pregnancy is difficult to diagnose based on changes in udder or abdominal size . Diagnosis based on abdominal palpation is inaccurate and unreliable.

Ultrasound pregnancy diagnosis: (1) Doppler ultrasound technology: Using the principle of the Doppler phenomenon, ultrasound can be used to detect uterine artery blood flow and fetal heartbeat sounds through the abdominal wall and rectum to diagnose pregnancy. (2) Real-time ultrasound technology: The detector has two forms: linear and sector (Figure 6-2-7), which are detected by the rectum and abdominal wall respectively. After receiving the ultrasound echo, it is converted into an image and displayed on the screen. The image of the fetus, amniotic fluid and uterine caruncle can be clearly distinguished. It can accurately diagnose as early as 30 days after pregnancy and the number of fetuses can be diagnosed within 45 days of pregnancy. In addition, it can also detect ewes with false pregnancies. It is currently the most accurate method for diagnosing pregnancy.

Enzyme immunoassay (EIA) progesterone concentration changes: About 21 days after mating, a commercial enzyme immunoassay kit can be used to measure progesterone concentrations in goat milk or blood to determine pregnancy. This method cannot distinguish between non-pregnant ewes with retained corpora lutea and those experiencing false pregnancies.

The Basic Concept of Humane Slaughter: In the information or knowledge economy, livestock production is viewed as part of the service industry, no longer solely focused on producing affordable, high-quality animal protein. It has evolved from simple production cost calculations to considerations of ecological conservation and, more recently, so-called ethical standards. Consequently, the consumption of livestock products is no longer solely driven by nutritional reasons; it is now viewed as a part of life's enjoyment. This focus on "production," "ecology," and "life" impacts the overall social image of the livestock industry and, of course, its sustainability. The livestock industry should cultivate an image of high quality, environmental compliance, and ethical practices based on the principles of caring for animals and respecting life. Animals may not possess human-like intelligence, but they do share our common emotions, including the ability to experience physical pain and psychological suffering. Caring for animals is not only about protecting their right to live, but also about preventing them from suffering unnecessary hardship. This concept is a shared understanding in developed societies and a crucial consideration in livestock production. Protecting animal welfare is a global ethical principle, an obligation and responsibility of society, and even a legal requirement. The suffering endured by animals during slaughter is a frequently criticized livestock welfare issue. The public is particularly concerned about unnecessary stress and suffering before slaughter. Transporting livestock and poultry before slaughter must be done with minimal stress, and stunning is mandatory before slaughter. This is a mandatory law, as practiced in developed European and American countries, and also included in Taiwan's Animal Welfare Act. A peaceful death is the fundamental principle of humane slaughter. Of course, death itself cannot be peaceful, but it can at least reduce suffering. Considering the welfare of livestock when they are brought to slaughter, and effectively stunning them before bleeding and slaughter, allowing them to die unconscious, are both legally mandated procedures. The transportation and slaughtering processes must be approached with compassion, kindness, and humanity. A "slaughter anyway" mentality is a serious concern.

Maintaining animal welfare and reducing pre-slaughter stress: With dedicated implementation, animal welfare measures can easily address some issues, and simple changes can yield significant results. For example, a holding area must be established within the slaughterhouse to allow sheep to recover from physical and mental fatigue after transportation, and clean drinking water must be available. Holding time should be at least two hours, and if it exceeds eight hours, feed should be provided. Fences should be used within the holding area to separate sheep from different groups. This prevents fighting between unfamiliar sheep, which can lead to stress and injury, and also facilitates veterinary inspection of the animals' health and helps identify the source of any problems. When moving sheep from the holding area for slaughter, it is advisable to pre-determine movement routes, ensuring that the animals move in one direction, in small groups, along a predetermined route. Using slow, steady, and quiet movements can avoid the use of brute force or electric prods, reduce stress, and improve efficiency. Furthermore, sheep are highly curious, and any noise, light, or equipment can distract them during the process. Using higher partitions to block external attraction is a simple but effective measure. Poor management and improper maintenance of equipment in slaughterhouses can easily reduce the effectiveness of use and increase animal suffering. Of course, there are also inappropriate planning and management, such as poor pen design and slippery walkways, which can also cause additional injuries and pain. Slaughterhouse design flaws and operations that violate animal behavior, such as dark walkways, strong winds, or people working noisily in front, can cause sheep to be frightened and difficult to drive, forcing staff to use more intense methods to drive them away, increasing the animals' suffering. In addition to following basic regulations when slaughtering sheep, operators should also use our wisdom and technology to develop methods to reduce stress.

The lack of awareness and education about animal welfare among industry professionals is often the crux of the problem. Some industry professionals, unaware of social trends and legal requirements, neglect animal welfare, even reacting with resentment, viewing these so-called humane appeals as merely hostile accusations, self-serving measures that are devoid of real value or meaning. The livestock industry must abandon this mindset, lest it be interpreted by the outside world as conservative, stubborn, and outdated, and thus illegal. While eating meat is natural, a civilized killing process should not involve additional cruelty. The livestock industry should encourage each other to uphold the principle of respecting life.

Effective stunning and reliable bleeding: Slaughtering livestock and poultry requires stunning before bleeding them out by piercing the heart or slitting the throat. This method causes the animal to lose consciousness and die from shock and blood loss, avoiding the conscious pain of death caused by direct bleeding. This is also a requirement of current animal protection regulations in Taiwan. The most common method used by the public to stun sheep is to hit the head with a handheld stick. This is not a method of stunning that is conducive to animal welfare because the location and force of the blow are difficult to control. Probes are often used abroad to cause stunning by damaging the brain. This is a cost-effective method, but it poses a threat of spreading sheep pruritus and is not promoted in Taiwan for safety reasons.

Stunning by electrical stimulation of the brain, also known as electric stunning, is an internationally recognized method for stunning sheep (goats and sheep). The required current/voltage conditions are 100-125 M/Amps, 1.0-1.25 Amps, 70-125 Volts, and a maximum duration of 10 seconds. The introduction site can be from the left and right cheeks in front of the ears or the nape of the neck behind the ears, depending on the direction of the restraint method. Electric stunning may have some adverse effects on meat quality due to improper operation, such as excessive loss of juice. Therefore, some slaughterhouses still use sharp knives to cut the throat to slaughter sheep, and slaughter them using traditional methods, with the meat quality appealing to the old-fashioned flavor. These myths certainly have their own market niches, but they are currently regarded as outdated practices that are contrary to animal welfare. In addition, it is not true that the meat quality of live slaughtered sheep is better. Scientific evidence and practice have proven that live slaughtered sheep have better carcasses. International experience has proven that electric shock is equally effective in slaughtering sheep. Improved electric stunning technology can now be tailored to suit the needs of traditional markets or processing operations, producing the appropriate meat quality. Taiwan has developed a smart electric stunning device for sheep that addresses the challenges of varying coat thickness and subcutaneous fat content, ensuring each sheep is reliably stunned and bleeding thoroughly with minimal strain. Lambs slaughtered this way retain their juiciness in a variety of dishes, whether stewed, roasted, pan-fried, stir-fried, or hot-dish, and has become popular with local businesses.

The key to successful electric stunning is effective restraint, a precise conductive point, and a stable stunner. When sheep are stressed, they not only push but also jump. Suspending their limbs (for example, with a net hammock supporting their abdomen), restraining their bodies with a clamp, and then pressing down on the neck behind the ears with a stunner before applying electricity can achieve satisfactory results. Of course, electric stunners must be routinely inspected to ensure their stable function. Abnormal current output, insecure animal restraint, incorrect shock site, and incomplete bleeding can not only stress the animal, causing it to consciously experience the pain of slaughter, but also affect the quality of the carcass.

Young lambs can be divided into the following three levels: hothouse lambs, which are about 2 months old and weigh 9-20kg; spring lambs, which are about 5-7 months old and weigh 20-25kg; and young lambs, which are 7-12 months old and weigh 25-30kg. Their carcasses are characterized by narrow ribs and a light red carcass.

One-year-old mutton carcass: The slaughter age is 10-12 months, the carcass characteristics are medium-wide ribs and the meat is slightly dark red.

Mature mutton carcasses: over 20 months old, divided into the following three grades according to sex: wether carcasses, ewe carcasses, and buck carcasses. Currently, wether rams available on the market in Taiwan are divided into three categories: pure black, parti-colored, and white.

Grading, cutting and utilization of domestic mutton carcasses: According to statistics from the Sheep Farming Association of the Republic of China, the number of domestic mutton slaughtered in 2004 was 17,258, and the trend is increasing year by year. This shows that domestic mutton still has room for development under the impact of imported mutton. The current standard specifications for grading, cutting and utilization of domestic mutton carcasses are described as follows: (1) Standard specifications for mutton carcasses: Taiwanese consumers prefer to eat mutton with skin, so Taiwanese mutton carcasses are based on unskinned mutton. The carcass specifications are as follows: 1. Complete bleeding. 2. Remove the toes between the carpal bones and metacarpal bones and between the tarsal bones and metatarsal bodies. 3. Remove the tail from the fifth caudal vertebra. 4. Remove the sexual organs and udder of the carcass. 5. Use flame to burn the surface of the carcass to make it slightly yellow. 6. Remove the horns. 7. Remove all internal organs. 8. Make a small amount of trimming of the carcass appearance, such as fat and minced meat. (2) Cutting and grading of mutton: 1. Whole-mutton cutting method: Cut the whole mutton into boneless skin-on mutton and boneless skinless mutton to supply the mutton stove. 2. Two-way cutting method: Cut the whole mutton into the front chest and abdomen and the hind leg, and then cut the front chest and abdomen into tenderloin (2%) , loin (4%) , flank (4%) , shoulder (4%) , and front leg (5%) ; and the hind leg into hind leg (7%) . 3. Three-way cutting method: Cut the whole mutton into shoulder, thoracic abdomen, and hind leg, and cut the shoulder into heart, middle rib, and front foot; cut the thoracic abdomen into flank, loin, and back, and then cut the flank into three layers of meat ( pork belly ) and belly ribs, and the back into large ribs, thick ribs, small ribs, and loin; and cut the hind leg into trotter, hind leg, and hind foot.

Foot-and-mouth disease (FMD): FMD is a highly contagious, acute disease caused by the foot-and-mouth disease virus (FMDV), a member of the genus Aphthovirus in the family Picornaviridae. The virus has seven serotypes: A, O, C, Asia I, South Africa I (SAT I), South Africa II (SAT II), and South Africa III (SAT III). Each serotype has multiple subtypes, and the seven serotypes do not cross-react or provide mutual protection. Transmission of FMD occurs through direct contact with blister fluid, saliva, semen, breast milk, exhaled air, feces, and urine from infected animals, which contain high levels of the virus. The virus can enter susceptible animals through the respiratory tract, oral cavity, and wounds. Feed trucks, animal transport vehicles, and carcass transporters can be contaminated and potentially spread FMD to other ranches. Tools, equipment, and cleaning supplies used at the site of an outbreak can spread foot-and-mouth disease. Workers' clothing, hats, and shoes can also serve as vectors. Unboiled viscera, lymph nodes, and bone marrow of infected animals can spread the disease. The virus can remain in the nasal cavity and throat of animal handlers, veterinarians, and public officials for 3 to 24 hours, potentially transmitting the disease to animals from humans. Infected animals excrete large quantities of virus through their respiratory tracts, especially pigs, which can excrete up to 400 million virus particles daily. These particles are carried by the wind, contributing to the rapid spread of the disease. Especially in low temperatures (below 10 degrees Celsius) and high humidity (above 60% relative humidity), transmission distances can reach tens or even 200 kilometers.

Taiwan has experienced an outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease (O/TAW/97) in pigs since March 1997, affecting only pigs and not cattle or sheep. In 1999, the foot-and-mouth disease virus (also known as FMD O Kinmen; O/TAW/99) was detected in Kinmen cattle. Although the virus was imported into Taiwan from Kinmen cattle and infected some beef cattle herds, there were no obvious clinical symptoms or losses in beef cattle at the time. Unfortunately, in late January 1990, foot-and-mouth disease broke out in dairy cows, with obvious clinical symptoms and lesions. In February 1991, another outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease occurred in sheep.

Clinical symptoms of foot-and-mouth disease: The incubation period is very short, approximately one to seven days. Affected animals clinically exhibit elevated body temperature, decreased milk production, decreased rumination, loss of appetite, and lameness. Small blisters form in the mouth and tongue. When these blisters rupture, shallow erosions may form, returning to normal after a few days. However, the sheep will only excrete the virus sporadically, acting as virus carriers. Newborn nursing lambs on infected farms will suddenly die. Autopsies of lambs aged 10 to 20 days will reveal tiger-striped heart lesions in the myocardium. Foot-and-mouth disease is generally only a subtle infection in sheep, especially with the O -type FMD virus, unlike cattle or pigs, which experience higher rates of morbidity and mortality after infection. The importance of FMD in sheep lies in the fact that when raised together with cattle, sheep often become a source of infection, acting as virus transmitters. The report indicates that after artificially infecting sheep, none showed clinical signs of disease. The virus could still be isolated from the throats of 87% of the sheep 28 days after inoculation. While the artificially inoculated sheep showed no visible clinical signs or signs of disease, the virus could still be isolated from their feces and throats 27 days after infection. Nursing lambs also develop tiger-striped heart disease because infected ewes excrete large amounts of virus in their milk. After suckling this milk, the virus invades the lamb's heart muscle, causing the disease.

Prevention of Foot-and-Mouth Disease: Avoid purchasing sheep from infected areas. If purchases are unavoidable, strengthen quarantine measures. When foot-and-mouth disease occurs, strict lockdown, isolation, and disinfection measures must be implemented. Sheep pens should be disinfected regularly, and feces, leftover feed, and bedding from infected animals should be immediately destroyed. If vaccination is necessary, confirm the prevalent virus serotype in the area and select the correct vaccine for optimal results.

Peste des Petits Ruminants (PPR): This disease is caused by Morbillivirus of the Paramyxoviridae family. The virus is slightly round and pleomorphic, larger than rinderpest virus, and contains a coiled, hollow, rod-shaped nucleocapsid composed of numerous herring-bone subunits. PPR is also known as Pseudorinderpest of Small Ruminants, Pest of Sheep and Goats, Kata, Stomatitis Pneumoenteritis Syndrome, and Pneumoenteritis Complex. It is an acute or subacute viral disease of goats and sheep characterized by fever, necrotizing stomatitis, conjunctivitis, gastroenteritis, and pneumonia. Sheep are less susceptible than goats, and cattle are asymptomatic. This disease is also known as rinderpest in sheep.

Clinical symptoms of PPR: Acutely infected sheep experience a fever of 40-41°C. Affected animals exhibit lethargy, a coarse coat, dry nose, loss of appetite, and mucosal congestion. Initially, a serous nasal discharge may develop, later becoming mucopurulent and accompanied by a putrid odor. The incubation period is generally 4-5 days. Small areas of necrosis may occur in the nasal mucosa, and the conjunctivae are often congested, with small crusts forming in the inner corners of the eyes. Some animals experience severe catarrhal conjunctivitis with purulent eyelids. Necrotizing stomatitis is common. Initially, the oral mucosal lesions are rough and red, and may extend locally to the dental lamina, jaw, cheeks, mastoid processes, and tip of the tongue. Salivation is profuse but not excessive. Diarrhea is common, often profuse but usually bloodless. Coughing is a symptom of tracheopneumonia in the later stages of the disease. Pregnant females may experience miscarriage. Morbidity and mortality rates are high. Secondary bacterial infection exacerbates the disease and may complicate its symptoms. Gross visual lesions in affected animals include emaciation, conjunctivitis, and necrotizing stomatitis, often located on the lower lip, gums, cheeks, and sublingual area. Severe lesions may extend to the hard palate and throat. Erosive lesions are superficial, with no clear boundary between the red, rough areas and the surrounding normal area. The abomasum is a common site of lesion. Erosions in the anterior duodenum and terminal ileum may present as linear hemorrhages. Peyer's patches are significantly affected, and lymphoid tissue is necrotic. Lesions in the large intestine are primarily located at the ileocecal valve, the ceco-colonic junction, and the rectum. The rectum exhibits linear congestion due to mucosal folds, giving it a "zebra-striped" appearance.

Diagnosis of PPR: A preliminary diagnosis can be made based on clinical symptoms, pathological changes, and epidemiological data. Confirmation requires virus isolation and identification. Samples submitted for examination should include anticoagulated blood, lymph nodes, tonsils, spleen , and lungs. Because of the presence of common antigens, complement fixation or agar gel sedimentation tests cannot distinguish PPR from rinderpest. Neutralizing antibody testing can assist in the diagnosis of PPR.

Rift Valley Fever ( RVF) : This disease is caused by an RNA virus of the genus Phlebovirus , family Bunyaviridae . The virus is very stable in protein-rich solutions ( near-neutral pH ) . In serum, the virus can survive for two years in the refrigerator. It is stable and infectious in air, but acidic pH , lipid solvents, detergents, and common disinfectants do not activate the virus. All naturally isolated strains of the virus have similar biological and serological properties. RVF is an acute, arthropod-transmitted viral disease with high mortality in young animals. It primarily affects sheep, cattle, and goats, with clinical manifestations primarily consisting of hepatitis and abortion. Symptoms vary greatly and vary by species and age. Abortion occurs in most species, occurring during the acute or convalescent phase. Kids, calves, and sheep are most susceptible.

Clinical symptoms of Reeve Valley Fever: This disease can be categorized into four types: hyperacute, acute, subacute, and inapparent. The hyperacute form typically occurs in goats, calves, and sheep under seven days of age. It has an incubation period of 12 hours, followed by collapse and death within 24-48 hours. The initial illness presents with a fever (105-108°F). Although weakness, listlessness, and loss of appetite may occur in the 24 hours before death, the deceased animal exhibits no characteristic symptoms. The acute form typically occurs in older lambs and less frequently in adult sheep. It is characterized by a rapid rise in temperature (104-106°F), accompanied by vomiting, bloody diarrhea, and unsteady gait. Death occurs within one to four days of infection. The subacute form, which typically occurs in adult sheep, is characterized by a fever lasting 24-96 hours, accompanied by loss of appetite and general weakness. The mortality rate is low. The inapparent form presents only with a mild fever and is generally detectable by serological tests. Field and laboratory studies have shown that within a week of infecting sheep herds, Rif Valley fever can cause a 90-100% mortality rate in animals under seven days old, a 10-30% mortality rate in older and adult sheep, and a 95-100% abortion rate in pregnant ewes. Mortality is particularly high in young animals, and abortion rates are extremely high in cattle and sheep. Characteristic lesions are found in the liver.

Bluetongue : The virus that causes bluetongue is classified as an Orbivirus in the family Reoviridae . It is a double-stranded RNA virus, 80-100 nm in size, unenveloped, resistant to lipid solvents, and sensitive to acid. Naturally infected animals include sheep, cattle, goats, white-tailed deer, mule deer, antelope, and bison. The incubation period in sheep is approximately 6-8 days, with symptoms including depression, fever, leukopenia, redness and swelling of the mucous membranes, lip and gingival ulcers, laminitis, coronitis, edema, torticollis, and vomiting. Ewes may miscarry or give birth to weak lambs. The incidence rate is 80-90% , but the mortality rate varies greatly, ranging from 0% to 50% . Infection in goats is generally asymptomatic . In Taiwan, the pathogen was isolated from asymptomatic sheep in Kinmen and Pingtung counties in June 2003 .

Clinical symptoms of bluetongue: Only sheep infected with this disease will have obvious pathological changes, such as bleeding at the base of the pulmonary artery, congestion and edema of the nasal mucosa and pharynx, pneumonia, bilateral bronchial lobular pneumonia, subcutaneous edema of the ear wings, congestion of the hoof crown, oral ulcers, heart bleeding, edema, skeletal muscle bleeding, necrosis, lymphadenopathy, lymph node congestion and bleeding, congenitally infected lambs, skeletal muscle infiltration of yellow gelatinous exudate, hydrocephalus, and cerebellar hypoplasia.

Sheep pox and goat pox: Caused by the Pox virus, sheep pox and goat pox are classified in the Capripox genus, which also includes the lumpy skin disease virus. These viruses are acid-labile and resistant to drying, and can survive in skin dander for over three months. While malignant pox diseases generally exhibit host preference, they are not host-specific for goats, sheep, or cattle.

Clinical symptoms of sheep pox include fever, rhinitis, lacrimation, and conjunctivitis. A rash is often found in areas of sparse fur , such as the axilla, groin, scrotum, udder, eyelids, lips, nostrils, and labia. Malignant sheep pox commonly occurs in goats and lambs, characterized by pox sores on hairless skin, respiratory mucosa, and oral cavity. This form has a mortality rate of 50-80% . Benign sheep pox, more commonly seen in adult animals, presents mild skin lesions and a low mortality rate of approximately 5-10% . Infected sheep develop pox sores, with varying degrees of congestion and edema surrounding the lesions. Tissue lesions resemble those of other poxvirus infections, characterized by acanthocyte hyperplasia and edema, with eosinophilic inclusions of varying sizes in the cytoplasm. This condition must be distinguished from contagious abscesses and foot-and-mouth disease. Diagnosis is primarily based on laboratory tests.

Caprine arthritis encephalitis (CAE): Caprine arthritis encephalitis, also known as caprine arthritis encephalitis, is caused by the caprine arthritis encephalitis virus (CAE), a member of the Lentivirinae subfamily of the Retroviridae family. It is very similar to the ovine Maedi-visna virus and causes similar symptoms in various natural hosts. Approximately 80% of sheep flocks in the United States, Western Europe, Australia, and New Zealand are contaminated, with fewer cases in Africa, possibly due to differences in milking methods. Antibody surveys have confirmed widespread infection in sheep in Taiwan. The primary route of infection is through the colostrum or milk of infected ewes. While transplacental transmission is possible, it is generally not considered the primary route. Horizontal transmission requires prolonged, close contact (more than several months).

Clinical symptoms of caprine arthritis encephalitis: Affected sheep develop arthritis, which progresses slowly over several months or even years. Arthritis primarily affects the joints, bursae, and tendon sheaths, most commonly in the knee joint. This type of disease typically occurs in adult sheep aged 2-9 years. If it occurs in lambs under 6 months of age, it often causes neurological symptoms, known as leukoencephalomyelitis (Infectious Caprine Encephalitis) or caprine Visna disease, with clinical manifestations of ataxia, lameness, and hind hind limb paralysis.

Diagnosis of caprine arthritis encephalitis: Diagnosis of this disease can be based on medical history, symptoms, gross visual examination, and histopathology. Infected sheep can be confirmed by serological methods (ELISA, indirect immunofluorescence staining, or immunodiffusion) or by isolating the virus from fetal synovial cells or primary goat testicular cells.

Prevention of caprine arthritis encephalitis: Immediately after birth, lambs should be separated from infected ewes and fed heat-treated (56 °C for one hour ) colostrum or colostrum from ewes not susceptible to the disease. Care should be taken to prevent lambs from coming into contact with any of the ewes' secretions. Lambs should be housed in well-ventilated isolation pens until weaning and fed goat milk, low-temperature sterilized cow's milk, or breast milk substitute that is free of the disease virus. Sheep that test positive for antibodies should be immediately culled. If two consecutive antibody tests show no positive results, the flock can be considered free of the disease. There is currently no vaccine available for this disease.

Contagious ovine ecthyme: Contagious ovine ecthyme, also known as contagious ecthyma, contagious pustular dermatitis, infectious dermatitis, or sore mouth, is a contagious ecthyma in sheep and goats caused by an Orf virus of the genus Parapoxvirus, subfamily Chordopoxvirinae, family Poxviridae. It is distinct from sheep pox and goat pox. Contagious ovine ecthyme is a common disease in both humans and animals. Contact with infected sheep can also cause localized lesions. In 1993 , the Changhua Show Chwan Hospital discovered that goat farmers who frequently came into contact with infected sheep developed multiple pus-filled nodules on their fingers.

Clinical symptoms of goat contact abscess: This disease primarily occurs on the lips of young goats and sheep, initially presenting as papules, blisters, and pustules that eventually form scabs. Lesions are most common on the lips, oral mucosa, and face, with lesions rarely seen on other skin sites. Rarely, they occur on the limbs (between the toes and around the hoof), nipples, and genitals . While the disease has a high incidence, its mortality rate is low. Newborn lambs and ewes often refuse to eat or nurse due to pain in the affected area, leading to emaciated lambs due to starvation. Secondary bacterial infection can affect growth or even lead to death. If there are no other secondary infections, recovery occurs in about a month.

Prevention of goat contagious abscess: This disease is transmitted through direct contact between sheep or through ingestion of contaminated feed or water. Infected sheep must be fully isolated, and shed scabs, contaminants, and housing must be disinfected. Antibiotics can also be administered to prevent bacterial secondary infection. Some countries use live virus vaccines to prevent this disease.

Mycoplasma arthritis of goats: Mycoplasma arthritis in goats, caused by infection with Mycoplasma spp., can cause high morbidity (100%) and mortality (10-30%) in goats. The disease is most common in late spring and summer. It occurs in all countries where goats are raised. Mycoplasma can infect goats of all ages, sexes, and breeds, but primarily affects young and yearling dairy goats. Affected goats often experience mastitis, agalactia, pneumonia, keratoconjunctivitis, and arthritis. Severe cases can lead to abortion, with minimal or no respiratory effects. In addition to arthritis, some goats may also experience corneal opacity and increased ocular and nasal discharge. Transmission occurs through direct contact with infected animals or contact with their secretions, such as milk, urine, tears, and nasal discharge. Transmission of the bacteria into the udder during lactation is a key mode of infection.

Clinical symptoms of mycoplasma arthritis in goats: Infected animals often develop septicemia, with clinical symptoms including fever (40°~42.5℃), lethargy, weakness, and coarse coat. Arthritis is usually multiple, with the wrist, tarsal, and knee joints most commonly affected. The joints will swell, become hot, and painful. Clinical symptoms last for about 4 to 10 days. If left untreated, the animal will generally die, though spontaneous recovery may occur occasionally. Pathological changes of this disease include joint swelling, and upon dissection, the accumulation of large amounts of synovial fluid and yellow-white pus in the joint cavity can be seen, as well as pulmonary edema, fibrinous pleurisy, and fibrinous pericarditis. Some cases may also present with serous or fibrinous peritonitis, meningitis, and mastitis.

Prevention of goat mycoplasma arthritis: This disease can be treated with antibiotics, mainly tetracyclines, tylosin erythromycin, oleandomycin, spiramycin, and Tiamulin. However, tiamulin has a severe irritating effect on the injection site when injected intramuscularly, so caution is required. The main transmission route of this disease is contact transmission, so the affected sheep must be isolated . At the same time, if a ewe develops mastitis, it is necessary to determine whether it is infected with mycoplasma. The affected ewe must not feed the lambs, and the newly born lambs must be fed with sterilized goat milk or artificial milk substitutes.
