Therefore, the control methods of this disease include (1) strictly removing the infected sheep, and the removal work should be carried out once a year or every six months to ensure the health of the flock. (2) Regular deworming. Goat ear mites play the role of a medium in the spread of mycoplasma, and can be dewormed with anthelmintics such as invermectin.

Caseous lymphadenitis: Caseous lymphadenitis, also known as pseudotuberculosis, is a chronic infectious disease that mimics tuberculosis. It is caused by the Gram-positive bacillus Corynebacterium pseudotuberculosis (Corynebacterium ovis), primarily infecting adult sheep and goats.

Clinical symptoms of caseous lymphadenitis include swelling of multiple lymph nodes throughout the body, with large amounts of thick, cheesy pus visible within these swollen nodes. In most dairy goats (75-87%), abscesses develop in the head and neck lymph nodes near the ears, jaw, and above the anterior scapula. Clinically, two spherical lumps are common in the neck. In severe cases, the swollen lymph nodes may rupture, discharging large amounts of yellowish-white pus. A cheesy, milky-white substance can be seen on cross-sections of swollen lymph nodes. In goats with systemic infection, abscesses of varying sizes may be seen in the lung parenchyma, spreading across the diaphragmatic or cardiac lobes, with accumulation of yellowish, milky material within the parenchyma. Clinically, this can lead to chronic coughing and dyspnea.

Prevention of caseous lymphadenitis: Surgical removal and drainage of mature superficial abscesses can be performed. Alternatively, antibiotics can be used. However, most antibiotics cannot penetrate the lymph nodes and kill the bacteria. Penicillin or tetracycline is generally used for naturally ruptured abscesses to prevent the bacteria from spreading or invading other lymph nodes.

Paratuberculosis (Johne's disease): Paratuberculosis, also known as Johne's disease, chronic bacterial enteritis, or chronic proliferative enteritis, is caused by infection with the acid-fast bacterium Mycobacterium paratuberculosis (M. johnei), resulting in chronic wasting diarrhea in ruminants.

Clinical symptoms of paratuberculosis: This disease typically occurs in adult sheep. Goats infected with this bacterium do not develop the persistent watery diarrhea seen in cattle. Affected sheep appear thin, lethargic, and depressed, with intermittent, intractable diarrhea and progressive weight loss. This disease is economically important. In dairy sheep, silent infection can lead to decreased milk production, significant losses such as prolonged interconception intervals, mastitis, and reproductive failure. Affected sheep display cachexia, such as hypoproteinemia and subcutaneous edema of the jaw. Gross lesions include localized or diffuse edema or thickening of the intestinal wall in the ileum, cecum, and colon, and enlarged inter-enteric lymph nodes.

Prevention of paratuberculosis: The treatment effect of this disease is limited, so prevention is more important than treatment to eliminate the source of infection, eliminate carriers and strengthen feeding management as the only way.

Tuberculosis : Tuberculosis is a chronic, wasting zoonosis that primarily occurs in densely confined, poorly managed pastures. The causative organism is Mycobacterium tuberculosis ( M. bovis) and Mycobacterium avium (M. avium) , members of the Mycobacterium tuberculosis complex . Mycobacteria are Gram-positive bacteria, and acid-fast staining is a common method for identification. The pathogen can infect sheep through the respiratory tract, digestive tract, skin wounds, semen, and the placenta.

Clinical symptoms of tuberculosis: Sheep infected with tuberculosis usually do not show symptoms. Some infected sheep may show emaciation, anemia, cough, nasal discharge, or severe respiratory symptoms. Macroscopic lesions are mainly in the lung tissue and lymph nodes. The cross-section surface may show yellow-white cheesy material. Some nodules may show sand-like calcifications on the cross-section surface. In severe cases, the disease may spread through the blood and form caseous lesions in other organs or lymph nodes.

intradermal tuberculin test ( ITT ) is the primary method for tuberculosis prevention . 0.1ml of M. bovis purified protein derivatives ( PPD) is injected into the inner side of one of the folds at the base of the sheep's tail. 72 hours after injection, any swelling at the injection site is considered positive. Animals with a suspected positive tuberculin reaction should be retested between 14 and 60 days later. Animals still suspected of being positive or positive are also considered infected. Sheep farms must cooperate with local county or city livestock disease control centers to conduct regular tuberculosis testing, remove infected sheep, and maintain dry, well-ventilated, and properly disinfected sheep pens.

Bacterial pneumonia ( Pasteurella pneumonia): Pasteurella pneumonia is an acute and fatal disease caused by the Gram-negative bacillus Pasteurella multocida and Pasteurella haemolytica, which can cause severe pneumonia and even death in goats.

Clinical symptoms of bacterial pneumonia: Acute cases can cause a fever of 40.0 to 41.1°C, with purulent, viscous nasal fluid, difficulty breathing, and a wet cough. The mortality rate in goats can exceed 10%, with sudden death typically occurring after the onset of symptoms. Lesions primarily occur on the anterior ventral side of the lungs (apex, cardiac, and anterior ventral sides of the diaphragmatic lobes), appearing as dark red to grayish-white, firm lesions. These lesions are sometimes accompanied by fibrinous pleuropneumonia.

Prevention of bacterial pneumonia: The prevention and treatment of this disease include the use of antibiotics, improving ventilation in the shed, improving sheep nutrition and reducing stress.

Listeriosis: Listeriosis, also known as circling disease, is caused by the bacterium Listeria monocytogenes and is a sporadic disease common to humans and livestock. It can be isolated from the feces or circling contents of healthy cattle, pigs, dogs, and rats. This bacterium is highly resistant to external influences and can survive in food for several months, making it a significant foodborne pathogen.

Clinical symptoms of listeriosis include encephalitis, stillbirth, and mastitis. Symptoms of encephalitis include vestibular tremors, circling, head tilt, and falling to the ground with swimming-like movements of the limbs. Transmission is typically through ingestion of contaminated silage (pH exceeding 5.5). Gross lesions may reveal meningeal congestion or edema, increased cerebrospinal fluid, and focal necrosis in the brainstem.

Scrapie: First discovered in British sheep in 1730, it was quite prevalent in Central Europe. Affected sheep experience itching and may rub against walls, tree trunks, and fences. Symptoms include unsteady gait, tremors, blindness, falls, and ultimately death. The pathogen is a mutated protein called a prion. In 1982, Dr. Prusiner SB of the United States published in Science that the causative agent of scrapie is a particle consisting solely of protein and no nucleic acid. He named it the proteinaceous infection particle, or prion. Prions, abbreviated as PrPC , are found on the surface of many cells in both normal animals and humans . The normal structure of PrPC is four α- helices . However, for unknown reasons, two of the β- helices are misfolded into a β -plate-like structure. This misfolded prion is called PrPSc , with Sc standing for scrapie , and is both infectious and pathogenic. Because PrPS cannot be hydrolyzed by normal proteases, it accumulates in brain tissue, causing nerve cells to die and eventually develop spongiform cavities in the brain. Humans or animals can be infected with PrPSc through two main pathways: oral infection through contaminated food, where PrPSc is ingested and proliferates in the tonsils. The immune system, primarily B cells, carry PrPSc to peripheral lymph nodes and the spleen. Follicular dendritic cells then facilitate PrPSc replication, where B cells carry PrPSc to peripheral nerves, ultimately infecting brain nerve cells. Another route of infection is iatrogenic infection, where PrPSc is acquired through medical procedures such as hormone injections, blood transfusions, and surgical procedures. This pathogenic prion can infect peripheral nerves and ultimately brain nerve cells through the blood or lymph, causing spongiform encephalopathy.

Prevention of scrapie: This pathogen is quite resistant to traditional standard disinfection methods. Under the most adverse conditions, highly effective disinfectants are high-concentration sodium hypochlorite or NaOH solutions. When preparing tissue sections, formalin-fixed tissues should be treated with 98% formic acid for at least one hour to inactivate isomerized prion.

Diarrhea in young sheep: Diarrhea is often caused by viral (rotavirus infection), bacterial (colibacillosis, Salmonella, enterotoxemia), parasitic pathogens (nematode enteritis, cryptosporidiosis, piroplasmosis, coccidiosis), toxic substances such as mycotoxins, and nutritional (feed-induced pullorum). The onset of disease in young sheep can be divided into three age groups: birth to four weeks, four weeks to twelve weeks, and over twelve weeks, each with different etiologies. Climate and environmental factors often exacerbate the condition, making it difficult to treat and should not be ignored.

Treatment of diarrhea in young lambs: The key to treating diarrhea in lambs is maintaining a balanced physiological function. This includes first moving the lamb to a warm, dry place to maintain body temperature. Appropriate fluid therapy, including lactated Ringer's solution, sodium chloride (NaCl), potassium chloride (KCl), sodium bicarbonate (NaHCO₃), glucose, and glycine, should be administered to prevent dehydration, electrolyte imbalance, hypoglycemia, and acidosis. Treatment is oral or intravenous, depending on the severity of the diarrhea. In acute diarrhea, extreme caution should be exercised when feeding breast milk or milk substitutes to avoid digestive disturbances. Lactose in breast milk is difficult to digest, creating high osmotic pressure in the digestive tract and worsening diarrhea. Lambs with persistent diarrhea should be fed small amounts of milk to prevent starvation. Alternatively, an appropriate electrolyte solution can be mixed with a small amount of milk. If the diarrhea improves, milk can be slowly reintroduced, followed by frequent small amounts to prevent overloading the intestinal digestive system. On the other hand, appropriate administration of antibiotics, in addition to killing pathogens, can also prevent the occurrence of secondary bacterial pneumonia.

Prevention of diarrhea in young lambs: Isolate affected lambs or temporarily move healthy lambs away from the affected area. For viral diarrhea or Cryptosporidium infection, isolation is an important emergency preventive measure, as there is no specific treatment. Newborn lambs must receive colostrum within 6 hours of birth, and start feeding slowly at 2 weeks of age to activate the rumen and establish the appropriate rumen flora.

Indigestion in young sheep: It includes simple and toxic indigestion, and the main features are digestive dysfunction and varying degrees of diarrhea. (1) Improper feeding of pregnant ewes is the main cause of indigestion in young sheep. Especially in the late pregnancy, it directly affects the development of the fetus and seriously affects the quality of breast milk. Poor quality and small amount of breast milk not only cannot meet the development needs of young sheep, but also makes the already underdeveloped young sheep hungry, thus causing disease. (2) Improper feeding and management of ewes. Common animal house hygiene conditions are too poor, or they are cold and damp. Unclean nipples or feeding devices of lactating ewes, long-term lack of straw changes, resulting in feces accumulation, etc., are all predisposing factors for this disease. (3) Improper feeding of young sheep, such as feeding colostrum too late, artificial feeding at irregular times and quantities, which makes the young sheep in an uneven state of fullness and hunger and licks debris. If these factors are not improved, it will also cause comprehensive symptoms such as indigestion in young sheep.

Clinical symptoms of indigestion in young sheep typically occur during the lactation period, often shortly after birth, when colostrum is ingested . Symptoms gradually subside after two to three months of age. If the affected sheep are listless, have a decreased appetite, and have a normal or slightly low body temperature, and have loose, watery, pale yellow, grayish-white, or dark green feces with a sour or fishy odor, and contain curds or undigested feed, this may indicate simple indigestion. Toxic indigestion, on the other hand, can manifest as severe digestive disorders and malnutrition, with symptoms such as depression, sluggish eyesight, loss of appetite, general weakness, fever, and severe diarrhea. Feces contain large amounts of mucus and blood, with a foul and putrid odor. Persistent diarrhea can lead to anal relaxation, fecal incontinence, and sunken eyes due to dehydration. In the later stages of the disease, the affected animal suffers from hypothermia, cold extremities, cold ears, and nose, and eventually falls into a coma and dies.

Treatment of indigestion in young sheep: (1) Adjust gastrointestinal function, usually taking pepsin or yeast orally three times a day. If the diarrhea is watery and has a foul odor, astringents such as activated charcoal or lysozyme can be used to stop diarrhea. (2) Antibiotics or sulfonamides are given to inhibit bacteria and reduce inflammation. (3) For severe diarrhea, intravenous therapy is used to adjust metabolic acidosis and supplement nutrition. (4) After eliminating the cause of the disease, attention should be paid to the hygiene of the sheep house, keeping it dry, and the artificial feeding device should be disinfected.

Prevention of indigestion in lambs: (1) Improve the management of ewes and strengthen the care of lambs. Feed ewes with complete feeds and sufficient vitamins and trace elements. (2) Improve sanitary conditions during pregnancy, provide outdoor exercise, and allow lambs to eat colostrum as soon as possible. If breast milk is insufficient, artificial feeding can be used. Feeding utensils should be kept clean and disinfected regularly. The owner should develop the habit of freezing and storing colostrum, the more the better, in case of emergency. (3) Prevent lambs from catching cold and provide them with plenty of drinking water.

Malnutrition in young lambs refers to a systemic nutritional and metabolic disorder that results in poor development in young lambs, often caused by nutritional deficiencies in pregnant ewes. Because young lambs have weak immune systems, they often develop other illnesses, exacerbating their condition and leading to death. Malnutrition in pregnant ewes, especially in the late stages of multiple pregnancies, can lead to nutritional deficiencies in the dam and fetus, especially when the feed is deficient in protein, vitamins, inorganic salts such as calcium and phosphorus, and trace elements. This can cause nutritional deficiencies in both the dam and the fetus, severely impacting fetal development. Furthermore, reproductive malformation in ewes, poor semen quality in rams, and inbreeding can all contribute to malnutrition in young lambs.

Clinical symptoms of malnutrition in lambs: lambs are light in weight, lack subcutaneous fat, are too weak to walk, have a low body temperature, and most die within 10 days of birth.

Prevention and treatment of malnutrition in young lambs: Feed adequate colostrum or an appropriate amount of milk replacer. If the lamb cannot breastfeed, switch to artificial feeding. Again, it's important to freeze and store colostrum. Also, ensure heat preservation and use straw bedding to keep it dry and clean. Providing lactating ewes with a full diet can strengthen their physique and promote the secretion of good milk, which is a source of nutrition for the lambs.

Rickets : Rickets is a chronic disease of young animals characterized by incomplete bone calcification caused by vitamin D deficiency and impaired calcium-phosphorus metabolism during growth and development. It commonly occurs in piglets, lambs, and calves. It is primarily caused by vitamin D deficiency or insufficiency and an inappropriate calcium-phosphorus ratio. Vitamin D can only be utilized by the liver and kidneys through oxygenation, where it plays a role in bone calcium extraction and intestinal calcium absorption. Vitamin D deficiency can occur in breast milk or feed , particularly during winter housing periods when feeding coarse hay . Alternatively, chronic digestive disorders in lambs can prevent adequate absorption of the vitamins in breast milk or feed. Insufficient sunlight and physical inactivity reduce the ability of the skin to convert provitamin D to vitamin D , potentially impairing calcium absorption and bone mineralization. Calcium-phosphorus deficiency or insufficiency in the diet results in a calcium phosphate deficiency, preventing osteoblasts from completing calcification. An inappropriate calcium-phosphorus ratio (normally 1:1-2:1) impairs calcium absorption and calcification. In addition, insufficient breast milk, early weaning, high protein or fat content in the diet, excessive strontium and fluoride in feed or drinking water, or parathyroid dysfunction are all causes of this disease.

Clinical symptoms of rickets: Early symptoms include pica, digestive disorders, a tendency to lie prone, lameness, and slow growth and development. Swollen bones in the joints of the limbs, bowed or X-legged when standing, and beaded swelling of the ribs and costal cartilage. Other symptoms include spinal curvature and skull enlargement.

Prevention and treatment of rickets: Get plenty of sun exposure. Medical treatment often involves cod liver oil or vitamin D. Lambs should be given 5-10 ml of cod liver oil orally daily (but discontinue use if diarrhea develops). Additionally, intramuscular injections of 75,000-150,000 IU of vitamin A and vitamin D should be administered for 10-20 days. In severe cases, the dosage can be increased, and calcium supplements such as oral calcium carbonate or calcium lactate or intravenous calcium gluconate should be administered.

Neonatal Hypoglycemia: Normal blood sugar levels in lambs are generally 2.8-3.9 mmol/L (50-70 mg/100 ml). Hypoglycemia is characterized by a significant decrease in blood sugar levels, leading to brain and nerve dysfunction. This is primarily caused by incomplete feed intake during pregnancy, postpartum milk deficiency, or the inability of the lamb to suckle. After birth, lambs experience increased activity and increased sugar consumption. If there is a lack of sugar during the fetal period or if adequate sugar is not provided after birth, blood sugar levels can plummet to below 1.7 mmol/L (30 mg/100 ml), severely impacting brain function and causing excitation or inhibition of brain nerves.

Clinical symptoms of neonatal hypoglycemia: Symptoms occur in lambs under one week old, and are more common in lambs 14 days old. They include sudden loss of appetite, depression, slowed reflexes, weak limbs, and an unsteady gait, followed by collapse. Neurological symptoms include muscle tremors, spasms, opisthotonos, paddling, dilated pupils, and loss of light sensitivity, ultimately leading to coma, hypothermia, and death within 2-5 hours of onset. However, prompt recovery is possible with timely feeding or glucose injections.

Prevention and treatment of hypoglycemia in newborns and young animals: When symptoms occur and the condition is suspected, administer 20-40ml of 5-10% glucose and amino acids intravenously as soon as possible, repeated every 3-4 hours for 2-3 days. Additionally, administer 20-30ml of 20-50% glucose orally twice daily for several days, along with vitamin B complex and vitamin C.

Pneumonia in young sheep: The respiratory mucosa of young animals has greater vascular permeability, the lymph nodes around the pharynx are not fully developed, the bronchial glands secrete less mucus, and the ciliated epithelium has poor motility, resulting in weaker foreign body clearance and barrier defenses. Therefore, improper husbandry, sudden changes in weather, and cold weather can weaken the physiological defenses of young animals, allowing pathogens that invade the respiratory tract to proliferate and secrete toxins, damaging tissues and causing symptoms.

Clinical symptoms of pneumonia in young sheep: The disease typically occurs in lambs aged 1-3 months. Symptoms include a fever between 39.5-41 °C, a rapid and weak pulse, rapid and labored breathing, coughing, and a serous, mucous, or mucopurulent nasal discharge. Diarrhea may also occur. Common causes include pasteurization , chlamydia pneumonia , and mycoplasmapneumonia .

Prevention and Treatment of Pneumonia in Young Sheep: Affected sheep should be housed in a clean, well-ventilated barn. Enrofloxacin or antibiotics such as Ceftiofur or Florfenicol should be administered intravenously with saline solution twice daily to replenish dehydration. In addition, general symptomatic treatments, cough suppressants, expectorants, anti-inflammatory, analgesic, antipyretic, and cardiotonic agents should be used.

Intestinal spasms in lambs: Intestinal spasms are intermittent abdominal pain caused by spasmodic contractions of intestinal smooth muscle due to factors such as cold weather. They often occur in lactating lambs, especially when they begin to graze, drink water, and ruminate. The incidence is high, and if left untreated, they can lead to intussusception, intestinal ectopy, and death. While the primary cause is cold weather stimulation, intestinal spasms can also be caused by improper husbandry and management, such as lambs ingesting rancid milk or moldy, indigestible feed, malnutrition in ewes, low-quality milk, and frequent starvation in lambs.

Clinical symptoms of intestinal spasms in lambs include cold ears and nose, hypothermia, pale mucous membranes, standing with an arched back, and lying in a curled-up position. During abdominal pain, the lamb may look back at the abdomen, kick with its hind legs, and sometimes assume a urinary posture. In severe cases, the lamb may suddenly jump and cry continuously before returning to a quiet state.

Prevention and treatment of lamb intestinal spasms: Lambs affected by the disease can be treated with intramuscular or intravenous injections of the analgesic Buscopan (0.2 mg/kg) three times daily, or the nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory analgesic Flunixin (1.1-2.2 mg/kg). For larger lambs, a warmed mixture of isotonic glucose and 0.5% procaine solution can be injected intraperitoneally. Applying a warm compress to the affected abdomen and administering warm milk simultaneously can achieve rapid results. Ensure lambs are kept warm, avoid starvation, and monitor their diet.

Osteodystrophia fibrosa in goats : The most common skeletal disease in goats is Osteodystrophia fibrosa in goats. Osteodystrophia fibrosa is a nutritional bone metabolic disease also known as Osteitis fibrosa cystica . Osteodystrophia fibrosa is often caused by a diet high in phosphorus and low in calcium, leading to hypocalcemia and hyperphosphatemia. This leads to nutritionally induced hyperparathyroidism, which causes excessive bone resorption and the pathological changes in bone tissue associated with Osteodystrophia fibrosa. Inexperienced goat farmers, unaware of their goats' nutritional needs, often feed them only soy dregs for convenience. This leads to an imbalance in the calcium-phosphorus ratio in the blood, triggering secondary hyperparathyroidism, which in turn causes calcium reabsorption from the bones, leading to the massive lysis of bone cells and their replacement by fibrous connective tissue, resulting in osteoporosis.

Clinical symptoms of fibrous osteodystrophy in goats: The most common sites of the disease are the upper and lower jaws. Affected animals often experience breathing difficulties due to swelling of the facial bones compressing the nasal passages. Swelling of the upper and lower jaws also makes it difficult for these animals to bite, leading to excessive salivation. Excessive mineral loss reduces bone structure, replacing it with fibrous connective tissue, resulting in a very soft bone. Goats have the highest incidence of the disease among ruminants, believed to be related to their difficulty regulating blood calcium and organic phosphorus levels.

Pregnancy toxemia (ketosis): Pregnancy toxemia, also known as ketonemia, is a metabolic disease in pregnant ewes, often occurring in lactating sheep before delivery. It causes black, tarry diarrhea, anal fouling, depression, anorexia, sunken eyes, inability to stand, and ultimately death. This condition occurs when the rapid growth of triplets in late pregnancy squeezes the abdominal cavity, leading to insufficient food intake and hypoglycemia. This causes the ewes to break down their own body fat and tissue to supply blood sugar, resulting in elevated acetone levels, ketonemia, ketonuria, proteinuria, and neurological symptoms. Autopsies of deceased triplets reveal enlarged, yellowish livers.

Prevention and Treatment of Pregnancy Toxemia: Affected ewes can be treated with intravenous glucose for several days. Affected ewes may develop acidemia, which can be prevented by administering sodium bicarbonate. During pregnancy, special attention should be paid to nutritional support, such as increased energy and protein intake during the last two months of pregnancy.

Goiter: There are five causes of goiter: (1) iodine deficiency; (2) ingestion of grasses that cause goiter; (3) ingestion of substances containing thiocyanate, such as crucifers; (4) ingestion of goitrin, such as iodized thiooxalyl sulfide and cabbage seeds; (5) blocking hormone formation; damaging the metabolic pathways of the thyroid gland, etc.

The causes of iodine-deficiency goiter are as follows: (1) iodine deficiency in soil; (2) iodine deficiency in drinking water or feed; (3) It is more common in Boer and Angra goats. If the feed of a pen-raised ewe is iodine-deficient (no salt added to the feed), the iodine stored in the body will be consumed during lactation and pregnancy. If the mother becomes pregnant again, the iodine necessary for the development and growth of the fetus will not be supplied, causing compensatory hyperplasia of the fetus's thyroid gland and enlargement.

Clinical symptoms of goiter: In severe cases of iodine deficiency, the fetus fails to develop, resulting in death and miscarriage. Aborted fetuses are underdeveloped and hairless. Iodine-deficient goiter in lambs born to grazing ewes is often a regional disease caused by iodine-deficient soil or high levels of calcium, iron, and other minerals in drinking water.

Prevention and treatment of goiter: (1) Iodine deficiency: Supplement iodine; provide iodized salt in the feed of pregnant ewes. (2) Congenital: During pregnancy, ewes should avoid grasses of the genus Clerodendrum or supplement with iodine. Lactating sheep should be provided with 0.8 ppm iodine; if there are cruciferous plants in the grazing area, increase the iodine supply to 2 ppm; the general iodine supply for sheep is 1.3 ppm.

Urinary stones in rams: Most urinary stones in sheep form in the bladder as silicates. Urinary stones are carried by urine into the bladder and can be carried to the narrow S-bend of the urethra or the urethral process at the end of the urethra, causing blockage. Improper calcium-phosphorus ratios in feed, insufficient vitamin A supplementation, and frequent contamination of drinking fountains, resulting in insufficient water for sheep, can all contribute to urinary stone blockage in rams.

Prevention and treatment of urethral stones in rams: Use lidocaine to anesthetize and relax the urethral mucosa, attempting to expel the stone. After urethrotomy, rams are no longer suitable for breeding. To prevent and treat stones, attention should be paid to the calcium-phosphorus ratio (Ca:P = 2-2.5:1) and vitamin A supply to prevent bladder epithelial sloughing. Furthermore, drinking troughs should be cleaned regularly to ensure that the sheep have access to clean water at all times, preventing urine from being stored in the bladder for long periods of time, which will naturally reduce the risk of stone formation. Adding 4% salt to the feed can increase water intake and urine output. Since phosphate and carbonate stones are more likely to form in alkaline urine, ammonium chloride can be added to increase urine acidity to prevent stone formation.

Acute bloat: A symptom of rapid and excessive gas production in the rumen and recombinant stomach. Causes include: overeating easily fermentable foods or foods low in fiber ; paralysis of the rumen and recombinant stomach muscles ; excessive foaming in the stomach, which hinders the excretion of belching gas ; esophageal obstruction or paralysis ; lymphadenopathy or tumor compression of the esophagus caused by lymphocytic leukemia ; rumen motility disorders; insufficient exercise or general weakness in the sheep ; drinking too quickly after feeding or grazing; and strenuous exercise after feeding.

Clinical symptoms of acute bloat include rapid abdominal distension, particularly on the left side, and rumen cessation. Rumination ceases, with frequent belching and occasional vomiting. Defecation occurs initially but ceases in later stages. Respiration is rapid, with the forelimbs extended and motionless. Rumen compression causes blood flow obstruction, palpitations, a rapid pulse, bluish-purple mucous membranes, cold ears and extremities, sweating, and suffocation. Severe cases can lead to death within a few hours, but some patients recover spontaneously after half a day or a day.

Prevention and treatment of acute bloat: (1) Raise the forelimbs, massage the flanks, and drive the sheep uphill to contract the stomach and release gas. (2) Pull the tongue up and down to help with belching. Alternatively, use a nasogastric tube to remove accumulated gas. (3) Stomach puncture. (4) Parasympathetic stimulants. (5) Intragastric instillation of anti-foaming agents, such as peanut oil. (6) After the acute symptoms subside, pay attention to feeding and management to prevent recurrence. Reduce the amount of food, do not give grass, and give bitter stomachic or laxatives. Feed hay before grazing.

The importance of sheep house planning: A good breeding environment can improve the growth and reproduction performance of sheep, and provide convenient working space for managers. More importantly, it can enable the sheep farming business to operate perfectly while taking into account the ecological balance. The so-called good breeding environment here refers to the sheep house and its surrounding related facilities.

The first challenge encountered when establishing a sheep farm is sheep shed design. The complexity of sheep shed design is closely related to the capital required to establish the farm, as well as the subsequent husbandry, management, disease control, and waste management. In other words, sheep shed design is closely linked to the success or failure of the farm. For example, poorly designed sheep sheds can lead to inefficient farm management, difficulty controlling diseases, and increased difficulty in wastewater treatment, all of which can reduce profits or even lead to losses for sheep farmers. Therefore, sheep shed design plays a crucial role in the future success or failure of a sheep farm. While sheep shed design is important, it must be complemented by good husbandry and management. However, it is difficult to find a perfect sheep shed, so coordination among all aspects is crucial for sheep shed users.

The key to sheep housing planning is profitability for sheep farmers. However, regardless of the amount of profit, one should not consider investing all capital or profits in building or renovating sheep housing. Doing so often leads to capital constraints, especially when sheep prices are low after a large investment, often rendering the farm unsustainable. Therefore, before renovating a sheep housing facility, one should carefully consider whether existing sheep housing problems can be overcome through improved husbandry and management practices. If this is possible and economically justifiable, it is best not to renovate the sheep housing facility.

Flexibility in sheep house planning: Even if we do not consider the cost at all, there is no perfect sheep house so far, because under different requirements or to achieve different purposes, sheep houses need to be designed with expedients. The factors that affect this are as follows: (1) Different objects of consideration: Some facilities are quite convenient for sheep farmers, but they are not necessarily beneficial to sheep, and vice versa. (2) The level of wages: The level of wages will affect the planning of sheep houses. For example: When wages are low, the equipment in the sheep house does not necessarily have to be automated, because automation will consume a lot of investment costs; but when wages are high, automated equipment in the sheep house will help reduce production costs. (3) The amount of funds: When funds are not very abundant, if most of the funds are invested in the sheep house, it will be detrimental to the normal operation of the sheep farm. (4) Different purposes of sheep house establishment: For example, the use of mesh beds in small sheep houses is safer than strip beds. (5) Environmental differences: In coastal areas, it is best to avoid using iron materials to build sheep sheds. This is because the air in coastal areas is high in salt, and this high salt content will accelerate the rusting of iron. (6) Differences in feeding sections: Some sheep farms raise ewes and lambs together, while other sheep farms raise ewes and lambs in separate groups. Therefore, there are naturally differences in the design of sheep sheds.

Basic principles of sheep house planning: Although sheep house planning has its own flexibility, its basic principles remain unchanged. They include: (1) Considering future development in advance: If there are plans to expand the scale of the sheep farm, future development should be carefully considered when building the sheep house. For example, if the sheep house is small, a plan should be made for how all the sheep houses will be arranged after future expansion. Another example is that if the sheep house is small, it is likely that the same sheep house will have both farrowing pens and mature sheep pens. For future development, consideration should be given at the beginning of the farm construction to how to easily convert the former to the latter, or the latter to the former. This is because after the sheep farm expands, for the convenience of management, the same sheep house is generally used to house the same period of sheep, so the original sheep house must be uniformly modified. (2) An environment suitable for the growth and reproduction of sheep: Only an appropriate environment for the growth and reproduction of sheep can achieve the best results for the sheep and make profits for the sheep farmer. (3) Convenient Operation and Labor Savings: A sheep house is not only a breeding environment for sheep, but also a place for managers to operate. Therefore, when designing a sheep house, considerations should be given to labor saving and reducing the risk of accidents. Saving labor not only reduces breeding costs, but more importantly, only when the house is conveniently operated can staff have more time to focus on sheep health and other management tasks. (4) Compatible with breeding and management methods: Breeding and management methods are changing rapidly, and many of them require appropriate sheep houses. Therefore, if a sheep farmer wishes to adopt a specific breeding and management method, the sheep house required for this method should be included in the design when designing the sheep house. (5) Good Sanitation Planning: When designing a sheep house, consideration must be given to maintaining a hygienic environment within the farm, including planning disinfection routes for personnel and sheep entering and exiting, and arranging the movement of sheep in and out of the house. For example, whether an "all-in, all-out" approach should be adopted to meet hygiene requirements. (6) Avoiding Facilities that Harm the Physical and Mental Safety of Sheep: The future trend in sheep farming is to provide a safe and welfare environment for sheep, and this trend also brings benefits to sheep farmers. (7) Convenient waste management: With the increasing number of sheep raised by sheep farmers, waste management has become a key issue in sheep barn planning. Only good management can prevent environmental pollution, the spread of disease, pollution of the surrounding environment, and low work morale. Proper waste management measures can also bring benefits, such as the reuse of feces and urine. (8) The most economical construction cost: This does not refer to the cheapest construction cost, but rather the most favorable construction cost after comprehensively considering factors such as the sheep environment, labor costs, waste management, and climate, such as typhoons.

Site selection: This includes two scenarios: selecting new land where there is no existing land, and arranging the entire sheep farm's location on existing land. The following summarizes the considerations for both scenarios: (1) Area: When selecting a site, consider the total area required for the sheep farm, the size of the pasture, future expansion plans, waste disposal facilities, and workshops. (2) Water Source and Water Quality: A sufficient water source and good water quality are essential for a successful sheep farm. In addition to drinking water, sheep farms also need water for flushing and other purposes. Water quality not only refers to the amount of minerals contained in the water, but also whether the water contains toxic organic substances, such as pesticides and herbicides, and whether the water source contains high bacterial counts. This is especially important if there are many pastures upstream of the river, as too many pastures can easily transmit diseases from other pastures to the farm. (3) Drainage and Wastewater Treatment: The treated wastewater from the sheep farm must have an outlet. The treated wastewater should be discharged into streams without contaminating the water source. When establishing a larger ranch on a hillside, drainage from the entire sheep shed can utilize the existing slope. Wastewater and rainwater should be drained separately, so these factors should also be considered when selecting a site. (IV) Sheep Sheep Orientation: 1. Sheep sheds should be oriented east-west, facing north-south. Sheep sheds with an east-west orientation and facing north-south are preferred. Avoid slopes facing east or west. This is because sheds on such slopes are exposed to the sun for one morning or afternoon each day, which can cause heat stress and prevent good performance. 2. Sheep sheds should be located on slopes that are sheltered from the wind. Choose slopes or land with little north wind or sheltered from the north wind. Generally, south-facing slopes are preferred, as wind speeds increase cold stress in cold weather. 3. Avoid humid areas. Unless you live in an area with heavy winter rain, avoid building sheep sheds in areas with heavy winter rain, as the damp and cold weather is not conducive to sheep production. 4. Avoid using steel building materials in coastal areas. When choosing a coastal area, it is important to avoid using building materials that are easily corroded by salt, such as steel. (V) Others: 1. Convenient transportation: Convenient for the transportation of feed and sheep, as well as for the movement of personnel. 2. Close to a power source. 3. Avoid being too close to other sheep farms. Sheep insulation and water supply systems require electricity.

Basic information for estimating sheep farm facilities: The sheep house is composed of sheep of different growth ages or physiological stages. In order to divide these sheep into different livestock houses, the following basic information of the sheep must be available, and the amount of livestock houses for each stage can be calculated based on this information. However, when planning livestock houses in general, the data used should be relatively loose so as to avoid the phenomenon of insufficient livestock house space when it is used.

The following basic information on livestock housing also lists the allowances used in sheep housing design. 1. Conception rate for ewes: Approximately 90.5% ; for females: 61.2% . To prevent excessively successful breeding performance and the resulting lack of space for sheep, allowances of 95% and 70% are used for the housing calculations . 2. Lamb-carrying period for ewes: 35-70 days , calculated as 45 days. 3. Ratio of rams to ewes: This varies from ranch to ranch, ranging from 1 : 5 to 1:45 . The recommended ratio is between 1:20 and 1:25 . 4. Average litter size: This ranges from 1.27 to 2.28 , with a recommended range of 1.5 to 1.8 . 5. Lamb survival rate: Lamb mortality is approximately 0 % to 28.6% , with an average of 6.56% . Using a loose value of 7-10% , the survival rate is calculated as 93-90% . 6. Ewe Survival Rate: The mortality rate for ewes is generally 0-4.3% , with an average mortality rate of approximately 1.31% . Therefore, the recommended survival rate is 95-98% . 7. Age at estrus: The average age for females is 7-10 months, and for rams is 4-8 months. However, light breeding is recommended between 8-10 months, with intensive breeding between 8-10 months. The estrus cycle for sheep is approximately 18-22 days ( generally estimated to be 21 days ) , with an estrus period of 12-36 hours.

The space required for sheep is divided into two categories: ( 1 ) Body size at different ages: The body size of sheep varies across breeds and ages. However, for the purpose of calculating barn space, the size of the largest Boer goat currently available can be used as a reference . Figure 9-4-2 shows the weight of Boer goats at different ages. According to a survey of Khari sheep, a body size of 40-50 kg results in a height and length range of 57-80 cm and 58-80 cm, respectively. ( 2 ) Space required per sheep: The Danish recommended space requirement for each ewe is 10.67 m² , with a maximum of 2.1 m² . For ewes with lambs, this area is 0.91 m² , with a maximum of 6.5 m² (Andersen , 2002) . The generally recommended housing space is as follows: 1. Rams: 1.9-3.1 m² solid floor per ram , 1.3-1.9 m² striped floor per ram; 2. Dry ewes: 1.1-1.5 m² solid floor per ram, 0.7-0.9 m² striped floor per ram; 3. Ewes with lambs: 1.4-1.9 m² solid floor per ram, 0.9-1.1 m² striped floor per ram; 4. Meat sheep: 0.7-0.9 m² solid floor per ram, 0.4-0.5 m² striped floor per ram. The farrowing pen should be 1.8 x 1.2 m² (Wathes and Charles, 1994).

The feeding trough and hay rack space required for each sheep: Denmark recommends a feeding trough space of 37 cm for each sheep. The generally recommended feeding trough space is as follows: 1. Rams: limited to 30 (cm/head), free to feed 15 (cm/head); 2. Dry ewes: limited to 40-50 (cm/head), free to feed 10-15 (cm/head); 3. Ewes with lambs: limited to 40-50 (cm/head), free to feed 15-20 (cm/head); 4. Meat sheep: limited to 23-30 (cm/head), free to feed 2.5-5 (cm/head).

The drinking space required for each sheep is as follows: The generally recommended drinking space is as follows: rams (180-300 pounds): 10 drinking bowls (number of sheep/drinking bowl), 10 nipple drinkers (number of sheep/nipple drinkers), 2 drinking troughs (number of sheep/30 cm); dry ewes (150-200 pounds): 40-50 drinking bowls (number of sheep/drinking bowl), 40-50 nipple drinkers (number of sheep/nipple drinkers), 15-25 drinking troughs (number of sheep/30 cm); Ewes with lambs (5-30 lbs): 40-50 drinking bowls (number of lambs/drinking bowl), 40-50 nipple drinkers (number of lambs/nipple drinkers), 15-25 drinking troughs (number of lambs/30 cm); Meat sheep (30-110 lbs): 50-75 drinking bowls (number of lambs/drinking bowl), 50-75 nipple drinkers (number of lambs/nipple drinkers), 25-40 drinking troughs (number of lambs/30 cm).

Required sheep sheds: 1. If the ratio of male and female sheep is 1:20 , 200 + 20 = 10 sheep, and the male sheep should rest for three days after every three days of mating. 2. Farrowing shed: (1) If the lambs are weaned at 45 days old and the ewes are in estrus year-round, eight batches of lambs can be raised per year, 200 + 8 = 25 lambs, and the ewes need about 25 × 110% = 28 pens. (2) If the lambs are weaned at 30 days old and the ewes are in estrus from July to February, eight batches of lambs can be raised per year , 200 ÷ 8 = 25 lambs , and the ewes need about 25 × 110% = 28 pens. 3. Meat sheep: If each litter is twins, 25 × 2 = 50 lambs / pen. If the farrowing shed (1) is used, a maximum of six batches of lambs, or 300 lambs, are needed before the first batch of meat sheep is sold . If the farrowing shed (2) is used , 50 × 8 = 400 lambs need to be raised .

Required feed storage: 200 ewes + 20 rams + 400 meat sheep = 620 sheep. If the average sheep consumes 0.7 kg of concentrate, then the weekly concentrate requirement is 620 kg x 0.7 kg x 7 days = 434 kg x 7 days = 3,038 kg. If the average sheep consumes 3-3.5% of their body weight minus 0.7 kg of concentrate, then a 60 kg sheep x 3.5% - 0.7 kg = 2.1 kg - 0.7 kg = 1.4 kg. 200 sheep x 1.4 kg x 7 days (purchased hay) = 1,960 kg/week, or 200 sheep x 1.4 kg x 90 days (silage or hay storage for three months) = 25,200 kg. Therefore, a sheep farm would require approximately five 600 kg concentrate storage silos and enough hay storage space to store 1,960 kg or 25,200 kg, depending on the design of the hay storage space.

Grazing Area Required: The grazing area required is based on the number of sheep. The approximate daily hay requirement per sheep, grass production (the soil and season are factors), and the available land area are used to calculate the number of sheep that can be raised, the required land area, or the number of days a fixed number of sheep can be grazed. Actual conditions may be more complex than the above, for example, if supplemental concentrate or roughage is required in addition to grazing, or if grazing is supplemented with purchased forage.

For example, a sheep farmer with one hectare of land planted with pangu grass can produce 80-150 metric tons of fresh grass or 15-28 metric tons of hay annually. Each adult sheep requires approximately 5-8 kg of grass per day and 2-2.5 kg of hay in winter (approximately 11-13 kg of grass). If the season lasts for four months and the season lasts for eight months, then the annual feed requirements for each sheep are: Hot season: 245 days x 5 or 8 = 1225 kg or 1960 kg. Cool season: 120 days x 11 or 13 = 1320 kg or 1560 kg. Or, 1225 kg + 1320 kg = 2545 kg. Total: 1960 kg + 1560 kg = 3520 kg. This means that raising one sheep requires approximately 2545 kg or 3520 kg of grass per year, or 2.5 tons or 3.5 tons of hay. A hectare producing 80-150 metric tons of grass per year is sufficient for sheep farming. 80 or 120 metric tons ÷ 2.5 = 32 or 48 heads; 80 or 120 metric tons ÷ 3.5 = 22 or 34 heads, that is, the pangu grass produced on poor land can support 22-32 sheep, and the pangu grass produced on good land can support 34-48 sheep.

Striped floor: Coart (1983) pointed out based on experiments that the strip width of 6 cm and the spacing between the strips of 1.5 cm are the best striped floor design for pig houses. Except for plastic mesh floors which are not suitable for sheep houses, the metal mesh used in pig houses can be used in sheep houses.

A cross-section of a large sheep shed design, featuring manual manure scraping. The netting on both sides of the shed primarily prevents sheep feces from entering the ditch, though urine can enter. Automatic manure scraping equipment is also planned for the future.

A general diagram of the farrowing house. The key point is that the farrowing house does not use striped floors, but mainly uses metal mesh floors to prevent the lambs' feet from sinking into the striped floors and getting injured.

A partial plan view of the farrowing pen emphasizes that the door opening should be oriented in the direction sheep enter and exit the pen. Ideally, the door and aisle should be of equal width, so that once the door is opened, the sheep are forced to enter and exit through this door. The diagram also clearly indicates the presence of three aisles, allowing the shepherd to feed the ewes from the center aisle and feed and manage the lambs from the side aisles. Separate feeding troughs prevent ewes from stealing the lambs' feed. The water troughs are positioned so that both ewes and lambs have access to water, eliminating the need for separate lamb drinking fountains.

70cm height of the outer aisle pen , making it easy for staff to operate. The trough and farrowing pen are separated by galvanized iron pipes ( which can be movable ) , primarily to prevent ewes from entering the lambing area.

The key to the design of the feeding trough is that the height should be kept to a minimum, approximately 45 cm. Diamond-shaped mesh is used underneath to prevent the sheep from slipping out, while ample space (approximately 36 cm) is provided above the trough for the sheep's heads to reach out and feed. The trough's height of 18 cm and width of 30 cm represent the maximum commercially available large water pipe dimensions. Using this large pipe as a water trough is also a good option. While a 110 cm high pen can be prone to jumping out for some sheep, the design of the pen's top allows for a 25 cm clearance to prevent such an escape.

The key elements of a hay rack's cross-section design are the height from the ground and the height of the rack itself. These values can vary depending on the specific sheep. The second key element is the spacing between the bars of the hay rack. Currently, a typical spacing of 15 cm is used. This allows for easier access to the grass and minimizes wasted grass.
